# 往生要集
## Essentials of Birth in the Pure Land
Taishō Tripiṭaka No. 2682
Composed by Genshin (源信) of Mount Hiei

---

## Preface

[p.0033] *Essentials of Birth in the Pure Land*, Upper Fascicle. Composed by the Śramaṇa Genshin of the Shuryōgon-in at Mount Hiei, Tendai.

The teaching and practice of birth in the Land of Utmost Bliss are the eyes and feet of this degenerate age at the end of the Dharma. Among monastics and laity, the highborn and the humble, who would not take refuge in them? Yet the exoteric and esoteric teachings are found in no single text, and the karmic causes, whether pertaining to principle or to practice, are exceedingly numerous. For those of sharp wisdom and vigorous effort, this may pose no difficulty; but for one as dull and foolish as myself, how could I dare attempt it?

For this reason, relying on the single gate of buddha-recollection, I have made a modest collection of essential passages from the sūtras and treatises. To open them and to practice them is easy to understand and easy to carry out. In all there are ten gates, divided into three fascicles:

1. Loathing the defiled world
2. Aspiring to the Pure Land
3. Evidence for the Land of Utmost Bliss
4. The correct practice of buddha-recollection
5. Auxiliary methods of buddha-recollection
6. Buddha-recollection on special occasions
7. The benefits of buddha-recollection
8. Scriptural evidence for buddha-recollection
9. Various practices for birth
10. Questions and answers for summary evaluation

I place this at my side to guard against negligence and forgetfulness.

---

## Gate One: Loathing the Defiled World

The three realms offer no peace; they are most worthy of loathing and renunciation. To set forth these characteristics, there are in all seven topics: (1) the hells, (2) hungry ghosts, (3) animals, (4) asuras, (5) humans, (6) celestial beings, and (7) a general summation.

---

### 1. The Hells

The hells are divided into eight: (1) the Hell of Repetition, (2) the Hell of Black Ropes, (3) the Hell of Crushing Together, (4) the Hell of Screaming, (5) the Hell of Great Screaming, (6) the Hell of Scorching Heat, (7) the Hell of Great Scorching Heat, and (8) the Hell Without Interval.

#### (1) The Hell of Repetition (*Saṃjīva*)

This hell lies one thousand yojanas beneath this Jambudvīpa, extending ten thousand yojanas in length and breadth. The sinners within it constantly harbor thoughts of mutual harm. When they catch sight of one another, it is as when a hunter encounters a deer. Each tears at the other with iron claws until flesh and blood are utterly consumed and only bare bones remain.

Or else the wardens of hell, grasping iron staffs and iron cudgels, beat them from head to foot until their bodies are shattered like grains of sand. Or they take exceedingly sharp knives and slice the flesh piece by piece, as a cook butchers fish and meat. Then a cool wind blows, and at once the sinners revive as before, only to rise suddenly and suffer the same torments again.

Some say a voice resounds from the sky: "May these sentient beings return to life once more!" Others say the wardens of hell strike the ground with iron pitchforks and cry out: "Live! Live!"

Such is the reckoning of time in this place: ten years here equal one day and night in the Heaven of the Four Great Kings. The lifespan there is five hundred years. Taking the full lifespan of the Heaven of the Four Great Kings as one day and night in this hell, the lifespan of this hell is five hundred years. (The *Upāsakaśīla Sūtra* states that one year of the first heaven equals one day and night of the first hell, and so on for each successive level.)

Outside the four gates of this hell, there are furthermore sixteen subsidiary places of punishment:

**First, the Place of Excrement and Mire.** There is scalding-hot excrement-mire of the most bitter taste. It swarms with diamond-beaked worms. Sinners are submerged within it, feeding on the burning excrement, while the worms gather together, all devouring at once: breaking the skin, gnawing the flesh, snapping the bones, and sucking out the marrow. Those who in former lives killed deer and slaughtered birds fall into this place.

**Second, the Place of Sword Wheels.** Iron walls surround it on all sides, ten yojanas high. Raging fire blazes ceaselessly within. The fires of the human world, compared with this, are like snow. The moment a body touches this fire, it is pulverized as fine as mustard seeds. Moreover, burning iron rains down like a torrent. There are also sword-forests with blades of the utmost sharpness, and showers of blades fall like rain. All these torments converge at once, beyond any endurance. Those who in former lives killed living beings out of greed for possessions fall into this place.

**Third, the Place of Cauldron Cooking.** Sinners are seized and thrown into iron cauldrons, where they are boiled like beans. Those who in former lives killed living beings and cooked their flesh fall into this place.

**Fourth, the Place of Manifold Sufferings.** In this hell there are ten billion kinds of countless agonies, impossible to describe in full. Those who in former lives bound people with ropes, beat people with sticks, drove people to march on distant roads, pushed people off precipices, tormented people with smoke, or frightened small children, along with all who afflicted others in such various ways, all fall into this place.

**Fifth, the Place of Darkness.** Within utter blackness, they are perpetually burned by dark fire. Mighty gales blow against diamond mountains that grind and crush together, reducing all to scattered sand. Burning winds slice like sharp blades. Those who in former lives smothered the mouths and noses of sheep, or placed turtles between two bricks and pressed them to death, fall into this place.

**Sixth, the Place of No Joy.** Great conflagrations blaze day and night. Birds with scorching beaks, dogs, foxes, and jackals, uttering the most hideous cries, extremely terrifying, constantly come to feed upon the sinners. Bones and flesh lie scattered everywhere. Diamond worms burrow through the bones and devour the marrow. Those who in former lives blew conches and beat drums to make terrifying sounds for the purpose of killing birds and beasts fall into this place.

**Seventh, the Place of Extreme Suffering.** Beneath sheer cliffs, sinners are perpetually burned by iron fire. Those who in former lives set fires upon mountains...

#### (2) The Hell of Black Ropes (*Kālasūtra*)

This hell lies below the Hell of Repetition, with the same dimensions as before. Wardens seize the sinners and lay them upon the burning iron ground, then stretch blazing iron ropes lengthwise and crosswise across their bodies. With burning iron axes they cut along the ropes. Some they saw apart; others they butcher with knives, hacking into hundreds and thousands of pieces scattered all about.

Furthermore, blazing iron ropes are strung crisscrossing without number. Sinners are driven among them. Foul winds blow violently, tangling the ropes about their bodies. On the burning mountains, iron pillars are erected at each summit, with iron ropes stretched between them. Below the ropes, intense heat rages. Sinners are forced to carry iron mountains on their backs and walk along the ropes. They fall from a great height, and all their sufferings are multiplied tenfold.

The wardens of hell rebuke the sinners, saying:

> The mind is the foremost enemy.
> This enemy is the most wicked of all.
> This enemy can bind a person
> And deliver them to the court of Yama.
> You burn alone in hell,
> Devoured by your own evil karma.
> Your wife and children cannot follow.

All the sufferings of the preceding hells are multiplied here tenfold. The reckoning of time: the lifespan of Trāyastriṃśa Heaven serves as one day and night, and this hell's lifespan is one thousand years. Those who have killed, stolen, and committed such acts fall into this place.

There is also a subsidiary place called the **Place of Shrieking Anguish.** Sinners are raised to the summit of sheer cliffs of countless yojanas. Bound and fettered with blazing black ropes, they are then pushed down onto ground bristling with sharp iron blades. Iron-fanged, fire-jawed dogs devour them, tearing every part of their body asunder, limb from limb. They shriek and wail, but there is no one to rescue them. Those who in former lives preached the Dharma according to false views, spoke nothing true, showed no regard for others, or threw themselves from cliffs to commit suicide fall into this place.

There is also a place called the **Place of Dreaded Vultures.** Wardens beat the sinners furiously with staffs and drive them running day and night. With blazing iron swords in their hands and iron bows and crossbows, they pursue and chase the sinners, hacking, beating, and shooting them. Those who in former lives killed people out of greed for possessions fall into this place.

#### (3) The Hell of Crushing Together (*Saṃghāta*)

This hell lies below the Hell of Black Ropes, with the same dimensions as before. There are many iron mountains standing in facing pairs. Ox-headed and horse-headed wardens and other jailers, grasping weapons and [p.0034] staffs, drive the sinners between the mountains. At that moment the two mountains close in and crush together, grinding their bodies to pulp until blood flows and fills the earth. Or iron mountains fall from the sky and strike the sinners, shattering them like grains of sand. Or they are placed upon stone slabs and pressed under boulders. Or they are thrown into iron mortars and pounded with iron pestles.

Supremely vile hell-demons, along with blazing iron lions and fiery vultures, rip out the sinners' intestines, hang them from treetops, and feed upon them. There is a great river in that place, filled with iron hooks, all ablaze. The wardens seize the sinners and hurl them into the river, where they are impaled upon the hooks. In the river flows scalding molten copper that sweeps the sinners along. Some have bodies that float to the surface like the rising sun. Some have bodies that sink to the bottom like heavy stones. Some raise their hands toward heaven and wail. Some draw near to one another and weep. They endure immense suffering for a long time, without any master or savior.

Furthermore, the wardens take the sinners of this hell and place them in a forest of blade-leafed trees. They see upon the treetops beautiful, exquisitely adorned women. Upon seeing them, they climb the trees, but the leaves are like swords and slice their flesh, sever their sinews, and flay every part of their bodies. When they have finally reached the top, they see those women below upon the ground. The women gaze upward with seductive eyes and say:

> "Thinking of you, I have come to this place. Why do you not come near me? Why do you not embrace me?"

Seeing them, the sinners burn with desire and descend again. But now the blade-leaves point upward, sharp as razors, slicing every part of their bodies as before. When they reach the ground, the women have returned to the treetops. Seeing them, the sinners climb again. Thus for countless hundreds of billions of years, deceived by their own minds, they go back and forth in that hell, thus walking, thus being burned. The cause of this is lustful desire.

Taking two hundred years in the human world as one day and night in Yāma Heaven, that heaven's lifespan is two thousand years. Taking the lifespan of Yāma Heaven as one day and night in this hell, its lifespan is two thousand years. Those who have killed, stolen, and committed sexual misconduct fall into this place.

This great hell also has sixteen subsidiary places. Among them is a place called the **Place of Vile Sights.** Those who seized others' children, forced illicit acts upon them, and made them cry out in anguish fall here to suffer. The sinners see their own children within the hell. Wardens stab them in their private parts with iron rods or iron awls, or nail iron hooks into their private parts. Seeing their own children suffering thus, the sinners' hearts break with unbearable grief and love. The anguish of this loving grief, compared with the agony of being burned by fire, is such that the burning does not equal even one-sixteenth of it. After being crushed by this anguish of heart, they also endure bodily suffering: suspended head-downward, scalding molten copper is poured into their fundaments, entering their bodies, burning through their organs and intestines, large and small, burning through in succession until it exits below. They fully experience the twofold suffering of body and mind for countless hundreds of thousands of years without ceasing.

There is also a place called the **Place of Much Anguish.** Men who committed sexual misconduct with other men fall here to suffer. They see the men from their former lives, every part of whose bodies blazes with searing flame. These men come and embrace them, and every part of their bodies falls apart. They die and revive, in extreme terror. They flee in panic and tumble from sheer cliffs, where fiery vultures and fire-mouthed foxes and jackals devour them.

There is also a place called the **Place of Enduring Agony.** Those who took others' wives fall here to suffer. The wardens hang them from trees, head downward, feet above, and kindle great fires below that burn their entire bodies. When burned to nothing, they revive. They open their mouths to cry out, and fire enters their mouths, burning their hearts and lungs.

#### (4) The Hell of Screaming (*Raurava*)

This hell lies below the Hell of Crushing Together, with the same dimensions. The wardens' heads are yellow as gold, fire blazes from their eyes, they wear red-ochre garments, their hands and feet are enormous, they run swift as the wind, and they cry out in dreadful voices as they shoot the sinners. The sinners, in abject terror, prostrate themselves and beg for mercy:

> "We implore your compassion! Please release us, if only a little!"

Despite such words, they are made to keep running. Some are placed upon scorching griddles and roasted, flipped back and forth. Some are thrown into scalding cauldrons and boiled. Some are driven into iron chambers filled with raging fire. Some have their mouths pried open with tongs while they recite this verse:

> "O Yama, how can you be so heartless?
> Why have you no peace?
> I am a vessel of compassion;
> How can you show none to me?"

Then Yama answers the sinner:

> "You have been deceived by the snares of craving
> And committed evil, unwholesome deeds.
> Now you reap the fruit of those evil deeds.
> Why do you rage at me?"

And again:

> "You yourself performed these evil deeds."

Taking four hundred human years as one day and night in Tuṣita Heaven, that heaven's lifespan is four thousand years. Taking the lifespan of Tuṣita Heaven as one day and night in this hell, its lifespan is four thousand years. Those who have killed, stolen, committed sexual misconduct, and consumed intoxicants fall into this place.

There are also sixteen subsidiary places. Among them is a place called the **Place of Fire-Tip Worms.** Those who in former lives sold wine and adulterated it with water. The worms' power is such that in a single day and night they could cause the death of countless people across all four continents. Moreover, worms emerge from the sinners' own bodies, breaking through skin, flesh, bone, and marrow to feed.

There is also a place called the **Place of Cloud-Fire Mist.** Those who in former lives gave others wine, waited until they were drunk, then mocked and humiliated them, causing them shame, fall here to suffer. The hell-fire fills a depth of two hundred cubits. Wardens seize the sinners and force them to walk through the fire. From feet to head, everything melts away. When they lift their feet, they are restored. Thus for countless hundreds of thousands of years the suffering does not cease. The remaining details are as described in the sūtras.

The wardens rebuke the sinners and recite this verse:

> "Toward the Buddha you gave rise to delusion,
> Ruining affairs both worldly and transcendent."

#### (5) The Hell of Great Screaming (*Mahāraurava*)

This hell lies below the Hell of Screaming, with the same dimensions. The forms of suffering are the same, but all the agonies of the preceding four hells and their sixteen subsidiary places are multiplied tenfold.

Taking eight hundred human years as one day and night in the Nirmāṇarati Heaven, that heaven's lifespan is eight thousand years. Taking that heaven's lifespan as one day and night in this hell, its lifespan is eight thousand years. Those who have killed, stolen, committed sexual misconduct, consumed intoxicants, and spoken false words fall into this place.

The wardens rebuke the sinners and recite this verse:

> "False speech is the foremost fire:
> It can even burn the great ocean dry.
> How much more easily it burns the false speaker,
> As fire consumes grass and kindling."

There are also sixteen subsidiary places. Among them is a place called the **Place of Suffering Sharp Points.** Searing iron needles pierce both mouth and tongue. The sinners cannot even cry out.

There is also a place called the **Place of Suffering Boundless Agony.** Wardens pull out the sinners' tongues with scorching iron tongs; once pulled out, the tongues grow back, and once regrown, are pulled out again. Their eyes are likewise plucked out and regrown repeatedly. Their bodies are pared with knives as thin and sharp as razors. The sinners endure such varied torments, all the fruit of false speech.

#### (6) The Hell of Scorching Heat (*Tapana*)

This hell lies below the Hell of Great Screaming, with the same dimensions as before.

[p.0035] The wardens seize the sinners and lay them upon the burning iron ground, face up or face down. From head to foot, they beat and pound them with great blazing iron rods until they are like patties of flesh. Or they place them upon immense, white-hot iron griddles and roast them over raging flames, turning them left and right until they are seared through on every side. Or they run great iron skewers through them from below, piercing up through the skull, and roast them, turning back and forth, so that flames burst from every sense-organ, every pore, and the mouth. Or they are thrown into boiling iron. Or they are placed in blazing iron towers.

If such a fire were set upon Jambudvīpa, it would burn the entire continent to nothing in an instant. How much less can the bodies of sinners, soft as fresh butter, endure being burned for so long? Truly this is unbearable.

Taking sixteen hundred years as one day and night in the Paranirmitavaśavartin Heaven, that heaven's lifespan is sixteen thousand years. Taking that heaven's lifespan as one day and night, this hell's lifespan is likewise. Those who have killed, stolen, committed sexual misconduct, consumed intoxicants, spoken false words, and held wrong views fall into this place.

Outside the four gates there are also sixteen subsidiary places. Among them is a place called **Puṇḍarīka.** Every part of the sinners' bodies blazes without so much as a mustard seed's space free from flame. Other hell-beings call out to them:

> "Come quickly! Come quickly! Here is the Puṇḍarīka Pool! There is water to drink! The grove has moist shade!"

The sinners run toward it. Along the road there are pits filled with raging fire. The sinners fall in, and every part of their bodies is burned to nothing. Burned, they revive; revived, they are burned again. Their craving and thirst unquenched, they press forward and enter. Once they reach the place, the Puṇḍarīka is ablaze, towering five hundred yojanas high. That fire scorches them; they die and revive. Those who in former lives starved themselves to death hoping to be reborn in heaven, and who taught others to abide in wrong views, fall into this place.

There is also a place called the **Place of Dark Fire Wind.** Evil winds blow the sinners through empty space, with nothing to support them, spinning them like wheels so fast their bodies cannot be seen. After being whirled thus, a different blade-wind arises that grinds their bodies to sand and scatters them in ten directions. Scattered, they revive; revived, they are scattered again; and so it continues without end. Those who held the view that "among all dharmas, some are permanent and some impermanent: the body is impermanent, but the four great elements are permanent," such holders of wrong views suffer thus.

#### (7) The Hell of Great Scorching Heat (*Pratāpana*)

This hell lies below the Hell of Scorching Heat, with the same dimensions. The sufferings are tenfold those of the preceding hell, and they cannot be fully described. Its lifespan is half an intermediate kalpa. Those who have killed, stolen, committed sexual misconduct, consumed intoxicants, spoken false words, held wrong views, and also defiled women of pure precepts fall into this place.

These evildoers, while still in the intermediate state (*antarābhava*), first perceive the appearance of the great hell. There are Yama's agents with dreadful countenances, their hands and feet blazing hot, twisting their bodies and flexing their arms. When the sinners see them, they are seized with overwhelming terror. Their voices rumble like thunder, and hearing this, the sinners' fear redoubles. They hold sharp blades; their bellies and arms are enormous, black as dark clouds; their eyes blaze like lamps; their hooked fangs are razor-sharp; their arms and hands are long; they sway and make threatening gestures, and every part of their bodies bristles hideously. With various terrifying forms, they bind the sinners tightly by the throat and lead them away.

They pass through sixty-eight hundred thousand yojanas of lands, seas, continents, and cities, beyond the ocean's edge. They travel another thirty-six hundred million yojanas, gradually descending ten hundred million yojanas. Among all winds, the wind of karma is foremost. This karmic wind carries the evildoers to that place. Once they arrive, King Yama rebukes them in various ways. After the rebuke, bound by the fetters of evil karma, they are led out toward the hell.

From afar they see the Hell of Great Scorching Heat blazing universally. They hear the wailing and weeping of the sinners in hell, and their grief and terror are boundless. Thus for countless hundreds of billions of years, hearing the sounds of wailing, their fear multiplies tenfold. Their hearts quake with dread.

Yama's agents rebuke them, saying:

> "Hearing the sounds of hell,
> You are already terrified thus.
> How much more when hell's fire burns you
> As it burns dry kindling and grass!
> It is not fire that truly burns;
> It is evil karma that burns."

They are then led toward the hell. There is a great mass of fire, towering five hundred yojanas high and two hundred yojanas wide, blazing fiercely. By the power of the sinners' evil karma, their bodies are hurled violently into that conflagration, as though flung from a great mountain cliff.

Outside there are sixteen subsidiary places. Among them is one place where every interval, even to the sky, blazes without ceasing. Not so much as a pinhole's space is free from flame. The sinners cry out from within the fire. For countless hundred millions of years they are constantly burned without respite. Those who defiled virtuous laywomen (*upāsikā*) fall into this place.

There is also a place called the **Place of Universal Suffering.** Blazing knives flay every inch of their skin without cutting the flesh. The skin, still connected to the body, is spread out on the burning ground and set afire. Boiling iron is poured over their bodies. Thus for countless hundred millions of years they suffer immensely. Monks who used wine to seduce and deceive women of pure precepts, corrupted their minds, and then engaged in misconduct with them, or who offered them material gifts for such purposes, fall into this place.

#### (8) The Avīci Hell

The Avīci Hell lies below the Hell of Great Scorching Heat, at the very bottom of the desire realm. When sinners are on their way to this hell, while still in the intermediate state, they weep and recite this verse:

> "All is nothing but flame;
> It fills the sky without interval.
> In the four directions and the four quarters,
> No space on earth is empty.
> Every place upon the earth
> Is filled with evildoers.
> I now have nowhere to turn,
> Alone, without a companion.
> In this evil place of darkness,
> I enter the great mass of blazing fire.
> In the empty sky above,
> I see neither sun, nor moon, nor stars."

Then Yama's agents answer with wrathful hearts:

> "Whether in kalpas of increase or kalpas of decrease,
> Great fire will burn your body.
> Foolish one, having already done evil,
> What use is remorse now?
> Neither gods, nor asuras,
> Nor gandharvas, nor nāgas, nor ghosts —
> Bound by the fetters of karma,
> No one can rescue you.
> As from the great ocean
> One might take but a single handful of water:
> This suffering is like that single handful;
> The suffering to come is like the ocean."

After this rebuke, they are led toward the hell. At a distance of twenty-five thousand yojanas, they hear the wailing of that hell's sinners, and their anguish multiplies tenfold. They swoon, their heads hanging downward and feet above. For two thousand years they continue descending.

The city has seven layers of iron netting. Below are eighteen partitions. Forests of blades surround it on every side. At the four corners stand four copper dogs, each forty yojanas in length, with eyes like lightning, fangs like swords, teeth like mountains of knives, and tongues like iron thorns. From every pore rages fierce fire, and their stench is foul beyond any worldly comparison.

There are eighteen wardens, with heads like rākṣasas and mouths like yakṣas, with sixty-four eyes that shoot forth iron pellets. Their hooked fangs protrude upward, four yojanas high. From their fang-tips, fire streams and fills the Avīci city. Upon their heads are eight ox-heads, each with eighteen horns, and from every horn-tip blazes fierce fire.

Within the seven-layered city walls stand seven iron pillars, from whose [p.0036] tops fire gushes like boiling springs, and the flames stream forth to fill the entire city. Upon the thresholds of the four gates are eighteen cauldrons from which boiling copper surges forth, also filling the city. In each of the eighteen partitions are eighty-four thousand iron bees and great serpents that spew venom and fire, their bodies filling the city. The serpents roar like a hundred thousand thunderclaps, and they rain down great iron pellets that likewise fill the city. There are five hundred million worms with eighty-four thousand beaks from which fire streams down like rain. When these worms descend, the hell-fire intensifies all the more, illuminating eighty-four thousand yojanas.

Moreover, eighty billion agonies upon agonies gather in this place. Upon the burning, blazing, great iron ground, raging fire rises in tongues of flame, piercing those sentient beings, penetrating the skin, entering the flesh, severing the sinews, shattering the bones, and boring through to the marrow, where it burns like a tallow candle. Thus their entire bodies become nothing but fierce flame. From the east, and likewise from the south, west, and north, it is the same. For this reason, those sentient beings are mingled and merged with the raging flames; they see only masses of fire converging from all four directions. The flames mingle without any gap, and the suffering likewise admits no interval. Only by the sound of agonized shrieking and crying can one know that sentient beings are there.

Furthermore, they are scooped up with iron shovels loaded with triply-heated iron coals and winnowed. Then they are placed upon the burning iron ground and made to ascend a great blazing iron mountain, climbing up and then down, descending and then climbing again. Their tongues are pulled from their mouths and stretched out with a hundred iron nails, spread flat without a wrinkle, like the stretching of an ox-hide.

Then they are laid upon their backs on the burning iron ground. Their mouths are pried open with scorching iron hooks, and triply-heated iron pellets are placed in their mouths, burning their mouths, throats, and all their innards, passing through and out below. Molten copper is then poured into their mouths, burning the throat and mouth, scorching through all the organs and exiting below.

If all the sufferings of the preceding seven great hells together with all their subsidiary places were taken as one portion, the Avīci Hell would surpass them a thousandfold. The sinners of the Avīci Hell, looking upon those of the Hell of Great Scorching Heat, see them as though they were in the Paranirmitavaśavartin Heaven.

The beings of the four continents and the six desire-realm heavens, if they caught so much as a whiff of hell's stench, would all perish at once. Why? Because the stench of the hell-beings is so overwhelmingly foul. Why does this stench not reach us? Because there are two great mountains, one called Mount Sunrise and the other Mount Sunset, which block the foul odor. If a person were merely to hear described all the sufferings of the hells, they would forget every other sorrow; hearing of this, they would die on the spot.

As for the great Avīci Hell, not even one-thousandth of it has been described here. Why? Because it cannot be fully told, cannot be fully heard, cannot be expressed by simile. If there were someone to tell of it and [p.0037] someone to hear, both would perish.

Outside the four gates of this hell there are likewise sixteen subsidiary places. Among them is a place called the **Place of Iron Jackals.** The sinners' bodies blaze with fire for a span of ten yojanas. Of all the hells, this suffering is the most extreme. Moreover, iron bricks rain down like a summer torrent, shattering their bodies like dried jerky. Flame-fanged jackals constantly come to devour them. At every moment their suffering does not cease. Those who in former lives burned images of the Buddha, burned monks' quarters, or burned monks' bedding fall into this place.

There is also a place called the **Place of Black Bellies.** Hunger and thirst burn their bodies. They eat their own flesh; when consumed, it grows back, and they eat it again. Black-bellied serpents coil around the sinners, gnawing upward from the toenails. Or they are cast into fierce fire to be burned. Or they are placed in iron vessels to be boiled. For countless hundred millions of years they suffer thus. Those who in former lives took the property of the Buddha and the Saṅgha for their own use fall into this place.

There is also a place called the **Place of Raining Mountains.** Iron mountains, each one yojana in size, fall from above and strike the sinners, crushing them like grains of sand. Crushed, they revive; revived, they are crushed again. There are also eleven kinds of flame that burn their entire bodies. Wardens slice every part of their bodies with knives, and scalding-hot molten tin is poured into the wounds. All four hundred and four diseases afflict them constantly, and their suffering endures without end and without reckoning of years. Those who in former lives stole the food of a pratyekabuddha, ate it themselves, and would not share it fall into this place.

There is also a place called the **Place of Yaṃbhavat.** There is an evil bird, large as an elephant, called Yaṃbhavat, whose body is wreathed in flame. It seizes the sinners and carries them high into the sky, flying east and west, and then drops them. They fall like stones to the earth and shatter into a hundred pieces. Shattered, they are put back together; reassembled, they are seized again. Sharp blades cover the roads and slice their feet. Flame-toothed dogs come to gnaw their bodies. For a very long time they endure immense suffering. Those who in former lives sat in judgment over others and thereby caused people harm fall into this place.

#### The Subsidiary Hells Common to All Eight

Speaking generally of the subsidiary places near all eight great hells:

> All the great narakas have four sides, four cliffs, and four gates. Iron walls surround them. Exiting from any of the four gates, outside each gate are four enclosures.

**First, the Pit of Smoldering Embers.** Smoldering coals reach up to the knees. When those sentient beings emerge seeking shelter and wander to this place, the moment they set foot upon it, their skin, flesh, and blood are instantly consumed and destroyed. When they lift their feet, these are restored.

**Second, the Swamp of Corpse-Excrement.** Adjacent to the embers without interval is a swamp of corpses and excrement-mire. When those sentient beings, seeking shelter, emerge from the embers and wander into it, they sink until head and feet are both submerged. Within the corpse-excrement swamp there are multitudes of worms called *nyāṅkuṭa*. They bore through the skin, enter the flesh, sever the sinews, shatter the bones, and extract the marrow to eat.

**Third, the Path of Upturned Blade-Swords.** Adjacent to the corpse-swamp without interval is a path of sharp sword-blades, edges pointing upward. When those sentient beings, seeking shelter, emerge and wander to this place, the moment they step down, their skin, flesh, sinews, and blood are all consumed and destroyed. When they lift their feet, they are restored as before.

**Fourth, the Forest of Blade-Leaves.** Adjacent to the blade-sword path without interval is a forest of blade-leaves. When those sentient beings, seeking shelter, emerge and approach its shade, the moment they sit down beneath the trees, a light breeze stirs, and blade-leaves fall, hacking and severing every joint and limb of their bodies. They collapse to the ground, and black, tawny dogs come to tear and rend their spinal flesh and devour it.

**Fifth, the Forest of Iron *Śālmalī* Trees.** Adjacent to the blade-leaf forest without interval is a forest of iron *śālmalī* trees. When those sentient beings, seeking shelter, come and climb the trees, as they ascend, every thorn-point turns downward to pierce them. When they try to descend, every thorn-point turns upward. By this means, the thorns pierce and impale their bodies through every joint and limb. Then great iron-beaked birds alight upon their heads or perch upon their shoulders, pecking out their eyeballs and devouring them.

**Sixth, the River of Scalding Ash-Water.** Adjacent to the iron *śālmalī* forest without interval is a vast river, brimming with boiling, scalding ash-water. When those sentient beings, seeking shelter, emerge and fall into this river, they are like beans tossed into a great cauldron set upon a raging, blazing fire: they are boiled and tossed, rising and falling, tumbling round and round.

On the southern bank of the river stand wardens of hell, grasping staffs, lassos, and great nets, standing in ranks. They prevent those sentient beings from escaping. Some are snared by lassos; some are scooped up in nets.

[p.0037] They are then placed upon the vast burning iron ground. The sinners are turned face upward, and the wardens ask:

> "What do you now desire?"

They answer:

> "We have now lost all sensation. But we are oppressed by all manner of hunger and suffering."

Then the wardens pry open their mouths with iron hooks and place in them blazing, scorching iron pellets, which burn the mouth, the throat, and all the internal organs, passing through and emerging below, as described before.

If they answer:

> "We are now oppressed only by thirst."

Then the wardens pour molten copper into their mouths, burning the throat and mouth, scorching through the organs and exiting below. By this cause and condition they suffer for a long time, until all the evil, unwholesome karma capable of producing rebirth in the naraka realms, created in their former lives, is exhausted; only then do they escape.

Whether the blade-sword path, the blade-leaf forest, or the iron *śālmalī* forest, all these are described in full in the sūtras and śāstras. There is no time to detail them all here.

---

### 2. The Realm of Hungry Ghosts

There are two kinds of dwelling places for hungry ghosts. The first is in the realm of King Yama, five hundred yojanas beneath the earth. The second is scattered among humans and celestial beings. Their forms are extremely varied; here only a small portion is described.

Some have bodies one foot long. Some have bodies as long as their names suggest. Some have bodies twice the height of a human, but without faces or eyes, and with hands and feet like mere stumps. Blazing fire fills their insides and burns their bodies. Those who in former lives killed out of greed for wealth receive this retribution.

Some ghosts are called "vomit-eaters." Their bodies are vast, half a yojana in length. They perpetually seek to vomit but cannot manage it. In former lives, a husband ate fine food alone and did not share with his wife and children, or a wife ate alone and did not share with her husband and children: such ones receive this retribution.

Some ghosts are called "scent-eaters." When people in the human world make offerings to the sick by rivers and in forests, these ghosts sniff the fragrance of the offerings and sustain their lives thereby. Those who in former lives ate fine food alone in front of their spouses and family receive this retribution.

Some ghosts are called "Dharma-eaters." They run about in dangerous, difficult places seeking food. Their complexion is dark as black clouds, and tears stream like rain. When they come to a monastery and someone recites blessings or expounds the Dharma, by this they gain strength to sustain their lives. Those who in former lives taught the Dharma out of greed for fame and profit, preaching with impure motives, receive this retribution.

Some ghosts are called "water-seekers." Hunger and thirst burn their bodies. They wander frantically seeking water but cannot find it. Long hair covers their faces so they cannot see. They run toward riverbanks, and when someone crosses the river, they quickly catch the drops of water that fall from the person's feet to sustain their lives. Or when someone scoops up water to offer to their deceased parents, they obtain a small share and their lives are briefly sustained. If they try to take water on their own, the ghosts who guard the water beat them with sticks. Those who in former lives sold wine and added water to dilute it, or who drowned insects and moths, and who did not cultivate wholesome dharmas, receive this retribution.

Some ghosts are called "hope-feeders." Only when people in the world make ancestral offerings for their deceased parents can they eat. At all other times they are unable to eat anything at all. Those who in former lives, when others labored to obtain small things, cheated and took their goods by deception, receive this retribution.

Some ghosts are born on ocean islands, where there are no trees, forests, or rivers. Those places are extremely hot; winter there, compared with the human summer, exceeds it a thousandfold. They sustain their lives only on the morning dew. Though they dwell on ocean islands, they see the ocean as dried up. Those who in former lives cheated sick and weary travelers on the road, taking their goods and giving far too little in return, receive this retribution.

Some ghosts constantly go to cemeteries and eat the fire of burning corpses, yet can never be satisfied. Those who in former lives were wardens of prisons and stole the food and drink of the inmates receive this retribution.

Some ghosts are born within trees, their bodies pressed and squeezed as a woodworm is pressed within wood, suffering immensely. Those who in former lives felled shade trees or cut down the orchards and groves of the Saṅgha, their hair becomes like blades that slice and cut their bodies, or transforms into fire that burns them all around.

Some ghosts give birth to five children every day and night, and as each is born, they eat it, yet still they are not sated. They crack open their own skulls and eat their brains.

Some ghosts have fire issuing from their mouths; moths fly into the fire and become their food.

Some ghosts feed upon excrement, nasal discharge, pus, and blood. Their hunger and thirst are always desperate; their bodies are withered and parched. When they glimpse a clear stream and run toward it, powerful ghosts beat them back with sticks, or the water transforms into fire, or it dries up entirely.

Some are ghosts obstructed internally, unable to eat. Their mouths are as narrow as the eye of a needle, but their bellies are as vast as mountains. Even when they encounter food and drink, they have no way to consume it.

Some are ghosts with neither internal nor external obstruction, yet still unable to make use of food. Whatever small morsel they happen upon, they try to eat, but each month constitutes one day and night, and each month becomes a year. Their lifespan is five hundred years.

The *Saddharmasmṛtyupasthāna Sūtra* says:

> Those who are miserly, greedy, and envious fall into the realm of hungry ghosts.

---

### 3. The Realm of Animals

Animals have two kinds of dwelling places. Their root abode is the great ocean; their derivative abodes are scattered among humans and celestial beings. Analyzed in detail, there are 3.4 billion species. In summary, they do not exceed three classes: (1) birds, (2) beasts, and (3) insects.

All such creatures prey upon the weak. Whether eating or drinking, they are never at peace for a moment. Day and night they live in constant fear. Moreover, all aquatic creatures are harmed by fishermen. All land creatures are harmed by hunters.

Consider elephants, horses, oxen, donkeys, camels, and mules: iron hooks cut into their brains, their noses are pierced, their heads are bridled, their bodies bear heavy loads, and they are beaten with rods and whips. They think only of grass and water; beyond that, they know nothing.

Centipedes, weasels, rats, and the like are born in darkness and die in darkness. Lice and nits are born upon the human body and die there as well.

The nāga hosts endure three kinds of burning suffering, day and night without rest. Great pythons, their bodies long and huge, deaf and dim-witted, without legs, crawling on their bellies, are gnawed upon by multitudes of small creatures.

Some creatures are as small as one-hundredth of a hair's breadth. Some are like motes of dust floating in a sunbeam. Some are as large as ten thousand yojanas.

Such animals may endure for one season, or seven seasons, or one kalpa, or a hundred kalpas, or a thousand, ten thousand, or a hundred million kalpas, or one intermediate kalpa, or a hundred thousand million intermediate kalpas, suffering immeasurably. Or, encountering adverse conditions, they are repeatedly slaughtered. These sufferings are beyond all reckoning.

Those who are foolish and shameless, who accept the faithful offerings of others without repaying them, receive this retribution.

---

### 4. The Realm of Asuras

There are two kinds of asuras. The superior ones dwell at the bottom of the great ocean north of Mount Sumeru. The inferior ones dwell scattered among the four great continents, within mountains and crags. When thunder rumbles, they take it for celestial war-drums and are stricken with fear, their hearts consumed by dread of battle. They are also constantly attacked and harmed by the devas, who shatter their bodies or cut short their lives.

Furthermore, it is said:

> Three times daily, suffering comes of its own accord to afflict them. Their varied griefs and torments are beyond description.

---

### 5. The Realm of Humans

There are three characteristics to be carefully contemplated: (1) impurity, (2) suffering, and (3) impermanence.

#### (i) Impurity

The human body contains three hundred and sixty bones, [p.0038] joint stacked upon joint. The toe bones support the foot bones; the foot bones support the ankle bones; the ankle bones support the shin bones; the shin bones support the knee bones; the knee bones support the thigh bones; the thigh bones support the hip bones; the hip bones support the waist bones; the waist bones support the spine; the spine supports the ribs. The spine further supports the neck bones; the neck bones support the jaw bones; the jaw bones support the teeth; above sits the skull. The neck bones also support the shoulder bones; the shoulder bones support the arm bones; the arm bones support the wrist bones; the wrist bones support the hand bones; the hand bones support the fingers.

The whole is assembled like a dilapidated house, its joints held together, wound about with four kinds of fine blood vessels spread throughout. Five hundred portions of flesh are smeared over it like plaster. Six types of connecting tissue bind it. Five hundred sinews wind around it. Seven hundred fine vessels form its lattice. Sixteen coarse vessels link it with hooks and ties. Two cords of flesh, three and a half arm-spans long, wind and knot within it. Sixteen intestines and stomachs wrap around the raw and cooked organs. Twenty-five breath-channels run through it like window-slits. One hundred and seven joints of flesh are like broken vessels. Eighty thousand pores are like wild grass covering it. The five sense-organs and seven orifices overflow with impurity. It is wrapped in seven layers of skin and nourished by six kinds of flavor, yet like a sacrificial fire, it swallows insatiably.

Such a body is utterly rank and foul. Nine hundred sinews connect its parts; within, three *shō* of blood course through thirty-six thousand vessels. Ninety-nine thousand pores constantly exude sweat. Ninety-nine layers of skin wrap it above and below. Shaped like a lotus, it is porous and hollow, with interior and exterior connected, each having ninety layers.

The lungs rest above, their color white. The liver, its color blue-green. The heart rests at the center, its color red. The spleen, its color yellow. The kidneys rest below, their color black.

There are also six bowels: the large intestine, which is the bowel of transmission, also the bowel of the lungs, three and a half arm-spans long, its color white; the gallbladder, which is the bowel of purity, also the bowel of the liver, its color blue-green; the small intestine, which is the bowel of reception, also the bowel of the heart, sixteen arm-spans long, its color red; the stomach, which is the bowel of the five grains, also the bowel of the spleen, containing three *shō* of excrement, its color yellow; the bladder, which is the bowel of fluids, also the bowel of the kidneys, containing one *to* of urine, its color black; and the triple burner, which is the bowel of the inner channels. All these substances are distributed vertically and horizontally.

The large and small intestines run from the crown to the soles. From the marrow to the skin, there are eighty thousand colonies of worms. Each has four heads, four mouths, and ninety-nine tails, and their forms are not uniform. Within each colony there are a further ninety thousand. After seven days, eighty thousand colonies of worms emerge from the body, crawling in all directions and feeding. Two colonies of worms called "hair-lickers" dwell at the roots of the hair and constantly consume it. Two colonies called "eye-circlers" dwell in the eyes and constantly consume them. Four colonies dwell in the brain and feed on the brain. One colony called "rice-leaf" dwells in the ears and feeds on the ears. One colony called "mouth-hider" dwells in the nose and feeds on the nose. Two colonies, one called "far-thrower" and another called "all-thrower," dwell on the lips and feed on the lips. One colony called "needle-mouth" dwells on the tongue and feeds on the tongue. Five hundred colonies dwell on the left side and feed on the left side; the same for the right. Four colonies feed on the raw organs. Two colonies feed on the cooked organs. Four colonies dwell in the urinary tract and feed on urine. Four colonies dwell in the fecal tract and feed on excrement. And so on, down to one colony called "black-head" that dwells in the feet and feeds on the feet.

Thus eighty thousand colonies depend upon this body, feeding upon it day and night, causing the body to burn with affliction and the mind to fill with anxiety. Manifold diseases appear.

The sūtra says:

> When a person is about to die, the worms become terrified and devour one another, causing immense suffering. The person's relatives, men and women, are stricken with great grief. As the worms consume one another, finally only two remain, which fight for seven days. After seven days, one worm perishes.

All is impurity. Just as excrement is foul whether in large or small quantity, so too this body: from youth to old age, it is nothing but impurity. Even if one were to pour the entire ocean over it, one could not wash it clean.

> Outwardly it may be adorned with fine appearance, but inwardly it is wrapped in nothing but filth. It is like a painted vase filled with excrement. The fool cherishes and loves it.
>
> Gazing at the fair complexion outside,
> Not seeing the impurity within.

After seven days, the body swells and bloats; its color changes to a blue-black bruise. It rots, and the skin splits open, discharging pus and blood. Hawks, vultures, kites, owls, foxes, jackals, and dogs, all manner of birds and beasts, tear and devour it. After the birds and beasts have eaten, impurity oozes and decays. Countless species of worms and maggots emerge from the foul places. It is more loathsome than a dead dog. At last, when it has become white bone, the joints scatter; hands, feet, and skull each lie in different places. Wind blows upon it, sun bleaches it, rain soaks it, frost encases it. After many years, it changes color and finally crumbles and decays. The body is impure from beginning to end. The beloved men and women are all just like this. What wise person would still find delight in it?

Therefore the *Mohe zhiguan* (摩訶止觀, *Great Calming and Contemplation*) says:

> Before seeing this reality, one's attachment and craving are extremely strong. Once one has seen it, all lustful desire ceases. One cannot bear it even from a distance. Just as one who has not seen excrement can still eat rice, but upon catching a whiff of the stench, immediately retches.

And again:

> If one realizes this truth, then even high brows, jade-green eyes, white teeth, and crimson lips appear like a heap of excrement coated in powder, or like a decomposing corpse dressed in colored paint. One would not wish even to look upon it, let alone draw near. One would sooner slash one's own body with a deer-bone staff than embrace and take pleasure in it.

#### (ii) Suffering

This body, from the moment of birth, constantly endures suffering. As the *Ratnakūṭa Sūtra* says:

> Whether male or female, the moment they are born and fall to the ground, whether caught in the hands or received on cloth, whether in winter or summer, the touch of cold and hot wind causes immense suffering.

As the *Sūtra on Birth* says:

> Having received this body, there are two kinds of suffering. The eyes, ears, nose, tongue, throat, teeth, chest, abdomen, hands, and feet are all subject to disease; thus the four hundred and four diseases press upon the body. This is called internal suffering.
>
> There is also external suffering: being imprisoned, beaten, and flogged; having the nose or ears cut off, or the hands or feet severed; being afflicted by evil spirits; being bitten by mosquitoes, gadflies, bees, and other venomous creatures; and being assailed by cold, heat, hunger, thirst, wind, and rain. All these various sufferings press upon the body.

This body of the five aggregates, in every posture, whether walking, standing, sitting, or lying down, is never free from suffering. If one walks for a long time without rest, that is suffering. These things are plainly evident and need not be discussed at length.

#### (iii) Impermanence

The *Nirvāṇa Sūtra* says:

> Human life does not pause; it passes more swiftly than mountain torrents. Though we exist today, tomorrow is difficult to guarantee. Why, then, would you let your mind dwell in unwholesome dharmas?

[p.0039] The *Chu yao jing* (出曜經, *Sūtra of Select Verses*) says:

> This day has passed; life has diminished accordingly. Like a fish in a shrinking pool, what joy is there in this?

A verse from the *Sūtra on Queen Māyā* says:

> Just as a *caṇḍāla* drives oxen to slaughter,
> Though one may have karma for long life,
> In the end one cannot escape impermanence.
> Though one may reap the fruit of wealth and honor,
> There will surely come a time of decline and calamity.

As a verse from the *Mahāparinirvāṇa Sūtra* says:

> All things in every world:
> Whatever is born must die.
> Though lifespan be immeasurable,
> It must surely reach its end.
> What flourishes must decline.
> What meets must part.
> Youth does not linger long;
> Vigor is ravaged by illness.
> Life is swallowed by death.
> There is nothing that endures.

And a verse from the *Sūtra on Retribution for Offenses* says:

> Sandbars do not stay full forever.
> Blazing fires do not burn for long.
> The sun, once risen, soon sets.
> The moon, once full, begins to wane.
> Even the exalted and noble:
> None can avoid this.

Not only do ordinary beings face this terror; even immortals who have attained supernatural powers are subject to it as well.

As a verse from the *Dhammapada* says:

> Not in the sky, not in the sea,
> Not in mountain caves:
> There is no place anywhere
> Where one can escape death.

Impermanence is the one thing from which there is ultimately no hiding place. One must, as taught, practice accordingly and aspire to the fruit of eternal bliss.

As the *Mohe zhiguan* says:

> The killing demon of impermanence does not choose between the mighty and the humble. This fragile, unreliable body is difficult to depend upon. How can one sit at ease, planning for a hundred years? Running in all four directions, hoarding and accumulating, yet before the hoarding is complete, one suddenly departs forever. All one's possessions become the property of others. Alone in darkness one goes; who will judge right from wrong?
>
> If one realizes that impermanence surpasses me like a flood, a gale, a bolt of lightning, with no place to hide in mountain, sea, sky, or marketplace, then upon such contemplation, the heart is seized with great dread. One cannot sleep in peace; food loses its savor. As though one's head were on fire, one seeks the way of escape.

And again:

> Consider the jackal that has lost its ears, tail, and fangs. It plays dead, hoping to escape. Then suddenly it hears that its head is to be severed, and its heart fills with overwhelming terror. We have already encountered birth, old age, and illness, and still we do not treat these as urgent. When the matter of death looms before us, how can we not be terrified? When this terror arises, it is as though one were treading on boiling water or burning coals. The five dusts and six desires no longer hold any allure.

---

### 6. The Realm of Celestial Beings

There are three: (1) the desire realm, (2) the form realm, and (3) the formless realm. Their characteristics are vast and difficult to describe in full. Here I take a single example to illustrate the rest.

Consider the Trāyastriṃśa Heaven: though its bliss is boundless, when the time of death approaches, the five signs of decay appear. (1) The flower garlands upon the head suddenly wilt. (2) The celestial garments become soiled with dust. (3) Sweat issues from the armpits. (4) The eyes blink repeatedly. (5) One becomes discontented with one's dwelling.

When these signs appear, all the celestial maidens and attendants abandon the dying deva and depart, casting him aside like grass. He lies fallen in the grove, weeping and sighing:

> "These celestial maidens, whom I have always cherished, how can they cast me aside like grass in a single moment? I am now without support, without refuge. Who will save me?
>
> "The Sudarśana palace city will now be lost to me forever. The jeweled throne of Śakra: I shall never again attend his audience. Within the Sudharmā Hall, all my hopes are severed forever. The jeweled elephant of Śakra: on what day shall I ride with him again? In the Caitrarathaprāsāda Park, I shall never be seen again. In the Paruṣyaka Garden, armor is put aside for the last time. In the Miśra Grove, there shall be no more feasts. In the Nandana Garden, no more wandering, no more rest. Beneath the *kalpa* trees, upon the white jade cushions, I shall never sit again. In the waters of the Mandākinī, that wondrous pool, I shall never bathe again. The four kinds of ambrosia will now be suddenly beyond my reach. The five celestial musics are silenced to my ears forever.
>
> "Alas for this body, alone bearing such suffering! I beg you, in your compassion, to save my life! Extend it even a few more days, would that not be a joy? Do not let me fall into the Mountain of Horse-Heads, or the Ocean of Boiling Brine, or the hells!"

Therefore, a verse from the *Saddharmasmṛtyupasthāna Sūtra* says:

> When a celestial being is about to decline,
> Immense suffering arises in the heart.
> Of the myriad torments of hell,
> Not one-sixteenth compares to this.

Furthermore, after a deva of great merit is reborn, his former celestial attendants abandon the old deva and follow the new one. Or a deva of great power, when things do not go as he wishes, is driven from the palace and can no longer dwell there.

The delights of this realm all end eventually in the suffering of decline and fall, and even the anguish of that decline cannot deliver one from the lower realms.

---

### 7. General Summation

The whole of saṃsāra is pervaded by suffering and is not a thing to be indulged in. The four mountains close in from every side, and there is nowhere to flee. Yet sentient beings, blinded by craving and attachment, are deeply mired in the five desires: taking what is impermanent for permanent, taking what is suffering for happiness.

They are like one who washes a wound yet refuses to see the filth, or like one who places something on an eyelash and still insists on looking through it. How much more when the mountains of swords and cauldrons of fire draw ever nearer! What wise person would treasure and cling to this body?

Therefore, a verse from the *Saddharmasmṛtyupasthāna Sūtra* says:

> The wise always carry anxiety in their hearts,
> Like prisoners confined in a jail.
> The foolish are always merry,
> Like the gods of the Ābhāsvara Heaven.

A verse from the *Ratnakūṭa Sūtra* says:

> Through every kind of evil deed one seeks wealth;
> Raising wife and children, one calls it joy.
> But when death approaches and suffering presses the body,
> Wife and children cannot rescue you.
>
> In the terror of the three evil paths,
> No wife, no child, no kin can be seen.
> Carriages, horses, and wealth belong to others;
> Who can share in the suffering you must bear?
>
> Father, mother, brother, wife, and child,
> Friends, servants, and precious possessions —
> Death always tells the sinner:
> "Not the slightest sin did I impose upon you.
> You yourself committed the sins, and now you come.
> The retribution of karma comes of itself; none can take your place.
> Father, mother, wife, and child cannot save you."
>
> Therefore one should cast aside the chains and fetters of karma,
> Know well the way of renunciation,
> And seek peace and bliss.

And a verse from the *Mahāsaṃnipāta Sūtra* says:

> Wife, children, jewels, and royal throne:
> At the moment of death, none of these will follow.
> Only morality, generosity, and heedfulness —
> These alone, in this life and the next, are one's companions.

Thus, in this endless cycle of doing evil and enduring suffering, one is born in vain and dies in vain; the wheel of transmigration turns without limit. As a sūtra verse says:

> The bones that a single person accumulates
> In a single kalpa, if piled without decay,
> Would equal Mount Vipula.

One kalpa alone is thus; how much more so countless kalpas! We have never yet cultivated the Way, and so we have passed through boundless kalpas in vain. If we do not strive now, the future will be no different.

In this immeasurable cycle of birth and death, to obtain a human body is exceedingly rare. Even having obtained a human body, to possess all one's faculties is also rare. Even with all faculties intact, to encounter the Buddha's teaching is also rare. Even encountering the Buddha's teaching, to give rise to faith is also rare.

Therefore the *Mahāparinirvāṇa Sūtra* says:

> Those born in the human realm are like the dirt on a fingernail; those who fall into the three evil paths are like the dirt of the ten directions.

The *Lotus Sūtra* says:

> Throughout countless, innumerable kalpas, it is rare to hear this Dharma. One who can hear this Dharma: such a person is also rare.

Now at last we have assembled all these conditions. Know, then, that it is in this very life that we should leave the sea of suffering and be born in the Pure Land.

Yet our heads are already dusted with frost and snow, while our hearts remain stained by worldly dust. Though this one life is nearly spent, our yearning does not end. At last we will bid farewell to the white sun and descend alone into the Yellow Springs. At that time, we will fall into a blazing inferno of many hundreds of yojanas. Though we cry out to heaven and beat the earth, what good will it do?

I implore all practitioners: swiftly give rise to the mind of revulsion and renunciation! Quickly follow the path of escape! Do not enter the jeweled mountain only to return empty-handed.

---

**Question:** With what kind of contemplation should one give rise to this mind of revulsion and renunciation?

**Answer:** If one wishes to contemplate broadly, one should reflect as described above on the causes and fruits of the six realms, on impurity, suffering, and so forth.

[p.0040] Or one may recite the verses in which Nāgārjuna Bodhisattva exhorted and encouraged King Śālaprabhā:

> This body is impure: nine orifices flow ceaselessly,
> Without end, like rivers and seas.
> Thin skin conceals it, appearing clean,
> Further adorned with ornaments and jewels.
>
> Those with wisdom analyze and distinguish,
> Knowing it is all deception, and cast it aside.
> Just as one with scabies draws near a fierce flame:
> At first there is brief relief, but afterward the pain intensifies.
> The thought of sensual desire is just so:
> At the start one delights in attachment, but in the end come many afflictions.
>
> See the body's true aspect: all is impure.
> This is contemplation of emptiness and no-self.
> One who can cultivate this contemplation
> Has found the most supreme among all benefits.
>
> Though one possess beauty, noble birth, and wide learning,
> Without morality and wisdom, one is no different from an animal.
> Though one be of humble birth and narrow learning,
> One who cultivates morality and wisdom is called truly superior.
>
> From gain and loss, the eight worldly winds, none can escape.
> If there is one who has severed them, that one is truly without equal.
>
> Among all śramaṇas and brāhmaṇas,
> Among parents, wife, children, and kin:
> Do not follow their wishes or heed their words
> To widely commit unwholesome, unlawful deeds.
> For even if such deeds are done for their sake,
> The great suffering of the future only you yourself must bear.
>
> One who commits many evils does not receive immediate retribution;
> It is not like swords and blades that wound upon contact.
> At the moment of death, the signs of sin first fully appear,
> And then one enters hell, wedded to every suffering.
>
> Faith, morality, generosity, learning, wisdom, conscience, and shame:
> These seven are called the noble wealth.
> True and incomparable, declared by the Sage;
> They surpass all worldly treasures.
>
> One who is content, though poor, may be called rich.
> One who has wealth but many desires: that is called poverty.
> When one's fortune and enterprises increase, suffering also increases,
> Like a dragon with many heads whose venom only grows more fierce.
>
> Regard fine flavors as poison;
> Sprinkle them with the water of wisdom to purify them.
> Though one should eat to sustain the body,
> Do not crave taste and savor, lest pride and arrogance grow.
>
> Toward all objects of desire, give rise to revulsion.
> Diligently seek the unsurpassed path of nirvāṇa.
> Harmonize this body and bring it to ease,
> Then fittingly cultivate the pure precepts.
>
> Of the five divisions of the night,
> During two one should rest.
> In the first, middle, and last watches, contemplate birth and death;
> Strive diligently for liberation; do not let the time pass idly.
>
> Just as a pinch of salt placed in the Ganges
> Cannot make the water taste of brine,
> A small evil that encounters many virtues
> Is likewise dissolved and scattered away.
>
> Though one enjoy the pleasure of Brahmā Heaven, free from desire,
> One will still fall back into the fierce fires of the Avīci Hell.
> Though one dwell in a celestial palace suffused with radiance,
> Afterward one enters the dark blackness of hell.
>
> The so-called Black Ropes, Repetition,
> Scorching, Hacking, Flaying, Piercing, and Without Interval:
> These eight hells blaze eternally;
> All are the retribution of beings' evil karma.
>
> If merely seeing a painting, or hearing another's account,
> Or recalling through reading the scriptures,
> Even then it is difficult to endure:
> How much more when one's own body passes through the experience!
>
> If a person in one day
> Were pierced by three hundred spears,
> Compared with a single instant of suffering in Avīci,
> A hundred thousand million parts would not equal one.
>
> In the animal realm, suffering is immeasurable:
> Some are bound, some are whipped and beaten,
> Some are killed for pearls, feathers, horns, tusks, bones, fur, skin, or flesh.
>
> In the realm of hungry ghosts, suffering is the same:
> Whatever one desires does not come as wished.
> Oppressed by hunger and thirst, afflicted by cold and heat,
> Exhaustion and such sufferings are measureless.
>
> Excrement, urine, filth, and impurity:
> In a hundred thousand ten-thousand kalpas one cannot obtain them.
> Even if, searching, one finds a small portion,
> Others come to rob and snatch it, and it is soon lost.
>
> The cool autumn moon feels like scorching flame;
> The warm spring day turns to bitter cold.
> If one approaches a grove, all its fruits are gone;
> If one reaches a clear stream, it has turned to a dried-up channel.
>
> Because of the karma of sin, their lifespan is long:
> Some endure fifteen thousand years
> Receiving all manner of torment without any respite.
> Such is the retribution of the hungry ghosts.
>
> The swift river of affliction sweeps sentient beings away
> Into deep terror, into blazing suffering.
> To extinguish such clouds of defilement,
> One should cultivate the true truth of liberation.
> Leave behind the provisional, conventional dharmas of the world.

Then the bodhisattva Rāṣṭrapāla's troupe sang:

> "Conditioned dharmas are like illusions, like transformations.
> The three realms are a prison; not one thing in them is worthy of delight.
> Royal power, high position, strength, and sovereignty:
> When impermanence arrives, who can endure?
>
> Like clouds in the sky, they scatter in an instant.
> This body is false, like a plantain tree:
> It is an enemy, a thief; one must not befriend it.
> Like a basket containing a venomous snake: who would cherish it?
>
> Therefore the buddhas constantly reprove the path of desire."

Or again, the verses on the wall of the monk Jiānláo say:

> Birth and death turn without cease,
> Because of craving and attachment to flavors.
> Nourishing enemies, one enters the grave,
> Enduring all manner of bitter toil in vain.
>
> The body stinks like a corpse;
> Nine orifices stream with impurity.
> Like a dung-beetle delighting in excrement,
> The fool who craves the body is no different.
>
> Thinking and imagining, falsely discriminating:
> This is the very root of the five desires.
> The wise one does not discriminate,
> And the five desires are thereupon extinguished.
>
> Unwholesome thought gives rise to attachment;
> Attachment gives rise to affliction.

After the parinirvāṇa of the Buddha Kanakamuni, when the true Dharma was passing away, the bodhisattva Dharmaśrī sought out this verse and broadly propagated the Buddha-Dharma, benefiting countless sentient beings.

Or again, in the *Renwang jing* (仁王經, *Sūtra for Humane Kings*), there are four verses on impermanence that may be consulted.

If one prefers the utmost brevity, consider the verse from the *Vajracchedikā Prajñāpāramitā Sūtra*:

> All conditioned dharmas
> Are like dreams, illusions, bubbles, and shadows,
> Like dew and like lightning.
> One should contemplate them thus.

Or the verse from the *Mahāparinirvāṇa Sūtra*:

> All formations are impermanent.

This verse was also proclaimed by the beryl bells at the four corners of the Hall of Impermanence at the Jetavana Monastery in Śrāvastī. When ailing monks heard the sound of the bells, their suffering and affliction immediately ceased, and they attained the cool bliss of one entering the third dhyāna and approaching rebirth in the Pure Land.

How much more so the Great Being of the Himalayas, who surrendered his entire body for the sake of this verse! Practitioners, reflect deeply upon this. Do not take it lightly.

Contemplating as described, one should renounce the defilements of craving, anger, and delusion, together with their karmic consequences. One should be like a lion pursuing its quarry and not like an unwise dog chasing a thrown stone. One should not practice the fruitless austerities of non-Buddhist paths.

**Question:** Impurity, suffering, and impermanence are easy to understand. But there visibly exist real dharmas; why call them "empty"?

**Answer:** Do not the sūtras say they are "like dreams, illusions, and transformations"? By analogy with dream-states, one should contemplate the meaning of emptiness.

As the *Record of the Western Regions* relates: In the kingdom of Vārāṇasī, two or three *li* east of the Deer Park, there was a dried-up pond. Long ago, a hermit had built his hut beside this pond, withdrawing from the world. He mastered the arts and plumbed the depths of spiritual principles. He could transform clay and stone into jewels and alter the forms of humans and beasts. But he had not yet learned to ride the wind and clouds or ascend with the immortals. Poring over ancient texts, he sought further techniques. The method stated:

"Command a brave man to stand at the corner of the altar, holding a long sword, suppressing his breath and refraining from speech, from dusk until dawn. The seeker of immortality shall sit at the center of the altar, grasping the long sword in hand, reciting the divine incantation, focusing his gaze and turning his hearing inward. At dawn, he shall ascend to immortality."

He followed the method and found a brave man. He rewarded him handsomely and secretly performed acts of hidden virtue. The hermit said: "I ask only that for one night you do not make a sound." The brave man said: "I would not refuse even death. How much less mere silence!"

And so the altar was prepared and the rites commenced. Dusk fell, and each assumed his post. The hermit recited his incantation; the brave man gripped his sharp sword. Dawn was nearly breaking when the brave man suddenly let out a cry. The hermit asked: "You sincerely promised silence. Why did you cry out?"

The brave man said: "After receiving my charge, around midnight, I fell into something like a stupor, like a dream. Strange visions arose. I saw my former master come in person to thank me with kind words. Moved by his deep kindness, I restrained myself from speaking. He flew into a rage and had me killed. I received an intermediate-state body. Looking at my corpse, I sighed with regret. Still, I resolved to remain silent through lifetime after lifetime to repay his great kindness.

"Then I was reborn in the household of a great brahmin in southern India. From conception onward [p.0041] through birth, I endured every hardship. Bearing kindness upon kindness, I never once spoke. Relatives and kin all thought it strange. Past the age of sixty-five, my wife said to me: 'You must speak now. If you do not, I shall kill your son.'

"At that moment I thought: already a whole lifetime had passed. I looked at myself, old and frail. I had only this young child. To spare him from her threat, I opened my mouth and cried out."

The hermit said: "The fault was mine. This was the work of a demon, nothing more."

Just so are all dharmas. Delusive thoughts are an unawakened dream. Within emptiness, one mistakes things for real. Therefore the *Vijñaptimātratā Treatise* says:

> Before attaining true awakening, one perpetually dwells within a dream. Therefore the Buddha calls it the long night of birth and death.

**Question:** If one performs contemplations of impurity, suffering, emptiness, and the like, is this not the same as the Hīnayāna practice of self-regulation and self-liberation?

**Answer:** This contemplation is not limited to the lesser vehicle; it also belongs to the Great Vehicle. As the *Lotus Sūtra* says:

> "Great compassion is one's dwelling; gentle patience is one's robe."

It does not conflict with the mind of great compassion. Indeed, how could contemplations of suffering and impermanence fail to spur the bodhisattva's compassion and vows?

Therefore, the *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Sūtra* and other texts also include contemplation of impurity as a practice for bodhisattvas. Those who wish to know more should read the sūtras themselves.

**Question:** What benefit does such contemplation yield?

**Answer:** If one constantly disciplines the mind in this way, the desires of the five senses grow faint. At the moment of death, one's right mindfulness will not be disordered, and one will not fall into the evil realms.

As a verse from the *Mahāvibhāṣā Śāstra* on exhortation to diligent mindfulness says:

> In the prime of youth, when free from illness,
> One is heedless and does not strive.
> Greedily tending to worldly affairs,
> One does not cultivate generosity, morality, or meditation.
> When death at last devours,
> Only then does one repent and seek to practice virtue.
>
> The wise should observe and reflect,
> Cutting off the thoughts of the five desires.
> One who diligently trains the mind
> Will have no regret at the time of death.
> When the mind is focused and attentive,
> There will be no confused or disordered thoughts.
> The wise one diligently holds the mind,
> And at the end, the mind is settled.

A verse from the *Saddharmasmṛtyupasthāna Sūtra*, chapter fifty-seven, says:

> Contemplate this body:
> Sinews and vessels wind and intertwine;
> Moist skin wraps it over.
> Nine openings are wounds
> That everywhere ooze and overflow
> With excrement, urine, and all impurity.
>
> Like a granary or bin
> Filled with various kinds of grain:
> This body is just so,
> Packed full of mixed filth.
>
> The mechanism of bones sets it in motion;
> It is fragile, not solid.
> The fool constantly delights in it;
> The wise one has no attachment.
>
> Snot and saliva and sweat constantly flow;
> Pus and blood are always brimming.
> Yellow fat and disordered humors,
> And the brain fills the skull.
>
> In the chest, phlegm and mucus flow;
> Within are the raw and cooked organs.
> Suet and membranes,
> The five viscera and the bowels of the abdomen:
> All such reeking, rotting things
> Dwell together in impurity.
>
> This sinful body is deeply fearsome;
> It is in truth one's enemy.
> The witless person, steeped in desire,
> Foolishly and constantly protects it.
>
> This reeking, filthy body
> Is like a crumbling city wall.
> Day and night, afflictions press upon it;
> It shifts and flows, never pausing for a moment.
>
> The body-city has walls of bone
> And is plastered with the mud of blood and flesh,
> Painted with the colors of greed, anger, and delusion,
> Adorned falsely wherever one looks.
>
> The loathsome bone-city of the body:
> Blood and flesh are joined together.
> It is always beset by evil companions;
> Inside and out, suffering presses it.
>
> Nanda, you should know:
> What I have taught is thus.
> Day and night, keep your mind tethered to this;
> Do not think upon the realm of desire.
> If you wish to be far removed from it,
> Always practice this contemplation.
> Diligently seek the contemplation of liberation.

---

## Gate Two: Aspiring to the Pure Land

The meritorious qualities of the Land of Utmost Bliss, both its setting and its inhabitants, are immeasurable. Even a hundred kalpas or a thousand kalpas of explanation could not exhaust them. Neither calculation nor analogy can encompass them. Yet the *Qunyi lun* (群疑論, *Treatise Resolving Various Doubts*) sets forth thirty kinds of benefit, and the *Anguo chao* (安國鈔) enumerates twenty-four joys. Since we know that praise and celebration rest entirely in the human heart, I shall now take up ten joys in celebration of the Pure Land, though this is like a single drop of dew upon the great ocean.

1. The joy of the holy assembly coming to welcome the dying
2. The joy of the lotus flower first opening
3. The joy of bodily marks and supernatural powers
4. The joy of the five wondrous sense-fields
5. The joy of bliss without regression
6. The joy of guiding and forming karmic connections with others
7. The joy of gathering together with the holy assembly
8. The joy of seeing the Buddha and hearing the Dharma
9. The joy of making offerings to buddhas as one wishes
10. The joy of advancing along the path to buddhahood

### The First Joy: The Holy Assembly Coming to Welcome the Dying

When a person of evil karma reaches the end of life, wind and fire depart first, so there is much agitation, fever, and suffering. When a person of good conduct reaches the end of life, earth and water depart first, so there is gentleness, ease, and no suffering. How much more so for one whose accumulated merit of buddha-recollection is deep and whose directed mind has matured over many years! At the moment of death, great joy arises of its own accord.

Why is this so? The Tathāgata Amitābha, by the power of his original vows, together with all the bodhisattvas and a retinue of hundreds of thousands of bhikṣus, emits a great radiance that shines brilliantly before the dying person's eyes. At that time, the greatly compassionate Avalokiteśvara extends hands adorned with a hundred merits and brings the jeweled lotus dais before the practitioner. The bodhisattva Mahāsthāmaprāpta, together with a boundless holy assembly, simultaneously praises and commends the practitioner, extending hands to receive and guide them.

At this moment the practitioner beholds this sight with their own eyes. Their heart is filled with delight. Body and mind are at peace, as though entering meditative absorption. Know then that between the closing of the eyes in a humble grass hut and the crossing of the legs upon the lotus dais, there is but an instant. Following behind Amitābha Buddha, taking one's place among the assembly of bodhisattvas, in the span of a single thought-moment one attains birth and the joy of a thousand ages.
