The bliss of deep meditative absorption in the palace of the great Brahma King and all such pleasures are not worthy to be called joy, for the cycle of rebirth turns without end and one cannot escape the three evil paths. But now, cradled in the palm of Avalokiteśvara and entrusted to the womb of a jeweled lotus, one crosses forever beyond the sea of suffering and is born for the first time in the Pure Land. The joy in one's heart at that moment cannot be expressed in words.

A verse of Nāgārjuna says:

> If a person, at the moment of death,
> attains birth in that land,
> they are at once endowed with immeasurable virtue.
> Therefore I take refuge with my life.

### The Second Joy: The Lotus Flower First Opening

After the practitioner is born in that land and the lotus flower first opens, the joys experienced surpass those before a hundred-thousandfold. It is like a person born blind who for the first time gains sight, or like a rustic from the hinterlands who suddenly enters the king's palace.

One beholds one's own body. The body has become the color of burnished purple-gold. One is clad in naturally appearing jeweled garments, with rings, bracelets, and jeweled crowns, adorned beyond measure. Beholding the Buddha's radiance, one's eyes become pure and clear. Through the force of past practice, one hears the multitude of Dharma-sounds. Every form one beholds and every sound one hears is marvelous beyond compare.

The adornments that fill the expanse of space dazzle the eyes amid paths of cloud. The sound of the wondrous Dharma-wheel turning fills the jeweled realm. Towers, palaces, groves, and pools shine through and through, inside and out. Waterfowl, wild geese, mandarin ducks, and teals fly in flocks near and far.

Some see beings arriving like a torrent of rain from the worlds of the ten directions to be born there. Some see members of the holy assembly, numberless as the sands of the Ganges, coming from countless buddha-fields. Some ascend pavilion towers to gaze upon the ten directions. Some ride on palaces that rest in open space. Some dwell in the sky reciting sūtras and expounding the Dharma. Some dwell in the sky sitting in meditation and entering samādhi. Upon the ground and amid the groves it is likewise. Everywhere there are those wading through streams and bathing in flowing waters, playing music and scattering flowers, coming and going among the pavilions, paying homage to and praising the Tathāgata. Such are the boundless heavenly beings and holy assembly, sporting freely as their hearts desire. And the transformation-buddhas and bodhisattvas, with clouds of incense and clouds of flowers, fill the realm throughout, beyond all possibility of naming.

Then, gradually turning one's gaze to look into the distance, one sees the Tathāgata Amitābha like a golden mountain king, seated upon a jeweled lotus flower in the center of a jeweled pool. Avalokiteśvara and Mahāsthāmaprāpta, majestic and dignified, also sit upon jeweled lotuses, attending the Buddha on his left and right. The boundless holy assembly reverently surrounds them. Upon the jeweled ground, jeweled trees stand in rows, and beneath each jeweled tree sits one buddha and two bodhisattvas. Their radiant adornments pervade the lapis lazuli ground, like great torches blazing in the darkness of night.

Then Avalokiteśvara and Mahāsthāmaprāpta come before the practitioner and, with voices of great compassion, console and encourage them in various ways. The practitioner descends from the lotus dais, prostrates with the five limbs touching the ground, and pays homage with head bowed. Then, following the bodhisattvas, the practitioner gradually approaches the Buddha's presence, kneeling upon the steps of the seven jewels, gazing up at the countenance of ten thousand virtues, hearing the one true path, and entering the ocean of Samantabhadra's vows. Tears of joy fall like rain; longing pierces to the bone.

Entering the realm of the Buddha for the first time, one experiences what has never been experienced before. The practitioner who, back in the sahā world, merely read scriptural passages now beholds these things in their very reality. How the heart of joy unfolds!

> Those whose faith is pure and clear:
> When the flower opens, they see the Buddha.

### The Third Joy: Bodily Marks and Supernatural Powers

The beings of that land have bodies of true gold, pure within and without, constantly radiant. Their light illumines one another. They are fully adorned with the thirty-two major marks, supremely beautiful and marvelous, without compare in the world. The light from the bodies of the śrāvakas extends one fathom. The radiance of the bodhisattvas illumines a hundred yojanas, or, some say, a hundred thousand yojanas. If one were to compare the lord of the Sixth Heaven to the beings of that land, it would be like placing a beggar beside an emperor.

Moreover, all the beings of that land possess the five supernatural powers, whose wondrous functions are unfathomable. They act freely as their minds direct. If they wish to see the forms of the worlds in the ten directions, they see them without taking a step. If they wish to hear the sounds of the worlds in the ten directions, they hear them without rising from their seats. They recall events spanning immeasurable past lives as clearly as things heard today. They perceive the minds of beings in the six destinies as though seeing reflections in a clear mirror. They travel to and from countless buddha-fields as though they were but an arm's length away. Traversing horizontally across hundreds of thousands of millions of billions of nayutas of lands, and vertically across hundreds of thousands of millions of billions of nayutas of kalpas, in a single thought-moment they move freely and without obstruction.

Now, among the beings of this present world, who possesses even one of the thirty-two marks? Who has attained even one of the five supernatural powers? Without a lamp or the sun, we cannot illuminate. Without walking, we cannot arrive. Though it be but a single sheet of paper, we cannot see what lies beyond it. Though it be but a single moment, we cannot know what comes after. We have not escaped the cage, and at every turn we meet with obstruction. Yet the beings of that land, not one among them lacks these qualities. They did not cultivate the karma of marks and excellences over a hundred great kalpas; they did not practice the causes of supernatural powers in the four dhyanas. This is simply the natural endowment of that land.

> The forms of the heavenly and human inhabitants are alike,
> like golden mountain peaks.
> The refuge to which all the victorious ones return:
> therefore I pay homage with my head.
>
> Those born in that land
> possess the divine eye and divine ear,
> unobstructed throughout the ten directions.
> I bow my head to the Honored One among sages.
>
> The beings of that land
> possess miraculous transformation and bodily powers,
> and are also endowed with the knowledge of past lives.
> Therefore I take refuge with my life.

### The Fourth Joy: The Five Wondrous Sense-Fields

The forty-eight vows adorn the Pure Land. All things there are of consummate beauty and exquisite subtlety. Every sight is a pure and wondrous form. Every sound, without exception, is the voice of liberation. The realms of fragrance, taste, and touch are likewise.

That world has lapis lazuli for its ground, with golden cords marking its paths. The land is level and smooth, without high or low places. It extends vast and boundless, gleaming with subtle, marvelous, rare, and radiant purity. The whole ground is spread with exquisite garments. All the heavenly and human beings tread upon palaces and pavilions made of the seven jewels. Their height and breadth conform to the heart's wish and the mind's intent. On wondrous jeweled couches lie fine garments spread above. Seven-tiered balustrades, a hundred billion flower banners, pearl necklaces hanging down, and jeweled pennants and canopies above. Within the halls and upon the towers dwell heavenly beings, and all around, inside and out, on left and right, are bathing pools.

Pools with golden bottoms have silver sand. Pools with silver bottoms have golden sand. Crystal pools have lapis lazuli sand. Lapis lazuli pools have crystal sand. Coral, amber, giant clam shell, and agate; white jade and purple gold are likewise. The water of eight excellent qualities fills them to the brim. Jeweled sand shines through; no depth is unsounded. Jeweled flowers cover the pools completely. Blue lotuses emit blue light. Yellow lotuses emit yellow light. Red and white lotuses each emit their own light. Gentle breezes blow, and the lights of the flowers whirl and interlace. Within each flower is a bodhisattva. Within each ray of light are transformation-buddhas. Gentle ripples flow and circle, pouring into one another. The waters flow with tranquil ease, neither too slow nor too swift. Their sound is subtle and wondrous, expounding nothing other than the buddha-dharma. Some streams set forth suffering, emptiness, non-self, and the pāramitās. Some pour forth the sounds of the ten powers, the four fearlessnesses, and the dharmas exclusive to buddhas. Or the voice of great compassion. Or the voice of the patience of non-arising. Whatever one hears brings immeasurable joy, in accord with the truth of purity, quiescence, and cessation, in accord with the paths practiced by bodhisattvas and śrāvakas.

And there are geese, mandarin ducks, herons, storks, swans, and cranes; peacocks, parrots, kalavinkas, and other hundred-jeweled-colored birds. Day and night, throughout the six periods, they produce harmonious and elegant sounds, praising mindfulness of the Buddha, mindfulness of the Dharma, and mindfulness of the Saṅgha, and proclaiming the five faculties, the five powers, and the seven factors of awakening. The name of the three evil paths and their afflictions is nowhere to be found. There are only the spontaneous sounds of bliss.

When the bodhisattvas and the assembly of śrāvakas enter the jeweled pools to bathe, the water becomes shallow or deep according to their wish, never contrary to their hearts. It washes away the defilements of the mind. Clear, bright, pure, and pellucid, when they have finished bathing, each goes their own way. Some dwell in the sky, some beneath the trees. There are those who expound the sūtras and those who recite them, those who receive the teaching and those who listen to it, those who sit in meditation and those who practice walking meditation. Among them, those who have not yet attained the fruit of stream-entry attain stream-entry, and so on up to those who have not yet attained arhatship, who attain arhatship. Those who have not yet reached the stage of non-retrogression reach non-retrogression. All attain the path, and none is without joy.

There are also clear streams, their beds spread with golden sand, shallow or deep, cool or warm as desired. Winding among sandalwood trees, row upon row aligned, leaf upon leaf rustling together, with leaves of purple gold and branches of white silver, flowers of coral and fruits of jade, of one jewel or of seven jewels, pure or variegated. Branches, leaves, flowers, and fruits are all splendidly adorned. Gentle breezes come in due time and blow through the jeweled trees. The jeweled nets sway gently. Exquisite flowers drift slowly down and scatter with the wind, spreading their fragrance, mingling their scent with the flowing water. And there come forth subtle, wondrous sounds, with tones harmonizing as in song. It is as if hundreds of thousands of kinds of music were all played at once. Those who hear them naturally become mindful of the Buddha, the Dharma, and the Saṅgha. The ten thousand kinds of music in the Sixth Heaven cannot match even a single strain from these trees.

Between the leaves, flowers grow; upon the flowers, fruits appear, and all emit radiance that transforms into jeweled canopies. All the deeds of the buddhas are reflected within those canopies. Indeed, when one wishes to see the magnificently pure buddha-fields of the ten directions, one sees them all illuminated among the jeweled trees. Above the trees are seven-tiered jeweled nets. Between the jeweled nets are five hundred billion wondrous flower palaces, and within the palaces are heavenly children, their necklaces shining, sporting freely.

Such are the jeweled trees of the seven treasures, covering the entire world. Beautiful flowers and soft grasses also grow everywhere, tender, fragrant, and pure. Those who touch them are filled with joy. From the sound of the Dharma, heavenly flowers of wondrous color cascade down in colorful profusion. Jeweled garments and beautiful ornaments spiral gently down from above, like birds flying through the sky, to be scattered as offerings before the Buddha.

And there are immeasurable joys, wish-fulfilling wondrous incense, rubbing incense, powdered incense, and immeasurable fragrances, whose scent pervades the entire world. Any who smell them find that the habits of defilement and affliction naturally cease to arise. From the ground to the sky, every palace, flower, and tree, all the myriad things, are composed of innumerable mixed jewels and a hundred thousand kinds of incense blended together. That fragrance perfumes all the worlds of the ten directions. When bodhisattvas smell it, they all cultivate the practice of the Buddha.

Furthermore, when the bodhisattvas, arhats, and all the beings of that land wish to eat, tables of the seven jewels appear naturally before them. Bowls of the seven jewels are filled with wondrous flavors, unlike any taste in the human world, nor like any taste in the heavens. The fragrance and deliciousness are beyond compare. Sweetness or sourness follows one's wish. Beholding the color and smelling the fragrance, body and mind become pure and clean. It is as though one had already eaten; vitality and strength increase. When the meal is done, all vanishes, and when the time comes, it appears again.

Moreover, the beings of that land, when they wish for clothing, receive it the instant the thought arises. As the Buddha has praised, garments suited to the Dharma naturally appear upon the body. There is no need for cutting, sewing, dyeing, washing, or mending.

And radiance pervades everywhere; there is no need for sun, moon, lamp, or candle. Cold and warmth are perfectly tempered; there is no spring, autumn, winter, or summer. A natural breeze of virtue adjusts warmth and coolness to perfect comfort. When it touches the bodies of beings, all experience bliss, like a bhikṣu who has attained the samādhi of cessation.

Each morning the breeze scatters wondrous flowers throughout the buddha-land. Their scent is rich and fragrant. They are subtle and soft, like tula cotton. When one treads upon them, the foot sinks four inches; when one lifts the foot, they spring back as before. After the morning passes, the flowers vanish into the ground. The old flowers having disappeared, new flowers rain down. At midday, in the afternoon, and during the first, middle, and last watches of the night, it is the same.

All these exquisite sense-fields of the five faculties, though they delight the body and mind of those who see, hear, and feel them, do not increase the attachment and craving of sentient beings. Rather, they increase immeasurable and supreme merit. Among the countless buddha-fields in the eight directions and above and below, the Land of Utmost Bliss stands first in meritorious qualities. The magnificently pure and wondrous features of two hundred and ten billion buddha-fields are all gathered within it.

> If one contemplates the features of that land,
> one eliminates the extreme evil deeds of countless kalpas.
>
> Vast as space, its breadth and reach are boundless.
> A thousand myriad kinds of jeweled flowers
> blanket the pools and flowing springs.
> Gentle breezes stir the petals and leaves;
> their interlacing lights whirl and turn.
> Palaces and many-storied towers:
> gazing out in the ten directions, unobstructed.
> Trees of variegated light and hue,
> jeweled railings encircling all around.
> Immeasurable jeweled lattices
> and nets spread throughout the sky.
> All manner of bells ring out,
> proclaiming the wondrous Dharma-sound.
> Whatever sentient beings desire,
> all is completely fulfilled.
> Therefore I vow to be born
> in the land of Amitābha Buddha.

### The Fifth Joy: Bliss Without Regression

In this sahā world there is nothing truly worth relishing. The throne of a wheel-turning king with its seven treasures does not endure. The pleasures of the heavens soon give way to the five signs of decay. Even at the Summit of Existence, the cycle turns without end. How much more so for ordinary people! Events run contrary to wishes; pleasure is always paired with pain. The wealthy are not necessarily long-lived; the long-lived are not necessarily wealthy. One may be rich yesterday and poor today. One may be born at dawn and dead by dusk.

Therefore the sūtra says:

> The out-breath does not wait for the in-breath; the in-breath does not wait for the out-breath.

Not only do present joys depart and sorrows arrive, but at the moment of death one falls according to one's transgressions into suffering.

In the Western Land, the enjoyment of bliss is without end. Heavenly beings and humans meet freely and see one another. Compassion permeates every heart; all regard one another as their only child. Together they walk upon the lapis lazuli ground. Together they play among the sandalwood groves. From palace to palace, from grove and pool to grove and pool. When one desires stillness, the sounds of wind, waves, strings, and pipes fall silent beneath one's ears. When one desires sight, mountains, rivers, streams, and valleys appear before one's eyes. Fragrance, taste, touch, and dharmas likewise follow one's thought.

Some cross jeweled bridges and make music. Some soar through the sky and display supernatural powers. Some welcome and escort great beings from other realms. Some accompany the heavenly and human holy assembly on their excursions. Some go to the edge of the jeweled pools to comfort those newly born, saying: "Do you know? This place is called the Land of Utmost Bliss. The lord of this realm is called Amitābha Buddha. You should now take refuge in him."

Or together they sit within the jeweled pools, each upon a lotus dais, and speak to one another of their past lives: "I was originally in such-and-such a land. At the time I aroused the aspiration and sought the path, I upheld such-and-such scriptures, I kept such-and-such precepts, I performed such-and-such wholesome deeds, I practiced such-and-such giving." Each speaks joyfully of the merits they cherished and recounts in full the circumstances of the life from which they came.

Or together they discuss how the buddhas of the ten directions employ skillful means for the benefit of beings. Or together they deliberate on the causes and conditions by which the beings of the three realms of existence may be rescued from suffering. When the discussion is finished, they follow their affinities and part. When the conversation is over, they return together to their joyful pursuits in the pools.

In tranquil silence they read, recite, interpret, and expound. Such are the pleasures, continuing without interruption.

Dwelling in this place of non-retrogression, one is forever free from the dread of the three evil paths and the eight difficulties. One's lifespan is also immeasurable; in the end there is no suffering of birth, old age, sickness, or death. Heart and circumstances accord with one another, so there is no suffering of separation from the beloved. The compassionate eye regards all equally, so there is no suffering of meeting the despised. As the result of pure karma, there is no suffering of not obtaining what one seeks. The body is like vajra, so there is no suffering of the five burning aggregates.

Once one has entrusted oneself to the dais adorned with the seven jewels, one has parted forever from the sea of suffering and the wheel of the three realms. If one has a particular vow and is born in another realm, that birth and death is a freely chosen manifestation, not the birth and death of karmic retribution. There is not even the name of that which is neither suffering nor bliss. How much less any actual suffering!

A verse of Nāgārjuna says:

> If a person is born in that land,
> they will never fall into the evil destinies
> or among the asuras.
> I now take refuge with my life.

### The Sixth Joy: Guiding and Forming Karmic Connections with Others

While a person lives in this world, their wishes are rarely fulfilled. The trees long for stillness, yet the wind does not cease. The child wishes to serve the parent, but the parent will not wait. Though one's resolve may be as strong as liver and gall, one's strength may not suffice even for gruel and water. Lord and minister, teacher and disciple, wife and child, friends: all those bound by kindness, all those one has known, are likewise. We exhaust ourselves in foolish attachment, and only increase the karma of rebirth. And when the fruits of karma shift and drive us on, the places of our birth are cut off from one another. Among the six destinies and the four modes of birth, who knows where one may end up? Among the wild beasts of the field and the birds of the mountain, who can recognize a former parent?

As a verse of the *Xindi guan jing* (心地觀經, *Mind-Ground Contemplation Sūtra*) says:

> People of the world create all manner of sins for the sake of their children,
> and fall into the three evil paths to suffer for ages.
> Sons and daughters are not sages; they have no supernatural powers.
> Unable to see the cycle of rebirth, they can hardly repay the debt.
> Sentient beings revolve through the six destinies
> like a wheel that has no beginning and no end.
> Sometimes as father or mother, sometimes as son or daughter:
> life after life, generation after generation, all share mutual debts of kindness.

If one is born in the Land of Utmost Bliss, one's wisdom is lofty and clear, one's supernatural powers penetrate all things. The kindred and acquaintances of every life, one can guide and receive as one wishes. With the divine eye, one sees their places of birth. With the divine ear, one hears their voices. With the knowledge of past lives, one recalls one's debts to them. With the knowledge of others' minds, one fathoms their thoughts. With the power of miraculous travel, one pursues them and manifests transformations. With the power of skillful means, one instructs and guides them.

As the *Pingdeng jing* (平等覺經, *Sūtra of Equal Awakening*) says:

> The beings of that land all know from what previous existence they have come to be born, and they know the affairs, past, present, and future, of the eight directions and above and below. They know the thoughts and intentions of all the heavenly beings, humans, insects, and crawling creatures. They know what others are about to say. They know in what year and what kalpa each will be born in that land, will practice the bodhisattva path, and will attain arhatship. All this they know in advance.

And the "Vows of Samantabhadra" in the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra* say:

> May I, when I approach the moment of death,
> remove every last obstruction.
> May I behold that Buddha, Amitābha,
> and at once attain birth in the Land of Peace and Bliss.
> Having been born in that land,
> I shall bring to fulfillment these great vows,
> complete them all without remainder,
> and benefit the entire realm of sentient beings.

If those without any prior connection can receive such benefit, how much more so those with whom one has already formed karmic bonds!

A verse of Nāgārjuna says:

> With stainless, adorned radiance,
> in a single thought and a single moment,
> universally illuminating the assemblies of all buddhas,
> benefiting all sentient beings.

### The Seventh Joy: Gathering Together with the Holy Assembly

As the sūtra says:

> Sentient beings who hear of this should arouse the vow to be born in that land. Why? Because one will be able to gather together with such inconceivable beings.

Samantabhadra Bodhisattva has said:

> If there are sentient beings who have not yet planted good roots, or who have planted only meager good roots, whether śrāvaka or bodhisattva, they still cannot even hear my name, much less behold my body. If there are sentient beings who hear my name, they shall never regress from supreme, perfect awakening. Even those who see or hear me in a dream shall, in all future kalpas, forever practice the vast deeds of Samantabhadra and bring to completion the supreme awakening.
>
> Samantabhadra's bodily form is like space,
> abiding in truth, not in any land.
> Following the hearts' desires of all beings,
> he manifests his universal body, equal to all.
> In all the buddha-fields, in the presence of all buddhas,
> he displays supernatural powers in various samadhis.
> Each and every supernatural power pervades
> the buddha-lands of the ten directions, leaving none out.

Mañjuśrī:

> In all the lands, as the Honored One teaches,
> the buddhas of the three ages have him as their mother.
> When all the tathagatas of the ten directions first aroused the aspiration,
> it was entirely through the transformative power of Mañjuśrī.
> All the sentient beings in every world,
> hearing his name, seeing his body and its radiance,
> and also seeing the various transformations he manifests according to their kinds,
> all accomplish the path of buddhahood, inconceivable.

If one worships and makes offerings to Mañjuśrī, one is forever born into the family of the Buddha. If one recites his name for one day or seven days, Mañjuśrī will surely come. If one has obstructions from past lives, one may see him in a dream, and all one's wishes will be fulfilled. If one beholds his image, for a hundred thousand kalpas one will not fall into the evil paths. If one practices the mind of compassion, one will at once behold Mañjuśrī. If one upholds, reads, and recites his name, even with grave obstructions, one will not fall into the fires of the Avīci Hell.

> The buddhas of countless nayutas of lands, benefiting sentient beings,
> cannot equal the benefit Mañjuśrī performs in a single kalpa.

Therefore, if one recites the name of Mañjuśrī Bodhisattva, merit is immeasurable.

Maitreya: Merely hearing his name, one does not fall into places of darkness. With a single recitation of his name, one eliminates the sins of twelve hundred kalpas of birth and death.

Kṣitigarbha Bodhisattva: Each day at dawn he enters a samādhi as numerous as the sands of the Ganges, pervading the Dharma-realm, rescuing suffering beings.

> Praising Kṣitigarbha's great and renowned merit
> surpasses praising the virtue of other sages for kotis of kalpas.
> Even if for a hundred kalpas one were to praise his merit,
> one still could not exhaust it.

Avalokiteśvara:

> If there are those in suffering, who call upon my name three times,
> and I do not go to save them, I shall not attain true awakening.
>
> And again, those who for even a moment sincerely recite my name:
> their merit is equal, perfectly equal.
>
> All who recite my name
> shall attain that which transcends kalpas, inconceivable.
>
> Attendant to many thousands of billions of buddhas,
> having aroused the great, pure vow,
> endowed with supernatural powers,
> vastly cultivating wise skillful means.
> Throughout the lands of the ten directions,
> there is no realm where he does not appear.
> Moment after moment, let no doubt arise:
> Avalokiteśvara, the pure and holy one,
> in suffering, affliction, and the peril of death,
> is able to serve as one's refuge.
> Endowed with every merit,
> with compassionate eyes he gazes upon all.

Mahāsthāmaprāpta:

> By the power of this bodhisattva's name,
> one eliminates the sins of countless asamkhyeya kalpas of birth and death.
> One does not dwell in a womb,
> but ever courses through the power of vows, assisting Amitābha.
> Ever among the great assembly, proclaiming the Dharma.
> Beings who hear attain the pure eye.
> Supernatural powers pervade the lands of the ten directions.
> Universally appearing among all beings,
> fully endowed with compassion, he saves the world.
> Roaming everywhere through the sea of all sentient beings.

Such bodhisattvas who stand at the stage of one-lifetime succession to buddhahood, their numbers like the sands of the Ganges, beautiful in form, perfect in merit, ever dwell in the Land of Utmost Bliss, surrounding Amitābha Buddha.

And the assembly of śrāvakas, whose number is difficult to fathom: their spiritual wisdom penetrates all, their majestic power is unhindered. They can hold all the worlds in the palms of their hands. Suppose there were a hundred thousand million billion innumerable Mahamaudgalyayanas, and for asamkhyeya kalpas they all together tried to count the śrāvakas of the first assembly: what they could count would be like a single drop, and what they could not count would be like the great ocean. Among them, those who pass into final nirvāṇa are beyond reckoning, and those who newly attain arhatship are also beyond reckoning, yet the total is neither increased nor decreased, just as the great ocean, though the waters of the Ganges may lessen or increase, itself neither grows nor diminishes.

The assembly of bodhisattvas is yet more numerous. As the *Dazhidu lun* (大智度論) says:

> In the land of Amitābha Buddha, the bodhisattvas see one another from afar, gaze upon one another from afar, and hear one another's voices from afar. All pursue the same path; there are no differing kinds.

How much more so when from the buddha-fields of the ten directions, numberless as the sands of the Ganges, bodhisattvas and holy beings as numerous as motes of dust each display their supernatural powers and come to the Land of Peace and Bliss. They gaze up at the Buddha's countenance and reverently make offerings. Some bear wondrous heavenly flowers. Some burn exquisite jeweled incense. Some present priceless garments. Some play heavenly music, producing harmonious sounds to sing the praises of the World-Honored One. They receive the teaching of the sūtras and spread the way of transformation. Coming and going like this, day and night without ceasing: those from the east depart and those from the west arrive; those from the west depart and those from the north arrive; those from the north depart and those from the south arrive. The four intermediate directions and above and below are likewise. They make way for one another in their comings and goings, as in a bustling marketplace.

To hear the name of even one of these great beings is no small karmic connection. Over hundreds of thousands of ten-thousands of kalpas, who could meet them face to face? Yet in that land, the beings dwell constantly together, exchanging words, paying their respects to one another, drawing near to and attending upon all the bodhisattvas.

> Endowed with all the excellent marks,
> each adorning their own body.
> I now pay homage with my life
> to those who have transcended the prison of the three realms.
>
> Their eyes are like lotus petals.
> The assembly of śrāvakas is beyond measure.
> Therefore I bow my head in reverence.
>
> And again:
> From the ten directions, the children of the Buddha come,
> displaying supernatural powers, arriving in the Land of Peace and Bliss,
> ever gazing up at the Buddha's countenance in reverence.
> Therefore I bow my head to Amitābha Buddha.

### The Eighth Joy: Seeing the Buddha and Hearing the Dharma

In this sahā world, it is exceedingly difficult to see the Buddha and hear the Dharma. The bodhisattva Siṃhanāda said:

> We have practiced the four immeasurables and the three liberations for countless hundreds of thousands of kalpas, and now, seeing the great sage, the Honored One Śākyamuni, it is like a blind turtle encountering a floating log.

The youth Sudhana gave up his entire body to obtain just half a verse. Sadaprarudita ("Ever-Weeping") cut open his own flesh in his far quest for the perfection of wisdom. Even bodhisattvas are thus. How much more so for ordinary beings!

When the Buddha was in Śrāvastī for twenty-five years, among the nine hundred million households of that city, three hundred million saw the Buddha, three hundred million merely heard of him, and the remaining three hundred million neither saw nor heard. While the Buddha was still in the world, it was already so. How much more so after his final passing!

Therefore the *Lotus Sūtra* says:

> These beings burdened with sin,
> because of their evil karmic causes and conditions,
> pass through asamkhyeya kalpas
> without ever hearing the name of the Three Treasures.

But the beings of that land constantly behold Amitābha Buddha and ceaselessly hear the profound and wondrous Dharma.

Upon the magnificently pure ground there is a bodhi tree whose branches and leaves spread out in all four directions, composed of many jewels blended together. Above the tree hangs a jeweled net. Between its branches dangle pearl necklaces. When the wind stirs its branches and leaves, the sound expounds the wondrous Dharma. That sound streams forth to pervade all buddha-lands. Those who hear it attain deep Dharma-patience and dwell in non-retrogression. Their faculties of hearing become clear and pure. Beholding the tree's color, smelling its fragrance, tasting its flavor, touching its light, contemplating its characteristics: all these effects are the same, continuing until the attainment of buddhahood. The six faculties become completely purified.

Beneath the tree is a seat, adorned beyond measure. Upon the seat is a buddha, whose marks and excellences are boundless. The ushnisha rises prominently. The clear sky seems deep green by comparison. The white curl between his brows spirals to the right, as full as the autumn moon's light. His eyes are like blue lotuses. His lips are like cinnabar fruit. His voice is like the kalavinka bird. His chest is like a lion's. His calves are like the legs of the royal stag. His soles bear the thousand-spoked wheel. Such are the eighty-four thousand marks and excellences, adorning a body of burnished purple-gold. Immeasurable lights as numerous as motes of dust radiate forth, as though gathering together the brilliance of a billion suns and moons.

At times the Buddha sits in a lecture hall of the seven jewels, proclaiming the wondrous Dharma. His Brahma-voice is deep and subtle, delighting the hearts of all. The bodhisattvas, śrāvakas, heavenly and human beings of the great assembly press their palms together with one mind and gaze up at his honored countenance. At that very moment, a gentle breeze naturally arises, blowing through the seven-jeweled trees, and immeasurable wondrous flowers scatter on the wind in all four directions. All the devas play their various instruments. At such a time, the serene joy and bliss cannot be expressed in words.

Or again, the Buddha manifests a vast and boundless body, or a body of sixteen feet, or of eight feet, or appears beneath a jeweled tree, or upon a jeweled pool. According to the original aspirations of the beings, following what each longed for when they first sought the path, whether great or small, the Buddha expounds the sūtras and the Dharma as each desires, causing them swiftly to attain understanding and realization. Thus, according to the various capacities, he teaches the various Dharmas.

And Avalokiteśvara and Mahāsthāmaprāpta, the two bodhisattvas, are always at the Buddha's left and right, seated beside him, discussing the governance of the Dharma. The Buddha constantly sits with these two bodhisattvas, deliberating on the affairs of the eight directions and above and below, past, present, and future.

Sometimes, from the buddha-lands in the east, as numerous as the sands of the Ganges, immeasurable and countless bodhisattvas all proceed to the presence of Amitāyus Buddha, to pay reverence and make offerings, and to attend upon the assembly of bodhisattvas and śrāvakas. From the south, the west, and the north, from the four intermediate directions and above and below, it is likewise.

> Seeing that magnificently pure land,
> subtle and wondrous, difficult to conceive,
> they arouse the boundless aspiration:
> "May my land be just like this."
>
> Then the World-Honored One stirs and gently smiles.
> From his mouth stream forth countless rays of light,
> illuminating all the lands of the ten directions.
> The light circles back around his body three times and enters the crown of his head.
> All the heavenly and human beings leap with joy.
>
> The great being Avalokiteśvara
> straightens his robes and bows, asking:
> "O Buddha, for what reason do you smile?
> We beseech you, please explain."
>
> Then, with a Brahma-voice like thunder,
> in eight-toned wondrous resonance, he responds:
> "I shall now confer upon these bodhisattvas their prophecies.
> Listen well, O benevolent ones.
> The practitioners who come from the ten directions:
> I know all their vows completely.
> They aspire to magnificently pure lands.
> Having received their prophecy, they shall become buddhas.
>
> Comprehending that all dharmas
> are like dreams, illusions, and echoes,
> they fulfill all their wondrous vows
> and will surely accomplish such a land.
>
> Knowing dharmas to be like lightning and reflections,
> they perfect the bodhisattva path,
> endowed with all the roots of merit.
> Having received their prophecy, they shall become buddhas.
>
> Penetrating the nature of all dharmas,
> all being empty and without self,
> they single-mindedly seek the pure buddha-land
> and will surely accomplish such a realm."

> Upon a wondrous dais, on that seat, like a mountain king:
> therefore I bow my head to Amitābha Buddha.
>
> All conditioned things are impermanent, without self, and so forth,
> like the moon in water, like lightning, reflections, and dew.
> For the sake of all, he teaches the Dharma, which is beyond all names:
> therefore I bow my head to Amitābha Buddha.
>
> May we, together with all sentient beings,
> be born in the Land of Peace and Bliss.

### The Ninth Joy: Making Offerings to Buddhas as One Wishes

The beings of that land, throughout the six periods of day and night, constantly bear all manner of heavenly flowers and make offerings to Amitāyus Buddha.

And when they wish to make offerings to the buddhas of other realms, they kneel in the Buddha's presence, join their palms, and address him. The Buddha grants his consent, and all are filled with great joy. Then thousands of billions of tens of thousands of people each take flight, following one another in equal procession, and together scatter flying flowers. They proceed to the presence of the countless buddhas of the eight directions and above and below, and before each they prostrate, make offerings, and pay reverence.

Thus, each morning, with garment-folds full of wondrous flowers, they make offerings to the buddhas of a hundred thousand billion realms in the ten directions. Along with garments, music, and all manner of provisions, everything arises spontaneously from their intention. They offer these with reverence, and just at mealtime they return to their own land, to eat, to practice walking meditation, and to enjoy the bliss of the Dharma.

Some say they make offerings to the buddhas three times each day.

Now the practitioner, through the transmitted teaching, has heard of the various meritorious qualities of the buddha-fields of the ten directions. As each vision and each sound arises, longing wells up from afar. They say to one another: "When will we be able to see the pure lands of the ten directions? When will we be able to meet the buddhas and bodhisattvas?"

Each time they face the scripture texts, they cannot help but sigh. But if one attains birth in the Land of Utmost Bliss, then, whether through one's own power or sustained by the Buddha's power, going in the morning and returning in the evening, departing in an instant and returning in an instant, one visits every buddha-field throughout the ten directions, pays respect before every buddha, and meets the great beings of every heaven. One ceaselessly hears the true Dharma and receives the prophecy of great awakening, even entering all the mote-dust-like lands, performing the deeds of the buddhas and cultivating the vast practices of a bodhisattva.

> Day after day, in the three periods,
> making offerings to the buddhas of the ten directions.
> Therefore I bow my head in reverence.

### The Tenth Joy: Advancing Along the Path to Buddhahood

In this sahā world, it is extremely difficult to practice the path and attain its fruit. Why? Those who experience suffering are always anxious. Those who experience pleasure are always attached. Whether one speaks of suffering or pleasure, both are far from liberation. Whether one rises or sinks, it is nothing other than the cycle of rebirth.

Even when someone does arouse the aspiration and undertake practice, it is still difficult to bring it to fruition. Afflictions press from within. Evil conditions pull from without. Some lapse into the aspiration of the two vehicles. Some fall back into the three evil paths. It is like the moon reflected in water, easily scattered by the waves. It is like soldiers at the front who turn back when they face the blade. Fish spawn rarely survive. The fruit of the amra tree seldom ripens. Śāriputra and others who regressed after sixty kalpas are examples of this.

Only the Tathāgata Śākyamuni, through immeasurable kalpas of arduous and painful practice, accumulated merit upon merit, seeking the bodhisattva path without ever ceasing. Looking throughout the great trichiliocosm, there is not so much as a mustard seed of space that is not a place where the Bodhisattva sacrificed his body and life. It was for the sake of sentient beings that he finally accomplished the path of awakening. For all other beings, this is beyond their own capacity. Like a baby elephant whose strength is small, the body is lost to sword and arrow.

Therefore the bodhisattva Nāgārjuna has said:

> It is like forty miles of ice. Suppose a person pours a single measure of hot water upon it. At that moment, it seems as though the ice diminishes. But by the time night has passed and dawn arrives, the ice has risen even higher than before. An ordinary being who arouses the aspiration in this world to save beings from suffering is just the same. Because the conditions that fuel craving and aversion, both favorable and unfavorable, are so many, one gives rise to one's own afflictions and falls back instead.

Yet in that land there is no regression; one advances ever onward toward the path of buddhahood.

First, because the power of the Buddha's compassionate vows constantly sustains and upholds one. Second, because the Buddha's light constantly shines, increasing the aspiration for awakening. Third, because the sounds of the water, birds, trees, groves, and wind-bells constantly cause one to give rise to mindfulness of the Buddha, mindfulness of the Dharma, and mindfulness of the Saṅgha. Fourth, because one has only bodhisattvas as good companions; there are no evil conditions without, and the deep afflictions within are subdued. Fifth, because one's lifespan equals the Buddha's, lasting for measureless kalpas, and one cultivates the path of buddhahood without the interruption of birth and death.

A verse of the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra* says:

> If there are sentient beings who behold the Buddha even once,
> their karmic obstructions are surely and entirely purified.

If even a single encounter is thus, how much more so to be in the Buddha's presence at all times!

By these causes and conditions, the beings of that land, regarding all the myriad things, are free from any thought of "I" or "mine." In all their goings and comings, their movements and their rest, the mind clings to nothing. Toward all sentient beings they give rise to the mind of great compassion. Naturally and progressively they awaken to the patience of non-arising. In the end they are certain to reach the stage of one-lifetime succession to buddhahood, and swiftly they realize the unsurpassed awakening.

For the sake of sentient beings they manifest the eight aspects of a buddha's life. According to their affinities, they dwell in magnificently pure lands, turn the wondrous Dharma-wheel, and liberate all sentient beings. They cause all sentient beings to aspire to their land, just as I today aspire to the Land of Utmost Bliss. They also go throughout the ten directions, guiding and receiving sentient beings, just as Amitābha Buddha does through his great compassionate original vows.

Is such benefit not a source of great joy?

One lifetime of diligent practice is but a moment. Why not set aside all other pursuits and seek the Pure Land? I beseech all practitioners: strive on, without slackening!

> In various destinies, evil is known,
> but birth without regression, up to awakening:
> therefore I bow my head to Amitābha Buddha.
>
> I have spoken of the meritorious deeds of that Honored One.
> Wholesome virtues, boundless as the waters of the sea.
> The pure roots of goodness thus obtained:
> may we, together with all sentient beings, be born in that land.
>
> May we, together with all sentient beings,
> be born in the Land of Peace and Bliss.

---

## Gate Three: Evidence for the Land of Utmost Bliss

This has two parts: 1) in comparison with the ten directions, and 2) in comparison with Tuṣita.

### Part One: In Comparison with the Ten Directions

**Question:** There are pure lands in all ten directions. Why should one aspire to be born exclusively in the Land of Utmost Bliss?

**Answer:** The Great Master Tiantai says:

> Throughout the sūtras and treatises, everywhere the Buddha urges sentient beings to recollect Amitābha Buddha single-mindedly and to seek the Western Land of Utmost Bliss.

The *Sūtra of Immeasurable Life*, the *Contemplation Sūtra*, the *Treatise on Birth in the Pure Land*, and more than ten other sūtras and treatises attest to this. He carefully read through the entire canon fifteen times. One should know that what he states cannot be doubted.

Master Jiācái, in his three-fascicle *Treatise on the Pure Land*, cites twelve sūtras and seven treatises: 1) the *Sūtra of Immeasurable Life*; 2) the *Contemplation Sūtra*; 3) the *Shorter Amitābha Sūtra*; 4) the *Sūtra of the Sound of the Drum*; 5) the *Sūtra Praising the Merits of All Buddhas*; 6) the *Sūtra of Awakening the Pure Mind*; 7) the *Great Collection Sūtra*; 8) the *Sūtra of Birth in the Ten Directions*; 9) the *Medicine Master Sūtra*; 10) the *Pratyutpanna Samādhi Sūtra*; 11) the *Larger Amitābha Sūtra*; 12) the *Treatise on Immeasurable Purity and Impartial Faith*. Among the treatises: 3) the *Daśabhūmika-vibhāṣā*; 4) the Amitābha verses found throughout the canon; 5) the *Ratnagotravibhāga*; 6) Nāgārjuna's Twelve Verses of Homage; and 7) the *Mahāyānasaṃgraha*.

Furthermore, the Samantabhadra Vows of the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra*, the *Sūtra of the Names of the Three Thousand Buddhas*, the *Sūtra of the Letterless Jewel Casket*, the *Thousand-Armed Dhāraṇī Sūtra*, the *Eleven-Faced Sūtra*, the *Amoghapāśa*, the *Cintāmaṇicakra*, the *Zuiqiu*, the *Uṣṇīṣavijayā*, the *Vimalaprabha*, the *Suvarṇaprabhāsa*, the *Amitābha Dhāraṇī*, and others throughout the exoteric and esoteric teachings all urge birth in the Land of Utmost Bliss beyond count. Therefore one should aspire to it above all.

**Question:** The Buddha says: "The pure lands of all buddhas are in truth without distinction." Why then does the Tathāgata particularly praise the West?

**Answer:** The *Sūtra of Birth According to One's Vow* records the Buddha resolving this very doubt:

> In the sahā world, people are mostly greedy and defiled; those with faith are few, and those who follow deviant paths are many. They do not believe the true Dharma, they cannot fix their minds single-pointedly, and their thoughts are scattered and aimless. In reality there is no distinction. But in order that sentient beings may fix their minds on a single place, therefore I praise that land. All who are born there, each in accord with their vows, will without exception attain the fruit.

And the *Sūtra of Contemplation of the Mind-Ground* says:

> All children of the Buddha should single-mindedly seek to see one buddha and one buddha-land.

**Question:** If the purpose is to focus the mind, why among all the pure lands is the Land of Utmost Bliss singled out and recommended?

**Answer:** Even if one were to urge some other pure land, the same objection could not be avoided. The Buddha's intention is difficult to fathom; one can only look up to it in faith. It is like a fool who has fallen into a fiery pit and cannot climb out on his own. A wise friend rescues him by a single expedient means. Having gained that strength, the fool should hasten to escape. Why would he idly debate other methods and stratagems? The practitioner should be the same: do not give rise to other thoughts.

As the *Sūtra on the Questions of Maudgalyāyana* says:

> It is like the myriad streams flowing ceaselessly, bearing floating grasses and driftwood: those in front do not look back, those behind do not look ahead, yet all converge in the great ocean. The world is just the same. Though there be the mighty and the noble, the wealthy and the free, none can escape birth, old age, sickness, and death. Only because they do not believe the Buddha's sūtras, in their future lives they become human again and suffer even more acutely, unable to attain birth in the lands of the thousand buddhas. Therefore I say: the land of the Buddha of Immeasurable Life is easy to reach and easy to attain, yet people do not cultivate the practice for birth there. [p.0047] Instead they serve the ninety-five kinds of deviant paths. I say such people are without eyes, without ears.

One should simply look up to this in faith. Moreover, it is not the case that there is no karmic connection; why forcefully reject it? As the *Ten Doubts* of Tiantai states:

> Amitābha Buddha possesses the special great compassion of the Forty-eight Vows, which draw in and guide sentient beings. Moreover, that Buddha's light illumines the entire dharma realm, embracing and never forsaking those sentient beings who practice buddha-recollection. In each of the ten directions, buddhas as numerous as the sands of the Ganges extend their tongues to cover the three-thousandfold world, attesting that all sentient beings who recollect Amitābha Buddha will, by the power of that Buddha's great compassionate original vows, certainly attain birth in the Land of Utmost Bliss.

And the *Sūtra of Immeasurable Life* says:

> When the Dharma finally perishes at the end of the age, this sūtra alone will be specially retained for one hundred years in the world, to draw in sentient beings and cause them to be born in that land.

Thus we know: Amitābha Buddha has a special affinity with this sahā world. In the future, when the ten thousand years are ended and all the other sūtras have perished, the single teaching of Amitābha will benefit beings all the more. The Great Sage has specially retained it for one hundred years beyond the end.

When the Dharma age has been fulfilled for ten thousand years and all the sūtras have vanished, the *Pratyutpanna Samādhi Sūtra* records that the Bodhisattva Bhadrapāla asked Śākyamuni Buddha:

> How can sentient beings of the future come to see the buddhas of the ten directions?

The Buddha instructed him to recollect Amitābha Buddha, and thereby one sees all the buddhas of the ten directions. Because this buddha has a special affinity with the beings of the sahā world, one should first recollect this buddha single-mindedly and with devotion, practicing the bodhisattva path. After being reborn in that land, driven by the force of past connections, how could there not be a corresponding response?

### Part Two: In Comparison with Tuṣita

**Question:** The Tripiṭaka master Xuánzàng says: "In India, monastics and laity alike all perform the practice of Maitreya, because Tuṣita belongs to the desire realm and the practice is therefore easy to accomplish. Masters of both the Greater and Lesser Vehicles all sanction this method. The Pure Land of Amitābha, one fears, is too exalted for ordinary, defiled beings, and the practice is difficult to achieve. According to the older sūtras and treatises, only bodhisattvas of the seventh stage and above can, to the degree of their attainment, see the reward-body buddha's pure land. According to the newer treatises, a bodhisattva must reach the third stage before being able to see the reward-body buddha." Why then do you now urge the Land of Utmost Bliss?

**Answer:** China and India may differ in locale, but the principles of the exoteric and esoteric teachings are the same. As the evidence cited above is already extensive, how can one turn one's back on the clear words of the Buddha's teaching and follow hearsay from India? Moreover, in the infirmary of the Jetavana monastery, patients were made to face west and contemplate being born in a buddha's pure land, as detailed below in the section on deathbed practices. Clearly, the Buddha's intention particularly urges the Land of Utmost Bliss. How could the customs of India contradict this?

Furthermore, Master Huáigǎn's *Treatise Resolving Doubts* establishes twelve points of superiority and inferiority between the Land of Utmost Bliss and Tuṣita: 1) the host of the teaching: the distinction between a buddha and a bodhisattva; 2) the distinction between a pure land and a defiled land; 3) the presence or absence of women; 4) longevity: limited or unlimited lifespans; 7) the presence or absence of excellent physical marks; 8) the presence or absence of the five supernatural powers; 9) whether unwholesome thoughts arise or not; 10) the degree to which sins are extinguished, namely, that reciting the name of Maitreya removes the sins of twelve hundred kalpas, while reciting the name of Amitābha extinguishes the sins of eighty koṭis of kalpas; 11) the presence or absence of suffering; 12) the manner of birth, namely, that in Tuṣita one is born on the knees or in the embrace of celestial men and women, while in the Western Land one is born within a lotus flower or in a jeweled palace.

Though the relative merits and demerits of the two places are as stated, there are also ten points of similarity and eight points of difference. The first eight points of difference do not go beyond what Master Huáigǎn established, so I do not copy them again here. The ninth point is: in the Western Land the Buddha comes to welcome the practitioner; in Tuṣita this is not so. Master Huáigǎn says welcoming is the same in both, but I hold they differ. The tenth point: sūtras and treatises earnestly and copiously urge the Land of Utmost Bliss, but do not do so for Tuṣita to the same degree. There are ten points of similarity and eight points of difference.

The eight points of difference are as follows. 1) The original vows differ: Amitābha has vows to draw in and embrace beings; Maitreya has no such vows. Without a vow it is like swimming across the water on one's own; with a vow it is like crossing the water aboard a boat. 2) The light differs: Amitābha Buddha's light illumines all beings who practice buddha-recollection, embracing and never forsaking them; Maitreya does not do so. With light it is like traveling in broad daylight; without light it is like coming and going in darkness. 3) The guarding and protection differs: countless transformation-body buddhas, Avalokiteśvara, and Mahāsthāmaprāpta constantly attend the practitioner. Moreover, the *Sūtra in Praise of the Pure Land* says that the buddhas of the ten directions, as numerous as the sands of ten Ganges rivers, embrace and protect the practitioner. And the *Sūtra of the Ten Kinds of Birth* says the Buddha dispatches twenty-five bodhisattvas to guard the practitioner constantly. In Tuṣita this is not so. With protection it is like traveling in a large company without fear of bandits; without protection it is like journeying alone through perilous passes, certain to be set upon by brigands. 4) The extending of tongues differs: the buddhas of the ten directions extend their tongues in attestation; for Tuṣita this is not so. 5) The assembly of saints differs: the Bodhisattva Huājù and the Bodhisattva Shānhǎihuì have made the great vow that if even one being aspiring to the Western Land has not yet fully attained birth, they themselves will not first depart to attain perfect awakening. They too will attain birth in the Western Land. For Tuṣita this is not so. 8) The teaching differs: the *Sūtra of Immeasurable Life* says: "He cuts across the five evil paths; the evil paths close of themselves. The way of ascent is without limit. It is easy to go, yet no one does." For Tuṣita this is not so.

Even with ten points of similarity one still cannot call it difficult to be born there. How much less so when there are eight points of difference? And yet some say it is difficult to reach? I respectfully ask all scholars: examine the principle and the teaching, and judge the difficulty and ease of both. The two traditions must not be left unreconciled.

**Answer:** The practices of India are obscure and difficult to resolve definitively. Let me tentatively concede the general point while not conceding the particular. Over there, because both Greater and Lesser Vehicle practitioners agree on Tuṣita, they all speak of it. But east of the flowing sands, the Greater Vehicle has flourished greatly; one cannot simply follow the mixed practices of India. Moreover, the various teachings did not necessarily all flourish at the same time. Among them, the teaching of buddha-recollection especially benefits those of the degenerate age. It is a plan devised for the turbid and evil beings after the extinction of the Dharma. Perhaps at that time in India it had not yet come into prominence. If this were not so, how could Kuījī, Xuánzàng's foremost disciple, have separately composed the *Essentials of the Western Land* and established ten points of superiority and inferiority, urging both himself and others?

**Question:** The *Sūtra of Contemplation of the Mind-Ground* says: "My present disciples I entrust to Maitreya; at the Dragon-Flower Assembly they shall attain liberation." Is this not the Tathāgata's encouragement to aspire to Tuṣita?

**Answer:** This too presents no contradiction. Who would obstruct aspiration to Tuṣita? The *Mind-Ground Sūtra* and two or three others do speak thus. Yet they do not compare with the texts on the Land of Utmost Bliss, which in exoteric and esoteric sources number over a thousand.

Furthermore, the third fascicle of the *Mahākaruṇā Sūtra* says:

> In the future, when the Dharma is about to perish, there will be monks and nuns who, having gone forth in my teaching, will walk about hand-in-hand with children, going from one tavern to the next, practicing within my Dharma while calling themselves śramaṇas, having the appearance of śramaṇas. And there will be those who wear the kāṣāya robe. Yet in this Fortunate Kalpa, beginning with Maitreya and ending with the Buddha Rucika, all of them shall enter parinirvāṇa with nothing remaining. Why? Because among all such śramaṇas, even one who utters a single name of a buddha, even one who gives rise to a single moment of faith, is included.

Therefore that sūtra speaks of the Dragon-Flower Assembly; it does not say Tuṣita.

Now I consider: from the passing of Śākyamuni to the appearance of Maitreya, 5,670,000,000 years will elapse. One evening one is reborn on a lotus pedestal, yet one would choose to linger through the long wandering of birth and death until the Dragon-Flower Assembly? How much more so if one attains birth in the Land of Utmost Bliss, where day and night at will one may go and come to Maitreya's palace in Tuṣita and, at the Dragon-Flower Assembly, take one's place in the front rank of the audience. It would be like a person of wealth and honor returning to their native village. Who would not delight in this?

If one has a particular karmic affinity, then other realms are also fine. In general, one should follow one's own inclination; do not give rise to sectarian attachments. Therefore Master Huáigǎn says: "Those who aspire to Tuṣita should not disparage practitioners of the Western Land; those who aspire to the Western Land should not disparage the practice of Tuṣita. Each should follow their own disposition and cultivate as they wish. Do not quarrel over this, for such quarreling will not only fail to secure birth in the superior place, but will also..." [The quotation breaks off here in the source text.]

---

## Gate Four: The Correct Practice of Buddha-Recollection

The fourth major topic, the correct practice of buddha-recollection, also comprises five divisions. As Vasubandhu Bodhisattva states in the *Treatise on Rebirth in the Pure Land*:

> By cultivating and completing the practice of the five gates of mindfulness, one will assuredly be born in the Land of Peace and Bliss and see Amitābha Buddha.

These are: (1) the gate of prostration, (2) the gate of praise, (3) the gate of vow-making, (4) the gate of contemplation, and (5) the gate of merit-dedication. The gates of vow-making and merit-dedication should be applied in common to all practices.

---

### 1. The Gate of Prostration

The first, the gate of prostration, is the bodily karma among the three kinds of karma brought into accord [with awakening]. With single-minded devotion, one prostrates with all five limbs touching the ground, venerating Amitābha Buddha in the west from afar. The number of prostrations is not fixed; one should simply employ sincerity of heart.

Alternatively, one may recollect the passage from the *Sūtra on the Samādhi of Contemplating the Buddha*:

> When I now venerate one buddha, I venerate all buddhas. If one contemplates a single buddha, one beholds all buddhas. Before each and every buddha there is a practitioner who touches the feet in prostration. All veneration is directed toward the nature of emptiness and quiescence. One's own body and others' bodies are in essence nondual. May we, together with all sentient beings, embody and comprehend the Way. May we arouse the supreme aspiration and return to the ultimate reality.

Or one may follow the six kinds of merit described in the *Sūtra on Contemplation of the Mind-Ground*: (1) the unsurpassed great field of merit, (2) the unsurpassed great benefactor, (3) the most honored among those with no feet, two feet, or many feet, (4) one supremely difficult to encounter, like the udumbara flower, (5) one who appears alone in the three-thousand great-thousand world-system, and (6) one whose merit, both mundane and supramundane, is perfectly complete, the basis of all meaning. One should add words corresponding to each of these six as the method of prostration:

First, one should recollect: "With a single utterance of 'Homage to the Buddha,' all have already accomplished the buddha way. Therefore I take refuge and bow before the unsurpassed field of merit."

Second: "With compassionate eyes he regards all sentient beings equally, as if each were his only child. Therefore I take refuge and bow before the supremely great compassionate mother."

Third: "The great bodhisattvas of the ten directions all revere Amitābha. Therefore I take refuge and bow before the unsurpassed honored one among two-footed beings."

Fourth: "Once one hears the Buddha's name, this surpasses even the udumbara flower. Therefore I take refuge and bow before the one supremely difficult to encounter."

Fifth: "In a hundred koṭis of world-systems, two honored ones do not appear simultaneously. Therefore I take refuge and bow before the rare and great Dharma King."

Sixth: "The ocean of merit of the Buddha, the Dharma, and the saṅgha is one body throughout the three times. Therefore I take refuge and bow before the honored one of perfectly interpenetrating myriad virtues."

For those who delight in extensive practice, one should follow the twelve prostrations of Nāgārjuna Bodhisattva. There is also Master Shandao's six-period prostration liturgy, which cannot be set out in full here. Even if one has no other practice and relies solely on prostration, one can still attain birth in the Pure Land. As the *Sūtra of the Names of Ākāśagarbha Bodhisattva and Buddhas* states:

> If one venerates Amitābha Buddha with utmost sincerity, one will be delivered from the three evil destinies and afterward be reborn there.

---

### 2. The Gate of Praise

The second, the gate of praise, is the verbal karma among the three kinds of karma brought into accord [with awakening]. As the third fascicle of the *Daśabhūmika-vibhāṣā* states:

> The original vow of Amitābha Buddha is as follows: "If any person is mindful of me, calls my name, and takes refuge in me, that person immediately enters the stage of certainty and will attain anuttarā samyaksaṃbodhi." Therefore one should always be mindful [of the Buddha] and praise him with verses.

The verses are:

> O Wisdom of Immeasurable Light,
> whose body is like a mountain of true gold,
> I now with body, speech, and mind
> join my palms and bow my head in veneration.
>
> The buddhas present in the ten directions,
> by various causes and conditions,
> extol the virtues of that Buddha.
> I now take refuge and bow at his feet.
>
> The Buddha's feet bear the thousand-spoked wheel;
> soft and of lotus hue,
> all who behold them are filled with joy.
> I bow my head at the Buddha's feet.
>
> The white ūrṇā light between his brows
> is like the pure and luminous moon;
> it enhances the radiance of his face.
> I bow my head at the Buddha's feet.
>
> What that Buddha has spoken
> eradicates the roots of all transgressions;
> his beautiful words bring abundant benefit.
> I now bow my head in veneration.
>
> All the noble saṅgha of worthies and sages,
> together with the hosts of gods and humans,
> all join in taking refuge.
> Therefore I too pay homage.
>
> Boarding the ship of the Eightfold Path,
> he can cross the sea so difficult to cross;
> having ferried himself, he ferries others.
> I pay homage to the Sovereign One.
>
> All the buddhas through immeasurable kalpas
> have praised and extolled his virtues,
> yet still cannot exhaust them.
> I take refuge in the Pure One.
>
> I now do likewise,
> praising his immeasurable virtues.
> By the merit of this cause and condition,
> may the Buddha always be mindful of me.
>
> By the merit of this cause and condition
> and the supreme, wondrous virtues I have gained,
> may all classes of sentient beings
> likewise fully attain them.

The treatise contains thirty-two verses in all; here I have excerpted only the essentials. The full text is in a separate compilation. In addition, one may use the verse from the *Treatise on Rebirth*, the praise of the Buddha from the esoteric teachings, or the separate praise of Amitābha. Whether one recites a single time or many times, performs a single practice or many, one should simply be utterly sincere; the number is not fixed. Even if one has no other practice and relies solely on praise, one should, in accordance with one's aspiration, assuredly attain birth in the Pure Land. As the verse in the *Lotus Sūtra* says:

> Or with a joyful heart,
> singing and chanting the virtues of the Buddha,
> even with a single small sound,
> all have already accomplished the buddha way.

If even a single sound is like this, how much more so constant praise! If even the fruit of buddhahood is attained thereby, how much more so birth in the Pure Land! The benefits of praising the Buddha through mantras are especially profound and cannot be openly disclosed here.

---

### 3. The Gate of Vow-Making

The third, the gate of vow-making: the remaining three gates constitute the mental karma among the three kinds of karma brought into accord [with awakening].

Master Daochuo (道綽) states in the *Anle ji* (安樂集, *Collection on the Land of Peace and Bliss*):

> The *Larger Sūtra* says: Whoever wishes to be born in the Pure Land must make the arousal of the aspiration for awakening (bodhicitta) the source. What is meant by "bodhi"? It is the name for the unsurpassed buddha way. If one wishes to arouse the aspiration to become a buddha, this aspiration is vast, encompassing the entire dharma-realm. This aspiration is far-reaching, extending to the end of the future. This aspiration is all-embracing, free from the obstructions of the Two Vehicles. If one can arouse this aspiration even once, it overturns the wheel of existence that has turned since beginningless time.

The *Treatise on the Pure Land* states:

> To arouse the aspiration for awakening means precisely to aspire to become a buddha. The aspiration to become a buddha is the aspiration to liberate sentient beings. The aspiration to liberate sentient beings is the aspiration to gather in all sentient beings and bring them to birth in a buddha land.

Since the aspiration for awakening is the guiding principle of Pure Land bodhi, I shall briefly employ three divisions to distinguish its meaning. Practitioners should not be put off by the detail. The three are: (1) clarifying the character of the bodhicitta, (2) clarifying its benefits, and (3) critical distinctions.

#### (1) The Character of the Bodhicitta

In general terms, it is called the aspiration to become a buddha, also known as the aspiration to seek awakening above and transform sentient beings below. In specific terms, it is the four universal vows. These are of two kinds: (a) the four vows directed toward phenomena, which correspond to compassion toward sentient beings or compassion in accordance with dharmas; and (b) the four vows directed toward principle, which correspond to unconditioned compassion.

**The Four Vows Directed toward Phenomena:**

*First:* "Sentient beings are numberless; I vow to liberate them all." One should be mindful that all sentient beings possess the buddha-nature, and [vow]: "I shall cause them all to enter nirvāṇa without remainder." This aspiration is the precept of benefiting sentient beings. It is also called the mind of gratitude-virtue. It is also the contributory cause of buddha-nature and the cause of the nirmāṇakāya bodhi.

*Second:* "Afflictions are inexhaustible; I vow to sever them all." This is the precept of gathering in wholesome discipline. It is also the mind of severance-virtue, the direct cause of buddha-nature, and the cause of the dharmakāya bodhi.

*Third:* "Dharma gates are boundless; I vow to know them all." This is the precept of gathering in wholesome dharmas. It is also the mind of wisdom-virtue, the elucidating cause of buddha-nature, and the cause of the saṃbhogakāya bodhi.

*Fourth:* "The unsurpassed bodhi; I vow to realize it." This is the aspiration to seek the fruit of buddhahood. That is, by fully fulfilling the preceding three vows and practices, one realizes the complete bodhi of the three bodies, and thereupon broadly liberates all sentient beings once more.

**The Four Vows Directed toward Principle:**

All dharmas are primordially quiescent, neither existent nor nonexistent, neither permanent nor annihilated, unborn and unceasing, neither defiled nor pure. Each single form, each single fragrance, is none other than the Middle Way. Birth-and-death is nirvāṇa. Affliction is bodhi. When each and every gate of defilement is reversed, it becomes eighty-four thousand pāramitās. Ignorance transforms into illumination, as when ice melts into water: it is not something distant, nor does it come from elsewhere. A single thought-moment of mind universally contains all of it, like a wish-fulfilling jewel that is neither possessed of treasure nor devoid of treasure. To say it is without [treasure] is a lie; to say it has [treasure] is a wrong view. It cannot be known by the mind; it cannot be distinguished by words. Sentient beings, within this inconceivable, unfettered dharma, engage in conceptual thinking and create their own fetters. Within the dharma of no-liberation, they seek liberation. Therefore, universally toward all sentient beings throughout the dharma-realm, one gives rise to great compassion and undertakes the four universal vows. This is called "according with principle."

[Further,] to know that all dharmas are non-dharma, and to know that all sentient beings are non-sentient-beings, is what is called a bodhisattva arousing the unsurpassed aspiration for awakening. The *Sūtra on Adorning the Bodhicitta* also states:

> The bodhicitta is neither existent nor fabricated, apart from words and letters. Bodhi is precisely the mind, and the mind is precisely sentient beings. If one can understand in this way, it is called "cultivating bodhi through bodhi." Bodhi is neither past, nor future, nor present. The mind and sentient beings are likewise neither past, nor future, nor present. To be able to understand in this way is called being a bodhisattva. Yet within this there is truly nothing to be attained. It is because there is nothing to be attained that one attains. If one has nothing to attain with regard to all dharmas, this is called attaining bodhi.

For the sake of sentient beings who are just beginning to practice, [the Buddha] fashioned [the teaching of] the mind, yet there is no bodhi and no one who fashions bodhi. Each of these also has two meanings. One interpretation says: the first two vows remove the suffering of the two truths (suffering and its accumulation) from sentient beings, and the latter two vows bestow the bliss of the two truths (the path and cessation) upon sentient beings. A second interpretation says: the first vow pertains to others, and the latter three to oneself. That is, removing the suffering of the two truths from sentient beings and bestowing the bliss of the two truths upon them are all contained within the first vow; in order to bring this vow to ultimate and complete fulfillment, one further arouses the latter three vows for oneself. As the *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Sūtra* says:

> For the benefit of sentient beings one seeks great bodhi; therefore one is called a bodhisattva. Yet one does not cling to it; therefore one is called a mahāsattva.

After [reciting] the four universal vows, one should say: "May the dharma-realm of self and other equally benefit; may we together be born in the Land of Utmost Bliss and accomplish the buddha way." In one's mind one should be mindful: "May I, together with all sentient beings, be born in the Land of Utmost Bliss and bring to ultimate, perfect fulfillment the four universal vows above." If one has a specific personal vow, one should recite it before the four vows.

If the mind is impure, it is not a proper cause of the Way. If the mind is limited, it is not the great bodhi. If there is no utmost sincerity, its power is not strong. Therefore one must cultivate a mind that is pure, profound, vast, and sincere. One should not do this for the sake of surpassing others or gaining fame and profit. Rather, toward all sentient beings throughout the inexhaustible dharma-realm illuminated by the Buddha's eye, and toward all afflictions, all dharma gates, and all buddha-virtues, one arouses these four kinds of vow-practice.

**Question:** In what dharma should one seek the unsurpassed Way?

**Answer:** Here there are two kinds of difference, according to sharp and dull faculties. As the *Mahāprajñāpāramitopadeśa* states:

> Just as yellow stone contains the nature of gold and white stone the nature of silver, so too in all worldly dharmas there exists the nature of nirvāṇa. The buddhas, noble sages, and worthies, by means of the skillful means of wisdom, moral discipline, and meditative concentration, guide beings to attain this dharma-nature of nirvāṇa. Those of sharp faculties directly know that all dharmas are the dharma-nature, like a person of supernatural powers who can transform tiles and stones entirely into gold. Those of dull faculties must seek the dharma-nature through discriminating expedient means, like a great smelting furnace [that refines ore].

Enduring the suffering of contemplative meditation to attain the Way is the teaching of the śrāvakas. Contemplating the marks of all dharmas as neither bound nor liberated, so that the mind attains purity, is the teaching of the bodhisattvas.

As in the original circumstances of Mañjuśrī, the *Sūtra of No-Practice* quotes the verses of Bodhisattva Xigen (Joyful-Root):

> Desire itself is the Way;
> anger and delusion are likewise.
> Within these three things
> are the immeasurable paths of the buddhas.
>
> If someone discriminates
> desire, anger, delusion, and the Way,
> that person is far from the buddha way,
> as [far as the sky from the earth].
>
> All dharmas cannot be apprehended;
> this is called the buddha way,
> which is the true nature of all dharmas.

> Within all these dharmas
> there is the nature of peace and bliss.
> We beseech the great World-Honored One
> to explain this for us in detail.

And the *Prajñāpāramitā Sūtra* states:

> All existence possesses the nature [of the Tathāgata]; it is entirely tathāgatagarbha, because the self-nature of Samantabhadra Bodhisattva pervades [all things].

The *Dharmapada Sūtra* says:

> The buddhas abide in desire and aversion
> and yet dwell in the place of awakening.
> The defilements are the seed of buddhahood,
> primordially without any movement.
>
> The five hindrances and the five desires
> are the nature of the buddhas.
> They always adorn [the path] with these;
> primordially without any movement.
>
> All dharmas from the beginning
> are neither right nor wrong.
> Right and wrong are [the nature of...

**Question:** If bodhi and affliction are one in essence, should one simply follow one's inclinations and give rise to deluded karma?

**Answer:** Those who produce such an understanding are called those who "wrongly grasp emptiness" and are decidedly not disciples of the Buddha. Let me pose a counter-question: If you would, on the grounds that affliction is bodhi, happily give rise to affliction and evil karma, then you should also, on the grounds that birth-and-death is nirvāṇa, happily endure the fierce sufferings of birth-and-death. Why is it that you find even a moment's suffering in its karmic consequences unbearable and wish to avoid it, yet for the cause of suffering lasting through endless kalpas, you happily indulge without restraint?

Therefore one should know: affliction and bodhi, though one in essence, differ in their function according to the time, so that defilement and purity are not the same, just as water and ice [are the same substance but differ in state], and just as a seed and its fruit [are one in essence but differ according to time and function]. For this reason, those who cultivate the Way reveal the buddha-nature originally inherent within them; those who do not cultivate the Way will never have any principle revealed.

As the thirty-second fascicle of the *Nirvāṇa Sūtra* states:

> Good man, if someone asks whether within a seed there is fruit or no fruit, one should definitively answer: both existent and nonexistent. Why? Apart from the seed, one cannot produce the fruit; therefore it is called "existent." The seed has not yet sprouted; therefore it is called "nonexistent."
