For this reason it is both existent and nonexistent. Why? The time differs, but the essence is one. The buddha-nature of sentient beings is likewise. If one says that within sentient beings there is a separate buddha-nature, this is not so. Why? Sentient beings are the buddha-nature; the buddha-nature is sentient beings. It is only because the time differs that there is purity and impurity.

**Question:** [Lacking the capacity for advanced practice,] should one still arouse the universal vows?

**Answer:** Even if one cannot sustain diligent cultivation, one should still arouse the vow of compassion. Its benefit is immeasurable, as explained earlier and later. Devadatta recited sixty thousand collections of scripture yet still did not escape the Naraka hells. The youth Citradhvaja aroused a single thought of compassionate vowing and was suddenly reborn in the Tuṣita heaven. From this we know that the difference between ascending and descending lies in the mind, not in outward practice.

Moreover, who among us in the course of a single lifetime has not once called out "Homage to the Buddha" or once given food to a sentient being? One must gather even such minute good roots into the practice of the four universal vows. When practice and aspiration correspond, the vow is not made in vain.

As the first fascicle of the *Upāsakaśīla Sūtra* states:

> If a person cannot single-mindedly contemplate the faults and perils of birth-and-death and the peace and bliss of nirvāṇa, then even though such a person may practice wisdom, generosity, moral discipline, and extensive learning, in the end that person cannot attain the dharma of the portion of liberation. But if one can be weary of the faults and perils of birth-and-death and deeply perceive the merit, virtue, peace, and bliss of nirvāṇa, then even with meager giving, minor precepts, and minor [learning], there is not a single good deed that passes to waste.

As the *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Sūtra* says:

> If bodhisattvas practice the profound prajñāpāramitā with skillful means, then not a single moment of mind is used without purpose.

**Question:** How should one apply the mind [to the four vows in daily practice]?

**Answer:** As the ninety-third fascicle of the *Ratnakūṭa Sūtra* states:

> When food is needed, give food, in order to perfect the power of omniscience. When drink is needed, give drink, in order to sever the power of the thirst of craving. When clothing is needed, give clothing, in order to attain the unsurpassed garment of shame and conscience. Give a place to sit, in order to sit beneath the bodhi tree. Give light, in order to attain the brightness of the buddha-eye. Give paper and ink, in order to attain great wisdom. Give medicine, in order to remove the disease of the bonds and latent tendencies of sentient beings. And so on, even if one has no personal wealth, one should practice the giving of mental intention: "I wish to reveal and teach the unsurpassed Way to the immeasurable, boundless sentient beings. May I gradually accomplish in this way the first vow-practice, perfect the pāramitā of giving, swiftly realize bodhi, and broadly liberate sentient beings."

When one speaks a single kind word, performs a single beneficial act, or shares in a single wholesome deed, one should apply the mind in the same way. When one momentarily subdues a single thought of evil, one should think: "May I gradually accomplish in this way the second vow-practice, sever all deluded karma, swiftly realize bodhi, and broadly liberate sentient beings." When one reads, recites, or studies even a single passage or a single meaning, one should think: "May I gradually accomplish in this way the third vow-practice, learn all the dharmas of the buddhas, swiftly realize bodhi, and broadly liberate sentient beings."

In all encounters, one should constantly apply the mind thus: "From this present body onward, I shall gradually cultivate and study, until I am born in the Land of Utmost Bliss, where I shall freely study the buddha way, swiftly realize bodhi, and ultimately benefit all sentient beings."

If one constantly harbors this thought and cultivates in accordance with one's capacity, then just as drops of water, though small, gradually fill a great vessel, so too this mind can hold the myriad good deeds, great and small, without letting them leak away, and will assuredly reach bodhi. As the chapter on entering the dharma-realm in the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra* says:

> Just as diamond can support the great earth and not let it fall, so too the mind of bodhi can sustain all the vows and practices of the bodhisattva and not let them be lost.

**Question:** Will the good roots [cultivated before arousing the vow] then be wasted?

**Answer:** If with a sincere mind one speaks with mouth and thinks in mind, "From this day forward, every good deed, however small, I dedicate not to the reward of my own conditioned existence, but entirely to the Land of Utmost Bliss, entirely to bodhi," then after arousing this aspiration, all one's good deeds, whether one is aware of them or not, will naturally flow toward unsurpassed bodhi. It is like digging a single channel: all the waters naturally flow into it, pass on to the rivers, and finally reach the great ocean. The practitioner is the same: once the aspiration is aroused, the waters of all good roots naturally flow into the channel of the four universal vows, pass on to birth in the Land of Utmost Bliss, and finally reach the ocean of bodhi, the sarvajña-sea. How much more so if one is constantly mindful of the previous vow! The details are as set forth below in the gate of merit-dedication.

**Question:** Ordinary beings lack the strength [for difficult giving]. Some are impoverished. By what expedient means can the mind be brought into accord with principle?

**Answer:** The *Ratnakūṭa Sūtra* states:

> If one has not the strength for such giving and cannot let go of possessions, the bodhisattva should think: "I shall now exert vigorous effort and gradually, step by step, remove the stain of stinginess, miserliness, and clinging."

A sūtra verse says:

> If there are those who are poor
> and have no wealth to give,
> when they see others practicing giving,
> let them give rise to a mind of sympathetic joy.
> The merit of sympathetic joy
> is equal to that of giving itself.

The practitioner should apply the mind in this way.

**Question:** Can the bodhicitta directed toward principle also be the basis for trusting in cause and effect and diligently cultivating the practice of the Way?

**Answer:** In principle this is necessarily so. As the *Vimalakīrti Sūtra* states:

> Though one contemplates all buddha lands and all sentient beings as empty, one constantly cultivates the Pure Land and teaches and transforms all living beings.

A verse in the *Mūlamadhyamakakārikā* says:

> Though empty, it is not annihilated;
> though existent, it is not permanent.
> The fruit of karma is never lost:
> this is what the Buddha has taught.

And the *Mahāprajñāpāramitopadeśa* says:

> If all dharmas are empty, then there are no sentient beings. Who is there to be liberated? At such a time the mind of compassion grows weak. Or at another time, seeing sentient beings as pitiable, one's contemplation of the emptiness of all dharmas weakens. But if one attains the power of skillful means with regard to these two dharmas, holding them equally without partiality, then the mind of great compassion does not obstruct the true nature of all dharmas, and attaining the true nature of all dharmas does not obstruct great compassion. When such skillful means arise, one can then enter [the Way].

**Question:** What are the faults [of wrongly grasping emptiness]?

**Answer:** The *Anuttarāśraya Sūtra* clarifies the view of emptiness thus:

> If there is a person whose attachment to a view of self is as great as Mount Sumeru, I am neither alarmed nor do I censure that person. But a person of overweening pride who clings to a view of emptiness, even one as slight as a single sixteenth of a hair, I do not approve.

A verse in the second fascicle of the *Mūlamadhyamakakārikā* says:

> The Great Sage taught the dharma of emptiness
> in order to free beings from all views.
> If one further produces a view that there is emptiness,
> such a one the buddhas cannot transform.

The *Buddha Treasury Sūtra*, in its chapter on mindfulness of the saṅgha, refutes attachment to "having something to be attained":

> Those who grasp at having something to be attained speak of self, person, lifespan, and living being. They conceptualize and discriminate dharmas that are without any [inherent existence]. They teach either annihilationism or eternalism, either action or non-action. My pure Dharma, by these causes and conditions, is gradually extinguished.

And:

> The view of self, the view of person, and the view of sentient beings often cause one to fall into wrong views. The view of annihilation often enables one to attain the Way quickly. Why? Because it is easy to relinquish.

Therefore one should know: the practitioner should constantly contemplate all dharmas as primordially empty and quiescent, and should also constantly cultivate the four universal vows. It is like building a palace upon empty ground: with ground alone or empty space alone, one can never succeed. [Both are needed.] This is because the three truths of all dharmas are mutually interpenetrating.

Therefore a verse in the *Mūlamadhyamakakārikā* says:

> Dharmas arising from causes and conditions,
> these I declare to be emptiness,
> and also to be provisional designations,
> and also to be the meaning of the Middle Way.

**Question:** If [the bodhicitta] directed toward phenomena is inferior, does it still have superior benefits?

**Answer:** When one firmly clings to [phenomena as ultimately real], faults arise. But what is called directing [the vow] toward phenomena does not necessarily entail firm clinging. If it were otherwise, there should be no cases of those who see [conventional] existence and attain the Way. The same applies to seeing emptiness. It is like using fire: touching it with the hand brings harm, but not touching it brings benefit. Emptiness and existence are the same.

#### (2) The Benefits [of the Bodhicitta]

If a person arouses the bodhicitta as taught above, then even with few other practices, in accordance with one's vow one will assuredly be born in the Land of Utmost Bliss, as in the case of the lower grade of the highest class of birth. Such benefits are immeasurable; I shall now briefly indicate one aspect.

The *Mohe zhiguan* says, citing the *Ratnamegha Sūtra*:

> A monk who does not cultivate the dharmas of a monk has no place in the entire great-thousand world-system even to spit, let alone to receive the offerings of others.

Sixty monks wept and addressed the Buddha: "We would rather die than receive the offerings of others." The Buddha said: "That you give rise to a mind of shame is excellent, excellent indeed." One monk asked the Buddha: "What kind of monk can receive offerings?" The Buddha said: "One who is counted among the saṅgha, cultivates the practices of the saṅgha, and obtains the fruits of the saṅgha, such a person can receive offerings. The four fruits and their approaches constitute the saṅgha. The thirty-seven factors of awakening are the practices of the saṅgha. The four fruits are the fruits of the saṅgha."

The monks again addressed the Buddha: "What about one who has aroused the Mahāyāna aspiration?"

The Buddha said:

> If one arouses the Mahāyāna aspiration and seeks omniscience, even though one is not counted in the [śrāvaka] saṅgha, does not cultivate [their] practices, and does not obtain [their] fruits, one can still receive offerings.

The monks asked in astonishment: "How can such a person receive offerings?"

The Buddha said:

> Even if this person uses the cloth of robes to cover the entire great earth, and eats food the size of Mount Sumeru, this person can still fully repay the benefactor's kindness.

It is also said that the *Secret Treasury Sūtra of the Tathāgata* teaches:

> If a person whose father is a pratyekabuddha kills him, steals the property of the Three Treasures, whose mother is an arhat and defiles her, slanders the Buddha with falsehoods, causes dissension among the noble saṅgha with divisive speech, reviles the sages with harsh words, [harbors ill intentions toward those seeking the Dharma], harbors the initial karmic anger of the five heinous crimes, harbors the greed of stealing from those who keep the precepts, and harbors the delusion of extreme views, making these ten evils, yet if this person can know the teaching of dependent arising expounded by the Tathāgata, [understanding] no self, no person, no sentient being, no lifespan, no birth, no cessation, no defilement, no attachment, primordially pure in nature, and furthermore knows that all dharmas are primordially pure in nature, and understands, knows, and enters with faith, I do not say that this person is headed for the hells or the evil destinies.
>
> Why? Because dharmas have no accumulation. Dharmas do not gather and cause affliction. All dharmas are unborn and unabiding; they arise through the combination of causes and conditions, and having arisen, they cease. If the mind, having arisen, ceases, then all bonds and latent tendencies also arise and cease. For one who understands in this way, there is no place of transgression. If there were transgression and abiding, that would be impossible. It is like a room that has been dark for a hundred years: when a lamp is lit, the darkness cannot say, "I am the master of this room; I have dwelt here long and will not depart." When the lamp is born, the darkness is immediately extinguished. The meaning is the same.

This sūtra identifies all of the foregoing with the four kinds of bodhi [mind]:

> Just as the good-seeing medicine-king can cure all diseases, so too the bodhicitta can cure the disease of all the afflictions of sentient beings. Just as when the milk of a lion is poured into a vessel containing the milk of cattle, horses, or sheep, all the other milk is consumed, and the lion's milk passes through unobstructed, so too when the lion's-milk of the Tathāgata's bodhicitta is placed among the milk of the karma and afflictions accumulated over immeasurable kalpas, they are all consumed, and [the bodhicitta] does not abide in the dharmas of śrāvakas and pratyekabuddhas.

The *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Sūtra* states:

> Even if bodhisattvas frequently give rise to unwholesome mental attention associated with the five desires, enemies cannot gain advantage over them. Bodhisattva-mahāsattvas are also like this: having attained the indestructible dharma-medicine of the bodhicitta, they cannot be destroyed by any affliction or by māra the enemy. Just as a person who obtains a water-dwelling jeweled necklace and enters deep water will not sink or drown, so one who has attained the water-dwelling jewel of the bodhicitta enters the ocean of birth-and-death without sinking. Just as a diamond, even after a hundred thousand kalpas in water, is neither corroded nor changed, so too the mind of bodhi, even after immeasurable kalpas in the midst of birth-and-death, cannot be severed or diminished by karmic afflictions.

A verse by the Bodhisattva Dharmadhvaja in the same sūtra says:

> If there is a person of wisdom
> who in a single thought arouses the aspiration for the Way,
> that person will assuredly become an unsurpassed honored one.

> Just as jāmbūnada gold,
> apart from the wish-fulfilling jewel,
> surpasses all other treasures,
> the bodhicitta is like jāmbūnada gold:
> apart from omniscience,
> it surpasses all other merits.
>
> Just as the kalaviṅka bird,
> even while still within the egg,
> possesses a great power
> that other birds cannot match,
> so too the bodhisattva-mahāsattva
> who arouses the bodhicitta within birth-and-death,
> possesses a power of merit
> that śrāvakas and pratyekabuddhas cannot match.
>
> Just as the pārijātaka tree's blossoms,
> scenting a garment for a single day,
> surpass the scent of campaka
> or vārṣika blossoms over a thousand years,
> so too the blossom of the bodhicitta,
> scenting [the practitioner] for a single day,
> the fragrance of its merit
> pervades the buddha lands of the ten directions,
> while the merit that śrāvakas and pratyekabuddhas
> perfume with undefiled wisdom
> over a hundred thousand kalpas
> cannot match it.
>
> Just as a diamond, even when broken
> and no longer whole,
> still surpasses all other precious gems,
> so too the mind of bodhi,
> even though one may at times be somewhat lax,
> still surpasses [all else].

> The merit of a single thought
> directed solely toward bodhi
> is profound, vast, and boundless.
> Even if the Tathāgata were to explain it in detail,
> he could not exhaust it through all the kalpas.
>
> One might count all the atoms
> in all the lands;
> one might even measure
> the extent of space in the ten directions;
> but the initial aspiration of the bodhisattva
> can ultimately never be fathomed.

A verse from the *Sūtra on Producing the Bodhicitta* says:

> If all the sentient beings in this buddha land
> were established in faith and moral discipline,
> the great accumulation of supreme merit thereby
> would not equal one sixteenth of the aspiration for the Way.
>
> If all the sentient beings in this buddha land
> were established in faith and Dharma practice,
> the great accumulation of supreme merit thereby
> would not equal one sixteenth of the aspiration for the Way.
>
> If in all the buddha lands, as numerous as the sands of the Ganges,
> temples were built everywhere to seek merit,
> such persons would attain the supreme Dharma;
> yet if one seeks bodhi for the benefit of sentient beings,
> that sentient being is the most excellent of all.
> This is beyond comparison, let alone surpassed.
>
> Therefore, having heard these dharmas,
> the wise should always give rise to a mind that delights in the Dharma.
> They will attain a boundless great accumulation of merit
> and swiftly realize the unsurpassed Way.

A verse in the *Ratnakūṭa Sūtra* says:

> If the merit of the bodhicitta
> had form and spatial extent,
> it would fill the entire expanse of space
> with no room to contain it.

A verse of prostration says:

> The initial arousal of the aspiration and the ultimate [realization] are in essence no different.
> Between these two minds, the initial mind is the more difficult.
> Not yet having crossed over oneself, one first crosses over others:
> therefore I pay homage to the initial arousal of the aspiration.

Furthermore, when Megha, the great worthy, heard that the youth Sudhana had already aroused the bodhicitta, he descended from his lion seat, emitting great light illuminating the three-thousand world-system, and prostrated with all five limbs touching the ground [before Sudhana].

**Question:** Even if [the bodhicitta directed toward phenomena] is not equal to that directed toward principle, does it still have superior benefits?

**Answer:** Indeed it does. How do we know? The description of the karma of the lower grade of the highest class of birth says only "arouses the aspiration for the unsurpassed Way"; it does not say "comprehends the meaning of the first principle." From this we know it refers solely to the bodhicitta directed toward phenomena.

If it were not so, [the sūtra] would merely say: "Because one wishes to pull out the suffering of all sentient beings, and because one wishes to cause all sentient beings to attain great bodhi, and because one wishes to gather in sentient beings and bring them to birth in that buddha land." The treatise likewise simply says "aspires to become a buddha." This clarifies that the bodhicitta directed toward phenomena also fully repays the kindness of donors.

What is called "initial" [in the scholastic classification] refers to the bodhicitta directed toward phenomena within the [saṃsāric] realm, as taught in the Tripiṭaka. How much more so if one deeply trusts that all sentient beings possess the buddha-nature and universally vows that self and other together [attain liberation]!

As [detailed] below in the gate of merit-dedication.

**Question:** If one trusts and understands that sentient beings originally possess the buddha-nature, is this not [already the bodhicitta] directed toward principle?

**Answer:** This is trusting and understanding the ultimate principle of the Mahāyāna. It is not necessarily the contemplative wisdom that accords with the emptiness of the first principle.

**Question:** The *Shiyilun* (十疑論, *Ten Doubts*) quotes the *Yogācārabhūmi-saṃgraha* saying: "If one who vows to be born in the Land of Peace and Bliss immediately attains birth, and if one who hears the name of the Immaculate Buddha immediately attains anuttarā samyaksaṃbodhi, this is a cause pertaining to a different time and an intention pertaining to a different occasion." How then can a person of the lower grade of the highest class of birth attain rebirth simply through the vow of bodhi?

**Answer:** The power of the great bodhicitta is exceedingly profound: it extinguishes immeasurable transgressions and generates immeasurable merit. Therefore, when one seeks the Pure Land, one attains it in accordance with one's seeking. What is called "the intention pertaining to a different time" refers only to one who, for one's own sake, vows to seek the Land of Utmost Bliss. It does not refer to the vast bodhicitta of the four universal vows.

**Question:** If the great bodhicitta has this power, then all bodhisattvas from the time of their initial aspiration should decidedly never fall into the evil destinies.

**Answer:** Before a bodhisattva reaches the stage of non-retrogression, the defiled and pure minds arise in alternation. Although the preceding thought-moment may extinguish a multitude of transgressions, the succeeding thought-moment may create transgressions anew. Moreover, the bodhicitta has degrees of depth, strength, and weakness, while evil karma has differences of recency, remoteness, and fixed or unfixed [retribution]. Therefore, before the stage of non-retrogression, ascending and descending are not fixed. It is not that the bodhicitta lacks the power to extinguish transgressions. I merely set forth my foolish opinion; the reader may accept or reject it.

#### (3) Critical Distinctions

**Question:** The chapter on entering the dharma-realm [of the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra*] states:

> Just as diamond arises from the nature of gold and not from other gems, so too the jewel of the bodhicitta arises from the nature of great compassion that rescues and protects sentient beings, and not from other forms of goodness.

A verse in the *Mahāyānasūtrālaṃkāra* says:

> Though one forever abides in hell,
> this does not obstruct great bodhi.
> But if one gives rise to a mind of self-benefit,
> this is an obstruction to great bodhi.

And a verse in the *Treatise on the Great Man* says:

> The merit of giving to a single person with a compassionate mind
> is as great as the earth.
> Giving to everyone for one's own sake
> yields a reward [only] as [small as] a mustard seed.
>
> Rescuing one person in distress and danger
> surpasses all other giving.
> Though the stars possess light,
> [they are nothing compared to the sun.]

What one relies upon [determines the reward]; meager reliance yields meager reward. Why then should one solely aspire to swift rebirth in the Land of Utmost Bliss?

**Answer:** Did I not say earlier that one who aspires to the Land of Utmost Bliss must arouse the four universal vows and diligently cultivate in accordance with them? How is this not the practice of great compassion?

Moreover, aspiring to the Land of Utmost Bliss is not a mind of self-benefit. The reason is this: in this sahā world there are many obstacles and hindrances. The sweet dew [of the Dharma] has not yet moistened [us]; the ocean of suffering swallows us at dawn. Those who are beginners on the path have no opportunity to cultivate the Way. Therefore, in order to bring to perfect fulfillment the vows and practices of the bodhisattva and freely benefit all sentient beings, one first seeks the Land of Utmost Bliss. This is not for one's own benefit.

As the *Daśabhūmika-vibhāṣā* says:

> If one has not yet crossed over oneself, one cannot cross over others. It is like a person sinking in mud: how can that person rescue anyone else? Or like one carried away by water: that person cannot save the drowning.

Therefore it is said: "I shall first cross over, and then cross over others." As a verse in the *Dharmapada Sūtra* says:

> If one can first establish one's own person
> in a place of goodness,
> then afterward one can bring peace to others.

It is further explained in the *Treatise on the Pure Land*: wanting to rescue and deliver all sentient beings from suffering, one reflects: "I now have no power. If I remain in this defiled world among the conditions of affliction, since those conditions are strong, I myself will be entangled and bound, sinking into the three evil destinies, passing through numberless kalpas. Revolving in this way since beginningless time, I have never had a moment's rest. When will I ever be able to rescue sentient beings from suffering?" For this reason one seeks birth in the Pure Land, draws near to all the buddhas, realizes the patience of the unborn, and only then has the power [to save others].

This is [the position of] the *Ten Doubts*. One should understand: buddha-recollection and the cultivation of goodness are the karmic cause; birth in the Land of Utmost Bliss is the blossom-reward; realization of great bodhi is the fruit-reward; benefiting sentient beings is the fundamental intent. It is like planting a tree in the world: first the blossoms open; from the blossoms comes the fruit; from the fruit one partakes.

**Question:** Within the four universal vows, to which does the practice of buddha-recollection belong?

**Answer:** Cultivating the samādhi of buddha-recollection belongs to the third vow-practice. Whatever afflictions are thereby subdued and extinguished belong to the second vow-practice. Forming good karmic connections, near and far, belongs to the first vow-practice. Accumulating merit and virtue brings the fourth vow to completion. The same principle applies to all other wholesome deeds.

**Question:** If one is single-mindedly mindful of the Buddha, one should in principle attain birth [in the Pure Land]. Why then do the sūtras and treatises urge the vow of bodhi?

**Answer:** The *Mahāyānasūtrālaṃkāra* states:

> The work of the buddha-land is great. The merit of solitary practice alone cannot accomplish it. The power of the vow is required. It is like an ox that has strength to pull a cart but still requires a driver to reach its destination. The purification of the buddha-land is brought to completion through the guiding power of the vow.

Furthermore, of all dharmas, the vow is the root. Without the vow, nothing is accomplished. Therefore one arouses the vow. It also says:

> If a person aspires to become a buddha, that person should be mindful of Amitābha. Immediately for this reason [rebirth is attained].

Therefore the practitioner must arouse this vow.

**Question:** If one does not arouse this vow, will one never attain birth?

**Answer:** The masters disagree. Some say that all who attain the nine grades of birth have aroused the bodhicitta. Those of the middle grades, although originally followers of the Lesser Vehicle, later arouse the Great Vehicle aspiration and thereby attain birth in that land; due to their previous habits they temporarily realize the lesser fruit. Those of the lower grades [also have the bodhicitta], for [their practice constitutes] the bodhicitta in action.

**Question:** Just as the masters disagree on the bodhicitta, do they also disagree on the aspiration for the Pure Land?

**Answer:** Although there are differing explanations of the great bodhicitta, all nine grades should possess the aspiration for the Pure Land.

**Question:** If Pure Land karma yields its reward through the vow, then a person who commits evil without wishing for hell should not receive the fruit of hell.

**Answer:** The retribution of transgression has a fixed measure, whereas the reward of the Pure Land is immeasurable. Since the two fruits differ, how can the two causes be treated as one? As the eighth fascicle of the *Mahāprajñāpāramitopadeśa* says:

> Although transgressions and merit have fixed retribution, for one who makes a vow and cultivates even small merit, the power of the vow yields a great fruit-reward. All sentient beings wish for happiness; none wish for suffering. Therefore no one vows [to go to] hell. For this reason, merit accompanied by the immeasurable vow of bodhi [yields immeasurable reward].

**Question:** [How can one ensure that] the bodhicitta vow is not lost or forgotten?

**Answer:** A verse in the third fascicle of the *Daśabhūmika-vibhāṣā* says:

> Even at the cost of one's life
> or the position of a wheel-turning sage-king,
> one must never abandon
> deceitful or crooked speech and conduct.
>
> [One who] can cause all sentient beings
> in all the worlds
> to give rise to a mind of reverence
> toward the assembly of bodhisattvas...
>
> If there is a person who can practice
> such [conduct as described above]...

---

### 4. The Gate of Contemplation

*End of the upper fascicle of the Ōjōyōshū*

*Beginning of the middle fascicle of the Ōjōyōshū, composed by the śramaṇa Genshin of the Shuryōgon-in of Tendai.*

---

The fourth, the gate of contemplation: for those whose initial contemplative practice is not yet capable of plumbing the depths, as the *Daśabhūmika-vibhāṣā* states:

> A bodhisattva who has newly aroused the aspiration should first contemplate the physical form and marks of the Buddha.

Therefore one should now cultivate the contemplation of form and marks. This is divided into three parts: (1) the contemplation of individual marks, (2) the contemplation of the complete form, and (3) the condensed and abbreviated contemplation. One should employ whichever accords with one's inclination.

#### (1) The Contemplation of Individual Marks

This also has two parts. First, one contemplates the lotus seat. The *Sūtra on Contemplation of Immeasurable Life* states:

> One who wishes to contemplate that Buddha should first give rise to a mental image: upon the ground of seven jewels, imagine a lotus blossom. Each petal of that lotus has a hundred jeweled colors. It has eighty-four thousand veins, like a heavenly painting. Each vein has eighty-four thousand rays of light; all are clearly and distinctly visible. The smallest petals are two hundred and fifty yojanas in length and breadth. Such a lotus has eighty-four thousand petals. Between each pair of petals there are a hundred koṭis of maṇi jewel-kings to adorn and embellish it. Each maṇi jewel emits a thousand rays of light. Those lights form a canopy of seven jewels that spreads across the ground. The platform of this lotus is composed of the jewel called śakrābhilagna. This lotus platform is adorned with eighty-four thousand diamond, kiṃśuka gems, brahma-maṇi jewels, and nets of wondrous true pearls. Upon the platform there naturally arise four jeweled pillars. Each pillar is like a hundred thousand million koṭis of Mount Sumerus. The jeweled curtains upon the pillars are like the palaces of the Yāma heaven. There are five hundred koṭis of wondrous, subtle jewels to adorn and embellish them. Each jewel emits eighty-four thousand rays of light; each ray produces eighty-four thousand different hues of gold. Each golden hue pervades the jeweled land, transforming in every place into different forms: now appearing as a diamond platform, now as a net of true pearls, now as a cloud of varied blossoms. On all ten sides they transform at will, manifesting the work of the Buddha. This is the contemplation of the lotus seat.

These wondrous blossoms were brought into being by the power of the vows of the Bhikṣu Dharmākara. If one wishes to be mindful of that Buddha, one should first perform this contemplation of the lotus seat.

> When performing this contemplation, one must not contemplate in a disorderly fashion. One should contemplate each element one by one: each petal, each jewel, each ray of light, each platform, each pillar, all made clearly distinct, as if seeing one's own face reflected in a mirror. To contemplate in this way is called correct contemplation. To contemplate otherwise is called wrong contemplation.

Next, one contemplates the marks and adornments of the Buddha, that is, Amitābha Buddha seated upon the lotus platform, his marks and adornments brilliant and resplendent, adorning his body.

*One. The uṣṇīṣa (cranial protuberance):* No one can see the top of it. It is lofty, prominent, and round, like a heavenly canopy. For those who delight in the extensive contemplation, one should next contemplate: atop the uṣṇīṣa there is a great radiance endowed with a thousand colors. Each color produces eighty-four thousand branches of light. Within each branch there are eighty-four thousand transformation buddhas. Atop the heads of these transformation buddhas this light is also emitted. This light extends successively upward to immeasurable worlds. In those upper worlds there are transformation bodhisattvas, who descend like clouds and surround all the buddhas.

*Two. The hair:* Atop the head are eighty-four thousand hairs, all turning upward and curling to the right. They never fall out, nor are they disordered. They are deep indigo, dense and fine, fragrant, clean, and soft. For those who delight in the extensive contemplation, one should contemplate: from each pore, five-colored light arises in spirals. When the immeasurable light shines universally, it takes on the color of indigo lapis lazuli. Within that color, transformation buddhas beyond number appear. After manifesting these marks, [the light] returns to rest atop the Buddha's head, spiraling to the right in graceful turns, forming five thousand rays of light. The intervals between them are clear and distinct. All turn upward, encircling all the hairs. They circle the crown five times, like the painting of a heavenly artist, perfectly round and even, as fine as a single thread. Within the intervals between the threads, transformation buddhas arise, accompanied by transformation bodhisattvas as their retinues. All forms and images also appear therein.

*Three.* [This mark is indicated but not separately elaborated in the text.]

*Four. The ears:* Thick, broad, long, with the wheel and lobe fully formed. For the extensive contemplation, [one should contemplate that] fine hairs grow in spirals, emitting five-colored light. That light has a thousand colors, and each color has a thousand [rays].

*Five and six. The face and features:* The face is perfectly round, radiant, and joyful, upright and pure as the autumn moon. The twin eyebrows are luminous and pure, like the bow of Lord Śakra. Their color is incomparably deep indigo, with the light of lapis lazuli. The white ūrṇā between the brows curls to the right, soft as tūla cotton, brilliantly white, surpassing conch shell and snow. For the extensive contemplation: when extended straight, it is exceedingly long, like a tube of white lapis lazuli, emitting light in all directions. Immeasurable light appears, like ten thousand koṭis of suns, beyond what can be fully beheld.

> But within the light, there appear many lotuses. Rising above immeasurable worlds as numerous as dust motes, the lotuses succeed one another in perfect roundness and evenness. Upon each lotus sits a transformation buddha, adorned with marks of perfection, surrounded by retinues. Each transformation buddha in turn emits immeasurable light, and within each light there are also immeasurable transformation buddhas. Among all these World-Honored Ones: those walking are without number, those standing are without number, those sitting are without number, those reclining are without number. Some teach great loving-kindness and great compassion; some teach the thirty-seven factors of awakening; some teach the six pāramitās; some teach the unique and unshared dharmas of the buddhas.

[Continuing through the remaining marks of the Buddha's body, including the eyes, nose, lips, tongue, throat, neck, the svastika on the chest, the arms and hands, the navel, the body, the legs and feet, and the soles, each with its light, its transformation buddhas and bodhisattvas, and its teaching, the text describes in meticulous detail the visualization of each of the thirty-two major marks and the eighty minor marks. Each mark emits multicolored rays that illumine the ten directions; within each ray appear transformation buddhas who, with their bodhisattva retinues, preach the Dharma and benefit sentient beings.]

The various marks and their corresponding karmic causes have both general and specific [explanations]. As for the general cause, the *Yogācārabhūmi* (fascicle 49) states: beginning from the stage of pure superior aspiration, all that the bodhisattva does [generates these marks]. The specific causes are detailed in that treatise under three categories.

Regarding the order of contemplation, the sūtras differ. Here I have arranged them as seems appropriate. The interweaving of marks and minor adornments as the method of contemplation follows the precedent of the *Sūtra on the Samādhi of Contemplating the Buddha*.

The order of contemplation: in the forward direction, from the crown to the feet; in the reverse direction, from the feet to the crown. The *Sūtra on the Samādhi of Contemplating the Buddha* states:

> With closed eyes, by the power of the mind's contemplation, one sees clearly and distinctly, as though the Buddha were present in the world. Although one contemplates these marks, one must not attempt too many at once. Begin from one feature, give rise to the contemplation of one feature; having [completed] the contemplation of that one feature, proceed to the next. Going forward and backward, reversing and repeating, through sixteen cycles. Make the mental image of this contemplation exceedingly clear and sharp. Then rest the mind and fix the attention on a single point. Gradually, gradually, raise the tongue toward the palate and hold the tongue steady. After two periods of seven days, the body and mind can attain stability and ease.

Master [Shan]dao says: "After the sixteen cycles, rest the mind and contemplate the white ūrṇā. Do not let it become disordered."

#### (2) The Contemplation of the Complete Form

First, contemplate the lotus of many jewels, vast and magnificently adorned, as before. Next, contemplate Amitābha Buddha seated upon the lotus platform. His body is the color of a hundred thousand million koṭis of jāmbūnada gold. His height is six hundred thousand koṭis of nayutas of Ganges-river-sands of yojanas. The white ūrṇā between his brows curls to the right, of the size of five Mount Sumerus. His eyes are like the waters of the four great oceans, clear, white, and distinct. From all the pores of his body, he emits light as [great as] Mount Sumeru. His circular halo is like a hundred koṭis of great-thousand world-systems. Within the halo are immeasurable Ganges-river-sands of transformation buddhas, each with numberless bodhisattvas as attendants.

Thus he possesses eighty-four thousand marks. Each mark has eighty-four thousand minor adornments. Each adornment has eighty-four thousand rays of light. Each ray of light illumines the sentient beings of the ten directions who are mindful of the Buddha, gathering them in and never abandoning them. One should know that within each mark there are seven hundred and five koṭis and six million rays of light, blazing and radiant, of majestic spiritual power.

He is like a king of golden mountains in the great ocean. Immeasurable transformation buddhas and bodhisattvas fill his light, each manifesting supernatural powers, surrounding Amitābha Buddha. That Buddha, endowed with immeasurable merit and marks of adornment, dwells amid the assembly of bodhisattvas, expounding the true Dharma.

At that time the practitioner sees nothing else: no other forms or marks. Mount Sumeru and the Iron-Encircling Mountains, mountains great and small, none appear. The great oceans, rivers, lands, and forests, none appear. What fills the eyes is nothing but the marks of Amitābha Buddha, pervading the world. What fills the ears is nothing but the radiance of jāmbūnada gold.

It is like the waters at the end of a kalpa that fill the entire world: among all the myriad things, everything is submerged and nothing appears; vast, immense, and boundless, one sees only the great water. The light of that Buddha is the same: rising above all worlds, his marks and radiance illumine everything without exception.

The practitioner, with the mind's eye, sees that his own body also is within that light. The Buddha contemplated [in this way] is the body of the three bodies in one.

Within that single body, what is seen differs [according to the capacity of the practitioner]. Some see a body sixteen feet tall; some see a body of eight feet; some see the vast body [described above]. Whatever body appears is entirely golden in color. Whatever benefit it bestows is immeasurable in each case. He is one with all the buddhas; their work is the same. [Like Śāriputra who] could not see the top of his head; [like Maudgalyāyana who] could not exhaust [the reach of] his voice.

The formless, foremost body. Adorned through non-adornment. The ten powers, the four fearlessnesses, the three kinds of mindful abiding, great compassion, eighty-four thousand samādhi gates, eighty-four thousand pāramitā gates, Dharma gates as numerous as the sands of the Ganges and the motes of dust: the pure Dharma body, endowed with all marks and adornments. Each and every mark is the true nature of reality. The reality-nature and the dharma-realm are fully complete without diminishment. Unborn and unceasing, without going or coming. Neither one nor different. Neither cut off nor permanent. All merits, conditioned and unconditioned, depend upon this Dharma body. The three bodies of the buddhas of the ten directions, the universal gates and dust-mote-number of immeasurable Dharma gates, the ocean of the Buddha, the Dharma, and the assembly, the perfectly interpenetrating myriad virtues, the inexhaustible dharma-realm: all are contained within the single body of Amitābha Buddha. Neither vertical nor horizontal. Neither one nor different. Neither real nor unreal. Neither existent nor nonexistent. Primordially pure in nature. Beyond the paths of mind and language.

It is like a wish-fulfilling jewel: neither possessing treasure nor devoid of treasure. The myriad virtues of the Buddha's body are the same. He is also neither identical with the aggregates, sense-fields, and elements, nor separate from them. He is not called the Tathāgata by being identified with the aggregates, sense-fields, and elements, since all sentient beings also possess them. Nor is he called the Tathāgata by being separate from the aggregates, sense-fields, and elements, since that would be a dharma without cause or condition. Neither identical nor separate. Quiescent, existing only in name.

Therefore one should know: what one contemplates as the manifold marks is precisely the marks and radiance of the three bodies as one. The marks and radiance of all buddhas as one body. The marks and radiance of the perfectly interpenetrating myriad virtues. Form is emptiness; therefore it is called true suchness and the reality-nature. Emptiness is form; therefore it is called marks and radiance. Each single form, each single fragrance, is none other than the Middle Way. Feeling, perception, volition, and consciousness are likewise. The three evil destinies that belong to me and the myriad virtues of Amitābha Buddha are primordially empty and quiescent, one body without division.

#### (3) The Condensed and Abbreviated Contemplation

Between the Buddha's brows is a single white ūrṇā, curling to the right, of the size of five Mount Sumerus. Within it there are eighty-four thousand [minor] adornments; within each adornment, eighty-four thousand rays of light. The light is subtle and wondrous, endowed with the colors of many jewels. In sum, seven hundred and five koṭis and six million rays of light, blazing and radiant on all ten sides, like a billion thousand suns and moons. Within that light appear the bodies of all the buddhas. Numberless bodhisattvas surround the assembly.

Again, a subtle and wondrous sound issues forth, proclaiming the ocean of all dharmas. Each and every ray of light illumines the sentient beings of the ten directions who are mindful of the Buddha, gathering them in and never abandoning them. I too am within that gathering. Though the eye obstructed by affliction cannot see, the great compassion never wearies and its light constantly illumines my body.

Alternatively, one should imagine oneself being born in the Land of Utmost Bliss, seated in cross-legged meditation within a lotus blossom. Imagine the lotus closing. Then, as the lotus opens, gaze up at the honored countenance and contemplate the white ūrṇā. At that moment, five hundred colors of light come and illuminate one's body. One immediately sees immeasurable transformation buddhas and bodhisattvas filling all of space. The sounds issued by the waters, birds, trees, and all the buddhas all proclaim the wondrous Dharma. Contemplating in this way, let the mind be filled with joy.

"May we, together with all sentient beings," one should recite. One should be mindful: the white ūrṇā between that Buddha's brows spirals and turns like a vaiḍūrya jewel. Its light illumines and gathers in us all.

> May we, together with all sentient beings, be born in that land.

If there are those who cannot sustain the contemplation of the marks, they may rely on the contemplation of taking refuge, or the contemplation of being drawn in, or in sitting, lying, speaking, or silence, in all activities, they should constantly keep this thought within the breast, as the hungry think of food, as the thirsty pursue water. Whether bowing the head, raising a hand, or calling out the Name aloud, the outward form may differ, but the inner mindfulness remains. Thought after thought in continuous succession, waking or sleeping, never to be forgotten.

**Question:** The true body of that Buddha is beyond the reach of an ordinary person's mental power. Should one therefore contemplate only an image, and not the great body?

**Answer:** The *Sūtra on Contemplation of Immeasurable Life* says:

> The body of Amitāyus Buddha is boundless, beyond the reach of an ordinary person's mental power. However, by the power of that Tathāgata's original vow, whoever is mindful and visualizes will assuredly succeed. Simply by contemplating the Buddha's image, one attains immeasurable [merit].

Therefore, in accordance with one's inclination, one may contemplate the true body.

**Question:** When it is said that the single body of Amitābha is precisely the body of all buddhas, what is the evidence?

**Answer:** The Great Master Tiantai says: "To be mindful of Amitābha Buddha is to be mindful of all buddhas." Therefore the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra* states:

> The bodies of all the buddhas are precisely one buddha's body.
> One mind, one wisdom;
> the powers and fearlessnesses are also [one].

**Question:** Is it that all buddhas are one in their essential nature, or that the merit of the person who is mindful is also without distinction?

**Answer:** They are equal and without distinction. The *Mañjuśrī Prajñāpāramitā Sūtra*, second fascicle, states:

> The merit of being mindful of one buddha is immeasurable and boundless, and is no different from the merit of the immeasurable buddhas. It is inconceivable. The dharma of all buddhas is equal and without distinction. All ride upon the single suchness to accomplish the most perfect awakening. All are endowed with immeasurable merit, virtue, and eloquence. Thus, one who enters the single-practice samādhi fully knows the dharma-realm of all the buddhas, as numerous as the sands of the Ganges, without any distinction.

**Question:** Now you particularly urge contemplation of the white ūrṇā. What is the evidence?

**Answer:** The evidence is very extensive. I shall cite just one or two [sources]. The *Sūtra on Contemplation of Immeasurable Life* states:

> One who contemplates Amitāyus Buddha should enter through one single mark. Simply contemplate the white ūrṇā between his brows and make it exceedingly clear. When one sees the white ūrṇā, the eighty-four thousand marks will naturally appear.

The *Sūtra on the Samādhi of Contemplating the Buddha* also says:

> The Tathāgata has immeasurable marks and adornments. Within each mark there are eighty-four thousand minor marks. None of these compare even to a small fraction of the merit of the white ūrṇā. Therefore, today, for the sentient beings of evil in the future age, I teach the contemplation of the white ūrṇā's great light of compassion, the method for extinguishing evil. If any person of extreme wrong views and grave evil, upon hearing this contemplation method with its complete description of the marks, should give rise to a mind of anger and hatred, that is impossible. Even if such a one did give rise to anger, the light of the white ūrṇā would still cover and protect that person. Merely hearing these words removes the transgressions of three kalpas. In whatever place one is born afterward, one will be born before the buddhas.

> Such are the hundreds of billions of varieties of contemplative light and subtle, wondrous states, which cannot be fully described. When one is mindful of the white ūrṇā, they will naturally arise.

And also:

> If even the rough contemplation of an image yields such immeasurable merit, how much more so the sustained mindfulness of the white ūrṇā light between the Buddha's brows.

And also:

> Śākyamuni Buddha appeared before the practitioner and said: "By the power of your cultivation of the samādhi of contemplating the Buddha, I, with the power of my nirvāṇa-form, show you my visible body. You should contemplate it carefully. When you sit in meditation hereafter, do not attempt to contemplate many [marks at once]. For the people of the future age, who commit many evil deeds, simply contemplate the white ūrṇā light between the brows. When performing this contemplation, what one sees [will be as described]."

Therefore the ninth fascicle of the *Sūtra on the Samādhi of Contemplating the Buddha* says:

> If one can focus the mind and contemplate a single pore, that person is called one who practices the samādhi of buddha-recollection. Because of buddha-recollection, the buddhas of the ten directions constantly stand before that person and teach the true Dharma. This person thereby plants the seeds of all the tathāgatas of the three times. How much more so if one contemplates the complete physical form of the Buddha.

**Question:** Why do you not teach the contemplation of the adornments of the Pure Land?

**Answer:** My present purpose is to instruct those who cannot sustain the extensive practice; therefore I urge only the condensed contemplation. If one wishes to contemplate [the Pure Land], one should read the *Sūtra on Contemplation of Immeasurable Life*. Moreover, the ten kinds of practice previously described [in Gate Three] already constitute the adornments of the Pure Land.

**Question:** Why do you not teach the contemplation of Avalokiteśvara and Mahāsthāmaprāpta?

**Answer:** I have omitted them for the sake of brevity. After [the practice of] buddha-recollection, one should contemplate the two bodhisattvas or call upon their names, as much or as little as one wishes.

---

### 5. The Gate of Merit-Dedication

The fifth, [the gate of merit-dedication]: when all five elements are complete, this is true merit-dedication.

[The five aspects are:] (1) gathering all these good roots together with all sentient beings; (4) dedicating [them] to unsurpassed bodhi; and (5) contemplating that the giver, the recipient, and the gift are all unapprehendable [i.e., empty]. This enables all dharmas to be truly [in accord with reality].

[One should recite:] "May the vow-practices of Samantabhadra be swiftly and perfectly fulfilled. May self and other together realize unsurpassed bodhi."

**Question:** The *Avataṃsaka Sūtra* describes the conduct of the bodhisattva at the third stage of merit-dedication, saying: "With the good roots of the three times, yet without any attachment, without marks, apart from marks, all is dedicated." The *Kanding ji* (勘定記, *Record of Critical Assessments*) gives two interpretations: First, although future good roots do not yet exist, if one now makes a vow, the vow perfumes and establishes a seed; by the power of this sustaining [force], whatever one cultivates in the future will naturally be directed toward sentient beings and bodhi without the need to dedicate again. Second, according to this teaching, when a bodhisattva cultivates even a single thought of goodness, because it encompasses the dharma-nature, it pervades the nine periods of time. Therefore one uses those good roots for dedication.

**Question:** What is meant by the "sarvajña-corresponding mind" [the second of the five aspects]?

**Answer:** The treatise says: "The intention of anuttarā samyaksaṃbodhi" means the mind that corresponds to sarvajña. "Corresponds" means: one focuses the mind [with the thought], "May I become a buddha."

**Question:** Why must the third and fourth [aspects] involve sharing with all sentient beings and dedicating to unsurpassed bodhi?

**Answer:** The *Ṣaṭpāramitā Sūtra* says:

> How is it that small giving yields much merit? By the power of skillful means, when one practices even a small portion of giving and dedicates it with the vow that all sentient beings together realize unsurpassed, perfect awakening, the merit thereby becomes immeasurable and boundless, like a small [fire that burns a great forest].

The same sūtra says:

> All the excellent good roots that a bodhisattva-mahāsattva has already produced should all be dedicated to unsurpassed bodhi, so that these good roots are ultimately inexhaustible. It is like a small amount of water cast into the great ocean: even until the conflagration at the end of the kalpa, it will never be exhausted.

A verse from the *Mahāyānasūtrālaṃkāra* says:

> Practicing giving without seeking fine form or wealth,
> nor wishing for reward in the heavens or the human realm,
> but solely seeking the unsurpassed, supreme bodhi:
> even a small gift then yields immeasurable [merit].

It is like a miserly person who, without cause, will not give even a single coin, hoarding and accumulating, hoping only for increase. The bodhisattva is the same: whether merit is great or small, [one's dedication ensures that] it is never lost.

**Question:** If so, should one dedicate only to bodhi? Why also say "born in the Land of Utmost Bliss"?

**Answer:** Bodhi is the fruit-reward. The Land of Utmost Bliss is the blossom-reward. Should not one who seeks the fruit also look forward to the blossom? Therefore, for all nine grades of karma it is said: "Dedicate and aspire to be born in the Land of Utmost Bliss."

**Question:** What is the difference between making a vow and merit-dedication?

**Answer:** Pledging to seek what one aspires to is called a "vow." Turning the deeds one has performed toward that [goal] is called "merit-dedication."

**Question:** Are sarvajña and unsurpassed bodhi not the same? Why distinguish them as two?

**Answer:** The treatise, in explaining merit-dedication, divides them into two. I have simply followed its division. One should examine the treatise's text further.

**Question:** Why must one then contemplate all objects as empty?

**Answer:** The treatise says:

> The bodhisattva who cultivates merit with a mind of attachment and grasping at marks is like fire growing in grass: it is easily extinguished. But the bodhisattva who embodies the true nature of reality and practices the manifold deeds with the mind of great compassion is difficult to destroy, like fire within water, which no one can extinguish.

**Question:** If so, should one not simply declare "emptiness, nothing to be apprehended"? Why then say "dedicate to the dharma-realm"?

**Answer:** In principle that is correct. But here I follow the customs of the land, and therefore say "dharma-realm." There is no contradiction in principle, for the dharma-realm is precisely the perfectly interpenetrating, unconditioned, first-principle emptiness. To turn one's cultivated goodness toward correspondence with that first-principle emptiness is called "dedicating to the dharma-realm."

**Question:** Why does the [formula] conclude with "dedicate to great bodhi"?

**Answer:** This is to bring [the practice] into correspondence with sarvajña. This too follows the custom of the land, placing it at the end. Sarvajña is bodhi, as in the earlier passage from the treatise.

**Question:** Is dedication that involves marks [i.e., that is not done with the contemplation of emptiness] without benefit?

**Answer:** As discussed in the treatises above, although there are degrees of superiority and inferiority, there is still great benefit. As the seventh fascicle of the *Mahāprajñāpāramitopadeśa* says:

> There are small causes with great fruits, small conditions with great rewards. For instance, one who seeks the buddha way, with a single verse of praise, a single utterance of "Homage to the Buddha," or a single pinch of incense, will assuredly become a buddha. How much more so for one who hears and knows that all dharmas are truly unborn and unceasing, neither "un-born" nor "un-unceasing," and practices the way of causes and conditions: that person's attainment of buddhahood is beyond doubt.

Homage to the Honored Nāgārjuna! May you confirm and fulfill the aspiration of my heart.

---

## Gate Five: Auxiliary Methods of Buddha-Recollection

The fifth major topic concerns auxiliary methods of mindfulness. A net with only a single mesh cannot catch a bird. In the same way, ten thousand skillful techniques assist contemplation and mindfulness, bringing to fruition the great matter of birth in the Pure Land. I shall now briefly set forth the method under seven headings:

1. Place of practice and offerings
2. The manner of cultivation
3. Counteracting sloth
4. Ceasing evil and cultivating good
5. Repentance of all transgressions
6. Counteracting demonic hindrances
7. Summary of the essentials of practice

---

### 1. Place of Practice and Offerings

Both inner and outer purity should be maintained. Choose a secluded place. According to one's means, prepare flowers, incense, and offerings. If there is a shortage of flowers, incense, or other requisites, simply concentrate single-mindedly on the merits and spiritual power of the Buddha. If one practices directly before a buddha image, one should provide lamps and lights. In this, one should follow the intent of the passage on offerings in the *Guanfo sanmei jing* (觀佛三昧海經, *Contemplation of the Buddha Samādhi Sūtra*). The merit obtained thereby is immeasurable. For merit-making, one may use bodhi-seeds, or crystal beads, or lotus-seed beads.

---

### 2. The Manner of Cultivation

Following the *She lun* (攝論, *Compendium of the Mahāyāna*) and other texts, one employs four modes of practice.

**First, long-term practice.** The *Yaojue* (要決, *Essentials*) says:

> From the first arousing of the aspiration for enlightenment until the attainment of bodhi, one should constantly create pure causes and never turn back.

Master Shandao says:

> Make the end of this life your deadline, and vow never to stop midway.

**Second, practice with utmost sincerity.** This means that toward the Three Treasures of the Land of Utmost Bliss, the Buddha, the Dharma, and the Saṅgha, one's mind constantly recalls them and generates the deepest reverence. The *Yaojue* says:

> Whether walking, standing, sitting, or lying down, never turn your back on the west. When spitting, blowing your nose, or relieving yourself, never face the west.

Master Shandao says:

> Facing the west is the most excellent posture. Just as a tree that already leans will necessarily fall in that direction, so too will the practitioner. If there is some unavoidable obstruction preventing one from facing west, simply forming the mental image of facing west will also suffice.

**Third, uninterrupted practice.** The *Yaojue* says:

> This means constantly recollecting the Buddha and generating the mind of aspiration for birth in the Pure Land. At all times, the mind should be continuously and skillfully directed toward this. Consider the analogy: suppose a person has been seized and carried off by captors and made a lowly slave, enduring every kind of hardship. Suddenly he thinks of his parents and wishes to flee back to his homeland. His preparations are not yet complete, and he remains in a foreign land. Day and night he broods. The suffering is unbearable. Not for a single moment does he cease to think of his mother and father. When at last his plan is accomplished, he returns and arrives home. He draws close to his parents and gives himself over to joy and ease.

> The practitioner is just like this. In the past, afflictions disrupted and destroyed the wholesome mind, and the precious wealth of merit and wisdom was scattered and lost. Long submerged in birth and death, unable to control one's own fate, one has constantly served as a bondservant to Mara, driven through the six realms of rebirth, with suffering cutting into body and mind. Now, encountering good conditions, one suddenly hears that the compassionate father Amitābha, not abandoning his great vow, rescues and delivers all beings. Day and night, startled and urgent, one arouses the aspiration to go there. Therefore one is diligent and unwearying. One should be mindful of the Buddha's kindness, taking the exhaustion of this life as the deadline, with the mind constantly intent upon this.

Master Shandao says:

> Mind after mind in continuous succession, not interrupted by other activities, and not interrupted by greed, anger, or other afflictions. Whenever a transgression occurs, immediately repent, not allowing even a single thought, a single moment, or a single day to intervene. Always maintain purity.

I would add: during the six periods of day and night, or three periods, or two periods, one must employ the proper method and practice with earnest diligence. At all other times and places, without demanding proper deportment or formal method, one should let the mind and mouth never cease, and should always be recollecting the Buddha.

**Fourth, exclusive practice.** The *Yaojue* says:

> Devote yourself exclusively to seeking the Land of Utmost Bliss, paying homage to and recollecting Amitābha. Do not allow other miscellaneous activities to arise. Whatever practices one undertakes, each day one must unfailingly perform buddha-recollection and sūtra recitation, leaving no other assignments undone.

Master Shandao says:

> Exclusively call upon that Buddha's name. Exclusively recollect, exclusively contemplate, exclusively pay homage, exclusively praise that Buddha and all the holy assembly.

The *Baoji jing* (寶積經, *Ratnakūṭa Sūtra*), fascicle ninety-two, says:

> If there are bodhisattvas who delight in worldly occupations and busy themselves with various affairs, doing what should not be done, I declare that such persons abide in birth and death.

A verse in the same sūtra says:

> In places of idle chatter and pointless discussion, many afflictions arise. The wise should observe this.

**Question:** If that is so, then it must be difficult for laypeople to accomplish the practice of buddha-recollection.

**Answer:** For those living in the world, it is indeed hard to abandon worldly responsibilities. But if one constantly ties the mind to the west and with a sincere heart recollects that Buddha, it will suffice, as illustrated by the *Muhuanzi jing* (木槵子經, *Sūtra on Wooden Prayer Beads*) and the practice of King Virudhaka.

Moreover, the *Jingtu lun* (淨土論, *Pure Land Treatise*) of Jiacai asks: "How should one practice?" The *Guan jing* (觀經, *Contemplation Sūtra*) answers:

> If there are sentient beings who wish to be born in that land, they should arouse three kinds of mind and will thereby attain birth. First, the mind of utmost sincerity. Second, the deep mind. Third, the mind that dedicates merit and aspires to birth.

Master Shandao says:

> First, the mind of utmost sincerity means that in prostrations, praises, recollection, and contemplation, the three karmas of body, speech, and mind must all be genuine and real. Second, the deep mind means truly knowing that one is a common person fully endowed with afflictions, with scant roots of goodness, cycling through the three realms and not yet having escaped the burning house. Now one has faith and understands that through the original vow of Amitābha, by calling upon his name even ten times or once, one will certainly attain birth in the Pure Land. Extending this down to a single moment of mindfulness, one harbors not the slightest doubt. Third, the mind that dedicates merit and aspires to birth means that all the good roots one has cultivated are entirely dedicated to the aspiration for birth in the Pure Land.

> One who possesses these three minds without wavering will certainly attain birth in the land of Amitābha Buddha.

The *Niepan jing* (涅槃經, *Nirvāṇa Sūtra*) says:

> The cause of supreme perfect enlightenment (anuttara-samyak-sambodhi) is faith. Though the causes of bodhi are many, faith is foremost.

Master Shandao also says:

> When entering contemplation or about to sleep, one should make this aspiration: whether sitting or standing, single-mindedly, palms joined, facing directly west, call ten times upon the names of Amitābha Buddha, Avalokiteśvara, Mahāsthāmaprāpta, and all the bodhisattvas of the great ocean assembly of purity. Then give rise to the aspiration to see the Buddha, the bodhisattvas, and the characteristics of the Land of Utmost Bliss. If one then enters contemplation or falls asleep accordingly, one will gain the vision of them, except for those whose minds are not sincere.

**Question:** A practitioner ordinarily directs the mind toward birth in the Pure Land. What form does this take?

**Answer:** The analogy quoted earlier from the *Yaojue*, about wishing to return to one's homeland, illustrates this. Furthermore, the *Anle ji* (*Collection on the Land of Peace and Bliss*) says:

> Consider the analogy: suppose there is a person who, in an open and desolate place, encounters an enemy bandit. The bandit draws his sword and rushes forward to kill him. The person flees and sees that he must cross a river. Before reaching the river, he thinks: "When I reach the bank, shall I remove my clothes to swim across, or keep them on and float?" If he removes his clothes to swim, he fears there will be no time. If he keeps them on and floats, he fears he cannot make it across alive. At that moment, there is only the single-minded determination to find a way across the river, with no other thought intruding. The practitioner is just like this. When recollecting Amitābha Buddha, one should be like that person intent on crossing, thought after thought in succession, with no other thought intruding. Whether one contemplates the Buddha's dharma-body, or contemplates the Buddha's spiritual power, or contemplates the Buddha's wisdom, or contemplates the white tuft between the Buddha's brows, or contemplates the Buddha's marks of excellence, or contemplates the Buddha's original vow, it is the same whether one recites the name aloud.

**Question:** In buddha-recollection samādhi, does one only recollect mentally, or does one also chant aloud?

**Answer:** As the *Mohe zhiguan* (*Great Calming and Contemplation*), fascicle two, says:

> One may employ chanting and mental recollection together, or first recollect mentally and then chant, or first chant and then recollect mentally. Chanting and recollection follow one upon the other without rest. Sound after sound, thought after thought, they dwell solely upon Amitābha.

Furthermore, Master Huaigan says:

> The *Guan jing* (*Contemplation Sūtra*) says: "This person is pressed by suffering and has no leisure for buddha-recollection. A good friend instructs him to call upon the name of Amitābha Buddha. With utmost sincerity, he lets his voice not cease." Is this not because, pressed by suffering and anguish, contemplation is difficult to achieve, so one lets the voice not cease and thereby attains sincerity? In just this way, chanting aloud to learn the samādhi of buddha-recollection is the same: letting the voice not cease, one thereby attains samādhi. Seeing the Buddha and the holy assembly appears vividly before one's eyes. Therefore the *Daji jing richang fen* (大集經日藏分, *Sūryagarbha Chapter of the Great Collection Sūtra*) says: "Great recollection sees a great buddha. Small recollection sees a small buddha." Great recollection means calling the Buddha's name in a loud voice. Small recollection means calling in a soft voice. This is the teaching of the sacred scriptures. What doubt could there be? We can observe this in practice: among all who cultivate this today, those who exert themselves to chant aloud readily achieve samādhi, while those who chant softly tend to become distracted. This is what practitioners experience.

---

### 3. Counteracting Sloth

A practitioner cannot always maintain vigorous effort. At times the mind becomes dull and confused; at times the mind retreats and falters. At such times, one should turn to various excellent matters to encourage and inspire one's own mind. One may compare the bitter fruits of the three evil paths with the merits of the Pure Land, and reflect as follows:

> I have already passed through many aeons on the evil paths, enduring fruitless toil and hardship, and yet am able to survive. In all the worlds, all sentient beings, thought after thought, are being born in the Land of Peace and Bliss. They too are human beings.

One should then contemplate the wondrous and extraordinary merits of the Buddha. I shall briefly list the essentials.

**One: Contemplating the Forty-eight Original Vows.** The *Wuliang qingjing jue jing* (無量清淨平等覺經, *Sūtra of Immeasurable Purity and Equal Awakening*) says:

> Amitābha Buddha, together with Avalokiteśvara and Mahāsthāmaprāpta, rides the great ship of his vows across the ocean of birth and death, coming to this sahā world. He calls out to sentient beings, bidding them board the great ship of his vows and conveying them to the western land. If sentient beings are willing to board the great ship of his vows...

A verse says:

> Sentient beings are submerged in the ocean of birth and death, cycling through the five destinies with no escape. The Sugata is ever the wondrous Dharma-ship, able to sever the stream of craving and reach the other shore.

One should reflect: "When shall I board the ship of compassionate vows and depart?"

**Two: The merit of the Buddha's name.** As the *Weimo jing* (維摩經, *Vimalakīrti Sūtra*) says:

> The form-bodies of all buddhas, their majestic characteristics, lineage, morality, concentration, wisdom, liberation, knowledge and vision of liberation, the ten powers, the four fearlessnesses, the eighteen distinctive qualities, great compassion, great loving-kindness, dignified conduct, and their life spans, teaching of the Dharma, ripening of sentient beings, and purification of buddha-lands, their complete endowment with all buddha-qualities: these are all equal. Therefore they are called samyak-sambuddha; therefore they are called tathāgata.

> Ānanda, if I were to explain in full the meaning of these three epithets, you could not receive it all even with an aeon-spanning life. Even if the entire great trichiliocosm were filled with beings, all possessing, like Ānanda, the foremost capacity for learning and the power of total recall, these persons too, with an aeon-spanning life, could not receive it all.

The *Yaojue* says:

> The *Vimalakīrti Sūtra* says that the Buddha's first three epithets, if the Buddha were to explain them fully, Ānanda could not absorb them even over an entire aeon. The *Chengshi lun* (成實論, *Treatise on Establishing the Real*) explains the Buddha's titles: the first nine titles each derive from a particular meaning. Combining all nine titles, their meanings and merits constitute "Buddha, the World-Honored One." Even to explain the first three titles would take aeons beyond reckoning. Ānanda's understanding could not encompass them all. Adding the further six titles to form the Buddha's complete name, the supreme virtue is perfect. To recollect his great goodness is truly excellent.

One should reflect: "Now I have heard the honored name of the Buddha. May I attain buddhahood, like all the buddhas of the ten directions."

**Three: The merits of the Buddha's marks of excellence.** The *Liubo luomi jing* (六波羅蜜經, *Six Pāramitās Sūtra*) says:

> Consider all the sentient beings in all the worlds throughout the three times, whether learners or non-learners, and the pratyekabuddhas. The merits of all such beings, immeasurable and boundless, compared to the merit of a single hair-pore of the Tathāgata, do not equal even one hundred-thousandth part. Each and every hair-tip arises from the Tathāgata's immeasurable merit. The merits of all the hair-tips together compose the merit of a single strand of hair. The Buddha has eighty-four thousand such hairs, each endowed with the same merit as above. All of these combined compose the merit of a single minor mark. The merits of all the minor marks together compose the merit of a single major mark. All the merits of the major marks combined, multiplied a hundred thousand times, compose the merit of the white tuft between the brows. That mark is perfectly round, curling to the right, like a phalika jewel, bright, pure, and brilliant white. In darkness it shines like the morning star. When extended, it reaches upward to the Akanistha heaven at the summit of the realm of form. When retracted, it resumes its place as the tuft between the brows. The merit of the tuft between the brows, multiplied a hundred thousand times, composes the merit of the crown-protuberance.

> The thousandfold merit of the crown-protuberance does not equal the merit of the characteristic of the Brahma-voice.

The *Baoji jing* (*Ratnakūṭa Sūtra*) contains immeasurable further comparisons; scholars may consult it. The *Daji nianfo sanmei jing* (大集念佛三昧經, *Great Collection Buddha-Recollection Samādhi Sūtra*), fascicle five, says:

> If all the sentient beings in this world and in the immeasurable, boundless worlds of the ten directions were all at once to attain buddhahood, and those world-honored ones were to praise the merits of a single hair-pore of the Buddha for immeasurable aeons, they would never exhaust them.

A verse in the *Huayan jing* (華嚴經, *Avataṃsaka Sūtra*) says:

> Pure compassionate gates, numerous as the dust-motes in a buddha-land, together produce a single wondrous mark of the Tathāgata. Each and every mark is just like this. Therefore, those who behold him are never sated.

One should reflect: "May I one day behold the marks of the Buddha's boundless merit."

**Four: The radiance and spiritual might of the Buddha.** The *Pingdeng jue jing* (平等覺經, *Sūtra of Equal Awakening*) says:

> The radiance of the Buddha of Immeasurable Purity surpasses all others. Among the buddhas, some have a crown-radiance that illuminates seven feet; some illuminate one li; some five li; some twenty li, forty li, or eighty li, and so on up to a million buddha-lands, or two million buddha-lands. The crown-radiance of the buddhas in all eight directions and above and below illuminates in this manner. The crown-radiance and spiritual light of the Buddha of Immeasurable Purity is supreme and foremost; the radiance of all other buddhas cannot match it. Some buddhas' light illuminates a hundred buddha-worlds, or a thousand buddha-worlds. In brief, his light illuminates buddha-fields as numerous as the sands of the Ganges to the east, and likewise to the south, west, north, the four intermediate directions, above and below. Therefore Amitāyus Buddha is called the Buddha of Immeasurable Light. If beings in the three evil paths of suffering behold this light, their suffering ceases, and after their lifespans end, they all attain liberation.

> Not only do I now praise his light: all buddhas do likewise. If there are sentient beings who hear of this radiance and spiritual power, and day and night praise it with sincerity without interruption, they will, according to their wish, attain birth in that land. I declare: the radiance and spiritual power of Amitāyus Buddha is vast and magnificent.

There is also the story of King Asoka, who lived a hundred years after the Buddha's passing. The people of his kingdom sang of the Buddha's teachings, but the king did not believe. He thought: "What virtue does the Buddha possess that surpasses ordinary people, that everyone so devotedly believes in and recites his words?" He asked his ministers: "Is there anyone in the land who has actually seen the Buddha?" They replied: "We have heard that the sister of King Prasenajit left home and became a bhikṣuṇī. She lives in the western border regions and claims to have seen the Buddha." The king went to her in person and asked: "Venerable one, have you seen the Buddha?" She answered: "Indeed I have." The king asked: "What was extraordinary about him?" She said: "The merits of the Buddha are vast and immeasurable, beyond what someone as foolish and lowly as I can express. But I will describe one thing so you may know how extraordinary he was. I was eight years old at the time. The World-Honored One came into the royal palace, and I went forward to prostrate at his feet. The golden hairpin from my head fell to the ground, and I could not find it. I wondered why. After the Tathāgata passed by, there were thousand-spoked wheels in his footprints, emitting such brilliant light that for seven days it persisted. Only then did it fade. At the time, the golden hairpin had become the same color as the ground, and so I could not see it. After the light faded, I found the hairpin. From this I knew his extraordinariness."

A verse says:

> From a single pore, clouds of light appear, pervading all of space, emitting a great sound. All the dark realms are illuminated without exception. The manifold sufferings of hell are all extinguished.

One should reflect: "May the Buddha's light shine upon me, extinguishing the karmic suffering of birth and death."

**Five: None can harm the Buddha.** The *Baoji jing* (*Ratnakūṭa Sūtra*), fascicle thirty-seven, says:

> When the aeon-ending wind arises, a great wind called Samghata lifts up this entire trichiliocosm: Mount Sumeru, the Iron-Ring Mountains, the four great continents, eighty thousand lesser continents, great mountains, and great oceans, heights of a hundred yojanas and more up to immeasurable hundreds of thousands of yojanas, and grinds them all to dust. It further destroys the palaces of the Yama heavens and even the palaces of the Subhakrtsna heavens, scattering and annihilating them all.
