Yet if this same wind were to blow upon the Tathāgata's robe, it could not move even the tip of a single hair.

A treatise says:

> The buddhas are inconceivable. An analogy may illustrate this. Suppose all the sentient beings throughout the ten directions each possessed great power, and suppose each one had the power of a single Mara, and suppose further that every being in all the ten directions had power equal to Mara and together they all sought to harm the Buddha: they still could not move a single hair of the Buddha, let alone truly harm him.

A verse says:

> If anyone in all the worlds should wish to harm the Buddha, this can never be accomplished, for he has perfected the dharma of non-killing.

One should reflect: "May I attain the Buddha's indestructible vajra body."

**Six: Freedom of flight.** The same treatise says:

> In empty space, the Buddha lifts his feet and sets them down, walks, goes, sits, and reclines, all with complete freedom. If a great śrāvaka with mastery of supernormal powers can traverse, in a single day, fifty-three billion, two hundred ninety-six million, six hundred thousand trichiliocosms, then what such a śrāvaka traverses in a hundred years, the Buddha traverses in a single thought-moment. Extending this to the sands of the Ganges, where each sand-grain becomes a river, and the sands of all those rivers become great aeons, the lands traversed in all those aeons the Buddha traverses in a single thought-moment. If the Buddha wishes to walk upon jeweled lotus blossoms, they instantly appear. Such flight is utterly unobstructed.

The *Guanfo jing* (觀佛三昧海經, *Contemplation of the Buddha Sūtra*) says:

> When the Buddha walks through space, the thousand-spoked wheel marks rain down eighty-four thousand lotus blossoms. From the midst of these flowers, buddhas as numerous as dust-motes also walk through space. Wondrous fragrant patmaka blossoms naturally spring forth to receive the Tathāgata's feet. If any being in the animal realm is touched by the Tathāgata's feet, for a full seven nights it experiences every kind of bliss, and at the end of its life is born in the good destinies and the blissful world.

In traversing downward through the earth, a distance of eighteen thousand three hundred eighty-three years' descent to reach this ground: going directly downward is already inconceivable to this degree. From this one can infer the rest. The flight of a śrāvaka compared to the flight of the Tathāgata: each surpasses the other beyond all conception.

A verse by Huilin Bodhisattva praising the Buddha in the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra* says:

> Sovereign supernormal power, immeasurable and inconceivable. Without coming, without going, he teaches the Dharma and liberates sentient beings.

One should reflect: "May I attain supernormal powers and roam freely through all buddha-lands."

**Seven: Unobstructed supernormal powers.** The *Shizhu lun* (十住論, *Daśabhūmika-vibhāṣā*) says:

> The Buddha can grind worlds as numerous as the sands of the Ganges into fine dust, and can also restore them. He can transform immeasurable, boundless, incalculable worlds so that they are all made of gold, silver, and the like. He can transform the waters of all the great oceans in worlds as numerous as the sands of the Ganges into milk, cream, and so forth. He can take a trichiliocosm and, as a potter spins a wheel, place it on his right palm and hurl it past worlds as numerous as the sands of the Ganges. The beings within it neither perceive nor know that their abode has moved. Then he restores it to its original place, and no being has any notion of having gone or come, and the world's original characteristics remain as before. Further, from below, passing through worlds as numerous as the sands of the Ganges, he takes up a single buddha-land and lifts it above, past innumerable worlds as numerous as the sands of the Ganges, as one might lift a jujube leaf on the tip of a needle, without disturbing anything. He places Mount Sumeru inside a mustard seed; he puts the four great oceans into a single hair-pore. All of this is likewise. The beings within neither perceive nor know.

The *Du zhu fo jingjie jing* (度諸佛境界經, *Sūtra on Traversing the Realms of All Buddhas*) says:

> The Buddha can cause all the worlds of the ten directions to enter into a single world, and all sentient beings therein feel no constriction. The activities and karmic results of immeasurable, innumerable, inexpressible aeons he can manifest in a single thought-moment. The activities and karmic results of a single thought-moment he can manifest across immeasurable, innumerable, inexpressible aeons.

A verse by Shichuang (True Banner) Bodhisattva says:

> All the tathagatas, with sovereign supernormal power: search for it throughout the three times and it cannot be found.

One should reflect: "I do not even know, through the Buddha's spiritual power, in which buddha-land I currently abide, or within whose hair-pore I dwell. When shall I come to know this?"

**Eight: Manifesting in accord with beings.** The *Shizhu lun* says:

> In a single thought-moment, throughout immeasurable, boundless worlds as numerous as the sands of the Ganges in the ten directions, the Buddha transforms and manifests immeasurable buddha-bodies.

The *Yijingjie jing* (一境界經, *Sūtra on the One Realm*) says:

> What the Tathāgata manifests involves no distinct effort and no distinct intention. According to the natures of sentient beings, they perceive him differently. Just as on the fifteenth night of the month, each person in Jambudvīpa sees the moon directly above, yet the moon does not think, "I am appearing above them."

A verse in the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra* says:

> The Tathāgata's vast body reaches to the very limit of the dharma-realm. Without departing from this seat, he pervades all places.

And:

> The ocean of wisdom, profound, and the ocean of merit universally manifest in the immeasurable lands of the ten directions. According to what sentient beings should see, his radiance pervades everywhere and he turns the Dharma wheel.

One should reflect: "May I one day behold the body that pervades the dharma-realm."

**Nine: The divine eye sees clearly.** The *Shizhu lun* says:

> A great and powerful śrāvaka, with the divine eye, sees a small chiliocosm and also sees the beings within it at the time of birth and the time of death. A pratyekabuddha of lesser power sees ten small chiliocosms and the beings within at birth and death. A pratyekabuddha of middling power sees a hundred small chiliocosms. A pratyekabuddha of great power sees a trichiliocosm and the beings within, their births, deaths, and destinations. The world-honored buddhas see immeasurable, boundless, inconceivable realms.

A verse in a sūtra says:

> The Buddha-eye, vast and boundless, universally sees all lands of the ten directions. The sentient beings therein are beyond reckoning. With great supernormal powers, he tames and liberates them all.

One should reflect: "Now the Tathāgata Amitābha beholds my bodily karma from afar."

**Ten: Hearing all sounds freely.** The *Shizhu lun* says:

> Suppose that in trichiliocosms as numerous as the sands of the Ganges, all beings were to speak at once, and all at once were to play a hundred thousand kinds of music, both far and near: the Buddha could hear them all at will. If he wished to hear only a single sound among them, he could hear just that one, and not the others. He can also hear the most subtle sounds beyond boundless worlds. If he wishes sentient beings to hear, he can make them hear.

One should reflect: "Now the Tathāgata Amitābha surely hears my vocal karma."

**Eleven: Knowledge of others' minds.** The *Shizhu lun* says:

> The Buddha can know the minds of the sentient beings in immeasurable, boundless worlds in the present, together with all the defiled and pure objects of their awareness.

A verse says:

> The minds of sentient beings are universally present throughout the three times. The Tathāgata, in a single thought-moment, completely and clearly knows them all.

One should reflect: "Now the Tathāgata Amitābha surely knows my mental karma."

**Twelve: Knowledge of past lives.** The *Shizhu lun* says:

> If the Buddha wishes to recall his own and all sentient beings' immeasurable, boundless past lives and all that occurred therein, he knows it all completely. There is nothing he does not know, even events of aeons more numerous than the sands of the Ganges: where a person was born, their name, caste, high or low station, food and livelihood, suffering and joy, deeds performed, karmic fruits received, the inclinations of the mind, where one came from. All such matters he can know and see.

A verse says:

> The knowledge of past lives is immeasurable. The divine eye sees the boundless. Among all humans and devas, none can know its limit.

One should reflect: "May the Buddha purify my past karma."

**Thirteen: Unobstructed wisdom.** The *Baoji jing* (*Ratnakūṭa Sūtra*), fascicle thirty-seven, says:

> Suppose someone were to take all the grasses and trees in worlds as numerous as the sands of the Ganges, burn them all into ink, and cast them into the great oceans of worlds equally numerous, grinding them for a hundred thousand years until all became ink. The Buddha would take each drop of ink from the ocean and distinguish and know: this is from such-and-such a world, such-and-such a grass or tree, its root, its stem, its branches, its twigs, its flowers, fruits, and leaves.

> Again, suppose someone took a single hair-tip, dipped it in water to gather one drop, brought it before the Buddha, and said: "I respectfully entrust this drop of water to you. Should I need it later, please return it to me." The Tathāgata then places that drop into the Ganges, where the river's currents, eddies, and turbulence mix and carry it, merging it into the great ocean. After a full hundred years, that person says to the Buddha: "The drop of water I entrusted before, I now request its return." The Tathāgata then, with the tip of one hair-pore, retrieves that very drop from the great ocean.

The same sūtra continues:

> Even if the four continents and all the mountain kings were used as paper, the eight great oceans as ink, all the grasses and trees as brushes, and all humans and devas wrote for an entire aeon, this would not equal even one sixteenth part of the wisdom that Śāriputra has attained. And if all the sentient beings in this trichiliocosm possessed wisdom equal to Śāriputra's without difference, then a bodhisattva's wisdom in fully comprehending the pāramitā of generosity would surpass theirs a hundredfold. And if all beings in the trichiliocosm possessed the wisdom of the pāramitā of generosity, it would not equal the wisdom of a single bodhisattva's pāramitā of morality, and so on through prajñā. And if all the beings in this trichiliocosm possessed the wisdom of all six pāramitās, it would not equal the wisdom of a single first-stage bodhisattva, and so on progressively through the ten stages. And the wisdom of the ten-stage bodhisattvas, compared to the wisdom of Maitreya, a bodhisattva of the stage of one more birth, would not equal even one hundred-thousandth part. And if all the beings in the trichiliocosm all possessed wisdom equal to Maitreya's without difference, then the wisdom of a bodhisattva seated at the place of enlightenment, vanquishing Mara and about to attain perfect awakening, compared to the Buddha's wisdom, would not equal even one hundred-thousand-myriadth part.

The *Baoji jing* also says:

> Suppose all the sentient beings in all the immeasurable, boundless worlds of the ten directions all attained the wisdom of a one-more-birth bodhisattva. If one wished to compare this to even one of the Tathāgata's ten powers, the power of knowing what is possible and what is not, it would not equal even one hundred-thousand-myriadth part, nor could any calculation or analogy express it.

A verse in the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra* says:

> The Tathāgata's most profound wisdom universally penetrates the dharma-realm, able to turn with the three times, becoming a bright path for the world.

A verse by Puming Bodhisattva (Universal Illumination Bodhisattva) praising the Buddha in the same sūtra says:

> Among all dharmas, the gates of Dharma are without limit. Accomplishing omniscience, one enters the profound samādhi of the three karmas.

One should reflect: "May I attain the wisdom-eye of the World-Honored One, foremost in purity."

**Fourteen: The ability to master the mind.** The *Shizhu lun* says:

> Whether the buddhas enter samādhi or do not enter samādhi, if they wish to fix the mind on a single object, they can abide there as long as they wish, at will. From that object they move to another, and they can abide there too, at will. If the Buddha abides in his ordinary mind and does not wish others to know, then none can know. Even if all sentient beings possessed the power to know others' minds like Mahabrahma, like the great śrāvakas, like the pratyekabuddhas, endowed with the wisdom to know others' minds, if they wished to know the Buddha's ordinary mind but the Buddha did not permit it, they could not know.

One should reflect: "May I attain the Buddha's awakening-samādhi."

**Fifteen: Abiding always in settled wisdom.** The same treatise says:

> The buddhas abide in peace and stability, never agitated. Mindfulness constantly dwells in the mind. Why? Because they know first and then act, arising at will in whatever object, abiding in unobstructed action, having severed all afflictions, having transcended the nature of agitation. As the Buddha told Ānanda: "From the night the Buddha attained supreme perfect enlightenment until, having taught all worlds, devas, Mara, Brahma, śramaṇas, and brahmanas, having completed the path of ending suffering, he entered nirvāṇa without remainder: in the entire interval, for all sensations, the Buddha knew their arising and abiding, their birth and cessation. For all perceptions, contacts, cognitions, and thoughts, he likewise knew their arising and abiding, their birth and cessation. Mara the evil one followed the Buddha for seven years, day and night without ceasing, yet could not find a single fault. He never observed the Buddha's mindfulness to be other than settled wisdom."

A verse says:

> His mindfulness is like the great ocean, calm and settled in peace. Nothing in the world can disturb or disrupt it.

One should reflect: "May the Buddha remove my coarse and agitated discursive mind."

**Sixteen: Compassion for sentient beings.** The *Dabanruo jing* (大般若經, *Great Prajñā Sūtra*) says:

> Throughout the worlds of the ten directions, there is not a single sentient being whom the Tathāgata's great compassion does not illuminate.

The *Baoji jing* (*Ratnakūṭa Sūtra*) says:

> Even if, beyond worlds as numerous as the sands of the Ganges, there were but a single sentient being whom the Buddha could benefit, the Tathāgata would go there in person and teach the essential Dharma to bring about that being's awakening.

A verse in the same sūtra says:

> For the sake of a single sentient being, he abides for boundless ocean-aeons, bringing about that being's taming and liberation. Such is the mind of great compassion.

A verse by Mañjuśrī praising the Buddha in the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra* says:

> In each and every hell, through immeasurable aeons, for the sake of liberating sentient beings, he is able to endure such suffering.

A verse in the *Dajing* (大經, *Great Sūtra*) says:

> All sentient beings, because they experience differently, slander the Tathāgata, the Dharma, and the Saṅgha.

The *Da zhidu lun* (大智度論, *Great Treatise on the Perfection of Wisdom*) says:

> The Buddha, with the Buddha-eye, three times by day and three times by night, observes all sentient beings to see who can be liberated, never allowing the right moment to pass.

A certain treatise says:

> Consider the analogy of fish eggs: if the mother does not think of them, the offspring will spoil and die. Sentient beings are the same: if the Buddha does not think of them, their good roots will perish.

A verse in the *Zhuangyan lun* (莊嚴論, *Treatise on Adornment*) says:

> The bodhisattva thinks of sentient beings with a love that penetrates to the marrow of the bones. At all times wishing to benefit them, he regards them as his only child.

Based on these principles, there is a verse of repentance that says:

> Just as parents with a child born blind and deaf cherish it with earnest compassion, never abandoning it but raising and sustaining it: so the child cannot see the parents, yet the parents always see the child. The buddhas regard sentient beings as they regarded Rahula.

> Though sentient beings do not see the Buddha, the Buddha is truly present, guarding and sustaining my roots of goodness, observing my conditions and faculties. When my faculties are ripe, the Buddha will not let the moment pass but will come to guide and receive me.

**Seventeen: Unobstructed eloquence.** The *Shizhu lun* says:

> Suppose all the trichiliocosms within the four continents were filled with beings as numerous as the dust-motes in those worlds, and all possessed, like Śāriputra, like pratyekabuddhas, accomplished wisdom and delight in teaching, with lifespans lasting as many great aeons as those dust-motes. If all these persons, based on the four foundations of mindfulness, were to question the Tathāgata for the entirety of their lives, the Tathāgata could answer all their questions using the meaning of the four foundations of mindfulness alone, never repeating a word, his delight in teaching inexhaustible.

The same text also says:

> If all sentient beings possessed the wisdom and power of pratyekabuddhas, and if they did not act according to the Buddha's intention and wished to convert even a single person, this would be impossible. If these persons were to teach, they could not even sever the subtlest fraction of the fetters of the formless realm. But when the Buddha wishes to liberate sentient beings and teaches, even heterodox teachers, those with wrong views, nagas, yaksas, and all others who do not understand the Buddha's language all call the Buddha the supreme guide.

A verse says:

> In the four kinds of questions and answers, he surpasses all without peer. All sentient beings' questions are easily resolved. Whatever he says at any of the three times is never spoken in vain, but always brings great fruit.

A verse from the *Jingming jing* (淨名經, *Vimalakīrti Sūtra*) says:

> The Buddha teaches the Dharma with a single sound, and sentient beings each understand according to their kind. All think the World-Honored One speaks their own language. This is the supernormal and distinctive quality.

Furthermore, the *Piyu jing* (譬喻經, *Sūtra of Parables*), fascicle three, says:

> King Asoka did not believe in the Buddha. At that time, by the seashore there was a bird called the kalavinka. Its cry was most plaintive and harmonious, faintly resembling one ten-thousandth part of the Buddha's voice. When the king heard its song, he was filled with joy and immediately aroused the aspiration for the supreme Way. The seven thousand women of his palace also aroused the aspiration for the supreme Way. From that time, the king came to believe in the Three Treasures. If a mere bird's voice could liberate beings in this way, how much more the voice of the truly awakened one!

**Eighteen: Contemplating the Buddha's dharma-body.** As the Bodhisattva Mañjuśrī says:

> I observe the Tathāgata as the characteristic of true suchness: unmoving, without action, without discrimination, without differentiated discrimination, neither identical with location nor separate from location, neither existent nor non-existent, neither permanent nor annihilated, neither identical with the three times nor separate from the three times, without birth, without extinction, without going, without coming, without defilement and without non-defilement, without duality and without non-duality. The path of mind and language is cut off. To observe the Tathāgata with these characteristics of true suchness is called truly seeing the Buddha. It is also called paying homage to, drawing near to, and attending the Tathāgata.

> For sentient beings, there is truly one realm of reality. That is, the essence of sentient beings' minds, from the very beginning, is unborn and unextinguished, self-natured, pure, unobstructed, like empty space, beyond discrimination, equal and universally pervading, reaching everywhere, perfectly fulfilling the ten directions, ultimately of one characteristic, without two or difference, unchanging and unvarying, neither increasing nor decreasing. This is because the minds of all sentient beings, all śrāvakas, all pratyekabuddhas, all bodhisattvas, and all buddhas are equally unborn, unextinguished, undefiled, quiescent, and of the characteristic of true suchness. Why? Because in all minds, if one seeks a form or shape of the mind, not the slightest distinction can be found. It is only because of the dark conditioning of sentient beings' ignorance that deluded appearances of objects arise, giving rise to clinging. This mind does not know its own nature, and mistakenly claims that there is an arising of awareness, conceiving of a self and what belongs to a self. But in reality there is no awareness as conceived. Because of this delusion, all afflictions arise.

A verse by the Bodhisattva Yiqie Hui (Universal Wisdom) says:

> The nature of all dharmas is originally empty and quiescent, without grasping, without seeing. When one gains insight, what was originally present is revealed.

**Nineteen: Contemplating the Buddha's virtue as a whole.** As the Bodhisattva Samantabhadra says:

> The merits of the Tathāgata: even if all the buddhas of the ten directions were to praise them for unspeakable, unspeakable numbers of buddha-land aeons, they could not exhaust them.

Regarding the boundless spiritual power of Amitābha Buddha, the *Shuangguan jing* (雙觀經, *Sūtra of the Double Contemplation*) says:

> The spiritual power of Amitāyus Buddha is limitless. All the immeasurable, boundless, inconceivable tathagatas of the ten directions praise him without ceasing.

A verse by Nāgārjuna says:

> The merits of the World-Honored One cannot be fathomed or measured. Just as one who measures space with a ruler can never exhaust it.

A verse praising Amitābha says:

> All buddhas, for immeasurable aeons, praise his merits, yet can never exhaust them. I take refuge in the Pure One.

One should reflect: "May I attain the Buddha's equality as sovereign of the true Dharma."

**Twenty: Joyfully seeking this teaching.** The *Banzhousan jing* (般舟三昧經, *Pratyutpanna-samādhi Sūtra*) says:

> This samādhi is difficult to encounter. Even if one sought it for a hundred billion aeons, merely wishing to hear its name, one could not hear it, let alone learn it, and still less teach it to others.

A verse says:

> I myself recall my past lives, fully numbering sixty thousand years, during which I constantly attended the Dharma-master without ever parting from him, yet never once heard this samādhi. There was a buddha named Endowed with Utmost Sincerity, and at that time a wise bhikṣu named Helin. After that world-honored one entered nirvāṇa, the bhikṣu constantly upheld this samādhi. I was then a king, of noble lineage, and in a dream I heard this samādhi. The bhikṣu Helin had this sūtra. "Your Majesty should receive this meditation from him." Upon waking from the dream, I went to seek it. At once I found the bhikṣu who upheld this samādhi. I then shaved my hair and became a śramaṇa. I studied for eight thousand years before hearing it once. Fully eighty thousand years I attended and made offerings to this bhikṣu. During that time, on account of Mara, obstacles repeatedly arose. Not once was I able to hear it. Therefore, bhikṣus and bhiksunis, laymen and laywomen: I entrust this sūtra to you all. Hear this samādhi and swiftly take it up.

> Always respectfully uphold this teaching.

> For aeons upon aeons this samādhi is difficult to hear. Even if one were to fill worlds as numerous as the sands of the Ganges with precious treasures and give them in charity, if someone receives even a single verse of this teaching and respectfully recites it, that merit surpasses the former.

The *Shuangguan jing* says:

> Even if a great fire filled the entire trichiliocosm, one should pass through it to hear this sūtra. Hearing it, one should rejoice, believe, delight in it, receive it, uphold it, read it, recite it, and practice it as taught. Why? Because many bodhisattvas wish to hear this sūtra but are unable to. If there are sentient beings who hear this sūtra, they will never turn back from the supreme Way.

> Therefore one should single-mindedly recollect the Buddha.

One should reflect:

> Even through a great thousand-world conflagration, even over billions of aeons, one should seek this Dharma. I have now encountered this profound samādhi. How can I retreat in discouragement and fail to practice diligently?

The practitioner should, among all these matters, recall whichever ones are pleasing, whether many or few. If unable to recall them from memory, one should open the text and read it, either selecting passages, or chanting them aloud, or yearning toward them, or prostrating in reverence. In the near term, this serves as a means to make the mind earnest. In the long term, it establishes the karmic connection for seeing the Buddha. In all actions of the three karmas and four deportments, one should never forget the realm of the Buddha.

**Question:** What benefit comes from believing in, accepting, and recollecting the Tathāgata's manifold merits in this way?

**Answer:** The *Du zhu fo jingjie jing* (*Sūtra on Traversing the Realms of All Buddhas*) says:

> Suppose someone were to provide all the buddhas and śrāvaka assemblies throughout dust-mote worlds in the ten directions with the finest food and drink and celestial robes, day after day without ceasing, for aeons as numerous as the sands of the Ganges. Suppose that after those buddhas' passing, for each buddha throughout the ten directions, in each world, one were to erect stupas as numerous as dust-motes, adorned with jewels and various offerings, three times daily, day after day without ceasing, for aeons as numerous as the sands of the Ganges. And suppose one were to instruct measureless beings to offer similar worship. If there were a single person who placed faith in the Tathāgata's inconceivable wisdom and merits, that person's merit would exceed the former.

A verse says:

> The Tathāgata's sovereign power is difficult to encounter in immeasurable aeons. If one gives rise to even a single thought of faith, one swiftly realizes the supreme Way.

The remainder is as discussed in the section below on benefits.

**Question:** An ordinary practitioner's mind follows external objects and shifts constantly. How can one always maintain the mind of buddha-recollection?

**Answer:** If one cannot directly recollect the Buddha, one should use every occasion to arouse and encourage the mind. When playing and laughing, one should wish: "May I, in the jeweled pools and jeweled groves of the Land of Utmost Bliss, enjoy such delight with the celestial and holy assembly." When in sorrow and pain, one should wish: "Together with all beings, may we leave suffering behind and be born in the Land of Utmost Bliss." When encountering someone of noble virtue, one should wish: "May I be born in the Land of Utmost Bliss and attend the World-Honored One in this way." When seeing someone lowly and destitute, one should wish: "May I be born in the Land of Utmost Bliss and benefit all who are solitary and forsaken." Indeed, whenever one sees any person or animal, one should always reflect: "May I, together with this being, be born in the Land of Peace and Bliss." When eating or drinking, one should wish: "May I receive the spontaneous, exquisite food of the Land of Utmost Bliss." Likewise for clothing, bedding, walking, standing, sitting, and lying down, and for all adverse conditions.

---

### 4. Ceasing Evil and Cultivating Good

The *Guanfo sanmei jing* (*Contemplation of the Buddha Samādhi Sūtra*) says:

> For this buddha-recollection samādhi to be accomplished, five conditions are necessary. First, keeping the precepts without transgression. Second, not giving rise to wrong views. Third, not generating arrogance. Fourth, being free of anger and jealousy. Fifth, exerting vigorous effort as though extinguishing a fire on one's own head. Practicing these five, one correctly recollects the wondrous form-body of the buddhas and does not let the mind regress. One should also read and recite the Mahāyāna sūtras. Through this merit and the power of buddha-recollection, one will swiftly attain the vision of the Buddha.

The same sūtra says:

> Through pure precepts, one sees the Buddha's face as in a mirror of pure gold, clear and distinct.

The *Da zhidu lun* (*Great Treatise on the Perfection of Wisdom*) also says:

> The Buddha is like a king of physicians. The Dharma is like excellent medicine. But taking the medicine of the Dharma without observing the precepts cannot cure the disease of afflictions.

A sūtra says:

> If one gives rise to deviant thoughts and conceit, know that such a person is one of overweening pride who destroys and extinguishes the Buddha-Dharma, who causes many sentient beings to give rise to unwholesome minds, who disrupts the harmony of the Saṅgha, who displays anomalies to deceive sentient beings. Such a person is a companion of Mara. Though such an evil person may recollect the Buddha, he loses the taste of ambrosia. Wherever such a person is born, because of conceit, the body will be perpetually small and lowly, born into a base and impoverished family, adorned with innumerable evil karmas. Such are the manifold evils one must guard against.

One should cultivate all good deeds. Without the power of patient endurance and the eye of wisdom, a single thought of anger can incinerate everything without remainder. The *Yijiao jing* (遺教經, *Sūtra of Bequeathed Teachings*) says:

> No thief of meritorious karma is worse than anger.

And it is said elsewhere:

> Nothing damages great benefit more than anger. A single moment of such causes burns and annihilates the good cultivated over billions of vast aeons. Therefore, always and earnestly forsake it.

The *Daji yuezang fen* (大集月藏分, *Moon Treasury Chapter of the Great Collection Sūtra*) teaches the merits of freedom from anger:

> One always dwells with the noble sages.

The transgression of harming others is exceedingly grave. As the *Baoji jing* (*Ratnakūṭa Sūtra*), fascicle ninety-one, says:

> When the Buddha was in the Deer Park, sixty bodhisattvas were present whose karmic obstructions were deep and heavy and whose faculties were dull. They prostrated at the Buddha's feet, weeping with grief, unable to rise. The Buddha said to them: "You should rise. Do not weep and wail, generating great anguish. In the past, in the dispensation of the Buddha Krakucchanda, you went forth as renouncers. You clung to your own learning, precept-keeping, ascetic practices, and contentment with little. At that time there were two Dharma-preaching bhikṣus who had many friends and supporters, and were widely known and well provided for. Out of jealousy, you slandered them with false words, causing their friends and supporters and many sentient beings to lose all trust in them, severing their good roots. Because of this evil karma, for sixty hundred-thousand years you were born in the Avīci hell. When that karma was not yet exhausted, for forty hundred-thousand more years you were born in the Saṃjīva hell. Then for twenty hundred-thousand years in the Kalasutra hell. Then for sixty hundred-thousand years in the Tapana hell. Departing from there, you regained human birth, but for five hundred lifetimes you were born blind. In life after life, you forgot right mindfulness, and your good roots were obstructed. Your appearance was ugly and unpleasant, and no one wished to look at you. You were always born on the periphery, in poverty and lowliness. Departing from there, in the final five hundred years, when the Dharma is about to perish, you will again be born into poor and lowly families in remote regions, destitute and starving, forgetting right mindfulness. Even if you wish to cultivate good, many obstacles will arise. After five hundred years, this evil karma will finally be exhausted. Thereafter you will be born in the Land of Utmost Bliss of Amitābha Buddha. At that time, that Buddha will bestow upon you the prediction of supreme perfect enlightenment."

> When those bodhisattvas heard the Buddha's teaching, every hair on their bodies stood on end and they were deeply moved with regret. Wiping their tears, they said: "From this day until the end of the future, if we should see a follower of the bodhisattva vehicle who has committed a transgression and we expose that fault, then we are deceiving the Tathāgata. From this day until the end of the future, if we see any layperson or renunciant who follows the bodhisattva vehicle enjoying pleasures, amusement, and diversion, we will never seek out their faults. We will always generate faith and respect, regarding them as teachers. From this day until the end of the future, if we do not skillfully subdue our own persons and generate the most humble view of ourselves, as of a candala or a dog, then we are deceiving the Tathāgata. If we display and boast of any meritorious quality of precept-keeping, learning, asceticism, contentment with little, or any other virtue, then we are deceiving the Tathāgata. We will not exalt ourselves for the good we have cultivated, but will confess our transgressions with shame and candor. If we do otherwise, we are deceiving the Tathāgata."

> The Buddha praised them: "Excellent, excellent! With such a resolute mind, all karmic obstructions are entirely eliminated."

One should not, out of attachment to one's own Dharma, disparage others' teachings. Even a person who keeps the precepts, if lacking vigor, will find the practice difficult to accomplish.

A verse in the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra* says:

> Like drilling wood for fire, if one stops before the flame appears, the potential for fire dies with the stopping. So it is with the slothful.

A verse says:

> Merit without direction toward bodhi cannot lead to bodhi. Both together lead to bodhi.

Among those who study and recite this samādhi, there are ten things to practice:

1. Not being jealous of others' gains and offerings.
2. Being loving and respectful to all people, and filial toward elders.
3. Being mindful of repaying kindness.
4. Not forgetting one's words, and keeping away from wrongdoing.
5. Always begging for food and not accepting formal invitations.
6. Being vigorous in walking meditation.
7. Not lying down during the day or night, except when going out.
8. Always wishing to give generously and never regretting it.
9. Entering deep into wisdom without attachment.
10. Revering and serving the Dharma.

The *Shizhu piposha* (十住毘婆沙, *Daśabhūmika-vibhāṣā*), fascicle nine, lists over one hundred and forty kinds of practice. The *Nianfo sanmei jing* (念佛三昧經, *Buddha-Recollection Samādhi Sūtra*) has various further practices.

A verse in the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra*, "Entering the Dharma Realm" chapter, says:

> If one has faith and understanding, free from arrogance, one arouses the aspiration and immediately sees the Tathāgata. If one has a deceitful, impure mind, one may search for a billion aeons and never encounter him.

The *Guanfo jing* (*Contemplation of the Buddha Sūtra*) says:

> During the six periods of day and night, diligently practice six things: sit upright in proper absorption, delight in speaking little, and aside from reading and reciting the sūtras and broadly teaching the Dharma, never utter meaningless words. Constantly recollect the buddhas, mind after mind in succession, so that there is not even a single moment when one does not see the Buddha. Because the mind is focused, one never departs from the Buddha.

The *Yiri moni jing* (一日摩尼經, *Sūtra of the Wish-Fulfilling Jewel of a Single Day*) also teaches:

> A śramaṇa falls into a prison through many causes: or by seeking human attention and desiring offerings, or by having excessive desires and accumulating robes and bowls, or by forming close relationships with laypeople, or by always engaging in such pursuits.

**Question:** What are the relevant precepts?

**Answer:** If we were to enumerate them all extensively, it would only cause practitioners to become discouraged. Therefore I briefly state the essentials. If one firmly upholds the ten major and forty-eight minor bodhisattva precepts, this will certainly assist in the accomplishment of buddha-recollection samādhi. One will also naturally be able to maintain the other practices as well. How much more so if one fulfills the six practices or the ten practices! What practice would not be included? Therefore I do not describe them in detail.

However, strong and coarse delusions and karmic tendencies are something practitioners must be aware of. In particular, meaningless speech, though its fault is not obvious, constantly obstructs the right path. One should carefully counteract it. One may follow the passage in the *Da zhidu lun*:

> Suppose a person's house has caught fire on all four sides. How could one sit inside and speak of other things? In this context, the Buddha says: even speaking of the affairs of śrāvakas and pratyekabuddhas is still like idle talk in a burning house. One should conceive the thought that one is in a burning house, cut off all useless speech, and continuously recollect the Buddha.

**Question:** The *Wangsheng lun* (往生論, *Treatise on Birth in the Pure Land*) sets forth the practice of buddha-recollection as follows: "One should distance oneself from three kinds of dharmas contrary to the gate of bodhi. What are the three? First, relying on the gate of wisdom, one does not seek personal pleasure, distancing oneself from the selfish mind that clings to the self. Second, relying on the gate of compassion, one removes the suffering of all sentient beings, distancing oneself from the mind that gives no peace to sentient beings. Third, relying on the gate of skillful means, one has pity for all sentient beings, distancing oneself from the mind that seeks reverence and offerings for oneself. These are called distancing oneself from the three kinds of dharmas contrary to the gate of bodhi." Thus the bodhisattva, by distancing from these three, fulfills three kinds of dharmas that accord with the gate of bodhi. What are the three? First, the mind of undefiled purity, because one does not seek pleasures for the body. Second, the mind of peaceful purity, because one removes the suffering of all sentient beings. Third, the mind of joyful purity, because one enables all sentient beings to attain great bodhi, thereby gathering sentient beings into birth in that land. Why do you not follow that treatise?

**Answer:** The four great vows discussed earlier already contain these six principles. Though the wording differs, the meaning is complete.

**Question:** If buddha-recollection itself extinguishes transgressions, why must one firmly keep the precepts?

**Answer:** If one were single-mindedly recollecting the Buddha at all times, then indeed your point would stand. However, in a full day of buddha-recollection, if one honestly examines the matter, pure mind accounts for but an ounce or two, while all the rest is turbid and confused. A wild deer is hard to tether, but a household dog is naturally tame. How much more an unrestrained mind: how great are its evils! Therefore one must diligently and energetically uphold the pure precepts, guarding them as one would a precious jewel. If afterward one has regrets, what use are they? Reflect carefully on this.

**Question:** What you say is true. Good karma is something newly learned in this life; even when one delights in it, one easily retreats. But deluded thinking has been habituated over immeasurable aeons; even when one detests it, it still arises. Given this, what skillful means can counteract it?

**Answer:** The remedies are not just one. As the *Cidi chanmen* (次第禪門, *Sequential Gateway to Meditation*) says:

**First, counteracting the obstacle of sinking into dullness and darkness.** One should contemplate the Buddha. Of the thirty-two marks, take up any one, or first take up the mark of the white tuft between the brows and contemplate it with eyes closed. If the mind is dull and dim and the visualization does not form, one should face a beautiful and well-adorned image and single-mindedly apprehend its characteristics, using them as an object for entering samādhi. If it is not clear, open the eyes and look again, then close them once more. In this way, apprehend one mark until it becomes vivid, then progressively contemplate all the marks, causing the mind's eye to open clearly. This destroys the mind of dull drowsiness and sinking darkness. Through the merit of buddha-recollection, karmic obstructions are removed.

**Second, counteracting the obstacle of unwholesome thoughts.** One should recollect the merits of the reward-body Buddha. In right mindfulness, one takes as object the Buddha's ten powers, four fearlessnesses, eighteen distinctive qualities, and omniscient wisdom that illuminates the dharma-realm, eternally quiescent and unmoving, yet universally manifesting form-bodies to benefit all. His merits are immeasurable and inconceivable. Why? Because this merit of buddha-recollection arises from wholesome mental factors that take wholesome dharmas as their object. Unwholesome thoughts arise from evil mental factors that take unwholesome dharmas as their object. Since good can destroy evil, one should recollect the reward-body Buddha. It is like an ugly and unwise person who, in the presence of someone handsome and greatly wise, naturally feels humble and ashamed. Evil is the same: in the presence of a wholesome mind, it becomes ashamed and subsides. Contemplating the Buddha's merits, in every successive thought, all obstructions are extinguished.

**Third, counteracting the obstacle of oppressive external conditions.** One should recollect the dharma-body Buddha. The dharma-body Buddha is the equality of dharma-nature: unborn and unextinguished, without form or color, empty, quiescent, and unconditioned. Within the unconditioned, there are no external conditions. What, then, is the characteristic of oppression? Knowing that all conditions are empty is itself the remedy. If at this point one were to contemplate the thirty-two marks, that would not be a remedy. Why? Because this person, before contemplating the marks, was already disturbed by external conditions. To then further grasp at marks would risk demonic possession and derangement of the mind. Therefore one contemplates emptiness to destroy the marks.

Beyond these three specific remedies, there are also three general remedies.

**First general remedy:** One should clearly understand when afflictions arise, startle the mind awake, scold the afflictions as one would drive away a vicious thief, and guard the three karmas as one would carry a bowl brimming with oil. As the *Liubo luomi jing* (*Six Paramitas Sūtra*) says:

> Sit in the full cross-legged posture, with right mindfulness and contemplation. Take great compassion as your dwelling, wisdom as your drum, and the staff of awakening to strike against the afflictions, declaring: "You afflictions, know this! You thieves of affliction arise from deluded thinking. In the household of the Dharma-King, good things arise not through your doing. You should depart at once! If you do not leave immediately, I will end your existence."

> Having made this declaration, the affliction-thieves disperse of their own accord. Then one should skillfully guard oneself and not be negligent.

A verse from the *Pusa chutai jing* (菩薩處胎經, *Sūtra of the Bodhisattva in the Womb*) says:

> Like a criminal carrying a full bowl of oil: if he spills a single drop, his punishment is execution. Musicians play to his left and right, but fearing death he does not look aside. The bodhisattva cultivating pure contemplation holds the mind firm as vajra. Though reviled, praised, or afflicted, the mind does not waver. Understanding emptiness as originally pure, without this, that, or between.

**Second general remedy:** Use the four propositions to investigate the root source of all afflictions. Ask: does this affliction arise from the mind alone? From conditions alone? From both together? Or from neither? If it arises from the mind alone, it would not require conditions, and one might develop greed or anger toward the fur of a tortoise or the horns of a hare. If it arises from conditions alone, the mind would be unnecessary, and sleeping people should generate afflictions. If it arises from both together, then since each is nothing by itself, how can anything arise when they combine? Just as pressing two grains of sand together yields no oil. Or else, if mind and conditions always combine, there should never be a time when afflictions do not arise. If it arises from neither, then since it is apart from both mind and conditions, how could afflictions suddenly arise? Or else empty space, being apart from both, should constantly produce afflictions. Examining in all these ways, one finds no real arising. They come from nowhere and go nowhere. They are not within, not without, and not in between. They have no location whatsoever. All is like an illusion. Not only the deluded mind, but the observing mind itself is the same. Investigating in this way, the deluded mind naturally dissolves.

A verse in the *Xindi guan jing* (*Mind-Ground Contemplation Sūtra*) says:

> The nature of this mind-dharma fundamentally does not exist. Common people, deluded, claim it is not non-existent. If one can observe that the essence of mind is empty, deluded obstructions neither arise nor come from another, neither arise jointly nor without cause. Therefore know: they are unborn.

One should rely on this verse and extensively use the four propositions.

**Third general remedy:** One should reflect: "My deluded mind is replete with eighty-four thousand gates of affliction. That Amitābha Buddha is replete with eighty-four thousand gates of pāramitā. Both are originally empty, quiescent, one substance, and unobstructed. Desire is itself the Way. Anger and delusion are likewise. Like water and ice, their nature is not different."

Therefore a sūtra says:

> Affliction and bodhi are one in essence. Birth-and-death and nirvāṇa are not in different places.

The verse continues:

> The ice of affliction becomes the water of merit. May the Buddha compassionately regard me. According to the Dharma he has attained, may the powers of samādhi and wisdom be my adornments, and by this may I attain liberation.

Having reflected in this way, one should raise the voice and recollect the Buddha, calling out for rescue and protection. As the *Mohe zhiguan* (*Great Calming and Contemplation*) says:

> It is like someone hauling a heavy load who cannot move forward by strength alone. With assistance from the side, the load is effortlessly lifted. The practitioner is the same: when the mind is weak and cannot overcome its afflictions, one should not force it.

One who wishes to practice the general and specific remedies should understand their intent. One should always be the master of one's mind, and never let the mind be one's master.

**Question:** If a precept-breaker cannot accomplish the samādhi, then how does the *Guanfo sanmei jing* say that this buddha-recollection samādhi is "medicine for all beings who have committed transgressions, and protection for those who have broken the precepts"?

**Answer:** After breaking the precepts, one single-mindedly recollects the Buddha in order to extinguish former transgressions. For this reason it is called medicine. But if one continually transgresses, the samādhi is difficult to accomplish.

---

### 5. Repentance of All Transgressions

If, due to afflictions that confuse and disturb the mind, one has transgressed the precepts, one should not let even a single day pass without cultivating repentance. As the *Dajing* (*Great Sūtra*), fascicle nineteen, says:

> If one conceals a transgression, the transgression grows. If one confesses it openly, the transgression is extinguished.

The *Da zhidu lun* says:

> One whose body, speech, and mind are evil, who does not repent and wishes to see the Buddha, has no basis for doing so.

One should cast oneself to the ground, let tears flow freely, take refuge in Amitābha Buddha, contemplate the white tuft between the brows, confess openly, and weep. One should reflect as follows: "In the past, the Buddha of the Empty Aeon contemplated the white tuft between the brows. Amitābha, by paying reverence, extinguished his transgressions and thereby attained buddhahood. If I now pay reverence to Amitābha, I too shall in the future attain the same."

One should humbly beseech the Buddha's light according to the nature of one's transgressions: that the light of generosity extinguish the sin of miserliness; that the light of precepts extinguish the sin of precept-breaking; that the light of patience extinguish the sin of anger; that the light of vigor extinguish the sin of sloth; that the light of meditation extinguish the sin of distraction; that the light of wisdom extinguish the sin of delusion.

In this way, whether for one day or up to seven days, one can remove the heavy obstructions of afflictions accumulated over hundreds of thousands of aeons.

Or in a brief moment, sitting in meditation and entering samādhi, one may contemplate the white tuft of the Buddha, keeping the mind clear, free from confused thoughts, settling in right abiding, with attention unbroken. This removes the sins of birth and death accumulated over ninety-six hundred million nayutas of aeons.

Or one may single-mindedly recite that Buddha's dhāraṇī. A single recitation can extinguish the four grave offenses and the five heinous transgressions. Seven recitations can extinguish all manner of sins.

One may also practice the repentance of principle. A verse says:

> All sins are by nature like suchness. Through inverted thinking and deluded mind they arise. The characteristics of such sins are originally empty. Throughout the three times, nothing is obtained. They are not within, not without, and not in between. Their nature and characteristics are suchness, both unmoving. True and wondrous principle transcends words. Only the holy wisdom can penetrate it. Neither existent nor non-existent, neither both nor neither, beyond name and form. All buddhas are originally one substance. May all buddhas bestow their protective grace and extinguish all inverted minds. May I soon awaken to the source of true nature and swiftly realize the Tathāgata's supreme Way.

**Question:** If contemplative buddha-recollection itself can extinguish sins, why additionally practice the repentance of principle?

**Answer:** Who says one must practice each one separately? One practices whichever suits one's inclination. Moreover, to contemplate that the nature of all sins is empty and without substance is itself true and authentic buddha-recollection samādhi. As a verse in the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra* says:

> The present is not a compounding, the future is likewise. All dharmas are without characteristics. This is the true body of the Buddha.

The *Fozang jing* (佛藏經, *Buddha Treasury Sūtra*), in the chapter on buddha-recollection, says:

> To see that there is nothing to be seen is called buddha-recollection. To see the true characteristic of all dharmas is called buddha-recollection.

The contemplation of no-form and of equality: by the principle of such passages, all should be subsumed within buddha-recollection samādhi.

**Question:** What supreme merit does such repentance possess?

**Answer:** A verse in the *Xindi guan jing* (*Mind-Ground Contemplation Sūtra*) says:

> The household life produces the causes of affliction. The monastic life also may breach the pure precepts. Yet if one repents according to the Dharma as in the hell of the dharma-realm, repentance can open the flower of bodhi. Repentance can reveal the Buddha's great perfect mirror. Repentance can bring one to the place of treasures.

**Question:** Which of these two, repentance through practice and repentance through principle, is the more excellent?

**Answer:** For a single person, what suits their faculties is best. Speaking generally, repentance through principle is superior. The *Rulai mimi zang jing* (如來祕密藏經, *Sūtra of the Tathāgata's Secret Treasury*), lower fascicle, records the Buddha telling Kāśyapa:

> Even a minor unwholesome deed, if one rigidly clings to it, fixates on it, and grasps it firmly, I declare to be a transgression. Kāśyapa, even the five heinous transgressions, if one does not rigidly cling to, fixate on, or grasp them, and does not give rise to views about them, I do not declare to be transgressions. How much less other minor unwholesome actions.

> Kāśyapa, I do not say that one is troubled by unwholesome dharmas. Bodhi is attained through understanding causes and conditions. Kāśyapa, what does it mean to understand the afflictions that arise from causes and conditions? To understand that they are without self-nature, that what arises from conditions is unborn. To understand in this way is called attaining bodhi.

The *Jueding pini jing* (決定毘尼經, *Sūtra of Definitive Vinaya*) says:

> For one who has aroused the practice of the Mahāyāna: if in the first part of the day one transgresses a precept, yet in the middle part of the day one does not abandon the mind of omniscient wisdom, then that bodhisattva's body of precepts is not damaged. If in the middle part of the day one transgresses, yet in the latter part of the day or in the latter part of the night one does not abandon the mind of omniscient wisdom, then that bodhisattva's body of precepts is not damaged. Therefore, for one who follows the bodhisattva vehicle and observes the precepts of permission and prohibition, even if one transgresses, one should not lose mindfulness or generate vain worry and distress, tormenting the mind. For transgressions within the śrāvaka vehicle, the explanation is given elsewhere.

This "mind corresponding to ultimate emptiness" may also be understood as the mind that aspires to the Buddha's omniscient wisdom.

**Question:** If practicing repentance can extinguish all transgressions, then how does the *Da zhidu lun*, fascicle forty-six, say: "According to the Vinaya, even minor precept transgressions are purified through repentance. But for transgressions against the ten wholesome precepts, even with repentance, the sins leading to the three evil paths are not removed"? And the *Shilun jing* (十輪經, *Daśacakra Sūtra*) says that the sin of creating the ten evil wheels cannot be rescued even by all the buddhas.

**Answer:** The *Guan jing* (*Contemplation Sūtra*) says that ten recitations can extinguish the five heinous transgressions. The *Guanfo jing* says that a single contemplation of the Buddha's marks can extinguish the ten evils and five heinous sins. The *Dajing* relates that King Ajātaśatru repented and was freed from the sin of patricide. The *Bore jing* (般若經, *Prajñā Sūtra*) says that reading, reciting, and explaining the prajñā can extinguish even the sin of killing all beings in the three realms, and one will not fall into evil destinies. The *Avataṃsaka Sūtra* says that reciting the vows of Samantabhadra can, in a single thought, extinguish the ten evils and five heinous transgressions. Clearly, the Mahāyāna truly teaches that no sin cannot be extinguished.

As for the passage in the treatise, it may mean that heavy sins are received in a lighter form, not that they are entirely uneradicated. Or it may be a teaching according to one particular doctrinal perspective.

Master Huaigan also reconciles these passages in his section on analyzing the modes of buddha-recollection. All of these are cases of repentance practiced at specific times.

Nevertheless, the practitioner should always cultivate three things. As the *Da zhidu lun* says:

> The bodhisattva must, following each session of prostrations day and night, cultivate these practices.

A verse of repentance from the *Shizhu piposha* (*Daśabhūmika-vibhāṣā*) says:

> The buddhas of the ten directions are immeasurable, their knowledge all-encompassing. Before them all, I now confess and lay bare all my dark evils. The three threes make nine kinds, arising from the three afflictions. Whether of this body or of former bodies, all these sins I entirely repent. Among the three evil destinies, whatever suffering I should receive, and before all the buddhas now in the world who have attained awakening, I beseech them to turn the Dharma wheel, bringing peace and bliss to all sentient beings. All the buddhas of the ten directions, if they intend to abandon their lifespans, I now bow my head to the ground and beseech them to remain. I urge the cultivation of practice, meditation, and the discipline. Whatever merit arises from body, speech, and mind, past, present, and future, in all the practitioners of the three vehicles, and in all the merit of common beings, I rejoice in it all and dedicate it.

Each of these has its own textual source; one may use whichever one chooses. If one prefers brevity, one may use the single verse from the *Mile pusa benyuan jing* (彌勒菩薩本願經, *Sūtra of Maitreya Bodhisattva's Original Vow*). That sūtra says:

> The Buddha said to Ānanda: "When Maitreya Bodhisattva first sought the Way, he did not offer ears, nose, head, eyes, hands, feet, body, or life, nor precious treasures, cities, wife and children, or kingdom, in charity to others to attain buddhahood. He attained the supreme, true Way solely through wholesome roots and the practice of peace and bliss." Ānanda asked the Buddha: "Through what wholesome roots did Maitreya Bodhisattva attain the Way of the Buddha?" The Buddha said to Ānanda: "Maitreya Bodhisattva, three times by day and three times by night, adjusting his robes and composing himself, joins palms with right knee on the ground, faces the ten directions, and speaks this verse:

> I repent all my transgressions. I rejoice in and encourage all virtuous deeds of beings. I take refuge in and pay homage to all buddhas. May I attain unsurpassed wisdom."

> The Buddha said to Ānanda: "Through this wholesome root and such practice, Maitreya Bodhisattva attained the Way."

**Question:** How much merit does this produce?

**Answer:** A verse in the *Shizhu lun* says:

> If this merit, at any single moment, were to take physical form, the worlds as numerous as the sands of the Ganges could not contain it.

---

### 6. Counteracting Demonic Hindrances

**Question:** Various kinds of demonic hindrances can obstruct the right path. They may cause illness, or cause one to lose contemplation, or cause one to fall into wrong teachings. These include: views of existence, views of non-existence, clarity and confusion, wrong samādhi and scattered grasping, grief and joy, suffering and pleasure, misfortune and fortune, evil and good, aversion and attachment, rigidity and laxity of mind. In all such matters, whether excessive or deficient, all are demonic hindrances that obstruct the right path. How should one counteract them?

**Answer:** Though the remedies are many, here one should rely solely on buddha-recollection as the remedy. Within this, there is both the phenomenal and the noumenal approach.

**First, the phenomenal approach:** When words and actions are in accord and one recollects the Buddha with undivided mind, all evil maras cannot undermine one.

**Question:** Why can they not?

**Answer:** Because the Buddha protects one; because of the authority of the Dharma. They cannot undermine one. As the *Dabanruo jing* (*Great Prajñā Sūtra*) teaches regarding the counteracting of demonic hindrances, it presents pairs of factors, among which it says: first, one can accomplish all that one says; second, one is always protected by all the buddhas. Furthermore, the *Banzhousan jing* (*Pratyutpanna-samādhi Sūtra*) says:

> If yaksas, ghosts, or spirits seek to disrupt one's meditation and steal one's mindfulness, or seek to enter through a gate of vulnerability, they cannot.

**Second, the noumenal approach:** As the *Mohe zhiguan*, fascicle eight, says:

> Know that the realm of Mara is like the realm of the Buddha. One suchness, not two. Equal and of one characteristic. One does not make the realm of Mara into something other. But sentient beings do not know this. Confused about the realm of the Buddha, they erroneously create the realm of Mara. Within bodhi, they generate afflictions. Therefore compassion arises, and one wishes to enable all beings to realize that the realm of Mara is the realm of the Buddha, that affliction is bodhi.

Therefore what arises is identical with emptiness and signlessness. "All dharmas are without characteristics. This is the true body of the Buddha." One should know that the realm of Mara is the body of the Buddha and also one's own body. In principle, there is no duality. Yet sentient beings, in deluded dreaming, not yet awakened, do not understand the one true characteristic, and generate thoughts of right and wrong, cycling through the five destinies. "May all beings enter the wisdom of equality." Deeply generating unconditioned great compassion in this way, and though one contemplates the Buddha's wondrous form-body, one should enter the three gates of liberation and not cling to it. Like a red-hot golden ball: though one sees its beautiful color, one must not grasp it. How much less should one generate attachment or pride regarding other things. When one contemplates in this way, Mara cannot undermine one.

Therefore the *Dabanruo jing* also states its remedy:

> First, contemplate that all dharmas are ultimately empty. Second, do not abandon any sentient being.

The *Da zhidu lun* also says:

> The twelve sense-fields are all Mara's net, illusory and unreal. Within them arise the six kinds of consciousness, which are also Mara's illusory net. What, then, is real? Only the dharma of non-duality. No eye, no form, and so on through no mind and no dharma. This is what is real. In order to free sentient beings from the twelve sense-fields, the Buddha constantly uses various causes and conditions to teach them.

The same treatise says:

> In all dharmas, one is without attachment. Because one is without attachment, there are no errors. Because there are no errors, Mara cannot find an opening. It is like a person whose body has no wounds: though lying amid poisonous powder, the poison cannot enter. But if there is the slightest wound, it would be dangerous.

Those who have aroused the aspiration for bodhi and received the prediction of future buddhahood have made this vow: "We shall guard and protect the disciples of the Buddha, present and future, who abide in correspondence with the ultimate meaning. We shall provide for and serve them. If anyone does not follow our instruction and disturbs the practitioner, we shall cause that one to receive various forms of protective care."

Both of these two remedies are supported by scriptural evidence, so I do not additionally cite the explanations of the various masters.

---

### 7. Summary of the Essentials of Practice

**Question:** In the sections above, much has been presented. What, after all, is the essential karma for birth in the Pure Land?

**Answer:**

> The great mind of bodhi, guarding the three karmas, deep faith, sincerity, and constant buddha-recollection: by these, in accordance with one's aspiration, one will certainly be born in the Land of Utmost Bliss. How much more so if one additionally cultivates all the other excellent practices.

**Question:** Why are these the essentials for birth in the Pure Land?

**Answer:** The meaning of the bodhi-mind has been fully explained above. The three karmas, being the source of grave evil, can obstruct the right path; therefore one must guard them. The essential karma for birth in the Pure Land is buddha-recollection as its foundation. The mind of buddha-recollection must accord with principle; therefore it is endowed with three things: deep faith, sincerity, and constancy.

Constant practice has three benefits. As Jiacai says:

> First, all unwholesome thoughts and mental stirrings will ultimately never arise, and one will also extinguish karmic obstructions. Second, one's good roots will grow and increase, and one will also plant the karmic seeds of seeing the Buddha. Third, through repeated cultivation, the seeds will ripen, and at the moment of death, right mindfulness will be present.

Guarding the three karmas is the good of cessation. Reciting the Buddha's name is the good of action. The bodhi-mind and the vow assist and support these two goods.

Therefore, these practices are the essentials for birth in the Pure Land. Their principles are drawn from the sūtras and treatises, which cannot be cited in full.

---

## Gate Six: Buddha-Recollection on Special Occasions

The sixth major topic, buddha-recollection on special occasions, has two parts. First, an explanation of periodic special practice. Second, an explanation of the deathbed rite.

### Part One: Periodic Special Practice

In the course of one's daily practice, it is difficult to sustain constant vigor. Therefore, one should from time to time undertake special intensive practice: for one, two, or three days, up to seven days, or for ten days, up to ninety days, practicing as one is inclined.

#### The Practice of One to Seven Days

Regarding the practice of one to seven days, Master Shandao's *Guannianmen* (觀念法門, *Method of Contemplation and Recollection*) quotes the *Pratyutpanna-samādhi Sūtra* (*Banzhou sanmei jing*):

> The Buddha told Bhadrapāla: "If you maintain this practice, you will attain samādhi, and all the buddhas of the present will stand before you. Whether bhikṣu, bhikṣunī, upāsaka, or upāsikā, if one keeps the precepts fully and intact, dwells alone in a single place, and is mindful of Amitābha Buddha of the western direction, who now presides there, one should be mindful in accord with what one has heard: that his land, named Sukhāvatī, lies ten trillion buddha-lands from here. Concentrate the mind single-pointedly for one day and one night, or for seven days and seven nights. After seven days one will see him, just as one sees things in a dream, without distinguishing day from night, without distinguishing inside from outside. It is not because of darkness or obstruction that one does not see."

> "Bhadrapāla, when the four assemblies constantly engage in this mindfulness, all the great mountains, Sumeru, and dark places throughout the buddha-realms are opened and cleared away, without any obstruction. The four assemblies do not use the divine eye to see through, do not use the divine ear to hear through, do not use supernormal powers to travel to that buddha-land, and do not die here and be born there. Rather, while sitting right here, they see him."

> The Buddha said: "The four assemblies in this world are mindful of Amitābha Buddha. Because of their single-minded mindfulness, they are able to see him. They then ask: 'By what practice does one attain birth in this land?' Amitābha Buddha replies: 'Those who wish to be born here should constantly recite my name without ceasing. Then they will attain birth.' The Buddha says: 'Through single-minded mindfulness one attains birth. One should be mindful of the Buddha's body with its thirty-two major marks, eighty minor marks, and immeasurable radiance, perfectly upright and without compare, expounding the Dharma amid the assembly of bodhisattvas. Do not let this visualization deteriorate.'"

#### Practical Arrangements

Because the method of visualizing the Buddha's physical form requires preparation, one must first arrange the practice hall and install a sacred image, with fragrant water for cleaning and flowers for adornment. If there is no buddha-hall, a clean room will also suffice; sweep and sprinkle it. Install a single buddha image against the western wall.

The practitioners should select one of four periods each month: from the first to the eighth, from the eighth to the fifteenth, from the fifteenth to the twenty-third, or from the twenty-third to the thirtieth. Each month, four such sessions are ideal. Practitioners should assess the weight of their household obligations and, during these periods, enter the purified practice hall. Whether for one day or up to seven days, throughout the entire time one must wear clean clothes. Shoes and sandals must also be new and clean. During the seven days one eats only one meal a day, maintaining a strict vegetarian fast. Whether soft cakes or plain rice, one eats simply, with modest sauces and greens, moderate in quantity.

Within the practice hall, day and night, one restrains the mind in continuous, single-pointed mindfulness of Amitābha Buddha, keeping voice and mind in unbroken succession. One only sits or stands. During the seven days, one must not sleep. Nor should one engage in the regular schedule of prostrations or sūtra-chanting. One need not even hold a rosary. Simply join the palms and recite the Buddha's name, moment after moment visualizing that one sees the Buddha.

The Buddha says:

> "Visualize Amitābha Buddha's body of true gold, with radiance shining everywhere, perfectly upright and without compare, present before the eyes of the mind."

At the time of recollecting the Buddha, if standing, then stand and recite ten or twenty thousand times. If sitting, then sit and recite ten or twenty thousand times. Within the practice hall, do not gather in whispered conversation. Day and night, at three or six intervals, make formal declarations before all buddhas, all worthy sages, the officials of heaven, and the judges of the underworld. Confess and repent all the evil karma created through body, speech, and mind throughout this life. Confess each matter truthfully. When the confession is complete, resume the practice of buddha-recollection according to the proper method. Whatever visionary experiences arise, do not casually speak of them. If they are auspicious, one will know for oneself. If they are inauspicious, repent.

One must make a solemn vow not to touch or consume alcohol, meat, or the five pungent plants. If one violates this pledge, may one's body and mouth break out in terrible sores. One should vow to recite the *Amitābha Sūtra* one hundred thousand times in total, reciting the Buddha's name ten thousand times each day, and reciting the sūtra fifteen times, twenty times, or thirty times daily, according to one's capacity. Vow to be born in the Pure Land and pray that the Buddha will receive and protect you.

Furthermore, for those practitioners who wish throughout this present life to engage day and night in continuous single-pointed recollection of Amitābha Buddha, continuously reciting the *Amitābha Sūtra*, praising, venerating, and aspiring to birth among the holy assembly and adornments of the Pure Land: apart from entering the samādhi practice hall, one should recite Amitābha Buddha's name ten thousand times daily. Those who continue this without interruption until the end of life will receive Amitābha's added protection and the removal of karmic obstructions. Moreover, the Buddha and the holy assembly will constantly come to guard and sustain them. Having received this protection, one's lifespan will be extended and one will dwell in peace and happiness. These connections are described in detail in the *Piyu jing* (*Sūtra of Parables*), the *Weiwu sanmei jing* (*Sūtra of the Samādhi of Fearlessness*), the *Jingdu sanmei jing* (*Sūtra of the Samādhi of Pure Crossing*), and other texts.

Furthermore, the *Guanfo jing* (*Sūtra on the Contemplation of the Buddha*) says:

> "If any bhikṣu, bhikṣunī, man, or woman has committed the four grave offenses, the ten evil acts, or the five heinous crimes, or has slandered the Mahāyāna, such persons, if they are able to repent, prostrating their entire bodies to the ground six times daily and nightly like a great mountain collapsing, weeping tears like rain, joining palms before the Buddha, and contemplating the curl of white hair between the Buddha's brows, for one to seven days, then the former four kinds of offenses can be lightened."

If one contemplates the white hair curl but cannot see it clearly, one should enter a stūpa and contemplate the mark of white hair between the brows of a buddha image.

The seven-day practice passage also says:

> "If good men and good women, with minds free of doubt, for seven days bathe and purify themselves, wear new clean clothes, offer flowers and incense, and with single-pointed correct mindfulness contemplate the Tathāgata's merits and great awesome powers as described above, then the Tathāgata, with compassion and protective care, will manifest his body and allow them to see him, and their vows will be fulfilled."

> "Even if one lacks flowers, incense, and other offerings, merely recollecting the merits described above will suffice, for the Tathāgata's great compassion and great kindness, his unobstructed teaching of the Dharma, his power in a single moment of meditation to manifest limitless emanation bodies, his divine eye, divine ear, knowledge of others' minds, unfailing memory, freedom from defilement, and mastery over all dharmas, these are his equal merits and awesome powers."

The *Daiji xianhu jing* (*Bhadrapāla Sūtra* of the *Mahāsaṃnipāta*) also describes a seven-day practice, which will be explained below in the section on benefits.

Furthermore, Master Jiācái's *Jingtu lun* (*Treatise on the Pure Land*) reports that Meditation Master Daochuo examined the scriptural texts and determined that anyone who can recite the Buddha's name single-mindedly, without confusion, and reaches one million recitations, will certainly attain birth in the Pure Land. Master Daochuo also practiced seven-day buddha-recollection according to the shorter *Amitābha Sūtra* and verified that one million recitations could be completed. Thus the *Mahāsaṃnipāta Sūtra*, the *Bhaiṣajyaguru Sūtra*, and the shorter *Amitābha Sūtra* all encourage seven-day buddha-recollection. The significance of this will be made clear in the following section on benefits.

#### The Ninety-Day Practice

Regarding the ninety-day practice, the *Mohe zhiguan* (*Great Calming and Contemplation*), fascicle two, states:

The constantly-walking samādhi first explains the method and then encourages practice. The method addresses the body's activity and restraint, the mouth's speech and silence, and the mind's calming and contemplation. This method derives from the *Pratyutpanna-samādhi Sūtra*. "Pratyutpanna" is translated as "the buddha stands before one." There are three meanings to the buddha standing: first, the Buddha's awesome power; second, the power of samādhi; third, the power of the practitioner's own accumulated merit. Through these, within meditation one sees all the present buddhas of the ten directions standing before one. It is like a person with clear eyesight gazing at the stars on a clear night: one sees the buddhas of the ten directions, just as numerous as those stars. This is why it is called the samādhi of the buddha standing before one.

A verse in the *Daśabhūmika-vibhāṣā* (*Shizhu piposha lun*) says:

> This samādhi has various dwelling places,
> Distinguished as lesser, middling, and great.
> These various aspects
> Should also be discussed.

"Dwelling places" means that this power may arise in the first, second, third, or fourth dhyāna, or in the intermediate stages. "Lesser" is the first dhyāna, "middling" the second, and "great" the third and fourth. Or "lesser" means abiding for a short time, "middling" means seeing few worlds, "greater" means seeing few buddhas, and so on for middling and great.

For the body, one practices constant walking. During this practice one avoids evil companions, foolish people, relatives, and townspeople. One always dwells alone. One does not hope for anything from others and seeks nothing. One always begs for food and does not accept special invitations. One adorns the practice hall and prepares all offerings: fragrant delicacies, sweet fruit. One bathes the body. Coming and going, one changes clothes. One walks exclusively in circumambulation. Ninety days constitute one period. One must have a clear-sighted teacher, learned in both the inner and outer disciplines, who can remove obstacles. Toward the teacher from whom one has heard this samādhi, one should regard that teacher as one would regard the World-Honored One. One does not resent, does not become angry, does not see faults or virtues. One should be willing to cut one's own flesh and offer it to the teacher, to say nothing of other forms of service. One serves the teacher as a servant serves a great lord. If one gives rise to resentment toward the teacher, one will never attain this samādhi.

One needs an external protector, like a mother nurturing a child. One needs fellow practitioners, like companions crossing a dangerous pass together. One must make a solemn pledge and vow: "Even if my sinews and bones wither away, until I have attained this samādhi I will never rest." One arouses great faith that nothing can destroy, great diligence that nothing can match, and wisdom that nothing can overtake. One constantly follows a good teacher. For the entire three months, not even for the duration of a finger-snap may one entertain worldly thoughts or desires. For the entire three months, not even for a finger-snap may one lie down. For the entire three months, one walks without ceasing, except when sitting to eat or attending to bodily needs. One may expound the sūtras for others but must not seek clothing or food in return.

A verse in the *Vibhāṣā* says:

> Draw near to good teachers,
> Practice diligently without sloth,
> With wisdom firm and stable,
> And faith-power unwavering.

For the mouth's speech and silence: for ninety days the body constantly walks without rest; for ninety days the mouth constantly chants the name of Amitābha Buddha without rest; for ninety days the mind constantly recollects Amitābha Buddha without rest. One may combine chanting and recollection, or first recollect and then chant, or first chant and then recollect. Chanting and recollection follow one upon the other without a moment's rest. When one chants the name of Amitābha, this is equivalent to chanting the merits of all the buddhas of the ten directions. But one takes Amitābha exclusively as the primary focus of this Dharma-gate. In brief, step by step, sound by sound, moment by moment, one dwells solely on Amitābha Buddha.

For the mind's calming and contemplation: one recollects Amitābha Buddha of the western direction, whose land lies ten trillion buddha-lands from here, a realm of jeweled ground, jeweled pools, jeweled trees, and jeweled halls. Amid the assembly of bodhisattvas, the Buddha sits in the center and expounds the sūtras. For three months one constantly recollects the Buddha. How does one recollect?
