One recollects the thirty-two marks, beginning from the thousand-spoked wheel marks on the soles of the feet, contemplating each mark in ascending order up to the uṣṇīṣa. Then one should also proceed from the uṣṇīṣa in descending order down to the thousand-spoked wheels. One aspires: "May I too attain these marks."

Then one further recollects: "I should attain buddhahood through the mind. I should attain buddhahood through the body. The Buddha is not attained through the mind. The Buddha is not attained through body and mind. The Buddha's form is not attained through the mind. The Buddha's mind is not attained through form. Why? The mind: the Buddha has no mind. Form: the Buddha has no form. Therefore one does not attain the three kinds of perfect awakening through form and mind. The Buddha's form is already extinguished, and so too consciousness is extinguished."

> "What the Buddha calls 'extinguished,' the foolish do not understand, but the wise comprehend. One does not attain buddhahood through body or speech. One does not attain buddhahood through wisdom. Why? Wisdom, when sought, cannot be found. The self, when sought, is utterly unfindable, and there is nothing to be seen. All dharmas are originally without any existence. The original thought is destroyed, and one does not know where it has gone. To recollect the Buddha in this way,"

> "is like a woman named Sumati in Śrāvastī, who heard of it, rejoiced in her mind, and at night dreamed she engaged in it. When she awoke, she recollected. He did not come, and I did not go, yet the delightful experience occurred. One should recollect the Buddha in this way."

> "It is like a person traveling through a great wilderness who, hungry and thirsty, dreams of a fine meal. Upon waking, the belly is empty. One reflects: all that exists is like a dream. One should recollect the Buddha in this way."

> "Recollect ceaselessly. Through this recollection, one will be born in the land of Amitābha Buddha. This is called recollection through contemplation of characteristics."

> "It is like placing jewels upon lapis lazuli: their reflections appear within it. It is like a bhikṣu contemplating bones: various lights arise from the bones. No one has brought these; neither are these bones real. It is all made by the mind. It is like an image in a mirror: it does not come from outside, nor is it produced within. Because the mirror is clean, one sees one's own form."

> "When the practitioner's mind is pure, all that exists is pure. Wishing to see the Buddha, one immediately sees. When the Buddha is seen, where does he come from? I too have nowhere that I go. What I recollect, I immediately see. The mind creates the Buddha. The mind itself sees the mind. The mind that sees the Buddha: that mind is the Buddha's mind, that mind is my own mind. The mind that sees the Buddha does not itself know the mind. The mind cannot itself see the mind. When the mind gives rise to conceptualization, this is delusion. When the mind is without conceptualization, this is nirvāṇa."

> "These dharmas cannot be demonstrated to anyone. All is accomplished through recollection. If one does not understand the mind, there is a mind yet the mind does not see the mind. When the mind gives rise to conceptual thinking, that is delusion. When there is no conceptualization, that is nirvāṇa."

> "All buddhas attain liberation from the mind. The mind is without defilement and is called 'pure.' In the five destinies it is unblemished and does not receive form. Whoever understands this accomplishes the great Way."

> "This is called the Buddha's seal. Without craving, without attachment, without seeking, without conceptualization. All that exists is exhausted, all that is desired is exhausted. There is nowhere from which it arises. There is nothing that can be extinguished. Nothing is ruined or destroyed. This is the essential point of the Way, the root of the Way. This seal cannot be broken by the two vehicles."

Through recollecting the marks, the various powers, and the forty dharmas unique to a buddha, one attains the lesser degree of power. Through recollecting the marks and the dharma-body, one attains the middling degree. Through recollecting the true aspect of the Buddha, one attains the highest degree, no longer attached to either the form body or the dharma body.

A verse says:

> Do not cling to the form body;
> To the dharma body also do not cling.
> Thoroughly know all dharmas:
> Eternally quiescent, like empty space.

As for the encouragement to practice: if one wishes to attain wisdom vast as the great ocean, so that no one can serve as one's teacher; if one wishes, without exercising supernormal powers, to see all the buddhas while seated right here, to hear all they teach, and to be able to receive and retain it all, then the constantly-walking samādhi is supreme among all meritorious practices.

> "This samādhi is the mother of all buddhas, the eye of all buddhas, the father of all buddhas. It is born from birthlessness and great compassion, as from a mother. All tathāgatas are born from these two dharmas."

> "If you were to grind the great earth of a billion worlds, together with all its grasses and trees, into dust, making each mote of dust into a single buddha-land, and filled all those worlds with treasures to be offered as gifts, that merit would be immense. Yet it would not compare to hearing this samādhi without being startled or frightened, much less to believing, receiving, upholding, reading, reciting, and expounding it to others. Much less to practicing it in settled concentration for even the brief time it takes to milk a cow. Much less to fully accomplishing this samādhi. The merit is therefore immeasurable and boundless."

The *Vibhāṣā* says:

> "Neither the fire at the end of a kalpa, nor officials, bandits, enemies, poisonous dragons, wild beasts, or various illnesses can harm such a person. This is impossible. This person is constantly protected by devas, nāgas, and the eight classes of beings, and all buddhas together guard, sustain, praise, and commend this person. All wish to see this person and come to where he is."

> "If one hears this samādhi and rejoices in the four kinds of merit described above, then all buddhas and bodhisattvas of the three times will also rejoice. The merit of this rejoicing surpasses even the four kinds of merit mentioned above."

> "If one fails to practice this method, one loses immeasurable precious treasures. The beings of heaven and the human world grieve on account of it. It is like one with a blocked nose who holds sandalwood yet cannot smell it."

### Part Two: The Deathbed Rite

#### First: Practical Arrangements

The *Sifenlü chao* (四分律鈔, *Commentary on the Four-Part Vinaya*), in its chapter on tending the sick and sending off the dead, quotes from Chinese monastic tradition:

> In the northwest corner of the Jetavana, at the place where the sunlight vanishes, there is established a Hall of Impermanence. When anyone falls ill, they are brought there. Ordinary people develop attachments. When they see the room they have lived in, their robes, bowls, and possessions, they mostly grow nostalgic and have no mind for renunciation. Therefore it is required that they be taken to a separate place. The hall is called "Impermanence" because the many who come rarely return. By the very nature of the situation, one devotes the mind single-pointedly to recollecting the Dharma. In the hall, a standing buddha image is installed, coated in gold leaf, facing west. The image's right hand is raised, and from its left hand a five-colored banner is suspended, with its end trailing along the ground. The dying person is placed behind the image. With the left hand the dying person grasps the end of the banner, with the intention of following the Buddha to go to the Buddha's pure land. Those attending the sick burn incense, scatter flowers, and adorn the room of the dying person.

Even if there is filth such as excrement, urine, or vomit, [the attendants should not leave but continue their care]. Whether the sick person or those who are healthy, when the time of death approaches, all should follow the method of buddha-recollection samādhi described above. The dying person should face directly toward the west, both body and mind oriented westward. The mind should be concentrated in contemplation and visualization of Amitābha Buddha. Voice and mind should correspond, with each utterance following without interruption. The dying person should firmly form the resolve to be reborn, visualizing the jeweled lotus platform and the holy assembly coming to welcome and receive them.

If the dying person sees a visionary realm, they should describe it to the attendants. Once the attendants have heard it, they should record it in writing. If the dying person can no longer speak, the attendant must frequently ask what realm the dying person is seeing. If the dying person describes signs of wrongdoing, the attendants should immediately recite the Buddha's name on their behalf, assisting in repentance, and must ensure that the wrongdoing is eradicated. Once the wrongdoing is removed, the jeweled lotus platform and the holy assembly should appear before the dying person, and this should be recorded as before.

Furthermore, when relatives and family members come to visit the dying person, no one who has consumed alcohol, meat, or the five pungent plants should be permitted near the sick person. If such a person approaches, the dying person will lose right mindfulness, ghosts and spirits will create disturbance, and the dying person will die in madness and fall into the three evil destinies. Therefore, practitioners should exercise great caution, uphold the Buddha's teaching, and act in concord.

As the *Mahāprajnāpāramitā-upadeśa* (*Dazhidu lun*) explains regarding the supernormal power of mental transformation:

> "By taking up the characteristic of earth many times, one can walk on water as on earth. By taking up the characteristic of water many times, one can enter earth as into water."

By applying this principle of taking up a characteristic and allowing it to assist the result, one can achieve one's aim. This applies not only to the moment of death but to ordinary times as well.

Master Daochuo said:

> "Ten moments of continuous mindfulness may seem not difficult. Yet the minds of ordinary beings are like wild horses, and their consciousness is fiercer than a monkey, racing through the six sense-objects without ever stopping. Each person must sincerely establish faith and resolve in advance, disciplining the mind so that accumulated practice becomes one's nature and wholesome roots grow firm."

> "As the Buddha told the great king: 'A person who has accumulated good conduct will have no evil thoughts at death, just as a tree that has long been leaning will inevitably fall in the direction of its lean.' If, when the wind of the knife of death arrives and a hundred sufferings converge upon the body, one has not previously cultivated the habit of mindfulness, how will one be able to sustain recollection?"

> "Each person should join with three or five like-minded companions and establish a solemn agreement in advance: at the time of death, they will encourage one another in turn, chanting the name of Amitābha Buddha and aspiring to birth in the Land of Utmost Bliss."

There are various explanations, but single-mindedly pronouncing "Namo Amitābha Buddha" ten times constitutes the "ten moments of mindfulness." This interpretation accords with the sūtras. Other explanations will be addressed in the section of clarifications below.

#### Second: Encouragement at the Time of Death

If there are good friends and fellow practitioners who share this aspiration, they should, in accordance with the Buddha's teaching and for the benefit of sentient beings, for the sake of nurturing wholesome roots and forming karmic connections, come to the bedside from the onset of illness to offer encouragement and exhortation. The manner of encouragement should accord with the inclinations of the dying person. Here, by way of composing words for oneself, I offer this:

"Child of the Buddha: in recent years, remaining in this world, your sole aspiration has been to cultivate the practice of the western direction. Of all that you intended, the most essential was the ten moments of mindfulness at life's end. Now that you lie on the bed of illness, you must not be without vigilance. Close your eyes, join your palms, and single-mindedly make your solemn vow:

"Apart from the marks of the Buddha, do not look upon any other form. Apart from the sound of the Buddha's Dharma, do not listen to any other sound. Apart from the Buddha's correct teaching, do not speak of any other matter. Apart from the concern of birth in the Pure Land, do not think of any other thing.

"Continue thus even after the moment of death. Seated upon the jeweled lotus platform, following after Amitābha Buddha, encircled by the holy assembly, crossing the distance of ten trillion lands, maintain this same resolve. Do not direct the mind to any other realm. Only upon reaching the pool of seven jewels in the Land of Utmost Bliss should you open your eyes, join your palms, gaze upon the sacred countenance of Amitābha, hear the profound and wondrous teaching, smell the fragrance of the merits of all buddhas, taste the flavor of Dharma-joy and the bliss of meditation, pay homage to the holy assembly of the oceanic congregation, and awaken to the practice and vows of Samantabhadra.

"Now there are ten matters to which you should listen single-mindedly and recollect single-mindedly. At each recollection, do not give rise to doubt."

**First**, one should arouse the true wisdom of the Mahāyāna and understand the origin of birth and death. As a verse in the *Sūtra of Perfect Enlightenment* (*Yuanjuejing* (圓覺經, *Sūtra of Perfect Enlightenment*)) says:

> All sentient beings,
> Through beginningless illusory ignorance,
> Are established from the perfectly awakened mind
> Of all tathāgatas.

One should understand that birth-and-death is itself nirvāṇa, and that the afflictions are themselves bodhi. They are perfectly interpenetrating, without obstruction, neither two nor different. Yet through a single moment of deluded thought one has entered the realm of birth and death. Blinded by the disease of ignorance, one has long forgotten the path of original awakening. But all dharmas from the very beginning have always been of the nature of quiescent extinction: like an illusion, without fixed nature, they transform in accord with the mind. Therefore, the child of the Buddha should be mindful of the Three Jewels and turn from the wrong to the right. The Buddha is the king of physicians, the Dharma is the excellent medicine, the Saṅgha are the attendants of the sick. They remove the disease of ignorance, open the eye of right view, reveal the path of original awakening, and lead one to the Pure Land. Nothing surpasses the Buddha, the Dharma, and the Saṅgha. Therefore, the child of the Buddha should first generate the thought of the great physician-king and recollect the Buddha single-mindedly:

*Namo to the buddhas of the three times and the ten directions.*
*Namo to our original teacher, Śākyamuni Buddha.*

Next, generate the thought of the wondrous excellent medicine and recollect the Dharma single-mindedly:

*Namo to the mother of the buddhas of the three times, Mahāprajnāpāramitā.*
*Namo to the Sūtra of the Lotus of the Wonderful Dharma of Equal Great Wisdom.*
*Namo to the eighty thousand and twelve divisions of the correct Dharma.*

Next, generate the thought of constant protective care and recollect the Saṅgha single-mindedly:

*Namo to Avalokiteśvara Bodhisattva.*
*Namo to Mahāsthāmaprāpta Bodhisattva.*
*Namo to Samantabhadra Bodhisattva.*
*Namo to Mañjuśrī Bodhisattva.*
*Namo to Maitreya Bodhisattva.*
*Namo to Kṣitigarbha Bodhisattva.*
*Namo to Nāgārjuna Bodhisattva.*
*Namo to the holy assembly of the three times and the ten directions.*
*Namo to the Three Jewels of the assembly in the Land of Utmost Bliss.*
*Namo to the Three Jewels of the three times and the ten directions.*

These are also not separate from conventional existence. As Amitābha Buddha has said:

> "Penetrating the nature of all dharmas, all is emptiness and without self. Devote yourself to seeking the pure buddha-land, and you will certainly accomplish such a realm."

**Second**, therefore, in order to be born in the Pure Land, one should first develop aversion for this present world. This sahā world is brought about by unwholesome karma. It is the source of all suffering. Birth, old age, sickness, and death revolve without end. Bound within the prison of the three realms, there is not a single thing to delight in. If one does not develop aversion for it at this time, in what life will one leave the cycle of saṃsāra? Now, Amitābha Buddha possesses inconceivable awesome power. If one single-mindedly recites his name, in each moment of recollection one extinguishes the heavy sins of birth and death accumulated over eighty billion kalpas. Therefore, one should now single-mindedly recollect that Buddha and depart from this realm of suffering. One should form this thought: "May Amitābha Buddha [receive me]."

**Third**, one should joyfully aspire to the Pure Land. The western Land of Utmost Bliss is a realm of Mahāyāna wholesome roots, a place without suffering or affliction. Once one has been received into the lotus womb, one forever leaves birth and death. The eyes behold the sacred countenance of Amitābha; the ears hear the profound and wondrous teaching. All forms of bliss are fully present. If a person at life's end maintains ten moments of mindfulness of Amitābha Buddha, that person will certainly be born in the Land of Peace and Bliss. Child of the Buddha, you have obtained a human body and have also encountered the Buddha's teaching. This is like a one-eyed turtle finding the hole in a floating log. If at this time you do not attain birth, you will fall back into the three evil destinies and the eight difficulties. Even hearing the Dharma will be hard, to say nothing of attaining birth. Therefore, single-mindedly recite the name of that Buddha. Form this thought: "May the Buddha today certainly [receive me]."

**Fourth**, if one seeks that land, one must cultivate the appropriate practice. As that Buddha's original vow states:

> "If, when I attain buddhahood, sentient beings of the ten directions who hear my name, fix their minds on my land, plant various roots of virtue, and with sincere minds transfer the merit, desiring to be born in my land, should fail to achieve their wish, may I not attain perfect awakening."

Child of the Buddha, throughout your life you have devoted yourself to the practice of the western direction. Though the practices you have cultivated are many, your sole aspiration has been the Land of Utmost Bliss. Now you must gather together all the wholesome roots of the three times and transfer them entirely to the Land of Utmost Bliss. Form this thought: "May all that I possess [be transferred to the Pure Land]."

**Fifth**, another vow states:

> "If, when I attain buddhahood, sentient beings of the ten directions who arouse the mind of bodhi, cultivate various meritorious practices, and with sincere minds make the vow to be born in my land, should I fail, at the end of their lives, to appear before them surrounded by the great assembly, may I not attain perfect awakening."

Child of the Buddha, you have long since aroused the mind of bodhi, and your various wholesome roots have been transferred to the Land of Utmost Bliss. Now you must again arouse the mind of bodhi and recollect that Buddha. Form this thought: "May I, for the benefit and happiness of all sentient beings, today certainly be born in the Land of Utmost Bliss."

**Sixth**, recollect Amitābha Tathāgata to make your practice flourish. The merits of that Buddha are immeasurable, boundless, and cannot be fully described. The buddhas of the ten directions, each as numerous as the sands of the Ganges, constantly praise the merits of that Buddha. Even if such praise continued for kalpas as numerous as the sands of the Ganges, it could never be exhausted. Child of the Buddha, you should single-mindedly take refuge in the merits of that Buddha.

**Seventh**, recollect: "Now, in a single moment of thought, I take complete refuge in Amitābha Buddha." Fix the mind on a single characteristic of his form body. That Buddha's form is like the gold of the Jāmbūnada River. His majestic virtue towers like a golden mountain king. Immeasurable marks of excellence adorn his body. Among them, the white ūrṇā hair between his brows, spiraling to the right, is like five Mount Sumerus. Seven trillion fifty-six million rays of light blaze forth, brilliant as a billion suns and moons. This is the accomplishment of myriad meritless virtues and the emanation of great samādhi, wisdom, and compassion. One who recalls this mark even for a brief moment can extinguish the heavy sins of birth and death accumulated over ninety-six billion nayutas of kalpas as numerous as the atoms of the sands of the Ganges. Therefore, one should now recollect that mark and resolve to eradicate one's evil karma. Form this thought: "May the rays of that mark constantly illuminate the sentient beings who are mindful of the Buddha throughout the ten directions, receiving and never abandoning them." Know that the light of great compassion will certainly come to illumine you.

**Eighth**, as a verse of the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra* (*Huayan jing*) says:

> Again the Buddha emits a light called "Seeing the Buddha."
> That light awakens those whose lives are ending.
> Through buddha-recollection samādhi, one will certainly see the Buddha;
> After death, one will be born before the Buddha.

Therefore, now form this thought: "May Amitābha Buddha emit the light of purity, illumine my mind from afar, and awaken my mind." When the conditions of this world and of one's own body are about to give rise to three kinds of attachment, may this light shine from afar, and may Avalokiteśvara and Mahāsthāmaprāpta always come to embrace and protect the practitioner. How much more so for the Buddha himself, like a parent whose heart inclines especially toward the sick child. He moves the mountain of dharma-nature and enters the sea of birth and death. Know that at this time the Buddha emits great radiance and, together with the holy assembly, comes to guide and protect you. Though karmic obstructions may veil your perception, the vow of great compassion cannot be doubted. He will certainly enter this room. Therefore, child of the Buddha, form this thought: "May the Buddha emit great radiance and certainly come to welcome me. May I be born in the Land of Utmost Bliss."

**Ninth**, when strength gradually weakens, one should say: "The Buddha, together with Avalokiteśvara, Mahāsthāmaprāpta, and the immeasurable holy assembly, has come. The jeweled lotus flower guides and receives you, child of the Buddha."

**Tenth**, at the very moment of death, one should say: "Child of the Buddha, do you understand? This present moment is the final thought. The mindfulness of a single moment at life's end surpasses the karma of a hundred years. If this instant passes, the place of rebirth will be determined. Now is precisely the time. You should single-mindedly recollect the Buddha and resolve to be born in the western Land of Utmost Bliss, the wondrous Pure Land, in the pool of eight meritorious waters, upon the jeweled lotus platform of the seven treasures." Form this thought: "The Tathāgata's original vow is without the slightest error. May the Buddha certainly guide and receive me."

In this way, the attendant should observe the dying person's condition and adapt accordingly, but focus on a single matter for the final moment of thought. Do not multiply concerns. The wording and timing may vary, but one must be attentive not to allow the dying person to develop scattered mental attachments.

#### Question and Answer Regarding the Lotus

**Question:** The *Guanfo sanmei jing* (*Sūtra on the Samādhi of Contemplating the Buddha*) states:

> The Buddha told Ānanda: "If there are sentient beings who kill their father, harm their mother, insult their six kinds of relatives, and commit such crimes, then at the time of death, copper dogs with gaping mouths transform into eighteen carriages that appear as golden carriages with jeweled canopies above. All the flames transform into jade maidens. The sinner, seeing them from afar, gives rise to delight: 'I wish to go there.' When the wind-knives cut the body and one cries out in the bitter cold, one thinks, 'How I wish for a good fire.' Sitting in the carriage, looking at the jade maidens, they all seize iron axes and hack the body to pieces."

> Furthermore: "There are sentient beings who violate the four grave precepts, fraudulently consume the offerings of the faithful, hold wrong views that slander the Dharma, deny cause and effect, abandon the study of prajnā, destroy the buddhas of the ten directions, steal from the sangha's communal property, and live in lechery. They oppress and defile bhikṣunīs, sisters, and relatives, knowing no shame, disgracing their kin, and committing every form of evil. As retribution for their crimes, at the time of death the wind-knives carve their body, they cannot sit or lie still, as if beaten with rods. Their minds become wild and they generate deranged thoughts. They see their own dwelling, and all the men and women, great and small, appear as impure things: excrement and urine overflow everywhere."

> "The sinner then says: 'Why is there no fine city, no pleasant mountains and forests where I can roam? Why am I in such filth?' Having spoken thus, the hell-wardens and rākṣasas, with great iron pitchforks, transform the Avīci Hell and the mountains of knives into jeweled trees and cool pools. The flames transform into golden lotus blossoms. The iron-beaked worms transform into mandarin ducks and geese. The sounds of suffering in hell become like singing."

> "Hearing this, the sinner thinks: 'What a fine place! I shall go there.' Therefore, is it not the case that the lotus coming to welcome the dying person is actually a fire-blossom?"

**Answer:** Master [Shandao]'s commentary explains that four criteria distinguish the genuine welcoming lotus from the fire-blossom: (1) practice, (2) signs, (3) speech, and (4) the Buddha.

**First, practice:** In the *Sūtra on the Samādhi of Contemplating the Buddha*, the sinner committed crimes and violated the four grave precepts, and never encountered a good teacher to instruct in buddha-recollection. Therefore, what appears is the mark of hell. But the persons of the three grades of the lower rank, though they too committed evil throughout life, at the end encounter a good teacher, recollect the Buddha with a sincere mind, and thereby extinguish the sins of many kalpas and accomplish supreme merit. What they experience is the lotus coming from the jeweled pool to welcome them. How could it be the same as the former?

**Second, signs:** The sūtra says the wind-knives cut the body, one cannot sit or lie still, the mind becomes wild and deranged, and the dwelling appears as excrement and filth. But in the case of one who recollects the Buddha, the body and mind are at peace, evil thoughts are entirely extinguished, and one sees only the holy assembly and smells a wondrous fragrance. Thus they are not alike.

**Third, speech:** The sūtra says the sounds of hell suffering become like singing, and the sinner hears and thinks it a pleasant place. But in the *Contemplation Sūtra* (*Guan jing*), the Buddha praises: "Good man, because you have recited the Buddha's name, your sins are extinguished. I have come to welcome you." In the one case, it is the sound of singing; in the other, words proclaiming the eradication of sin. The two sounds are utterly different.

**Fourth, the Buddha:** In the former sūtra, all flames transform into jade maidens, and the sinner, having climbed onto the golden carriage, looks around to find the jade maidens wielding iron axes to hack his body. But in the *Contemplation Sūtra*, "at that time the Buddha sends an emanation buddha, an emanation Avalokiteśvara, and an emanation Mahāsthāmaprāpta to appear before the practitioner."

By these four criteria, we can confirm that the lotus coming to welcome the dying person is genuine. One should frequently ask the dying person about all that appears, and following the rite described above, provide various forms of guidance to ensure birth in the Pure Land.

*End of the middle fascicle of the* Ōjōyōshū.
*Beginning of the lower fascicle of the* Ōjōyōshū, *composed by the śramaṇa Genshin of the Shuryōgon-in of Tendai.*

---

## Gate Seven: The Benefits of Buddha-Recollection

The seventh major topic, the benefits of buddha-recollection, is broadly divided into seven sections: (1) the eradication of evil and the generation of good; (2) unseen protection and support; (3) seeing the Buddha in this present life; (4) excellent rewards in lives to come; (5) the special benefits of recollecting Amitābha; (6) examples offered to encourage faith; and (7) benefits even in the evil destinies. The textual sources for each are extensive; here I cite only the essentials.

### Section One: The Eradication of Evil and the Generation of Good

The *Guanfo jing* (*Sūtra on the Contemplation of the Buddha*), fascicle two, says:

> "If, within a single period, one takes even the smallest fraction, and within that fraction is able for a brief moment to recollect the Buddha's white ūrṇā hair, making the mind clear and free of confused thoughts, distinctly and correctly abiding, with attention unbroken in recollecting the white ūrṇā, then whether or not one actually sees the marks and features, such a person eliminates the sins of birth and death accumulated over ninety-six billion nayutas of kalpas as numerous as the atoms of the sands of the Ganges. Furthermore, if there is a person who merely hears of the white ūrṇā and, without being startled or doubting, joyfully believes and accepts it, that person also eliminates the sins of birth and death accumulated over eighty billion kalpas."

It also says:

> "After the Buddha's passing, those who practice the samādhi of right reception and contemplate the Buddha through practice also eliminate the extremely heavy sins of a thousand kalpas."

> "On a certain day the Tathāgata spoke and commanded his disciples to spread this teaching: After the Buddha's nirvāṇa, one should fashion fine images and make the bodily marks complete. One should also make immeasurable emanation images of the Buddha, as well as full-body images, and painted images of the Buddha's footprints. With exquisite threads and beryl gems, one should adorn the place of the white ūrṇā. When sentient beings see this mark and their hearts give rise to joy, they eliminate the sins of birth and death accumulated over a hundred billion nayutas of kalpas as numerous as the sands of the Ganges."

It also says:

> "One who can eliminate the sins of birth and death accumulated over eighty trillion kalpas, how much more so one who with a wholesome and reverential mind..."

> "...those who slander the Vaipulya sūtras, commit the five heinous crimes, violate the four grave precepts, steal from the sangha's communal property, defile bhikṣunīs, violate the eight precepts of abstinence, and commit every form of evil, holding all manner of wrong views: if such persons are able, with a sincere mind, for one day and one night to fix their minds and contemplate even a single mark of the Tathāgata's features, all their grave karmic obstructions will be utterly eradicated."

It also says:

> "If any person takes refuge in the World-Honored Buddha and recites his name, that person eliminates the heavy obstacles of afflictions accumulated over a hundred thousand kalpas. How much more so for one who with a correct mind cultivates the samādhi of buddha-recollection."

The *Ratnakūṭa Sūtra* (*Baoji jing*), fascicle five, says:

> "It is as though there were a jewel called 'Many Colors' in the great ocean. Though innumerable swift currents flow into the sea, by the fire-power of this jewel the waters are consumed and do not overflow. Just so, when the Tathāgata, the Worthy One, the Perfectly Awakened, has realized bodhi, through the fire of his wisdom he is able to consume and extinguish the afflictions of sentient beings."

> "The merit of the Tathāgata's name enables sentient beings to leave the darkness. Gradually they will come to burn away all afflictions. Thus, to recite 'Namo Buddha' is a verbal act that is not in vain. Such verbal action is called 'grasping a great torch,' able to burn away the afflictions."

The *Yirimoni jing* (*Sūtra of the Cintāmaṇi of the Sun*) says:

> "Though a bodhisattva may have been immersed in desire for thousands of billions of myriads of kalpas, covered over by wrongdoing, if that one hears a buddha-sūtra and turns the mind to goodness even once, the wrongdoing is immediately eradicated."

> "...including stream-enterers, once-returners, non-returners, and arhats. If a good man or good woman were, for one kalpa or less than a kalpa, to make offerings with every kind of pleasing and delightful object, with reverence, respect, humility, and honor, and if another person were to make a single añjali gesture or recite the name once before the buddhas, the merit of the latter, compared to the former, would not amount to one hundredth, one hundred-billionth, nor even one kalā-fraction. Why? Because the buddhas, the tathāgatas, are fields of merit [beyond measure]."

> "If one were to make offerings of food, drink, clothing, bedding, pounded incense, mixed incense, and fine flowers for billions of kalpas to a single tathāgata, and if another person were, with single-pointed concentration and palms joined with ten fingers, to take refuge with sincere mind in a single tathāgata, and with one's own mouth say 'Namo Buddha,' the merit of this is supreme."

The *Pratyutpanna-samādhi Sūtra* (*Banzhou sanmei jing*), in a verse on the buddha-recollection samādhi, says:

> Suppose all beings were to become buddhas,
> With sacred wisdom pure and foremost in discernment,
> And for billions upon billions of kalpas
> They were to expound the merit of a single verse;

> Even if they recited and praised until nirvāṇa,
> For countless billions of kalpas all praising together,
> They could not exhaust the merit
> Of a single verse-occasion of this samādhi.

> All the ground in every buddha-land,
> In the four directions, the four corners, above and below,
> Filled with precious treasures and given in offering
> To supply the buddhas, the gods among gods:

> If any person hears this samādhi,
> The merit gained surpasses even that.
> How much greater for one who calmly recites, chants, expounds, and teaches it.

It also says:

> "If sentient beings, taking the Tathāgata as their condition, generate various practices, they sever the births of countless kalpas in the realms of hell, hungry ghosts, animals, and King Yama. If any sentient being generates even a single moment of mindfulness directed toward the Tathāgata, the merit obtained is without limit or measure. Hundreds of thousands of millions of billions of nayutas of great bodhisattvas, all having attained the inconceivable liberation samādhi, could not calculate or know its extent."

The *Guanfo jing* says:

> The Buddha told Ānanda: "After my nirvāṇa, if any devas or humans recite my name or recite 'Namo to the buddhas,' the merit they obtain is immeasurable and boundless. How much more so for those who fix the mind and are mindful of the buddhas: could they fail to eradicate all obstacles?"

### Section Two: Unseen Protection and Support

The *Hushenzhoujing* (護身呪經, *Sūtra of the Body-Protection Mantra*) says:

> "The spirit-kings of the thirty-six divisions, with their retinue of myriads of billions of beings as numerous as the sands of the Ganges, protect those who have taken the threefold refuge."

The *Pratyutpanna-samādhi Sūtra* says:

> "When the kalpa ends and the world is consumed by fire, the bodhisattva who upholds this samādhi, even if cast into the flames, will see the fire extinguished, just as a great vat of water extinguishes a small fire."

> The Buddha told Bhadrapāla: "What I say is without falsehood. This bodhisattva who upholds this samādhi: whether kings, bandits, fire, water, or those who would disrupt one's mindfulness seek to harm this bodhisattva, they will never succeed."

> The Buddha said: "As I have spoken, it is without falsehood. Aside from the ripening of past karma, nothing else can harm this person."

A verse says:

> Ghosts and spirits, gandharvas, all protect this one;
> Devas and humans likewise do the same.
> Together with asuras and mahoragas,
> One who practices this samādhi gains all this.

> All the devas join in praising one's virtue;
> Devas, humans, nāgas, and kiṃnaras,
> All the buddhas commend and grant one's wishes.
> One who recites and teaches the sūtras for others' sake:

> When nations war and the people are in turmoil,
> When famine and calamity bring ruin and hardship,
> This one will never meet an untimely death.
> One who can recite this sūtra and teach it to others,

> Courageously subdues all māra-deeds,
> The mind knows no fear, not a hair stands on end.
> The merit of this practice is beyond reckoning.

A verse on one who practices this samādhi says:

> If a person upholds the Buddha's name,
> All māras and Pāpīyas,
> Whether one walks, stands, sits, or lies,
> Can find no advantage against this one.

### Section Three: Seeing the Buddha in This Present Life

The *Mañjuśrī Prajñāpāramitā Sūtra* (文殊師利般若經, *Wenshushili boruo jing*), lower fascicle, says:

> The Buddha said: "If a good man or good woman wishes to enter the single-practice samādhi, one should go to an empty and quiet place, abandon all confused thoughts, and without grasping at appearances, fix the mind on a single buddha and exclusively recite that buddha's name. Facing the direction of that buddha, sitting upright with correct posture, if one can maintain moment-to-moment continuity of mindfulness of that one buddha, then within that mindfulness one is able to see all the buddhas of past, present, and future."

Master Daochuo's commentary explains:

> "Because the obstructions of sentient beings are heavy, contemplation is difficult to accomplish. Therefore the Great Sage, out of compassion, directly encourages the exclusive recitation of the name."

The *Pratyutpanna-samādhi Sūtra* says:

> "Sūtra scrolls that one has never before heard: through the awesome power of this samādhi, this bodhisattva receives them all in dreams. All the sūtra scrolls one sees, one hears the sound of each. What one cannot obtain during the day, one obtains at night in dreams, seeing the Buddha."

> The Buddha told Bhadrapāla: "Whether for one kalpa or more, if I were to describe the merit of this bodhisattva who upholds this samādhi, I could never exhaust it. How much more so for one who is able to seek and attain this samādhi."

A verse from the same sūtra says:

> Just as the bodhisattvas of Amitābha's land
> See innumerable hundreds of thousands of buddhas,
> So too the bodhisattva who attains this samādhi
> Shall see innumerable hundreds of thousands of buddhas.

> Even in the final time of great terror,
> One who upholds this samādhi will have no fear.

A verse from the *Nianfo sanmei jing* (*Sūtra of the Buddha-Recollection Samādhi*), fascicle nine, says:

> If one wishes to see fully all the buddhas,
> Of the present, the future, and the ten directions,
> Or if one seeks to turn the wondrous Dharma-wheel,
> One should first cultivate and practice this samādhi.

A verse from the *Shier foming jing* (十二佛名經, *Sūtra of the Twelve Buddha-Names*) says:

> If a person can, with a sincere mind,
> For seven days recite the Buddha's names,
> That person obtains the pure eye
> And is able to see immeasurable buddhas.

### Section Four: Excellent Rewards in Lives to Come

A verse from the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra* (*Huayan jing*) says:

> If one recollects even a small portion of the Tathāgata's merit,
> Even a single moment of single-pointed devotion,
> All terrors of the evil destinies are forever removed.
> One does not fall into hell, one leaves the hungry ghost realm and the animal realm.
> In every life where one is born, one remembers past lives.
> One who studies this samādhi gains all this.

The *Guanfo jing* says:

> "If any sentient being hears even once the merits of the Buddha's body, his marks, features, and radiance as described above, then for billions of billions of thousands of kalpas that being will not fall into the evil destinies, will not be born in places of wrong views and defilement, and will constantly maintain right views, cultivating diligently without cease."

> "Merely hearing the Buddha's name brings such merit. How much more so when the buddhas appear in the world, for they have four methods of liberating sentient beings. What are these four? First, the mouth teaches the twelve divisions of the sūtras: this is liberating sentient beings through the gift of Dharma. Second, all tathāgatas possess immeasurable marks of radiance and excellence; all sentient beings who fix the mind and contemplate them receive benefit without exception: this is liberating sentient beings through bodily action. Third, there is immeasurable virtuous power, supernormal abilities, and various miraculous transformations: this is liberating sentient beings through supernormal power. Fourth, all tathāgatas have immeasurable names, whether collective or individual; any sentient being who fixes the mind and recites them will have all obstacles removed."

> "One who upholds the Buddha's name does not give rise to timidity. One's wisdom is without deceit. One is always in the presence of all buddhas."

> "If a person upholds the Buddha's name, that person is born within a seven-jeweled lotus. The lotus has a thousand [petals]."

> "If one can, with a sincere mind, fix the mind within, sitting upright in correct reception and contemplating the Buddha's form, one should know that this person's mind is like the Buddha's mind, no different from the Buddha. Though dwelling amid the afflictions, one is not covered over by evil. In lives to come, one will rain down the great Dharma."

The *Daiji nianfo sanmei jing* (*Mahāsaṃnipāta Sūtra on the Buddha-Recollection Samādhi*), fascicle seven, says:

> "You should know that this buddha-recollection samādhi subsumes all dharmas. Therefore it is not within the sphere of the two vehicles of śrāvakas and pratyekabuddhas. If any person even briefly hears this teaching, that person will certainly attain buddhahood in the future. There is no doubt."

The same sūtra, fascicle nine, says:

> "Even if one merely hears the name of this samādhi, even if one does not read, recite, receive, uphold, cultivate, practice, turn it for others, explain it to others, or provide a detailed commentary, such good men and good women will all, in due order, accomplish anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi."

A verse from the same sūtra says:

> If one wishes to perfect all the wondrous marks,
> To be adorned with the most excellent adornments,
> And if one seeks to turn [the Dharma-wheel]...

> ...if one plants even a small portion of wholesome roots in the Buddha's field of merit, one first attains the excellent reward of the good destinies, and afterward will certainly reach nirvāṇa.

The *Mahāprajnāpāramitā Sūtra* (*Dabanruo jing*) says:

> "Those who respectfully recollect the Buddha will certainly escape birth and death and reach nirvāṇa. Setting this aside, even one who, for the sake of offering to the Buddha, scatters a single flower into empty space, the result is the same."

> "Setting this aside, even if a good man or good woman merely recites 'Namo Buddha, the one of great compassion' a single time, the wholesome roots of such persons will never be exhausted, even until the end of saṃsāra, and will never be destroyed."

It also says:

> "If there is a bodhisattva who, with a mind of supreme aspiration, is able to generate the thought of the Buddha as a father, that person will enter the ranks of the tathāgatas and be no different from me."

A verse from the *Shier foming jing* (*Sūtra of the Twelve Buddha-Names*) says:

> If a person upholds the Buddha's name,
> In every life wherever born,
> The body traverses empty space with supernormal powers,
> And is able to expound the wondrous Dharma,
> Receiving a prediction of bodhi.

A verse from the *Saddharmapuṇḍarīka Sūtra* (*Fahuajing* (法華經, *Saddharmapuṇḍarīka Sūtra*)) says:

> If a person with a scattered mind
> Enters a stūpa or temple
> And recites "Namo Buddha" even once,
> That person has already accomplished the Buddha Way.

The *Mahākaruṇā Sūtra* (*Dabeijing* (大悲經, *Mahākaruṇā Sūtra*)), fascicle three:

> The Buddha told Ānanda: "If any sentient being hears the Buddha's name, I declare that this person will certainly attain entry into parinirvāṇa."

A verse of Dharmadhvaja Bodhisattva from the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra* says:

> If any sentient beings
> Have not yet aroused the mind of bodhi...

> ...hearing the name and its excellent rewards is already like this. How much more so for briefly contemplating and recollecting the marks, features, and merits, or for offering even a single flower or stick of incense. How much more so for a lifetime of diligent practice: that merit will never be in vain. One should therefore know that encountering the Buddha-Dharma and hearing the Buddha's name is no small affinity.

Thus a verse of Bhūtatathatā Bodhisattva from the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra* says:

> Better to endure the sufferings of hell
> And hear the names of the buddhas,
> Than to enjoy immeasurable pleasures
> And never hear the Buddha's name.

The above four sections explain in general the benefits of recollecting all the buddhas. Among the cited sources, the *Guanfo jing* takes Śākyamuni as its primary focus, while the *Pratyutpanna-samādhi Sūtra* most often takes Amitābha as its primary focus. In principle, all apply equally to every buddha. The *Nianfo jing* (念佛經, *Sūtra of Buddha-Recollection*) encompasses the buddhas of the three times.

**Question:** The *Guanfo jing* says: "This person's mind is like the Buddha's mind, no different from the Buddha." The *Guan jing* (*Contemplation Sūtra*) also says:

> The Buddha told Ānanda: "All buddhas are bodies of the dharma-realm. They enter into the thought and imagination of every sentient being. Therefore, when you think of the Buddha, that mind itself is the thirty-two marks and eighty minor excellences. That mind creates the Buddha. That mind is the Buddha."

The commentary of Zhiguang on the *Treatise on Birth in the Pure Land* (*Wangsheng lun*) explains this passage:

> "You should know that when sentient beings think of the Buddha, the Buddha's bodily marks all appear within the mind of the sentient being. It is like clear water in which reflections appear: the water and the image are neither one nor different. Therefore, the Buddha's marks and excellent body are precisely the mind's imagination."

> "'This mind creates the Buddha' means the mind is able to create the Buddha. 'This mind is the Buddha' means there is no Buddha outside the mind. It is like fire produced from wood: it cannot be separated from the wood, yet precisely because it is not separate from the wood, it is able to burn the wood. When the wood is burned by fire..."

The *Mahāsaṃnipāta Sūtra*, in the chapter on the Storehouse of the Sun, says:

> "The practitioner reflects: 'These buddhas come from nowhere and go nowhere. My mind alone creates them. Within the three realms, this body and its conditions are entirely created by the mind. According to my contemplation, when I wish to see many, I see many; when I wish to see few, I see few. All the tathāgatas are my own mind. Why? Because one sees in accord with the mind. The mind is this very body. It is identical with empty space. Through contemplation I see immeasurable buddhas. Through my awakened mind I see the Buddha and know the Buddha. The mind does not see the mind. The mind does not know the mind. I contemplate the nature of the dharma-realm: it has no solidity. All the buddhas arise from the conditions of contemplation. Therefore the nature of dharmas is itself empty space.'"

The teacher's commentary does not contradict this. **Question:** What is the excellent benefit of knowing that the mind creates the Buddha? **Answer:** If one contemplates this principle, one can understand the Dharma of all buddhas of the three times, and even upon a single hearing, one can be liberated from the sufferings of the three evil destinies. As a verse of Tathāgatavana Bodhisattva from the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra* says:

> If one wishes to know
> All the buddhas of the three times,
> One should contemplate thus:
> The mind creates all the tathāgatas.

The *Huayan zhuan* (華嚴傳, *Record of the Avataṃsaka*) relates: In the first year of the Wenming era, there was a man in the capital surnamed Wang, whose given name is lost. He kept no precepts and never cultivated goodness. He fell ill and died. Two figures led him to the gate of hell, where he saw a monk who said he was Kṣitigarbha Bodhisattva. The bodhisattva taught him to recite this single verse, saying: "If you can recite this verse, you can repel the hells." Wang then entered and saw King Yama. The king asked: "Does this person have any merit?" He replied: "I have only received and upheld a single four-line verse," and recited it as described above. The king thereupon released him. When he recited the verse, the sound reached all the places of suffering, and all beings there were liberated. Wang revived after three days and remembered the verse. He told it to various monastics and verified the text: it was from fascicle twelve of the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra*, the chapter on the assembly of limitless bodhisattvas expounding the Dharma in the Yama Heaven palace. Wang himself went to the monk Dingfa at Kongguansi and [reported these events].

### Section Five: The Special Benefits of Recollecting Amitābha

To bring the practitioner's mind to firm resolution, the *Contemplation Sūtra* (*Guan jing*) says regarding the visualization:

> "One who performs this contemplation eliminates the sins of birth and death accumulated over immeasurable billions of kalpas and attains, in this present body, the buddha-recollection samādhi."

It also says:

> "Merely hearing the Buddha's name and the names of the two bodhisattvas eliminates the sins of birth and death accumulated over immeasurable kalpas. How much more so for one who recollects them."

It also says:

> "Merely by visualizing the Buddha image, one obtains immeasurable merit. How much more so for one who contemplates the complete bodily marks of the Buddha."

The *Amitābha-dhyāna Sūtra* (*Amituo siwei jing* (阿彌陀思惟經, *Amitābha Contemplation Sūtra*)) says:

> "If a wheel-turning king, for thousands of myriads of years, were to fill the four continents with the seven treasures and offer them to the buddhas of the ten directions, this would not compare to a bhikṣu, bhikṣunī, upāsaka, or upāsikā who, for even the snap of a finger, sits in meditation with an equanimous mind of compassion for all sentient beings, recollecting the merit of Amitābha Buddha in the Land of Utmost Bliss, that supremely pure buddha-land adorned with merit."

> "Those who have already made the vow, those who will make the vow, and those who now make the vow will all certainly come to abide in this way, received by the buddhas of the ten directions, as numerous as the sands of ten Ganges Rivers. As described, those who practice will all certainly attain irreversibility in regard to anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi, and will all certainly be born in the land of the Buddha of Immeasurable Life, the Land of Utmost Bliss."

The *Contemplation Sūtra* says:

> "The radiance illumines the worlds of the ten directions. Sentient beings who recollect the Buddha are received and never abandoned."

It also says:

> "The Buddha of Immeasurable Life manifests innumerable emanation bodies, and together with Avalokiteśvara and Mahāsthāmaprāpta, constantly comes to the place of this practitioner."

The *Shiwangsheng jing* (十往生經, *Sūtra of the Ten [Stages of] Birth*): After Śākyamuni has described the merits, the land, and the adornments of Amitābha Buddha, he says:

> "Men and women of pure faith who read and recite this sūtra, circulate this sūtra, revere this sūtra, do not slander this sūtra, delight in this sūtra, and make offerings to this sūtra: on account of this faith and reverence, from this day forth I will constantly dispatch twenty-five bodhisattvas to protect such persons, ensuring they are always free from illness and affliction, and that evil ghosts and evil spirits can do them no harm."

Various masters say:

> "The twenty-five bodhisattvas also protect those who recollect Amitābha Buddha."

The Buddha's original vow states:

> "Devas and humans who hear my name shall prostrate with their five limbs, bow their heads in homage, rejoice in faith, cultivate the bodhisattva practice, and all devas and humans shall not look down upon them."

The *Bhadrapāla chapter* of the *Mahāsaṃnipāta Sūtra* (*Daji xianhufen jing*) says:

> "Good men and good women, sitting upright with minds fixed, single-mindedly contemplating Amitābha Tathāgata, Arhat, Samyak-saṃbuddha, his marks, his dignity, the great assembly, and his teaching of the Dharma: if one maintains continuous, sequential, undistracted mindfulness as one has heard it, for one day or one night, up to seven days and seven nights, in accord with what I have taught and with complete mindfulness, that person will certainly behold Amitābha Tathāgata, Arhat, Samyak-saṃbuddha. If one cannot see him during the day, then at night, in the stillness, or in a dream, Amitābha Buddha with his forty thousand marks of excellence will naturally appear. To see the Buddha of Immeasurable Life is to see all the immeasurable buddhas of the ten directions."

The *Zhu yinsheng jing* (諸音聲經, *Sūtra of Various Sounds*) says:

> "For ten days and ten nights, at the six daily intervals, with single-pointed mindfulness, prostrating with the five limbs, venerating that Buddha, with firm right mindfulness, removing all scatteredness, if one can maintain unbroken mindfulness, within ten days one will certainly see Amitābha Buddha and also see the tathāgatas of the ten directions and the places where they dwell, with the exception of those of heavy obstructions and dull faculties who cannot see within this time. All the wholesome deeds one has done should be transferred with the aspiration to be born in the world of peace and bliss. At the end of life, Amitābha Buddha, together with the great assembly, will appear before that person, bringing peace and praising them. At that time, that person gives rise to great joy. By this cause and condition, in accord with one's vow, one immediately attains birth."

The *Pingdeng jue jing* (*Sūtra of Equal Awakening*) says:

> The Buddha said: "One must maintain the precepts and the fast, with a single, pure mind. Day and night, constantly recollect and aspire to be born in the land of the Buddha of Immeasurable Purity. For ten days and ten nights without interruption. I will have compassion for all such persons and cause them all to be born in that land of immeasurable purity."

A verse says:

> Through that Buddha's power of the original vow,
> Those who hear his name and aspire to birth
> Will all reach that land
> And naturally attain non-retrogression.

The *Contemplation Sūtra*, regarding the upper birth of the lower rank: At the time of death, joining palms with hands together, one recites "Namo Amitābha Buddha." Because one recites the Buddha's name, one eliminates the sins of birth and death accumulated over fifty billion kalpas. An emanation buddha appears, and one is born in the jeweled pool.

Regarding the middle birth of the lower rank: At the time of death, the fierce fires of hell descend all at once. Upon hearing of Amitābha Buddha's ten powers, awesome virtue, radiance, and supernormal power, and of his discipline, concentration, wisdom, liberation, and knowledge and vision of liberation, the sins of birth and death accumulated over eighty billion kalpas are extinguished. The fierce fires of hell transform into a cool breeze. The breeze carries heavenly flowers, and upon each flower is an emanation buddha and bodhisattva who comes to welcome this person, who immediately attains birth.

Regarding the lower birth of the lower rank: At the time of death, pressed by suffering, one cannot recollect the Buddha. A good friend instructs: "Simply recite with a sincere mind, making the voice unbroken. Complete ten moments of mindfulness, reciting 'Namo Amitāyus Buddha.'" Because one recites the Buddha's name, in each successive moment of thought, the sins of birth and death accumulated over eighty billion kalpas are extinguished.

The original vow of Amitābha Buddha states:

> "Sentient beings of all the buddha-lands who hear my name and do not attain the bodhisattva's forbearance of the birthlessness of all dharmas, together with the various profound dhāraṇīs, shall not attain perfect awakening. Bodhisattvas of other lands who hear my name and do not immediately reach the stage of non-retrogression shall not attain perfect awakening."

The *Contemplation Sūtra* says:

> "If one is mindful of the Buddha, one should know that this person is a puṇḍarīka flower among human beings. Avalokiteśvara Bodhisattva and Mahāsthāmaprāpta Bodhisattva are that person's excellent friends. That person shall sit upon the seat of awakening and be born in the family of the buddhas."

### Section Six: Examples Offered to Encourage Faith

The *Guanfo jing*, fascicle three. The Buddha told the Śākya clansmen:

> "In the age of the semblance Dharma of Vipaśyin Buddha, there was a householder named Moonvirtue who had five hundred sons. They all contracted a serious illness. Their father came before his sons, weeping with palms joined, and said: 'You children hold wrong views and do not believe in the true Dharma. Now the knife of impermanence cuts your bodies. On what will you rely? There is a World-Honored One named Vipaśyin Buddha. You may recite his name.' The sons heard and, out of respect for their father, recited 'Namo Buddha.' The father further said: 'You may recite the Dharma. You may recite the Saṅgha.' Before they could recite it three times, the sons died. Because they had recited the Buddha's name, they were born in the Heaven of the Four Great Kings. When their heavenly lifespan ended, because of their previous wrong views, they fell into a great hell. When the hell-wardens and rākṣasas struck and ruined their eyes with hot iron forks, as they endured this suffering they recalled their father's instruction. Because of their recollection of the Buddha, they were reborn in the human realm."

> "In the time of Śikhin Buddha, they only heard the Buddha's name but did not see the Buddha's form. And so on through the time of Kāśyapa Buddha, they also heard his name. Because of the karmic connection of hearing the names of six buddhas, they were born in my time. These bhikṣus, in their former lives, slandered the Buddha's true Dharma with evil minds. But solely because, out of respect for their father, they recited 'Namo Buddha,' in every subsequent life they were able to hear the names of all the buddhas. And now, in this present life, they have encountered my appearance in the world, removed all obstructions, and attained arhatship."

It also says:

> "In the final age of the Dharma of Dīpaṃkara Buddha, there was an arhat whose thousand disciples, upon hearing the arhat's teaching, grew angry in their hearts. As each one neared the end of life, the arhat taught [them to recite the Buddha's name]."

Also, fascicle seven: Mañjuśrī himself recounted how he had once encountered and paid homage to the past buddha named Baoweideshangwang (寶威德上王, Jewel of Majestic Virtue Supreme King). At that time, Śākyamuni Buddha praised him:

> "Excellent, excellent! Mañjuśrī, because of a single prostration to the Buddha in the past, you have been able to encounter such an immeasurable number of buddhas. How much more so for my disciples in the future who diligently contemplate the Buddha."

> The Buddha commanded Ānanda: "Take Mañjuśrī's words and proclaim them to the great assembly and to sentient beings of the future. Any person who is able to prostrate, who is able to recollect the Buddha, who is able to contemplate the Buddha: know that this person is equal to Mañjuśrī, without any difference. After leaving this body in a future life, Mañjuśrī and the other great bodhisattvas will serve as that person's preceptor."

> The Buddha told the great assembly: "I recall that in the immeasurable past, a buddha appeared in the world named Baoweideshangwang (寶威德上王, Jewel of Majestic Virtue Supreme King). At that time, there was a bhikṣu who, with nine disciples, went to a buddha-stūpa to pay homage to the buddha-image. Seeing a jeweled image of great splendor, he prostrated and gazed upon it attentively, then spoke a verse of praise. Afterward, at the end of their lives, they were all born in the eastern land of Baoweideshangwang Buddha, appearing within a great lotus, seated in cross-legged posture, miraculously born. From that time on, they always encountered buddhas. In the presence of all buddhas, they purified the holy life and attained the buddha-recollection samādhi. Having attained the samādhi, the Buddha granted them a prediction. In each of the ten directions, each one attained buddhahood: Shandefo (Good Virtue Buddha) in the east is none other than myself. The buddhas of the southeast, south, southwest, west, northwest, north, northeast, above, and below each attained buddhahood likewise."

> "These ten buddhas, because of their past act of paying homage at the stūpa, contemplating the image, and reciting a single verse of praise, have each now attained buddhahood in the ten directions."

> Having spoken these words, they paid their respects to Śākyamuni Buddha, then emitted great radiance and each returned to their own land.

Also:

> "Four world-honored buddhas descended from the sky, sat upon the seat of Śākyamuni Buddha, and praised him: 'Excellent, excellent! You are able, for the sake of sentient beings in the turbid and evil age of the future, to teach the white ūrṇā radiance and marks of the buddhas of the three times, enabling sentient beings to eradicate wrongdoing.'"

> "'Why? I recall that once, in the time of Ākāśarāja (Space King) Buddha, we were fellow monastics, four bhikṣus studying the Buddha's true Dharma together. Afflictions covered our minds, and we could not firmly uphold the Buddha's Dharma-treasure. We committed many unwholesome deeds and were destined for the evil paths. A voice in the sky spoke to us: "Ākāśarāja Tathāgata, though he has entered nirvāṇa: your transgressions that you think beyond rescue, you may now enter the stūpa and contemplate the image. This is the same as when the Buddha was in the world." Following the voice from the sky, we entered the stūpa and contemplated the mark of white hair between the brows. We then formed this thought: "When the Tathāgata was in the world, his radiant form body: how is it different from this?" We said before the Buddha's great marks: "May our sins be removed." Having said this, we collapsed to the ground as a great mountain collapses, prostrating with five limbs, and repented of our sins. From then on, for eighty billion asaṃkhyeya kalpas, we did not fall into the evil destinies. In every life, we constantly saw the buddhas of the ten directions. In the presence of all buddhas, we received and upheld the profound buddha-recollection samādhi. Having attained the samādhi, all the buddhas appeared before us and granted us their predictions.'"

> "'Akṣobhya Buddha of the eastern land of Abhirati is the first bhikṣu. Ratnaketu Buddha of the southern land of Joy is the second bhikṣu. Amitāyus Buddha of the western Land of Utmost Bliss is the third bhikṣu. Sūkṣmadhvani Buddha of the northern land Adorned with Lotuses is the fourth bhikṣu.'"

> "At that time, the four tathāgatas each extended their right hand, touched Ānanda's head, and said: 'Uphold the Buddha's words. Proclaim them widely for the sentient beings of the future.' They said this three times."

> "Ānanda said: 'World-Honored One, I recall that in the immeasurable past, there was a world-honored buddha also named Śākyamuni. After that buddha's nirvāṇa, there was a prince named Golden Banner, arrogant and holding wrong views, who did not believe in the true Dharma. A wise bhikṣu named Mastery of Concentration told the prince: "In the world there are buddha-images adorned with many jewels. You may briefly enter the stūpa and look upon the Buddha's form." The prince, following his good friend's instruction, entered the stūpa and looked upon the image. Seeing the image's marks of excellence, he said to the bhikṣu: "Even the Buddha's image is this dignified and beautiful. How much more so the Buddha's true body." The bhikṣu told him: "Now that you have seen the image, if you cannot prostrate, you should recite Namo Buddha." The prince then joined his palms with reverence and recited "Namo Buddha." Returning to his palace, he kept recollecting the image in the stūpa. That very night, in the latter part of the evening, he dreamed of the buddha image. Because of seeing the buddha image, his heart was filled with great joy. He abandoned his wrong views and took refuge in the Three Jewels. When his lifespan ended, through the merit of having entered the stūpa and recited "Namo Buddha," he encountered ninety billion myriad nayutas of buddhas. He attained the profound buddha-recollection samādhi. Through the power of this samādhi, all the buddhas appeared before him and granted him their prediction. From then on, for a million asaṃkhyeya kalpas, he did not fall into the evil paths. He has now attained the profound Śūraṅgama Samādhi. That prince is now none other than Caishoushou (Wealth-Head).'"

Also:

> "The Buddha said: 'I and all the bodhisattvas of this Fortunate Kalpa once, in the presence of Candanakūṭa Buddha, heard of the contemplation of the buddhas' form-bodies, their transformations, and the samādhi ocean of buddha-contemplation. Through the merit of this cause and condition, we transcended the sins of birth and death accumulated over ninety billion myriad asaṃkhyeya kalpas. Within this Fortunate Kalpa, we have in due succession accomplished the three kinds of perfect awakening.'"

The *Kāśyapa Sūtra* (*Jiayejing* (迦葉經, *Kāśyapa Sūtra*)) says:

> "Long ago, in the immeasurable past of asaṃkhyeya kalpas, there appeared a buddha named Guangming (Radiance). After his nirvāṇa, there was a bodhisattva named Great Diligence, only sixteen years old, of the brahmin caste, handsome and without equal. A bhikṣu painted an image of the Buddha on a white surface and presented it to Great Diligence. When Great Diligence saw the image, his heart was filled with great joy, and he said: 'The Tathāgata's image is wondrous and beautiful to such a degree. How much more so the Buddha's true body. May I in the future also accomplish such a wondrous body.' Having spoken, he reflected: 'If I remain a householder, this body will be impossible to attain.' He then informed his parents and begged to leave home. His parents replied: 'We are now old, and you are our only child. If you leave home, we shall die.' The son said to his parents: 'If you do not permit me, from this day forth I will neither eat nor drink, I will not ascend my bed, and I will not speak.' Having made this vow, he did not eat for one day, two days, up to six days. His parents, relatives, and eighty-four thousand attendant women all wept together, prostrated before Great Diligence, and at last allowed him to leave home."

> "Having left home, he took the image into the mountains, gathered grass for a seat, and before the painted image sat in cross-legged posture, single-mindedly and attentively contemplating the painted image as no different from the Tathāgata. 'The image has no awareness, no knowledge. All dharmas are likewise: without marks, beyond marks, empty in nature and quiescent.' Having contemplated in this way through the days and nights, he accomplished the five supernormal powers, attained immeasurable eloquence, and realized the Samādhi of Universal Light, endowed with great radiance. With his pure divine eye, he saw the innumerable buddhas of the eastern direction. With his pure divine ear, he heard the teachings of all buddhas and was able to receive and retain them all. For a full seven months, he took wisdom as food. All the devas scattered flowers in offering. Descending from the mountain, he came to the villages and taught the Dharma to the people. Twenty thousand sentient beings aroused the mind of bodhi. Immeasurable asaṃkhyeya beings came to abide in the merits of śrāvakas and pratyekabuddhas. His parents and relatives all came to abide in non-retrogressive anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi."

> The Buddha told Kāśyapa: "That Great Diligence of the past is now none other than myself. Through this contemplation of the image, I have now attained buddhahood. If any person can study such contemplation, that person will certainly, in the future, accomplish the unsurpassed Way."

The *Piyu jing* (*Sūtra of Parables*), fascicle two:

> "Long ago, there was a bhikṣu who wished to liberate his mother. His mother had already died. He searched with his divine eye through the heavens, the human realm, and the intermediate realms, but could not find her. He looked into the hells and saw her there, and was overcome with grief and distress. He searched extensively for a means to free her from suffering."

> "At that time, there was a king on the border who had killed his father and seized the throne. The bhikṣu knew that this king had but seven days of life remaining, and that the place where he would receive punishment was the same hell as his mother's."

> "In the stillness of the night, the bhikṣu went to the king's bedchamber, pierced through the wall, and revealed half his body. The king, terrified, drew his sword and struck at the bhikṣu's head. The head fell to the ground, but the body remained as before. He struck again and again, and severed heads filled the ground, yet the bhikṣu did not move. The king's mind then understood that this was no ordinary being. He bowed his head in apology. The bhikṣu said: 'Do not be afraid. I wish to save you. Have you not killed your father and seized his throne?' The king replied: 'It is true. I beg for your merciful rescue.' The bhikṣu said: 'Performing great meritorious deeds may not be enough to save you. You must recite Namo Buddha for seven days without ceasing, and then you can be freed from punishment.' He warned him again: 'Do not forget this teaching.' Then the bhikṣu flew away."

> "The king then joined his palms and single-mindedly recited 'Namo Buddha' day and night without slacking. After seven days, his life ended. His spirit approached the gate of hell, reciting 'Namo Buddha.' All the beings in that hell, hearing the sound of the Buddha's name, all recited 'Namo Buddha' in unison. The hell immediately turned cold. The bhikṣu then taught the Dharma. The bhikṣu's mother, the king, and all the beings in the hell were all liberated."

> "Later, Great Diligence was reborn in a household of wrong views. Tangled in the net of delusion, caught up in a sense of 'self,' at that time his name was Guangli (Broad Benefit). His wife..."

> "...devoted herself to diligent effort and liberated immeasurable beings, guiding them through the ten wholesome practices. At that time, I was given to hunting and killing, craving alcohol and meat, lazy and slothful, unable to be diligent. My wife told me to stop hunting and killing, to abstain from alcohol and meat, and to exert myself diligently, so as to be freed from the suffering and affliction of the hells and to ascend to the heavenly palace."

> "At that time, my heart for killing would not stop. I could not give up the fine flavors of alcohol and meat. My mind for diligence was lazy and would not advance. Thoughts of the heavenly palace ceased; my share was the hells."

> "At that time, I lived in a village near a saṃghārāma, and I frequently heard the sound of the monastery bell. My wife told me: 'You cannot do anything else. When you hear the sound of the bell, snap your fingers three times and recite the Buddha's name once. Compose yourself with reverence and do not be arrogant. Even in the middle of the night, do not abandon this practice.' I followed her instruction and never failed in it for twelve years. When my wife's life ended, she was born in the Trāyastriṃśa Heaven. Three years later, my own life also ended."

> "When my case came before the officials of the underworld, they judged me guilty and directed me toward the gate of hell. As I was about to enter, I heard three strokes of a bell. I stopped and stood still, my heart filled with joy and delight. As the method prescribes, I snapped my fingers three times and in a long, clear voice chanted the Buddha's name. The sound was full of compassion, resonant and pure. The presiding official, upon hearing this, was moved with shame and said: 'This is a true bodhisattva. How was the judgment made in error?' He immediately sent for me to be brought back and dispatched me upward to heaven. Arriving there, I prostrated with five limbs and paid homage to my wife, saying: 'Great teacher, by your great kindness and great grace, you have rescued and delivered me.' [I vowed]: 'Until bodhi, I will never abandon the recollection of Amitābha Buddha and the aspiration for birth in the Pure Land.'"

> "More than fifty persons, both ordained and lay, men and women, have attained birth in the Pure Land through recollecting Amitābha Buddha. These are recorded in the *Jingtu lun* (*Treatise on the Pure Land*) and the *Ruiyingzhuan* (瑞應傳, *Record of Auspicious Responses*). In our own country of Japan as well, there are many ordained and lay persons, men and women, who have attained birth. They are recorded in Yoshishige [no Yasutane]'s *Nihon ōjō gokuraku ki* (*Record of Japanese Births in the Land of Utmost Bliss*). And how many more there must be, those of hidden virtue in the marketplaces and cities, those who have fled fame to dwell in the mountains and forests, who practiced alone and departed alone. Who could ever know them?"

**Question:** The persons of the lowest of the three lower grades and the five hundred sons of the Śākya clan both recollected the Buddha at the time of death. Why do their outcomes of ascent and descent differ?

**Answer:** The *Qunyi lun* (*Treatise Resolving Various Doubts*) explains: "The five hundred sons of the Śākya clan merely recited the Buddha's name in obedience to their father. They did not arouse the mind of bodhi or aspire to birth in the Pure Land with earnest remorse. Furthermore, they were not sincere, and they only had a single moment of recollection, without meeting all the conditions."

### Section Seven: Benefits Even in the Evil Destinies

The *Mahākaruṇā Sūtra* (*Dabeijing*), fascicle two, says:

> "If any person, throughout the course of a single life, maintains mindfulness of the Buddha with reverence and faith, I declare that this person will attain the fruit of nirvāṇa, will reach the true limit of nirvāṇa."

> "Ānanda, set aside the merit of buddha-recollection in the human realm. If any animal is able to give rise to mindfulness of the Buddha, the World-Honored One, I also declare that this being will attain nirvāṇa."

**Answer:** The same sūtra, fascicle three. The Buddha told Ānanda:

> "In the past, there was a great merchant leader who took a company of merchants out onto the great ocean. Their ship was suddenly attacked by a makara, a great fish that was about to swallow them. At that time, the merchant leader and all the merchants, with hair standing on end and hearts seized with terror, each began to weep and wail: 'Alas! How wonderful was Jambudvīpa! How rare and precious! A human body is so difficult to obtain! Now we must part from our parents, sisters, wives, children, relatives, and friends. Never again will we see them, nor will we see the Buddha, the Dharma, and the Saṅgha.' They wept and wailed bitterly."

> "At that time, the merchant leader bared his right shoulder, placed his right knee on the ground, and standing upon the ship, single-mindedly recollected the Buddha. Joining his palms in homage, he cried out in a loud voice: 'Namo to the buddhas who have attained great fearlessness, the ones of great compassion, the ones who have pity on all sentient beings!' He called out thus three times. The merchants likewise called out three times in unison. When the makara heard the Buddha's name and the sound of homage, it was filled with great love and reverence. Upon hearing, it closed its mouth."

> "At that time, the merchant leader and all the merchants were safe and freed from the peril of the great fish. The makara, having heard the sound of the Buddha's name, gave rise to joy in its heart and henceforth refrained from devouring other sentient beings. Because of this, when its life ended, it was born in the human realm. In the presence of the Buddha, it heard the Dharma, left home, drew near to good teachers, and attained arhatship."

> "Ānanda, behold: that fish, born in the animal realm, merely heard the Buddha's name and subsequently attained nirvāṇa. How much more so for those who..."

The *Baqizhai pin* (八關齋戒品, *Chapter on the Eight Precepts of Abstinence*) says:

> A young nāga addressed the garuḍa bird in verse:

> Killing is an unwholesome act;
> It destroys life and causes early death.
> The body is like morning dew upon an insect:
> When it meets the light, life ends.

> Uphold the precepts, follow the Buddha's word,
> And one will be born in the heavens of long life.
> Accumulate merit over many kalpas,
> And one will not fall into the animal realm.

> Now my body is that of a nāga,
> Yet my practice of the precepts is pure and bright.
> Though I have fallen among the animals,
> I look toward my own deliverance.

When the young nāga spoke this verse, the young nāgas and nāga maidens' minds opened with understanding. When their lives ended, by following the Buddha's word, they were born in the Pure Land. By analogy, we may infer the benefits for those in the hells as well, as in the story of the king described previously, and in the section below on "the coarse mind and the wondrous fruit." Other benefits are described below in the section on the capacities of buddha-recollection.

---

## Gate Eight: Scriptural Evidence for Buddha-Recollection

**Question:** Every wholesome practice has its own benefit, and each can lead to birth in the Pure Land. Why then do you urge the single gate of buddha-recollection alone?

**Answer:** In urging buddha-recollection now, I am not dismissing the many other excellent practices. It is simply that for men and women, the highborn and the humble, without regard to whether one is walking, standing, sitting, or lying down, and regardless of time, place, or circumstance, it is not difficult to practice.
