Especially when one is approaching death and aspires to birth in the Pure Land, nothing is as readily accessible as buddha-recollection.

Therefore the *Soapberry Seed Sūtra* says:

> The king of the country of Nanda, Vaidūrya, dispatched an envoy to address the Buddha: "I humbly beseech the World-Honored One to bestow upon me, out of special compassion, an essential method of practice, so that day and [p.0077] night I may easily cultivate it, and in future ages be far removed from all suffering."
>
> The Buddha replied: "Great King, if you wish to extinguish the obstructions of affliction and the obstructions of karmic retribution, you should string together one hundred and eight soapberry seeds and keep them always with you. Whether walking, sitting, or lying down, you should always recite with utmost sincerity and undistracted mind the names of Buddha, Dharma, and Saṅgha, passing one soapberry seed with each recitation. In this manner, whether ten, twenty, one hundred, one thousand, or even hundreds of thousands of millions of recitations, if you are able to complete two hundred thousand recitations with body and mind undisturbed and free of all dishonesty and crookedness, then upon relinquishing this life you will be born in the third heaven, that of Yāma. Clothing and food will come of themselves, and you will constantly enjoy peace and bliss. If further you are able to complete one million recitations, you will sever the one hundred and eight bonds of karmic affliction, turn your back on the current of birth and death, advance toward the path of nirvāṇa, and attain the unsurpassed fruit."

The passages on this are very numerous. I excerpt ten in brief:

1. The lower fascicle of the *Sūtra on Divination of the Retribution of Good and Evil Karma* says: "If a person wishes to be born in the present pure land of a buddha in another region, that person should recite the name of that world's buddha with focused intent and undistracted mind. Contemplating as described above, one will certainly attain birth in that buddha's pure land and will see one's wholesome roots increase, quickly bringing about the fruits of the shallow and the deep."

Yet all of these passages say uniformly: "single-mindedly and exclusively recollect the Buddha of Immeasurable Life."

3. Among the Forty-eight Vows, there is one vow made specifically concerning the gate of buddha-recollection: "Even with as few as ten recollections, if one is not born there, may I not attain perfect awakening."

4. The *Contemplation Sūtra* says: for the gravest of evil persons, when there is no other expedient, merely reciting the Buddha's name suffices to attain birth in the Land of Utmost Bliss.

5. The same sūtra says: "If one wishes to be born wholeheartedly in the Western Land, one should first contemplate an image sixteen feet tall, standing upon the waters of the jeweled pond."

6. The same sūtra says: "The light illumines all worlds in the ten directions, embracing and never forsaking the sentient beings who practice buddha-recollection."

7. The *Amitābha Sūtra* says:

> One cannot attain birth in that land through a small measure of wholesome roots, merit, and karmic conditions. If there is a good man or good woman who, hearing of Amitābha Buddha, holds fast to the name, whether for one day or up to seven days, with mind undistracted, then when that person's life is about to end, Amitābha Buddha together with the assembly of saints will appear before them. At the moment of death, with mind unconfused, that person will immediately attain birth in Amitābha Buddha's Land of Utmost Bliss.

8. The *Pratyutpanna Samādhi Sūtra* says:

> Amitābha Buddha declares: "Whoever wishes to come and be born in my land should recollect me constantly and continuously, devoting themselves single-mindedly without ceasing. In this way they will attain birth in my land."

9. The *Sūtra of the Sound of the Drum* says:

> If any of the fourfold assembly can rightly receive and uphold that buddha's name, then by the merit of this, at the time when their life is about to end, Amitābha will immediately come with a great assembly to that person's place and enable them to see him. Having seen him, they are thereupon born in his land.

10. The *Treatise on Birth in the Pure Land*, examining the lower-lower grade of the *Contemplation Sūtra*, the *Amitābha Sūtra*, and the *Sūtra of the Sound of the Drum*, shows that merely reciting the name constitutes the karma of birth. How much more so, then, the contemplative recollection of the Buddha's excellent marks and merits!

**Question:** Are there not passages in the sūtras urging faith in other practices as well?

**Answer:** The other practices, in the course of explaining their various powers and benefits, happen to mention birth in the Pure Land incidentally. They do not compare with the direct exposition of the essentials of birth, which overwhelmingly speaks of buddha-recollection. How much more so when the Buddha himself has already said, "You should recollect me"? Nor does the Buddha say that his light embraces those who practice other methods. These passages are perfectly clear. Why give rise to further doubt?

**Question:** What the sūtras teach is adapted to the myriad capacities of beings. How can one cling to a single passage through a narrow view?

**Answer:** In the *Awakening of Mahāyāna Faith* (大乘起信論, *Dàshèng qǐxìn lùn*), Bodhisattva Aśvaghoṣa says:

> Furthermore, when sentient beings first study this Dharma, their minds are timid and weak, fearing that their faith will be difficult to bring to completion, and they wish to withdraw. One should know that the Tathāgata possesses a supreme expedient means for protecting and nurturing faith. That is, by the condition of single-mindedly recollecting the Buddha, in accordance with one's vow one attains birth in a buddha land in another region. As the sūtras say: if a person recollects the Western Buddha Amitābha single-mindedly and dedicates all wholesome deeds performed, aspiring to be born in that buddha's land, then one will attain birth.

If buddha-recollection is not the essential of birth, then the four great bodhisattva-patriarchs would not have exhausted the principle in this way.

---

## Gate Nine: Various Practices for Birth

Those who seek the Land of Utmost Bliss need not practice buddha-recollection exclusively. One should also understand the other practices and follow one's own inclination. This section likewise has two parts: first, a separate exposition of passages from various sūtras; second, a general summary of the practices.

### Part One: Passages from Various Sūtras

The Samantabhadra Vows of the Forty-Fascicle *Avataṃsaka Sūtra*, the *Sūtra of the Names of the Three Thousand Buddhas*, the *Sūtra of the Letterless Jewel Casket*, the *Lotus Sūtra*, and other Mahāyāna sūtras; the *Zuiqiu Dhāraṇī*, the *Uṣṇīṣavijayā Dhāraṇī*, the *Vimalaprabha Dhāraṇī*, the *Cintāmaṇicakra Dhāraṇī*, the *Ārurika Dhāraṇī*, the *Amoghapāśa Dhāraṇī*, the *Suvarṇaprabhāsa Dhāraṇī*, the *Amitābha Dhāraṇī*, and the dhāraṇī of Nāgārjuna that has brought about birth in the Pure Land: throughout all these exoteric and esoteric Mahāyāna teachings, upholding, reciting, and the like are set forth as the karma of birth in the Land of Utmost Bliss.

The *Larger Amitābha Sūtra* says:

> You should observe the precepts and purify your mind, single-mindedly and purely. Day and night you should recollect, aspiring to be born in the land of Amitābha Buddha. For ten days and ten nights without interruption, I will have compassion on all of you and cause all of you to be born in the land of Amitābha Buddha.
>
> If you are unable to do even this, reflect deeply and make a careful accounting. If you wish to liberate yourself, you must never cease this recollection. Abandon attachment and do not think of household affairs. Do not share a bed with women. Straighten your body and mind, sever desire, and with single-minded observance of the precepts, purely and with utmost focus, recollect being born in the land of Amitābha Buddha. If even for one day and one night this is not interrupted, then at the end of your life you will be born in that land, dwelling in the jeweled bathing pool upon a lotus flower.

Now I shall explain for you: there are ten kinds of birth. What are the ten kinds of birth?

> 1. Contemplating the body with right mindfulness and always harboring joy, offering food, drink, and clothing to the Buddha and the Saṅgha: one attains birth in the land of Amitābha Buddha.
>
> 2. With right mindfulness, giving excellent medicine to a single sick monk and to all sentient beings: one attains birth in the land of Amitābha Buddha.
>
> 3. With right mindfulness, not harming a single life, having compassion for all: one attains birth in the land of Amitābha Buddha.
>
> 4. With right mindfulness, receiving the precepts from a teacher, cultivating pure conduct with the wisdom of the precepts, and always harboring joy in one's heart: one attains birth in the land of Amitābha Buddha.
>
> 5. With right mindfulness, being filial to one's parents, respectful toward one's teachers and elders, and not harboring arrogance: one attains birth in the land of Amitābha Buddha.
>
> 6. With right mindfulness, visiting monasteries, paying reverence to stūpas and temples, hearing the Dharma and understanding even a single principle: one attains birth in the land of Amitābha Buddha.
>
> 7. With right mindfulness, observing the eight precepts for one day and one night and not breaking a single one during that day and night: one attains birth in the land of Amitābha Buddha.
>
> 8. With right mindfulness, on precept days and precept months withdrawing from one's dwelling and constantly visiting a virtuous teacher: one attains birth in the land of Amitābha Buddha.
>
> 9. With right mindfulness, constantly upholding the pure precepts, diligently practicing and delighting in meditation, guarding the Dharma and refraining from harsh speech; if one is able to practice thus: one attains birth in the land of Amitābha Buddha.
>
> 10. With right mindfulness, not giving rise to a mind of slander toward the unsurpassed Way, diligently upholding the pure precepts, and further teaching the ignorant, disseminating this sūtra's Dharma and instructing immeasurable sentient beings: all such persons will attain birth in the land of Amitābha Buddha.

The *Sūtra on the Questions of Maitreya* says:

> As the Buddha has declared, if by the merit of aspiring to the virtues of Amitābha Buddha [p.0078] one is able to sustain ten continuous recollections of the Buddha without interruption, one will attain birth. But what should these recollections consist of?
>
> The Buddha said: There are in fact ten recollections. What are the ten?
>
> 1. Toward all sentient beings, constantly give rise to a mind of loving-kindness; do not disparage their conduct. If one disparages their conduct, one will never attain birth.
> 2. Toward all sentient beings, constantly give rise to a mind of compassion, removing thoughts of cruelty and harm.
> 3. Give rise to a mind that protects the Dharma, not sparing even one's own life, and not slandering any teaching.
> 4. In the practice of patience, give rise to a mind of firm resolve.
> 5. With a deep mind of purity, do not be stained by offerings and personal gain.
> 6. Give rise to the mind of omniscience, recollecting it daily without forgetfulness or neglect.
> 7. Toward all sentient beings, give rise to a mind of respect, removing arrogance, and speak with humility.
> 8. In worldly talk and conversation, do not develop a taste for it.
> 9. Draw near to the intention of awakening and deeply cultivate all kinds of wholesome roots and conditions; distance yourself from clamor and confusion and from a scattered mind.
> 10. With right mindfulness, contemplate the Buddha and remove all other thoughts.

The *Ratnakūṭa Sūtra*, fascicle ninety-two, records the Buddha likewise answering Maitreya's question with these same ten minds. Among them, the sixth mind says: "Seek the wisdom of the buddhas, at all times maintaining a mind without forgetfulness." The remaining nine, though differing slightly in wording, are the same in meaning as the foregoing sūtra. But the concluding passage says: "If a person, among these ten minds, succeeds in fulfilling even a single one, and joyfully aspires to birth in that buddha's world, then it is impossible that such a person will not be born there."

The *Contemplation Sūtra* says:

> Those who wish to be born in that land should cultivate three kinds of merit. First: being filial to one's parents, serving one's teachers and elders, having a mind of compassion and not killing, and practicing the ten wholesome deeds. Second: receiving and upholding the threefold refuge, observing all the precepts completely, and not transgressing dignified conduct. Third: awakening the mind of bodhi, deeply believing in cause and effect, reciting the Mahāyāna sūtras, and encouraging others in practice. These three constitute the pure karma.
>
> The Buddha said to Vaidehī: "Do you now understand? These three kinds of karma are the direct cause of the pure karma of all buddhas of the past, present, and future."

And further:

> Those of the upper-upper grade of birth: if there are sentient beings who aspire to be born in that land, they should give rise to three kinds of mind, and thereby they will be born there. What are the three? First, the mind of utmost sincerity. Second, the deep mind. Third, the mind of dedication and vow. One who possesses these three minds will certainly be born in that land.
>
> There are also three kinds of beings who will attain birth. What are the three? First, those with a compassionate mind who do not kill, and who observe all the precepts. Second, those who recite the Mahāyāna vaipulya sūtras. Third, those who practice the six recollections, who dedicate the merit and make the vow to be born in that land. Possessing these merits, in one day or up to seven days, one attains birth.
>
> Those of the upper-middle grade: one need not necessarily receive and uphold the vaipulya sūtras, but should understand their meaning well. Regarding the first principle, one's mind is not shaken. One deeply believes in cause and effect and does not slander the Mahāyāna. By the merit of dedicating this, one aspires to be born in the Land of Utmost Bliss.
>
> Those of the upper-lower grade: one also believes in cause and effect and does not slander the Mahāyāna, but simply awakens the mind of the unsurpassed Way. By the merit of dedicating this, one aspires to be born in the Land of Utmost Bliss.
>
> Those of the middle-upper grade: if there are sentient beings who uphold the five precepts, observe the eight precepts, practice various precepts, do not commit the five grave offenses, and are without fault or transgression, then by dedicating these wholesome roots and aspiring, they attain birth.
>
> Those of the middle-middle grade: if there are sentient beings who observe the eight precepts for one day and one night, or observe the novice precepts for one day and one night, or observe the full precepts for one day and one night with dignified conduct intact, then by dedicating this merit and aspiring, they attain birth.
>
> Those of the middle-lower grade: if there are good men and good women who are filial to their parents and practice worldly benevolence, they attain birth.
>
> Those of the lower-upper grade: if there are sentient beings who commit many evil deeds but do not slander the vaipulya sūtras, such foolish people, having committed much evil without shame, at the time of death hear the title headings of the twelve divisions of the canon, join their palms, and call out "Homage to Amitābha Buddha," they attain birth.
>
> Those of the lower-middle grade: if there are sentient beings who violate the five precepts, the eight precepts, and the full precepts, such foolish people, when their lives are about to end, find the fires of the hells all arriving at once. Meeting a virtuous friend who, with great compassion, expounds for them the ten powers and awesome virtues of Amitābha Buddha and broadly praises the light and spiritual power of that buddha, and who also praises the precepts, concentration, wisdom, liberation, and the knowledge and vision of liberation, then upon hearing this, the person's sins of eighty koṭis of kalpas of birth and death are extinguished, and they attain birth.
>
> Those of the lower-lower grade: if there are sentient beings who commit unwholesome deeds, the five grave offenses and the ten evil acts, complete in every kind of unwholesomeness, such foolish people, due to their evil karma, should fall into the evil destinies. At the time of death, they meet a virtuous friend who, though unable to lead them in recollecting the Buddha, simply with utmost sincerity prompts them to call out without ceasing. Completing ten recollections, they call "Homage to the Buddha of Immeasurable Life." By reciting the Buddha's name, in each thought-moment they extinguish the sins of eighty koṭis of kalpas of birth and death.

The nine grades of the *Contemplation Sūtra* and the three grades of the *Sūtra of Immeasurable Life* do not go beyond this. Furthermore, the *Contemplation Sūtra* establishes the sixteen contemplations as the cause of birth. The *Ratnakūṭa Sūtra* explains four conditions for lotus-flower transformation-birth in the presence of the Buddha, in a verse:

> Offering flowers and incense to the Buddha and to stūpas,
> Refraining from harming others, and making images:
> Those who deeply aspire to great bodhi
> Will attain a place upon a lotus flower.

### Part Two: General Summary of the Practices

Master Huìyuǎn identifies four essential causes of birth in the Pure Land: 1) birth through the cultivation of contemplation, as in the sixteen contemplations; 2) birth through the cultivation of meritorious deeds, as in the three kinds of merit; 3) birth through the cultivation of the mind, that is, the three minds of utmost sincerity and the rest; 4) birth through dedication, that is, hearing of the Pure Land, then dedicating the merit, reciting, and praising.

Now I add my own summary: the practices taught in the various sūtras, taken as a whole, do not go beyond the precept teachings of the *Brahmā Net Sūtra*. Taken separately, they do not go beyond the six pāramitās. Examined in detail, there are thirteen categories: 1) giving of wealth and of Dharma; 2) observance of the precepts, whether the threefold refuge, the five precepts, the eight precepts, the ten precepts, or other degrees of precept-keeping; 3) patience; 4) diligence; 5) meditation; 6) wisdom; 7) filial devotion to one's parents; 8) serving one's teachers and elders; 9) not killing; 10) the ten wholesome deeds; 11) awakening the mind of bodhi; 12) freedom from arrogance; 13) freedom from attachment to offerings and personal gain.

A verse in the *Candragarbha* chapter of the *Mahāsaṃnipāta Sūtra* says:

> As a tree laden with fruit quickly destroys itself,
> And bamboo and reeds that bear seed likewise perish,
> As a mule that conceives destroys its own body,
> So too the unwise who pursue profit and gain.
>
> If there is a monk who receives offerings
> And joyfully seeks profit and clings firmly to it,
> There is no greater evil in the world than this,
> For it bars the way to liberation.
>
> One who thus craves profit and gain,
> Having attained the Way, will lose it again.

And the *Buddha Treasury Sūtra* records the prediction of Kāśyapa Buddha:

> In the time of Śākyamuni Buddha...

How much more for ordinary people. A great elephant passes through a window, and yet in the end it is caught by its tail. A renunciant leaves home, and yet in the end is bound by fame and profit. Thus we know: for one who seeks liberation, the final enemy is none greater than fame and profit.

Yet the great layman Vimalakīrti was in the household life while his mind had left it. The bodhisattva Medicine King in his original story fled the dust of the world and dwelt among the clouds and mountains. Practitioners of today should do likewise. Assess your own faculties and advance or withdraw accordingly. If you cannot control your mind, you should at least avoid such places. A hemp plant among the reeds grows straight without a support; an apprentice among butchers...

---

## Gate Ten: Questions and Answers for Summary Evaluation

The tenth major section, on questions and answers for summary evaluation, contains ten topics in brief:

1. The dependent and direct rewards of the Land of Utmost Bliss
2. The stages of those born in the Pure Land
3. The number of those who attain birth
4. The character of ordinary mindfulness
5. The character of mindfulness at the time of death
6. Coarse mind and sublime fruition
7. The relative merits of various practices
8. The causes and conditions of faith and slander
9. Auxiliary conditions that aid the path
10. Persons and teachings that aid the path

---

### Topic One: The Dependent and Direct Rewards of the Land of Utmost Bliss

**Question:** Amitābha Buddha's Pure Land of Utmost Bliss: what kind of body and what kind of land are they?

**Answer:** Tiantai says it is a response-body (*nirmāṇakāya*) buddha in a land of ordinary saints and common beings. The Venerable [Hui]yuan says it is a response-body in a response-land. Master [Dao]zhuo says it is a reward-body (*saṃbhogakāya*) buddha in a reward-land. The old traditional transmission all says it is a transformation-land with a transformation-body. This is a great error. According to the Mahāyāna *Sūtra of Identical Nature* (*Dasheng tongxing jing* (大乘同性經, *Mahāyāna Sūtra of Identical Nature*)):

> Those who attain buddhahood in a pure land are all reward-bodies. Those who attain buddhahood in a defiled land are all transformation-bodies.

The same sūtra further says:

> Amitābha Tathāgata, Lotus-Blossom Star King Tathāgata, Dragon Lord Tathāgata, Jewel Virtue Tathāgata, and all such tathagatas in their pure buddha-fields, whether those who have already attained the path or those who shall attain it in the future, all of these are reward-body buddhas.

**Question:** How long has it been since that buddha attained the path?

**Answer:** Many sūtras say ten kalpas. The *Larger Amitābha Sūtra* says ten minor kalpas. The *Sūtra of Equal Enlightenment* (*Pingdeng jue jing*) says eighteen kalpas. The *Sūtra in Praise of the Pure Land* (*Chengzan jingtu jing* (稱讚淨土經, *Sūtra in Praise of the Pure Land*)) says ten great kalpas. It is hard to know which is correct and which in error. However, Master Jinxing's commentary on the *Contemplation Sūtra* reconciles the *Sūtra of Equal Enlightenment* by saying that "eighteen kalpas" is a scribal error, the character for "eight" being "ten" with the middle dot omitted.

**Question:** How long will his future lifespan be?

**Answer:** The *Smaller Sūtra* says immeasurable, boundless, incalculable kalpas. The *Sūtra of the Prediction for Avalokiteśvara* (*Guanyin shouji jing* (觀音授記經, *Sūtra of the Prediction for Avalokiteśvara*)) says:

> The lifespan of Amitābha Buddha extends through immeasurable hundreds of thousands of billions of kalpas, yet it shall have an end. After that buddha enters nirvāṇa, the true Dharma shall abide for a period equal to his lifespan. Good sons, after the true Dharma of Amitābha Buddha has been extinguished, when the bright phase appears after the middle of the night, Avalokiteśvara Bodhisattva shall attain perfect, complete awakening beneath the bodhi tree, with the name Universal Light and Merit Mountain King Tathāgata. In that buddha's land there shall be no mention of śrāvakas or pratyekabuddhas. That buddha's land shall be called "Universally Assembled Treasures in Splendid Array." After the nirvāṇa of Universal Light and Merit Tathāgata and the extinction of his true Dharma, Mahāsthāmaprāpta Bodhisattva shall attain buddhahood in that very land, with the name Firmly Abiding Merit and Jewel King Tathāgata. The radiance of that land, the lifespan, and the duration of the Dharma shall be no different.

**Question:** The *Sūtra of Identical Nature* says it is a reward-body, but the *Prediction Sūtra* says he enters nirvāṇa. How do the various masters reconcile these two contradictory sūtras?

**Answer:** Master Zhuo reconciles the *Prediction Sūtra* by saying: "This is the reward-body manifesting the appearance of concealment; it is not an actual passing into extinction." Master Jiacao reconciles the *Sūtra of Identical Nature* by saying: "When it says that those who attain buddhahood in a pure land are judged to be reward-bodies, this refers to the enjoyment-aspect body (*shōyōjishin*), not the true reward-body."

**Question:** Which view is correct?

**Answer:** Master Jiacao says: "Since sentient beings undertake practice in a thousand different ways, the lands they see upon birth are also of ten thousand varieties." If one adopts this understanding, then when the sūtras and treatises judge it to be either a reward-land or a transformation-land, neither presents any difficulty. One should simply know that the buddhas' practice brings about both reward and transformation lands. As the *Compendium* (*She lun*) says, preliminary practice produces the transformation-land, while the true essence produces the reward-land. Whether reward or transformation, both aim to bring sentient beings to accomplishment. Thus the land is not established in vain, and practice is not cultivated to no purpose. One should simply trust the Buddha's words and, relying on the sūtras, devote oneself single-mindedly to buddha-recollection, and one will attain birth. There is no need to deliberate whether it is a reward-land or a transformation-land.

**Question:** Why do the descriptions of the buddha's physical marks differ?

**Answer:** The *Sūtra on Contemplation of the Buddha* explains the marks of all buddhas, saying:

> Because they resemble human form, thirty-two marks are taught. Because they surpass the devas, eighty subsidiary marks are taught.

Other cases should be understood by analogy.

**Question:** The *Contemplation Sūtra* says the bodhi tree is four million *li* tall. The *Ratnakūṭa Sūtra* says the bodhi tree is 1.6 billion *yojanas* tall. The *Sūtra of Ten Rebirths* says the bodhi tree is 400,000 *yojanas* tall, with a lion throne beneath it 500 *yojanas* high. The *Contemplation Sūtra* says the Buddha's body measures 600,000 billion *nayutas*. How do we account for such discrepancies?

**Answer:** There are different explanations. Some say that in the realm of a buddha, large and small do not obstruct one another. Others say that when discussing the tree's measurements, the sūtra speaks from the standpoint of a response-body buddha, while the body's measurements are given from the standpoint of the true buddha. There are still other interpretations too numerous to detail.

**Question:** The *Avataṃsaka Sūtra* says that one kalpa in the sahā world corresponds to one day and one night in Sukhāvatī, until the lotus opens after half a kalpa, and that in the lowest rebirth, twelve kalpas here correspond to as many kalpas as grains of dust. How then can it be called "Utmost Bliss"?

**Answer:** Even if one passes kalpas as numerous as the sands of the Ganges before the lotus opens, since there is not the slightest suffering, how could it not be "Utmost Bliss"? As the *Contemplation Sūtra* says:

> Those born through the womb-birth dwell in palaces of one hundred or five hundred *yojanas*, and each enjoys all manner of pleasures within them, just as the gods of the Trāyastriṃśa heaven do.

Even those outside the nine grades of birth, though there are differing accounts, do not differ in their bliss.

Moreover, the various masters disagree on the actual time-spans associated with the nine grades. Masters such as Huaigan and Zhijing, who grant that the days, nights, and kalpas spoken of are according to that land's own time, are rightly criticized. There is a later interpretation that is without error.

I now support this with four analogies. First, the Buddha's body measures a certain number of *yojanas*, but we do not calculate that land's *yojanas* by stacking up the finger-widths of that buddha. If that were the case, it would be like a person as vast as Mount Sumeru using the tip of a single hair as the width of a finger-joint. Therefore, just as we do not use the buddha's finger to measure his body, why must we use that pure land's time to measure how quickly the lotus opens?

Second, according to the *Uṣṇīṣavijayā Dhāraṇī Sūtra*, in the Heaven of the Thirty-Three, the deva Sujāta heard a voice from space saying, "You shall die in seven days." The Lord Sakra, following the Buddha's instruction, had that deva practice diligently for seven days and so he survived. Now, seven days in that heaven equals seven hundred years in the human world. One should not insist that matters must be settled within the eighty years of the Buddha's lifetime. The days and nights of the nine grades should be understood similarly.

Third, in Master Fahu's translation, it says that those born through the womb-birth see the Buddha after five hundred years have passed. The *Sūtra of Equal Enlightenment* says they are born by transformation within the lotus. Masters such as [Jing]xing take this passage as evidence that "five hundred years" refers to this world's years. I say: since the years of those in the womb-birth are already reckoned according to this world, on what grounds should the time-spans of the nine grades be calculated differently?

Fourth, if the nine grades were reckoned according to that world's time, then for the upper-middle grade, one night, and for the upper-lower grade, one day and night, would correspond to half a kalpa or a full kalpa in this world. If we accept that, then those born through the womb-birth, whose faith is tainted by doubt, would pass only five hundred years of this sahā world and quickly see the Buddha, while those of the upper grades, who practice with faith, would wait more than half a kalpa before their lotus opens. That would make no sense. For these reasons, the later interpretation is without error.

**Question:** If we reckon the days and nights of that world according to this world's time, then one born in the upper-upper grade should not immediately realize the tolerance of the non-arising of dharmas (*anutpattikadharmakṣānti*) upon arriving in that land. Why? Because a brief time of practice in this world is superior, while a long period of meritorious roots in that land would be inferior. If so, then one of the upper-upper grade who, in this world, completes the three meritorious practices in one to seven days still cannot realize the tolerance of non-arising. How could one realize it immediately upon hearing the Dharma after being born there?

**Answer:** It is not established that many meritorious roots cultivated over a long time in that land are inferior, or that few meritorious roots cultivated in a short time in this world are superior.

**Question:** The *Contemplation Sūtra* says:

> One who broadly plants roots of virtue in this world, bestows kindness and practices generosity and wisdom, who does not transgress the precepts of the path, who practices patience and diligence, who is single-minded in wisdom, who teaches and transforms others, who establishes good and right intention, who keeps the precepts in purity for one day and one night, surpasses one who does good for a hundred years in the land of the Buddha of Immeasurable Life. Why? Because in that buddha-land, everything arises naturally through non-action; all is an accumulation of goodness, with not a hair's-breadth of evil. And one who cultivates good in this world for ten days and ten nights surpasses one who does good for a thousand years in other buddha-lands.

**Answer:** This passage pertains to the difficulty of encountering the Buddha and the superior conditions for practice here, making swift realization reasonable. Alternatively, this sūtra simply illustrates the difficulty or ease of practice; it does not demonstrate the superiority or inferiority of the meritorious roots themselves. For example, when a poor and lowly person gives a single coin, though it may be praiseworthy, it cannot accomplish great things. When a wealthy and noble person gives a thousand pieces of gold, though no one praises it, it can accomplish ten thousand things. The practice in the two worlds should be understood in the same way. As the *Diamond Sūtra* says, faith and understanding during the Buddha's lifetime are not counted as supreme; it is in the age after the Buddha's passing that they become supreme. There may be still other meanings, which cannot be elaborated fully.

**Question:** Just as rebirth corresponds to different stages based on practices performed in this sahā world, do the rewards of merit also differ?

**Answer:** In general there is no difference, though in fine detail there are distinctions. As the second fascicle of the *Dhāraṇī Collection Sūtra* says:

> If a person does not make offerings of incense, flowers, clothing, food, and so on, then even though born in that pure land, one will not receive the reward of such offerings.

So in theory there are also distinctions of superiority and inferiority. However, because the appearances there are similar, it is said that there is no distinction of beauty and ugliness.

**Question:** How far is the Land of Utmost Bliss from here?

**Answer:** The sūtra says:

> From here, passing westward beyond ten trillion buddha-lands, there is the Land of Utmost Bliss.

Another sūtra says:

> To the west of this world, passing beyond a hundred thousand *koṭi-nayuta* buddha-lands, there is a buddha-world called Utmost Bliss.

**Question:** Why do the two sūtras differ?

**Answer:** According to the commentary by Master Zhiguang on the treatise, *koṭi* means "a hundred million" in our reckoning, and *nayuta* corresponds to the number *yi* (the largest unit in our counting system). In common parlance, ten thousand is *wan*, a hundred thousand is *yi* [in one system], ten *yi* is *zhao*, ten *zhao* is *jing*, and ten *jing* is *yi* [in the great reckoning]. Thus *yi* is itself a very large number. "A hundred thousand *koṭi*" is exactly ten trillion. However, there are four levels of *yi*: (1) a hundred thousand, (2) a million, (3) ten million, and (4) a hundred million. The *yi* referred to here means a hundred million. To make this clear, *nayuta* is also mentioned. There are still other explanations.

**Question:** Does Amitābha Buddha also have an impure land?

**Answer:** The *Mahāprajñāpāramitā-śāstra* says: "Amitābha Buddha also has both a pure and an impure land, just as Śākyamuni does."

**Question:** What is that impure land?

**Answer:** The Land of Utmost Bliss is the pure land. As for the impure land, its location is unknown. However, Master Daozhuo and others identify the land described in the *Sūtra of the Drum Sound* (*Guyinsheng jing* (鼓音聲經, *Sūtra of the Drum Sound*)) as that impure land. That sūtra says:

> Amitābha Buddha dwells together with his śrāvakas. His country is called Qingtai ("Peaceful Tranquility"), ruled by a wheel-turning sage-king. The city extends ten thousand *yojanas* in length and breadth, and is filled with the kṣatriya caste. Amitābha Tathāgata, Worthy of Offerings, Perfectly Enlightened, has a father named Moon-Supreme Wheel-Turning Sage-King, a mother named Supreme and Wondrous Countenance, and a son named Moon-Bright. His foremost attendant disciple is named Immaculate Renown, his foremost disciple in wisdom is named Gathering Light, and his foremost disciple in supernatural powers and diligence is named Great Transformation. At that time the Mara king is named Invincible, and his Devadatta is named Tranquility.

**Question:** Do Amitābha Buddha and the two lands of Bliss and Peaceful Tranquility really exist in a fixed location?

**Answer:** The textual teachings, adapting to conditions, provisionally indicate one corner. As for the true location, it is inconceivable. As a verse in the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra* says:

> The bodhisattva practices the ocean of all vows,
> universally following the hearts' desires of all beings.
> The hearts and deeds of beings are vast without limit;
> the bodhisattva's lands pervade the ten directions.

And again:

> The Tathāgata's appearance pervades the ten directions.
> In each mote of dust, immeasurable lands;
> the realms therein are likewise immeasurable,
> all abiding through boundless, inexhaustible kalpas.

**Question:** The Tathāgata's transformative activities do not arise in isolation; they must correspond to those beings with the right conditions. How can they pervade all ten directions?

**Answer:** Through practice over vast kalpas, incalculable numbers of beings have been brought to readiness. Therefore those with whom the Buddha has affinity also pervade the ten directions. As a verse of the *Avataṃsaka* says:

> In the past, through diligent practice over many ocean-like kalpas,
> he was able to turn aside the deep and heavy obstructions of sentient beings.
> Therefore he can manifest emanation-bodies throughout the ten directions,
> appearing beneath the king of bodhi trees in every land.

---

### Topic Two: The Stages of Those Born in the Pure Land

**Question:** The *Yogācārabhūmi-śāstra* says that only bodhisattvas of the third *bhūmi* and above are born in a pure land. What is the basis for now encouraging ordinary beings and śrāvakas before that stage?

**Answer:** Because pure lands are of various kinds, there is no fault. As Master [Huai]gan explains:

> The passages in the sūtras and treatises that discuss birth in pure lands each address one aspect. Since pure lands range from coarse to subtle, from superior to inferior, those who attain birth likewise range from higher to lower stages.

Master Daoxuan also says: "Bodhisattvas of the third *bhūmi* first see the reward-body buddha's pure land."

**Question:** Even if it is not a reward-land, how can those burdened with heavy afflictions and karma attain a pure land?

**Answer:** Tiantai says:

> Although the land of the Buddha of Immeasurable Life is supremely excellent in its reward, at the time of death, if one repents and recollects the Buddha, one's karmic obstructions are transformed and one attains birth. Even though one still possesses afflictions and defilements, by the sustaining power of one's vows, one can dwell there.

**Question:** If ordinary beings can also attain birth there, how do we account for the *Sūtra of Maitreya's Questions*, which says: "The mindfulness of those who recollect the Buddha is not the mindfulness of ordinary fools"?

**Answer:** "Not stained by afflictions" means that one attains birth having forever left the defiled realm. "Not a shallow, ordinary person" means that one shall become a buddha in the future, with the aspiration to broadly liberate all sentient beings throughout the dharma-realm. Possessing this excellent understanding, one is "not a fool." At the very moment of right mindfulness, the practitioner is endowed with these virtues.

**Question:** All sentient beings in that land are non-retrogressing. Clearly it is not a place where ordinary beings are born.

**Answer:** The "non-retrogression" spoken of is not necessarily a saintly attainment. As the *Essentials* (*Yaojue*) says:

> There are four kinds of non-retrogression. The *Daśabhūmika-vibhāṣā* says: (1) Non-retrogression in stage: having practiced the causes for ten thousand kalpas, one never again falls back into evil rebirths or the cycle of birth-and-death. (2) Non-retrogression in practice: having attained the first *bhūmi*, one's altruistic practice does not regress. (3) Non-retrogression in thought: from the eighth *bhūmi* onward, one attains effortless spontaneity. (4) Non-retrogression by place: though without textual proof, this is established by principle. Just as those in the heavens who attain the fruit thereby become non-retrogressing, so too in the Pure Land. Life is long, there is no illness, excellent companions guide one, what is right prevails without error, and only pure goodness exists.

**Question:** What are the stages of the nine grades?

**Answer:** The various masters disagree. The Venerable [Hui]yuan says: upper-upper birth corresponds to the fourth through sixth *bhūmis*; upper-middle corresponds to the first through third *bhūmis*; upper-lower corresponds to the thirty mental stages before the *bhūmis*. Master Li says: upper-upper is the stages of practice and dedication; upper-middle is the ten stages of understanding; upper-lower is the ten stages of faith. Master [Ci'en] Ji says: upper-upper is the ten dedications; upper-middle is the stages of understanding and practice; upper-lower is the ten faiths. Some say: upper-upper is the first mental stage of the ten abidings; upper-middle is the final stage of the ten faiths; upper-lower is the initial stage of the ten faiths. Others say: upper-upper corresponds to those at the level of ten faiths and before who are able to arouse the three minds and cultivate the three practices, while upper-middle and upper-lower encompass only those at the ten faiths and before who have aroused the mind of awakening and cultivate good as ordinary beings, distinguished into two grades by the depth or shallowness of their practice.

The reason the various masters' judgments differ is that they disagree on the stage of the tolerance of non-arising. The *Renwang jing* places the tolerance of non-arising at the seventh through ninth *bhūmis*. Various treatises place it at the first *bhūmi*, or at the stage of patience. The *Yingluo jing* places it at the ten abidings. The *Avataṃsaka Sūtra* places it at the ten abidings. The *Zhanchashan'e yebao jing* says that those who cultivate the single-practice samādhi and attain an approximate tolerance of non-arising are included. Thus each master relies on one particular interpretation.

Regarding the three grades of middle birth: [Hui]yuan says middle-upper corresponds to the first three fruits of the path; middle-middle to the seven preparatory stages; and middle-lower to those who have planted the seeds of liberation. Master Li agrees. Master Ji says: middle-upper is the four roots of wholesome qualities (*kuśalamūla*); middle-middle is the three worthies; middle-lower is those at the preparatory stage. Some say these correspond respectively to the stages of warmth, summit, and patience. Others say all three middle grades correspond to those who have planted seeds of liberation.

As for the lower three grades, the various masters all say these are ordinary beings burdened with transgressions who have committed evil.

**Question:** We learn that those who attain birth have their positions and stages defined. How do we know this still includes people like us?

**Answer:** Even if the stages of those in the upper grades are deep, are not the three lower grades meant precisely for people like us? Moreover, the later interpretation already takes ordinary beings at the level of the ten faiths and before as the upper two grades. And in Meditation Master Shandao's preface to the *Contemplation Sūtra*, he assigns the nine grades to ordinary beings of the Mahāyāna and Hīnayāna preparatory stages, and does not accept the lofty assignments of the other masters. Furthermore, the practices described in the sūtra for the upper three grades need not be taken as the deeds of those at advanced stages.

**Question:** If so, those born there should not quickly realize the tolerance of non-arising.

**Answer:** In Tiantai's teaching there are two levels of the tolerance of non-arising. For those of the Distinct Teaching, one realizes it through practice over many kalpas. For those of the Perfect Teaching, even beings in the evil destinies may have sudden realization. If this is so even in the defiled world, how much more so in the Pure Land. The conditions in that land cannot be compared with those anywhere else. Where else do all ordinary beings, even before reaching that stage, never retrogress? Where else do all ordinary beings attain the five supernatural powers and wield them without obstruction? The speed or slowness of attaining the fruit should be understood similarly.

**Question:** Is the benefit attained by those born in the upper grades always as described?

**Answer:** The sūtra presents only one general type. Therefore, Venerable Huiyuan's *Commentary on the Contemplation Sūtra* says:

> When the nine grades of people are born in that land, the kalpas required for them to receive their benefits may be discussed in terms of the nine grades. But there may also be some who attain results somewhat faster than this.

**Question:** The *Contemplation Sūtra* also says that great bodhisattvas such as Maitreya will be born in the Land of Utmost Bliss. Does this not prove that the nine grades of benefit as described in the sūtra are stated according to the inferior, not the superior?

**Answer:** In terms of those born in that land who first realize the tolerance of non-arising, their relative earliness or lateness is what is called "stated according to the superior." The higher-stage great beings are not discussed further. Whether those great beings are included within the nine grades or not is a separate matter requiring careful consideration.

**Question:** If those of lesser capacity can also attain birth, why is it that in recent times, among the thousands and tens of thousands who seek that land, scarcely one or two attain it?

**Answer:** Master [Dao]zhuo says:

> Their faith is not deep, sometimes present and sometimes absent. Their faith is not single-minded and not resolute. Their faith is not continuous, being interrupted by other thoughts. If these three are not in accord, one cannot attain birth. But if one is complete in these three minds, and yet does not attain birth, such a thing is impossible.

Master [Shan]dao says:

> If one is able to maintain thought after thought in continuous succession, making the end of one's life the limit, then ten out of ten will be born, and a hundred out of a hundred will be born. But if one wishes to abandon focused practice and instead engage in mixed practices, then out of a hundred, one will rarely attain it.

**Question:** Is there not a contradiction between "even a short time of practice leads to birth" and "one must practice throughout one's entire life"?

**Answer:** The types of karma are not uniform, so both masters are without fault. However, making the end of one's life the limit and diligently practicing without slackening so as to make one's karma decisive: this is the essential foundation.

**Question:** The second fascicle of the *Bodhisattva Dwelling in the Womb Sūtra* (*Pusa chutai jing*) says:

> To the west of this Jambudvīpa, at a distance of twelve billion *nayutas*, there is a Realm of Indolence. The land is pleasant, with music and entertainment, clothing and adornments, incense and flowers in splendid array, and seven-jeweled couches that turn. If one looks to the east, the jeweled couch turns; if one looks to the north, west, or south, it likewise turns. Sentient beings who previously aspired to be born in the land of Amitābha Buddha all become deeply attached to this Realm of Indolence and cannot advance further to be born in the land of Amitābha Buddha. Of hundreds of thousands of tens of thousands, at that time perhaps one person can reach it.

How should this be understood?

**Answer:** In the *Treatise Resolving Doubts* (*Qunyi lun*), the passage by Meditation Master Shandao cited above is quoted to address this difficulty. It further explains:

> The passage below in that same sūtra says: "Why? Because they are all indolent and their minds are not firmly fixed." This shows that those who engage in mixed practice are people whose minds are not firmly held, and therefore they are born in the Realm of Indolence.

If one does not engage in mixed practice but practices exclusively, such a person is exceedingly rare in that Realm of Indolence. But those born in the transformed pure land are not few. Therefore the sūtra speaks of this separately.

**Question:** We truly aspire to make our life's end the limit. But what about those who hear the name only once? How can we say it is of no benefit?

**Answer:** It may seem temporarily of no benefit, yet in the end it is not in vain. As a verse of the *Avataṃsaka* says regarding the benefits that come to those who hear the sūtra in future lives:

> If a person has the capacity to hear it,
> even while dwelling among difficulties due to other karma,
> because of former faith, they will accomplish it.

How could such a person lack this benefit? Consider: when a person who has committed evil all his life encounters a good friend at the time of death and with just ten recollections of the Buddha attains birth, such cases are mostly people who in past lives aspired to the Pure Land and recollected that buddha. Their wholesome roots, ripened within, are now brought forth. Therefore the *Ten Doubts Treatise* says:

> Those who, at the time of death, encounter a good teacher and accomplish ten recollections...

The meaning is the same. Therefore it is not in vain.

**Question:** If the lower-lower grade's birth depends on past wholesome roots, then the ten recollections of the original vow would be merely nominal and without substance.

**Answer:** Even if there are past wholesome roots, without the ten recollections one would certainly fall into the Avīci hell and suffer endlessly. This makes clear that the ten recollections at the time of death are the supreme condition for birth.

---

### Topic Three: The Number of Those Who Attain Birth

The *Contemplation Sūtra* says:

> The Buddha told Maitreya: "In this world, sixty-seven billion non-retrogressing bodhisattvas will be born in that land. Each one has already made offerings to innumerable buddhas, just as Maitreya has. As for the lesser bodhisattvas and those who have cultivated only a small amount of merit, their number is incalculable; they too shall all be born there."

> "From other buddha-lands it is likewise. In the land of the Buddha Distant Illumination, 18 billion bodhisattvas; from the land of the Buddha Treasure Store, 9 billion bodhisattvas; from the land of the Buddha Immeasurable Sound, 22 billion bodhisattvas; from the land of the Buddha Sweet Dew Flavor, 25 billion bodhisattvas; from the land of the Buddha Dragon Victory, 1.4 billion bodhisattvas; from the land of the Buddha Superior Power, 14,000 bodhisattvas; from the land of the Buddha Lion, 500 bodhisattvas; from the land of the Buddha Stainless Light, 8 billion bodhisattvas; from the land of the Buddha Foremost in Virtue, 6 billion bodhisattvas; from the land of the Buddha Wondrous Virtue Mountain, 6 billion bodhisattvas; from the land of the Buddha Human King, 1 billion bodhisattvas; from the land of the Buddha Unsurpassed Flower, innumerable and incalculable non-retrogressing bodhisattvas of wisdom and valor, all of whom have already made offerings to immeasurable buddhas and who, within seven days, are able to gather up the firmly established Dharma that great beings cultivate over a hundred thousand billion kalpas; from the land of the Buddha Fearlessness, 79 billion great bodhisattvas, together with lesser bodhisattvas and monks too numerous to count: all shall be born there."

> "Not only in these fourteen buddha-lands will bodhisattvas be born there. Throughout the ten directions, in immeasurable buddha-lands, those who attain birth are likewise exceedingly numerous and beyond count. If I were to speak merely the names of the buddhas of the ten directions and of the bodhisattvas and monks born in that land, even a full day and night would not suffice."

Those who cultivate even a small amount of good and will attain birth are likewise incalculable. How fortunate we are to have encountered the legacy of Śākyamuni Buddha! In the span of a hundred million kalpas, this opportunity comes but once. Let us take our place among those who, through even modest good, attain birth. We should strive diligently in our practice. Let us not lose this moment.

**Question:** If even a small amount of good leads to birth, how does the sūtra also say, "One cannot be born in that land through a small amount of good roots, merit, and causal conditions"?

**Answer:** There are various interpretations of this, too many to enumerate. I offer my own tentative view: "great" and "small" have no fixed standard; they are names given in relative terms. Compared to the great bodhisattvas, it is called "a small amount of good." Compared to the karma of cyclic existence, it is called "great." Therefore the two sūtras do not contradict each other.

---

### Topic Four: The Character of Ordinary Mindfulness

This has many varieties, broadly divided into four:

1. **Settled practice** (*jōgyō*): sitting in meditation, entering *samādhi*, and contemplating the Buddha.
2. **Scattered practice** (*sangyō*): recollecting the Buddha with a scattered mind while walking, standing, sitting, or lying down.
3. **Practice with characteristics** (*usōgyō*): either contemplating the Buddha's physical marks or reciting the Buddha's name, single-mindedly loathing the defiled world and exclusively seeking the Pure Land.
4. **Practice without characteristics** (*musōgyō*): though one recites the Buddha's name and aspires to the Pure Land, one contemplates that both body and land are ultimately empty, like illusions, like dreams, empty in their very substance, yet though empty they exist; neither existent nor empty. To penetrate this non-duality and enter the primary meaning: this is called practice without characteristics. This is the supreme *samādhi*.

Therefore, in the *Contemplation Sūtra*, Amitābha Buddha says:

> One who penetrates the nature of all dharmas, the emptiness and selflessness of all,
> and exclusively seeks the pure buddha-land,
> shall certainly accomplish such a realm.

In the constantly-walking *samādhi* section of the *Mohe zhiguan*, there are three relevant passages, which have already been cited above in the section on specific practices.

**Question:** Do both settled and scattered mindfulness of the Buddha lead to birth?

**Answer:** Earnest and devoted mindfulness unfailingly leads to birth. Therefore Master [Huai]gan explains the distinctions of buddha-recollection:

> Whether deep or shallow, whether in concentration or in scattered states: in concentration, it extends from ordinary beings all the way to the tenth *bhūmi*. It is like the bodhisattva Sudhana who, at the place of the monk Meghaśrī, requested instruction in the buddha-recollection *samādhi*. This is an extremely profound teaching. In scattered practice, it is for all sentient beings. Whether walking or sitting, at all times and in all places, one can recollect the Buddha without it interfering with any other affairs.

**Question:** Must one practice the contemplation without characteristics to attain birth, or can one also attain birth through practice with characteristics?

**Answer:** Master [Dao]zhuo says:

> If one is a beginner and not yet able to see through characteristics, one need only rely on characteristics and practice with single-minded devotion, and one will unfailingly attain birth. One should not doubt this.

Master [Huai]gan also says:

> Since those who attain birth differ in kind and grade, the causes they cultivate also vary in depth. One cannot simply say that only by cultivating non-apprehension does one attain birth.

However, there is a passage in a sūtra where a monk teaches other monks:

> You should recollect the Buddha, recollect the Dharma, recollect the Saṅgha, recollect the precepts, recollect generosity, recollect the heavens. Contemplate in this way the peace, bliss, quiescence, and cessation of nirvāṇa. Cherish nirvāṇa alone, the ultimate purity.

And it says that one who teaches in this way is called a false teacher and an evil companion, and that such a person is one who slanders the Buddha, who aids non-Buddhist paths. It says: "Such an evil person I would not permit even a sip of water." And further: "Better to ripen the five deadly transgressions than to nurture the view of self, the view of beings, and the view of a lifespan." And: "Better to have a view of self as great as Mount Sumeru than to have a view of emptiness even as small as a mustard seed."

These and similar passages in the Mahāyāna sūtras, which sometimes criticize attachment to existence and sometimes criticize attachment to emptiness, which sometimes praise the Great Vehicle and sometimes praise the Lesser, are all adapted to the capacities of different beings. There is also a sūtra passage that says:

> Now, Amitābha Tathāgata, Worthy of Offerings, Perfectly Enlightened, possesses thirty-two major marks and eighty subsidiary marks. His body's radiance is like a mass of molten gold...

And continuing through to: "not recollecting that Tathāgata, and also not apprehending that Tathāgata, one thus successively attains the emptiness *samādhi*."

The *Sūtra on the Contemplation of the Buddha's Samādhi* also says:

> The Tathāgata also possesses the Dharma-body, the ten powers, the fearlessnesses, the *samādhis*, and liberations, and all supernatural activities. Such wondrous matters are not within the range of perception of ordinary beings. You should simply give rise to deep-hearted sympathetic joy. Having given rise to this thought, you should then fix your mind on recollecting the Buddha's merits.

Thus we know: beginners should contemplate the form-body; advanced practitioners recollect the Dharma-body. Therefore it says, "thus successively one attains the emptiness *samādhi*." One must skillfully understand the sūtra's intent and not give rise to either denigration or praise.

The sūtra also says that after contemplating one hair-pore of the Buddha, and so on up to contemplating the complete form-body, there follows the passage on the ten powers, fearlessnesses, and *samādhis*.

**Question:** Among the nine grades, to which grade does the practice of buddha-recollection belong?

**Answer:** If practiced as taught, it properly belongs to the upper-upper grade. Depending on its relative superiority or inferiority, it should be assigned among the nine grades. However, the practices described for the nine grades in the sūtra are merely one illustration; in principle, the actual practices are immeasurable.

**Question:** As with settled and scattered practice both leading to birth, can one also see the Buddha in this present body through both?

**Answer:** The sūtras and treatises mostly say that when *samādhi* is accomplished, one immediately sees the Buddha. This makes clear that through scattered practice one cannot see the Buddha, except through special conditions.

**Question:** Can one see the Buddha through both the contemplation with characteristics and the contemplation without characteristics?

**Answer:** That one sees the Buddha through the contemplation without characteristics is a principle beyond doubt. Through the contemplation with characteristics, one may also see the Buddha. Therefore the *Contemplation Sūtra* and others encourage the contemplation of the Buddha's physical marks.

**Question:** If one may also see the Buddha through the contemplation with characteristics, how do we account for the verse in the *Avataṃsaka Sūtra*:

> Ordinary beings see all dharmas
> and merely follow after characteristics.
> Not understanding that dharmas are without characteristics,
> for this reason they do not see the Buddha.
>
> To have views is to be defiled;
> this is not yet true seeing.
> Far removed from all views,
> thus one truly sees the Buddha.

And again:

> Knowing that all dharmas
> are by nature without own-being,
> understanding the nature of dharmas in this way,
> one then sees Vairocana.

And the *Diamond Sūtra* says:

> If one sees me through form,
> or seeks me through sound,
> that person walks a mistaken path
> and cannot see the Tathāgata.

**Answer:** The *Essentials* (*Yaojue*) offers a comprehensive explanation:

> The Great Teacher's doctrinal approaches have many gates, each suited to the time and the capacity of beings, and none is at variance. The *Prajñāpāramitā Sūtra* constitutes one gate; the *Amitābha Sūtras* and others constitute another. All buddhas possess three bodies. The Dharma-body buddha has no form; its essence is neither color nor sound. Because śrāvakas and lesser bodhisattvas, hearing that the three bodies are not different, might then suppose they all share color and sound, and seeing only the form-characteristics of the transformation-body, might cling to the idea that the Dharma-body is the same, therefore it is called "a mistaken path." The *Amitābha Sūtras* and others encourage recollection of the Buddha's name and contemplation of his form so as to seek birth in the Pure Land. This is because ordinary beings' obstructions are heavy and the Dharma-body is hidden and subtle, making its essence difficult to take as an object. Therefore, as an expedient means, one is first taught to recollect the Buddha and contemplate his form.

**Question:** By what means does an ordinary being actually come to see the Buddha?

**Answer:** The vision arises through the conjunction of multiple conditions, not through one's own power alone. The *Pratyutpanna Samādhi Sūtra* describes three conditions, as already cited above in the passage on the ninety-day practice from the *Mohe zhiguan*.

**Question:** Through how many causes and conditions is one born in that land?

**Answer:** Examining the sūtras, there are four: (1) the power of one's own wholesome roots, (2) the power of one's own aspiration, (3) the power of Amitābha's original vow, and (4) the power of Amitābha's present radiance.

---

### Topic Five: The Character of Mindfulness at the Time of Death

**Question:** A person of the lower-lower grade attains birth through ten recollections at the time of death. What kind of recollections are these ten?

**Answer:** Master [Dao]zhuo says:

> It is simply to recall Amitābha Buddha, whether in general aspect or in specific features, and to sustain whatever contemplation one takes as object through ten recollections without any other thought intervening. This is what is called "ten recollections."

He also says:

> "Ten recollections in succession" is merely a name given by sages for one method of counting. If one can concentrate one's thoughts without being distracted by other matters, the path of karma is accomplished. One need not trouble oneself with counting the exact number.

He further says that for long-term practitioners, many recollections should follow this method.

Others say: to single-mindedly recite "Namu Amida Butsu" (*Namo Amitābhāya Buddhāya*), and the duration of these six characters constitutes one recollection.

**Question:** The *Sūtra of Maitreya's Questions* says the ten recollections for birth are each deep and vast. How then can ten vocal recitations of the Buddha lead to birth?

**Answer:** The various masters' explanations differ. Master Ji says:

> This passage teaches that at the time of single-mindedly reciting the Buddha's name, one naturally and completely possesses such ten qualities. It is not that one must separately contemplate compassion and the rest, nor that one counts compassion and the rest to reach ten. How can the ten be complete without separately contemplating them? It is like taking the three refuges when receiving the precepts: although one does not separately contemplate refraining from killing and so on, one nonetheless fully receives the precepts of refraining from killing and the rest. The principle here is the same.

He also says:

> Furthermore, "fully possessing the ten recollections while reciting Namu Amida Butsu" means being able to fully possess the ten recollections, including compassion and the rest, while reciting the Buddha's name. If one can do this, then whether one recites once or many times, every gate of the Pure Land teaching becomes a pure practice.

Why would you take that passage as correct and reject this one as wrong? You simply fail to understand the sūtra, and in doing so, you also confuse other practitioners.

**Question:** If "even a single recollection leads to birth," how does this relate to "ten recollections"?

**Answer:** Master [Huai]gan says: "For one who has committed the most extreme evil, ten recollections are required for birth. For all others, even a single recollection also leads to birth."

**Question:** How can a person who has committed evil all his life and never cultivated a single good, at the very moment of death with merely ten vocal recitations, extinguish his transgressions, permanently escape the three realms, and be born in the Pure Land?

**Answer:** This is like the exchange in the *Sūtra of the Questions of Milinda* (*Naxian biqiu wen fo jing* (那先比丘問佛經, *Sūtra of the Questions of Nāgasena*)):

> King Milinda asked the Arhat Nāgasena: "It is said that a person who commits evil for a hundred years, but at the moment of death recollects the Buddha, is born in heaven after death. I do not believe this teaching. It is also said that one who kills a single living being falls into hell after death. This too I do not believe."
>
> The monk asked the king: "If a person takes a small stone and places it in water, does the stone float or sink?"
> The king said: "The stone sinks."
> Nāgasena said: "But if one places a hundred-foot boulder upon a ship, does it sink?"
> The king said: "It does not sink."
> Nāgasena said: "The hundred-foot boulder in the ship does not sink because of the ship. Likewise, although a person has committed evil, through a single moment of buddha-recollection, one does not sink into hell but is born in heaven. Why do you not believe this? The small stone that sinks is like one who commits evil without knowing the sūtras and Dharma: after death one enters hell. Why do you not believe this?"
> The king said: "Excellent! Excellent!"

The monk continued:

> "Suppose two people die at the same time: one is born in the seventh Brahma heaven, and the other is born in the land of Kapisa. Though the distance between the two is vast, they arrive at the same instant. It is like two birds: one alights on a tall tree, the other on a low tree. When both take flight at once, their shadows reach the ground at the same time."

> "When a foolish person commits evil, the retribution is great. When a wise person commits evil, the retribution is small. It is like heated iron placed on the ground: one person knows it is hot, another does not. Both pick it up, but the one who did not know suffers severe burns, while the one who knew suffers only slight injury. The retribution for evil is the same: the fool cannot repent, so the retribution is great; the wise person knows it was wrong."

Thus we know: if one boards the ship of the Buddha's compassionate vow, one can attain birth in an instant. The principle is entirely reasonable.

The *Ten Doubts Treatise* also explains:

> I now weigh the matter with three kinds of reasoning. Heaviness and lightness are not fixed, and do not depend on the length of time, the frequency, or the quantity. What are the three? First, it lies in the mind. Second, it lies in the object. Third, it lies in the decisiveness.
>
> **In the mind**: when one commits transgressions, they arise from one's own false, deluded, and inverted mind. But when the mind recollects the Buddha, it arises from hearing a good teacher describe the true and real merits of Amitābha Buddha's name. One is false, the other real: how can they be compared? It is as if a room has been dark for ten thousand years, yet when the sun's light enters for a moment, the darkness is instantly dispelled. How could it be that long-accumulated darkness refuses to be eliminated?
>
> **In the object**: when one commits transgressions, the false, deluded, and darkened mind takes false objects and gives rise to inverted thought. When the mind recollects the Buddha, it hears the Buddha's pure, true, and real merits and name, and from this, the aspiration for unsurpassed awakening arises. One is true, the other false: how can they be compared? It is as if a person struck by a poisoned arrow, the arrow deep and the poison fierce, wounding muscle and reaching bone, upon hearing the single beat of the poison-dispelling drum, the poisoned arrow is immediately extracted. How could it be that deep poison refuses to come out?
>
> **In the decisiveness**: when one commits transgressions, it is with an intermittent mind and a mind that allows for a next time. But when one recollects the Buddha at the moment of death, it is with an uninterrupted mind and a mind that allows no next time, for one is about to relinquish one's life. The good mind is fierce and powerful, and therefore one is immediately born. It is as if a rope of ten strands, though a thousand men cannot break it, a single stroke of a boy's sword severs it in two. Or as if a thousand years' accumulation of grass, when touched by a great fire, is consumed in an instant.

> Or consider a person who has practiced the ten good deeds all his life and should be born in heaven, but at the moment of death gives rise to a single thought of fixed wrong view, and immediately falls into the Avīci hell. Since false and deluded evil karma, being fierce and powerful, can sweep away a lifetime of good and cause one to fall into evil destinies, how much more should the fierce and powerful recollection of the Buddha at the moment of death, being true and real, uninterrupted good karma, be able to sweep away beginningless evil karma? That it should fail to produce birth in the Pure Land is simply impossible.

Furthermore, there are parables to illustrate this: the fire parable is as above. A lame man, boarding another's ship and borne by the power of wind and sail, travels a thousand *li* in a single day. A poor man obtains a single auspicious treasure and presents it to the king; the king, delighted, bestows a lavish reward, and in a single instant the man's wealth and honor overflow. A man of low status who follows a wheel-turning king can mount the sky and fly freely. The ten-strand rope parable is as above. When the *zhèn* bird enters the water, fish and insects all perish; but when a rhinoceros horn touches them, all the dead come back to life. The yellow crane calls to Zi'an, and Zi'an comes back to life. How can one say that because a thousand years have passed beneath the tomb, there can be no restoration?

> All the myriad dharmas have both self-power and other-power, self-gathering and other-gathering. A thousand openings, ten thousand closings, immeasurable and boundless. How can one, with an obstructed consciousness, doubt the unobstructed Dharma?

Moreover, among the five inconceivables, the Buddha-Dharma is the most inconceivable. How can the bound karma of the three realms be compared to it? Consider these parables:

1. When a single sandalwood tree reaches maturity, it can transform the foul-smelling *eranda* forest for forty *yojanas* around, making it all fragrant.
2. When a lion's sinew is used as a lute string and a single note is played, all other strings snap.
3. A single *jin* of mercury can transform a thousand *jin* of copper into gold.
4. *Vajra*, though the hardest substance, when struck with an antelope horn, crumbles like... melted butter.
5. The *shāhē* medicine: merely seeing it grants immeasurable lifespan, and merely thinking of it grants knowledge of past lives.
6. When a peacock hears the sound of thunder, it immediately conceives.
7. The *śirīṣa* fruit, previously without form, upon seeing the Pleiades, produces fruit five inches long.
8. A turtle-hair rope cannot be broken by any force, yet it floats.
9. Though sand and gravel are small, they still cannot float on water.

The power of buddha-recollection should be understood by analogy. Do not doubt it.

**Question:** How great is the power of the mind at the time of death, that it can accomplish such a great matter?

**Answer:** Its power surpasses a hundred years of practice. The *Mahāprajñāpāramitā-śāstra* says:

> Though this mind lasts but a moment, its force is fierce and sharp, like fire, like poison: though small, it can accomplish great things. This is because, at the time of approaching death, the mind is resolute and powerful. Therefore it surpasses the force of a hundred years' practice. This final mind is called the "great mind," because the matter of relinquishing one's body and sense-faculties is urgent. It is like a person entering battle who does not spare his own life and is called "brave and strong." It is like the arhat who, at the moment of relinquishing attachment to this body, attains the arhat path.

When the wind dissolves one's form and the suffering of death presses upon one, giving rise to great fear and dread, one may then attain birth. One recitation of the Buddha's name can extinguish immeasurable transgressions. If that is so, then this should indeed be possible.

**Question:** If that were so, then pointing one's finger at the moon should cause the finger itself to dispel the darkness.

**Answer:** Master [Dao]zhuo explains:

> All dharmas are infinitely varied and cannot be reduced to one standard. Some names are identical with the reality. Some names differ from the reality. Names identical with the reality include: the names of all buddhas and bodhisattvas, the syllables of *dhāraṇīs*, the phrases of *sūtras*, and so on. Consider how in healing incantations one says, "The sun rises in the east, now red, now yellow." Even if one chants the spell at dusk in the west, the patient still recovers. Or consider how, when a person is bitten by a dog, one heats a tiger bone and applies it, and the patient recovers. Sometimes, when no bone is available, one rubs the spot with one's palm, calling out "Tiger! Tiger!" and the patient likewise recovers. Or again, when a person suffers from foot cramps, one heats a quince staff and applies it, and the patient recovers. If no quince is available, one rubs with one's hand, calling out "Quince!" and the patient likewise recovers. Names that differ from the reality are like pointing a finger at the moon.

**Question:** How does the *Buddhapitaka Sūtra*, fascicle three, account for the case of the four evil monks after the extinction of Great Adornment Buddha?

That sūtra says:

> After the nirvāṇa of Great Adornment Buddha, four evil monks abandoned the first principle, the Dharma of ultimate emptiness and non-existence, and took delight in the treatises of non-Buddhist Nirgrantha ascetics. These people, at the end of their lives, fell into the Avīci hell, where lying face-up, face-down, on the left side, and on the right side, they each endured nine hundred million billion years, being burned, scorched, and consumed on hot iron. After dying there, they were further reborn in the Ash Hell, the Great Ash Hell, the Living Hell, and the Black Rope Hell, in each enduring the same number of years of suffering. After dying in the Black Rope Hell, they were again born in the Avīci hell. Their lay and monastic followers, and their donors, numbering sixty-four trillion people, together with these four teachers, were born and died together, all in the great hells, subjected to burning and boiling. When the kalpa ended, they were transferred to hells in other worlds. When the kalpa reformed, they returned to this world's hells. After a very long time, they were freed from hell and born in the human realm for five hundred lifetimes, blind from birth. Later they encountered All-Illuminating King Buddha, went forth from home, and for one hundred trillion years practiced diligently as if their heads were on fire, but could not attain the tolerance of acquiescence, let alone the fruits of the path. After death, they returned to the Avīci hell. They subsequently encountered ninety-nine billion buddhas, and still could not attain the tolerance of acquiescence. Why? Because when the Buddha taught the profound Dharma, these people did not believe it; they opposed and violated it.

If even keeping the precepts and diligent practice cannot extinguish their transgressions and they return to hell, how can a single or tenfold recitation of the Buddha extinguish transgressions and lead to birth in the Pure Land?

**Answer:** Master [Huai]gan explains:

> Buddha-recollection extinguishes transgressions through five conditions: (1) the condition of arousing the Mahāyāna mind, (2) the condition of aspiring to birth in the Pure Land, (3) the condition of Amitābha Buddha's original vow (those Hīnayāna monks did not believe in the ten-direction buddhas), (4) the condition of the merit of buddha-recollection (those monks merely practiced the four foundations of mindfulness), and (5) the condition of the Buddha's sustaining power. Therefore transgressions are extinguished and birth in the Pure Land is attained.

**Question:** The *Contemplation Sūtra* speaks of birth through ten recollections, but also says, "Excluding those who commit the five deadly transgressions and those who slander the true Dharma." How do we reconcile this?

**Answer:** Masters such as Zhijing say: if one has committed only the five deadly transgressions, one can attain birth through ten recollections. But if one has both committed the deadly transgressions and slandered the Dharma, one cannot attain birth.

Others say: if the five deadly transgressions constitute indeterminate karma, one can attain birth; if they constitute determinate karma, one cannot.

There are fifteen different interpretations of this. Master [Huai]gan does not accept any of the other masters' explanations. He offers his own:

> If a person who has not committed deadly transgressions is considered, then whether one recollection or ten, one will be born in the Pure Land. If a person who has committed deadly transgressions is considered, then the passages elsewhere that broadly show the categories of those who attain birth: the original vow mentions only those whose birth is definite, saying, "If this is not so, may I not attain perfect awakening." Others' ten recollections lead to definite birth. Whether those who have committed deadly transgressions, through one recollection, will or will not attain birth is indeterminate. Therefore the vow mentions only the ten recollections of others, while other passages also take up the case of one recollection from those who have committed deadly transgressions.

This matter is unsettled and requires separate careful deliberation.

**Question:** Why is the birth of those who committed deadly transgressions through ten recollections indeterminate?

**Answer:** Because their past wholesome roots vary, and because the power of their recollection differs. Also, the circumstances at the time of death versus those of ordinary practice are different.

**Question:** The five deadly transgressions are karma whose retribution and timing are both determinate. How can they be extinguished?

**Answer:** Master [Huai]gan explains:

> In the incomplete teachings of the nine divisions, speaking to ordinary beings who do not believe in karmic cause and effect, the Buddha purposefully says there is determinate retributive karma. In the complete Mahāyāna teachings, all karma is said to be indeterminate.

As the eighteenth fascicle of the *Mahāparinirvāṇa Sūtra* says:
