> The physician Jivaka taught King Ajātaśatru the method of repentance, and his transgressions were extinguished.

And also:

> Your servant has heard the Buddha say: cultivating a single good thought can destroy a hundred kinds of evil. Just as a small amount of poison can harm a living being, so too a small amount of good can destroy great evil.

And in fascicle thirty-one:

> Good son, there are sentient beings who are frivolous and faithless regarding the workings of karma. To guide them, the Buddha teaches as follows. Good son, all actions have both heavy and light aspects, and both heavy and light actions each have two kinds: (1) determinate, and (2) indeterminate. Furthermore, heavy karma can be made light, and light karma can be made heavy. A person of wisdom, through the power of wisdom, can cause the extremely heavy karma that leads to hell to be experienced lightly in the present life. A foolish person causes presently light karma to be experienced heavily in hell. King Ajātaśatru repented his transgressions and did not enter hell. Angulimala attained arhatship.

The *Yogācārabhūmi-śāstra* says: "Before liberation is attained, karma is called determinate. After liberation is attained, it is called indeterminate."

These and similar passages from the Mahāyāna sūtras and treatises all say that the five deadly transgressions and other such evil karma can be transformed by the wise so as to be experienced lightly.

**Question:** A person of the lower grades attains birth through merely ten recollections and is immediately in the Pure Land. Where is the "light experience"?

**Answer:** The *Contemplation Sūtra* says of those in the womb-birth within that land:

> For five hundred years they do not see the Three Treasures, cannot make offerings, and cannot cultivate the roots of goodness. They regard this as suffering. Although there are other pleasures, they still do not take joy in them.

Not seeing the Buddha and not hearing the Dharma: this is the "light experience" of suffering.

**Question:** As with a single recollection of the Buddha's name at the time of death extinguishing eighty billion kalpas of transgressions, can ordinary practice accomplish the same?

**Answer:** The power of the mind at the time of death is strong and can extinguish immeasurable transgressions. Ordinary recitation should not be expected to match this. However, if contemplative mindfulness is accomplished, it too can extinguish immeasurable transgressions. If one merely recites the name, depending on the depth or shallowness of one's mind, the benefit should differ. This is as described in the earlier section on benefits.

**Question:** How do we know that even shallow-minded recitation of the Buddha has benefit?

**Answer:** The *Śūraṅgama Samādhi Sūtra* says:

> It is like a great medicine king called "Dispeller." In time of battle, when it is used to coat a drum, all those wounded by arrows, swords, and spears, upon hearing the drum's sound, find the arrows extracted and the poisons dispelled. Likewise, when a bodhisattva abides in the *śūraṅgama samādhi*, those who merely hear his name find the arrows of greed, hatred, and delusion naturally removed, and the poisons of wrong views entirely eliminated.

Thus we know that even shallow-hearted recollection brings benefit that is not in vain.

---

### Topic Six: Coarse Mind and Sublime Fruition

**Question:** If one creates good karma directed toward awakening in relation to the Buddha and thereby attains a sublime result, that stands to reason. But if one cultivates meritorious roots seeking only the rewards of human or heavenly existence, what then?

**Answer:** Whether one's motivation is tainted or pure, good performed in relation to the Buddha, though the result may be distant or near, will inevitably lead to nirvāṇa. The third fascicle of the *Mahākaruṇā Sūtra* says:

> The Buddha told Ānanda: "If there are sentient beings who delight in the fruits of attachment within the three realms of existence, and who plant good roots in the field of the Buddha, making this declaration: 'May this good root ensure that I never enter nirvāṇa,' Ānanda, that this person should fail to attain nirvāṇa is impossible. Ānanda, though this person does not seek nirvāṇa, because of the good roots planted in relation to the Buddha, I say that one who makes merit in relation to the Buddha, even though intending worldly rewards, will attain the transcendent fruit."

**Question:** How can one who seeks worldly rewards obtain a transcendent result?

**Answer:** The workings of karmic fruit are not uniform. When one dedicates all good deeds toward the Buddha's path, karma follows the direction of the mind. But when, with unwholesome views, one seeks heavenly pleasures through the practices of a dog or a chicken, such wrong views prevent karma from being redirected. Therefore, good karma cultivated in relation to the Buddha, regardless of one's motivation, will inevitably lead to nirvāṇa.

That sūtra offers a parable:

> It is like a landowner who plants seeds in good soil at the right season, irrigates at the proper time, and carefully tends them. If at some later time the landowner goes to his field and says, "Alas, seeds! Do not be seeds! Do not sprout! Do not grow!" still those seeds will inevitably sprout and grow.

**Question:** Even granting that one will eventually reach nirvāṇa, must one not still wander through birth-and-death for a long time?

**Answer:** Even if one wanders through birth-and-death for a very long time, the good roots will not perish and one will inevitably attain nirvāṇa. That sūtra says:

> The Buddha told Ānanda: "It is like a fisherman who, wanting to catch fish, places a hook with bait in a great pond. Once a fish swallows it..."

So too, sentient beings who come to have faith and reverence toward all buddhas, who plant good roots and practice generosity, and who, even for a single moment, give rise to faith: though they may afterward be pulled by evil and unwholesome karma, the World-Honored One, with his buddha-eye, sees these beings, and because the power of their initial aspiration is supreme, draws them out of hell and places them on the shore of nirvāṇa.

**Question:** According to such sūtra passages, because of faith one attains nirvāṇa. If so, should not a single hearing of the Buddha's name also be a cause of nirvāṇa? And how then do we account for the verse of the *Avataṃsaka*:

> If there are sentient beings
> who have not yet aroused the aspiration for awakening,
> upon once hearing a buddha's name,
> they shall certainly attain awakening.

**Answer:** The causes and conditions of all dharmas are inconceivable. It is like a peacock that conceives upon hearing the sound of thunder, or the *śirīṣa* fruit that, previously formless, upon seeing the Pleiades, produces a fruit five inches long. Through the Buddha's name, the seed of buddhahood is formed in just such a way. From this subtle cause, the great fruit eventually comes. It is like the *nyagrodha* tree, which grows from a seed as small as a mustard grain and spreads its branches to shade five hundred ox-carts.

If even the shallow causes of the mundane world are hard to fathom, how much more so the profound causes and fruits that transcend the world. One should simply trust and revere; one must not doubt.

**Question:** Does even one who takes the Tathāgata as object with a tainted mind receive benefit?

**Answer:** The eighth fascicle of the *Ratnakūṭa Sūtra* records that Guhyapada (the Vajra-bearer) told Sthiramati Bodhisattva:

> The physician Jivaka assembled various medicines and fashioned them into the form of a boy, handsome and exquisite, rare in the world, poised in every action, flawless in every posture, walking, sitting, lying down, and moving about. There was nothing deficient about this figure. In its displayed transformations, whether great kings, princes, ministers, officials, or wealthy elders came to the place of the physician Jivaka and gazed upon this medicine-boy, engaged with it, and examined its appearance, their illnesses were all cured, and they attained peace, tranquility, and freedom from desire.
>
> Consider then, Sthiramati: the physician Jivaka's treatment of worldly ailments already surpasses what other physicians can do. How much more so for a bodhisattva who practices the Dharma-body! When women, men, young and old, with desire, longing, and delight approach and engage with it, their cravings and defilements all come to rest through the Dharma-body.

If even a bodhisattva practicing the Dharma-body can produce such effects, how much more so the Buddha who has realized the Dharma-body?

**Question:** If desire directed toward the Buddha can produce such benefits, does slander or hostile aversion also produce benefit?

**Answer:** It is not only desire. The text speaks of "lust, anger, and delusion." Furthermore, the *Tathāgataguhya Sūtra* says:

> Better to commit unwholesome karma toward the Tathāgata than to make offerings and serve those of non-Buddhist, wrong views. Why? If one commits unwholesome karma toward the Tathāgata, one will eventually have a heart of remorse and will certainly reach nirvāṇa in the end. But if one follows non-Buddhist views, one will fall into hell, hungry ghosts, and animal realms.

**Question:** This passage seems to contradict the principle of cause and effect. It might also encourage delusion among sentient beings. How can an evil mind lead to the great bliss of nirvāṇa?

**Answer:** Because of the evil mind, one falls into the three evil destinies. Because of the single condition of having taken the Tathāgata as one's object, one will eventually reach nirvāṇa. Therefore it does not contradict the principle of cause and effect. The reason is that when such a being falls into hell, faith in the Buddha arises and a mind of repentance is born. Through this, by a chain of successive developments, one inevitably reaches nirvāṇa.

Knowing this, one should contemplate the great benevolence and virtue of the Buddha in a single recollection, a single utterance of his name.

**Question:** The awakening and nirvāṇa spoken of in these passages: among the three vehicles, to which fruit do they refer?

**Answer:** Initially, according to the being's capacity, one attains the fruit of one of the three vehicles. Ultimately, one will inevitably reach the unsurpassed fruit of buddhahood. As the *Lotus Sūtra* says:

> In the buddha-lands of the ten directions,
> there is only the One Vehicle Dharma,
> neither two nor three,
> except for the Buddha's expedient teaching.

And the *Mahāparinirvāṇa Sūtra*, explaining the definitive teaching of the Tathāgata, says:

> All sentient beings possess the buddha-nature. The Tathāgata is eternal, without change or alteration.

And again:

> All sentient beings will certainly attain *anuttarā samyaksaṃbodhi*. Therefore I say that all sentient beings possess the buddha-nature.

And again:

> All sentient beings without exception have mind. Whoever has mind will certainly attain *anuttarā samyaksaṃbodhi*.

**Question:** Why do the various texts say different things? Some say that a single hearing of the Buddha's name definitely leads to awakening. Others say one should practice diligently as if one's head were on fire. And a verse of the *Avataṃsaka* says:

> Like a person counting another's treasure
> without having half a coin of his own:
> one who does not practice the Dharma
> is just like this, despite much learning.

**Answer:** If one seeks swift liberation, then without diligent practice, it is as if one has nothing. If one takes the perspective of the long course of kalpas, even a single hearing is not in vain. Therefore the various texts do not contradict one another.

---

### Topic Seven: The Relative Merits of Various Practices

**Question:** Among the practices leading to birth, buddha-recollection is the highest. But is it also the highest among all other practices?

**Answer:** Among all other practices as well, this is the most excellent. The *Sūtra on the Contemplation of the Buddha's Samādhi* offers six parables.

**First:** The Buddha told Ānanda:

> It is like an elder on the verge of death who entrusts all his storehouses to his son. The son, having received them, amuses himself as he pleases. But one day he encounters trouble with the king, and countless bandits compete to plunder the stored goods. Only one piece of gold remains: Jambudvīpa gold, weighing sixteen *liang*, sixteen inches long. The value of one *liang* of this gold equals a hundred thousand *liang* of ordinary jewels. He wraps the true gold in rags and places it inside a mud ball. The bandits see it and, not recognizing it as gold, trample it underfoot and leave. After the bandits depart, the owner retrieves the gold and his heart is filled with great joy. The buddha-recollection *samādhi* is just like this; one should store it secretly.

**Second:**

> It is like a poor man who holds the king's jeweled seal and flees up a tree. Six soldiers pursue him. The poor man, seeing them, swallows the seal. The soldiers arrive and fell the tree. The poor man falls to the ground and his body is shattered. But the golden seal alone remains intact. The indestructible seal of buddha-recollection is just like this.

**Third:**

> It is like an elder on the verge of death who tells his one daughter: "I now possess a treasure, the finest of treasures. If you obtain this treasure, keep it well hidden. Do not let the king know." The daughter follows her father's instruction and takes a *maṇi* jewel and other precious gems and hides them in the refuse-heap. The household, great and small, all remain unaware. Later, in a time of famine, she brings out the wish-fulfilling jewel, and it rains down a hundred flavors of food and drink as she desires. In this way, from all manner of wishes, she obtains treasure. The buddha-recollection *samādhi*, firmly held and unmoved, is just like this.

**Fourth:**

> It is like a great drought in which rain cannot be obtained. A single sage chants a spell, and through his supernatural power, sweet rain falls from heaven and spring water gushes from the earth. One who attains buddha-recollection is like this skilled spell-chanter.

**Fifth:**

> It is like a strong man who has repeatedly offended the king's law and been locked in prison. He escapes to the seashore, opens the bright jewel tied in his topknot, and pays a ship's captain, who takes him to the far shore, safe and without fear. The practitioner of buddha-recollection is like this strong man, who pulls the chain of the mind-king and reaches the shore of wisdom.

**Sixth:**

> It is like a world-ending conflagration that burns the entire earth, yet the vajra mountain cannot be destroyed and remains in its original place. The buddha-recollection *samādhi* is just like this. One who practices this concentration abides in the place of all past buddhas.

This passage also says: "One should constantly practice and maintain this. One should constantly guard it and not follow any other dharma."

Furthermore, among the difficult and easy paths: the easy path is precisely buddha-recollection. The third fascicle of the *Daśabhūmika-vibhāṣā* says:

> Just as worldly roads may be difficult or easy: traveling on land by foot is hard, while traveling by water on a boat is pleasant. So too the path to awakening: some practice with strenuous diligence, while some, through the expedient of faith, easily and swiftly reach the stage of non-retrogression.

The text then goes on to enumerate more than a hundred buddhas and more than a hundred great bodhisattvas, including Maitreya, Vajragarbha, Vimalakīrti, Akṣayamati, Bhadrapāla, Mañjuśrī, Gadgadasvara, Siṃhanāda, Gandhahastin, Sadāparibhūta, Avalokiteśvara, Mahāsthāmaprāpta, and others. Among them, Amitābha Buddha is praised at greatest length.

> Among all practices, only the practice of buddha-recollection
> is easy to cultivate and leads to a high stage.
> Know that this is the most excellent practice.

And the ninety-second fascicle of the *Ratnakūṭa Sūtra* says:

> If a bodhisattva busies himself with many affairs and constructs seven-jeweled stupas filling the entire three-thousand great-thousand world system, such a bodhisattva cannot bring me joy, nor is this making offerings to me or paying me reverence. But if a bodhisattva takes up even a single four-line verse of the teachings that accord with the *pāramitās*, reads, recites, and practices it, and expounds it for others, this person truly makes offerings to me. Why? Because the awakening of all buddhas arises from extensive learning.

Regarding practitioners: a bodhisattva who reads, recites, practices, and teaches at a single location should be served by a bodhisattva who only listens and recites. A bodhisattva who listens and recites throughout Jambudvīpa should attend upon and serve a single bodhisattva who diligently cultivates meditation. Such meritorious practice, the Tathāgata rejoices in and the Tathāgata approves. And one who attends upon and serves a bodhisattva who diligently cultivates wisdom shall obtain an immeasurable accumulation of merit.

> Why? The practice of wisdom is unsurpassed and supreme, surpassing all practices of the three realms.

A verse of the *Mahāsaṃnipāta Candragarbha Sūtra* says:

> If a person, in the presence of a hundred billion buddhas,
> over many years constantly makes offerings,
> and if another can abide for seven days in samādhi
> in this world, the latter attains pure awakening.
>
> If a person slanders one who abides in meditation,
> this is called slandering all the tathagatas.
> If a person destroys many hundreds of thousands of stupas
> and even burns hundreds of thousands of monasteries,
> if another slanders one who abides in meditation,
> that person's transgression is far greater than these.
>
> If there is one who makes offerings to one who abides in meditation,
> with food, drink, clothing, and medicine,
> that person extinguishes immeasurable transgressions
> and will not fall into the three evil destinies.
>
> Therefore I now universally declare to you:
> those who seek to accomplish the Buddha's path should always be in meditation.

If this is the case for meditation in general, how much more so for the buddha-recollection *samādhi*, which is the king of all *samādhis*.

**Question:** If the practice of meditation is superior to reading, reciting, and expounding, how do we account for the *Lotus Sūtra*, "Distinguishing Merits" chapter, which says that the merit of the first five *pāramitās* practiced over eighty trillion *nayuta* kalpas is compared to a single thought of faith and understanding of the *Lotus Sūtra*, and the former does not equal even a hundred-thousand-million-billionth part of the latter? And how much more for one who broadly expounds it for others?

**Answer:** Each of these practices has its own relative depth, because the distinction between partial and perfect teachings makes a difference. If we compare practices within the same teaching, the ranking is as described above. If we compare across different teachings, the meditation of a partial teaching does not match even the reading and practice of a perfect teaching. The *Mahāsaṃnipāta* and the *Ratnakūṭa* discuss ranking within a single teaching. The *Lotus Sūtra*'s comparison contrasts the partial and perfect teachings against each other. Therefore the various texts do not contradict one another.

The buddha-recollection *samādhi* should be understood in the same way. The *samādhi* of a partial teaching is supreme within that teaching. The *samādhi* of one who practices the perfect teaching surpasses all other practices universally.

Furthermore, there are two kinds of meditation: (1) meditation in accord with wisdom, which is supreme; and (2) dark meditation (meditation without insight), which cannot be considered supreme. The buddha-recollection *samādhi* should be classified under the first.

---

### Topic Eight: The Causes and Conditions of Faith and Slander

The *Pratyutpanna Samādhi Sūtra* says:

> This is not due to meritorious deeds performed at only one buddha's assembly, nor at two, three, or even ten. It is at the assemblies of a hundred buddhas that one hears this *samādhi*. Those who in a future age hear this *samādhi*, copy it, study it, recite it, and preserve the sūtra scrolls, and who, moreover, guard it for a single day and night: their merit is incalculable. Through this, one attains the stage of non-retrogression, and whatever one wishes is fulfilled.

**Question:** If that is so, then all who hear it should certainly have faith. Why is it that among the śrāvakas, some believe and some do not?

**Answer:** The *Sūtra of Immeasurable Purity* (*Wuliang qingjing jue jing*) says:

> Good men and good women who hear the name of the Buddha of Immeasurable Purity and leap for joy, with their body-hair standing on end as if being pulled out: all of these have already performed the Buddha's work in past lives. But ordinary people who doubt and do not believe: all of them have come from the evil destinies.

And the seventh fascicle of the *Mahāsaṃnipāta Sūtra* says:

> If there are sentient beings who have planted roots of virtue at the assemblies of immeasurable, boundless buddhas, only then can they hear the Tathāgata's ten powers, four kinds of fearlessness, and other such true Dharma. Even if they hear it, they may not necessarily believe it. Its causes and conditions are difficult to know. It is like a single green bean in a pile of black beans.

Those who hear but do not believe are simply those of meager virtue.

**Question:** Even the Buddha, in his former lives, practiced all the *pāramitās* for eighty thousand years and still could not hear this Dharma. How can a person of meager virtue hear it so easily? Even granting it is rare, it still seems to violate reason.

**Answer:** This meaning is hard to understand. Let me tentatively analyze it. The good and evil of sentient beings have four distinct phases:

1. **When evil is overwhelmingly dominant**: In this phase, one does not hear the Dharma. As the *Lotus Sūtra* says, "Those of overweening pride will not hear the Dharma for two hundred billion kalpas."
2. **When good is overwhelmingly dominant**: In this phase, one constantly hears the Dharma. This includes great bodhisattvas from the stages of the ten abidings upward.
3. **At the juncture of good and evil**: This refers to the moment when one is about to leave the ordinary and enter the sacred. Among those in this phase, there is one type for whom hearing the Dharma is extremely difficult, but who, upon hearing, immediately awakens. Examples include the Bodhisattva Sadaparudita ("Ever-Weeping") and the old woman Sudatta. Some are obstructed by Mara, or by their own delusions, and though their hearing and seeing are blocked, they will soon awaken.
4. **When good and evil are evenly mixed**: In this phase, since both good and evil belong to the realm of cyclic existence, it is generally difficult to hear the Dharma. But since evil does not dominate, it is not entirely impossible. And since one is not at the juncture, even if one hears, there is no great benefit. The vast multitude of beings in the six destinies and four modes of birth are of this type.

Therefore, even among those of high station, there are some who find it hard to hear. And among those of ordinary rank, there are some who do hear. This analysis is not yet definitive; I leave it for the wise to accept or reject.

**Question:** What is the retribution for those who do not believe?

**Answer:** The *Sūtra Proclaiming the Merits of All Buddhas* (*Chengyang zhufo gongde jing* (稱揚諸佛功德經, *Sūtra Proclaiming the Merits of All Buddhas*)) says:

> If anyone does not believe in praising and proclaiming the merit of Amitābha Buddha's name and slanders it, that person will spend five kalpas in hell, enduring all manner of suffering.

**Question:** If one lacks deep faith and gives rise to doubt, will one ultimately fail to attain birth?

**Answer:** If one entirely lacks faith, does not cultivate the practices, and does not aspire to that land, one should not expect to attain birth. However, if one doubts the Buddha's wisdom yet still aspires to that land and cultivates the practices, one can also attain birth. As the *Contemplation Sūtra* says:

> If there are sentient beings who, with a doubting and hesitant mind, cultivate meritorious deeds and aspire to be born in that land, but who do not understand the Buddha's wisdom, the inconceivable wisdom, the indescribable wisdom, the vast Mahāyāna wisdom, or the peerless, unequaled, supreme, and most excellent wisdom, and who doubt and disbelieve these wisdoms, yet still believe in the karmic consequences of good and evil, cultivate wholesome roots, and aspire to be born in that land: such beings will be born in the palaces of that land and will dwell there for five hundred years, during which time they will not see the Buddha, not hear the sūtras and Dharma, and not see the bodhisattvas and śrāvakas.

As a penalty for their doubt, they are like criminals. Yet they still attain birth according to their aspiration, through the compassionate power of the Buddha's vow.

The *Sūtra of Immeasurable Purity* classifies those born through the womb-birth as people of the middle and lower grades.

The various masters' interpretations are too numerous to enumerate here.

**Question:** What are the "Buddha's wisdom and so on" in detail?

**Answer:** Master Jinxing matches them to the five dharmas of the *Buddhabhūmi Sūtra* to derive five wisdoms. The pure dharma-realm is "the Buddha's wisdom." The four great mirror-wisdoms and so on correspond in order to the other four: inconceivable, indescribable, vast Mahāyāna, and peerless supreme.

Master Xuanyi reverses the correspondence: the Buddha's wisdom is as above, while the latter four wisdoms are matched in reverse order to the four, beginning with the wisdom of accomplishing what is to be done.

There are other different interpretations as well, but they need not be elaborated.

---

### Topic Nine: Auxiliary Conditions That Aid the Path

**Question:** Ordinary practitioners require food and clothing. Though these are minor conditions, they make possible the great work. Without clothing or food, one cannot be at ease; how then can the way of Dharma be practiced?

**Answer:** There are two types of practitioners: lay and monastic. For the layperson, domestic affairs are managed freely; meals and clothing present no obstacle to buddha-recollection. As in the example of King Prasenajit in the *Mūlasarvāstivāda Vinaya*.

For monastics there are also three types. Those of the highest capacity take grass for their seat and deerskin for their robe, with a single vegetable or fruit for food, like the Great Being in the Himalayas. Those of middle capacity always beg for food and wear robes made from discarded rags. Those of the lowest capacity accept the faithful offerings of donors and are content with even a small amount. Details are found in the fourth fascicle of the *Mohe zhiguan*.

Moreover, if a disciple of the Buddha devotes himself exclusively to the correct path and is without covetous desires, material needs will naturally be provided. As the *Mahāprajñāpāramitā-śāstra* says:

> It is like a monk who covets offerings yet receives none, while one who covets nothing lacks for nothing. The mind is the same: if one discriminates and grasps at characteristics, one does not attain the true Dharma.

And in the *Mahāsaṃnipāta Candragarbha Sūtra*, the devas of the six desire heavens, the sun, moon, and stars, the *nāgas*, the eight classes of beings, each in the Buddha's presence, vowed:

> If the Buddha's śrāvaka disciples abide in the Dharma, follow the Dharma, and practice with their three karmas in accord, we shall all protect and nurture them, supply what they need, and ensure they lack for nothing.

And also:

> If the World-Honored One's śrāvaka disciples accumulate nothing, we shall protect, nurture, and provide for them.

And further:

> If the World-Honored One's śrāvaka disciples do accumulate, and if their three karmas are not in accord with the Dharma, we shall abandon them and no longer nurture them.

**Question:** Ordinary beings cannot necessarily keep their three karmas in perfect accord. If they have deficiencies, will they not lose this protection?

**Answer:** Such a question is precisely what people who are lazy and lack the aspiration for the path would raise. If one truly seeks awakening and truly aspires to the Pure Land, one would sooner give up one's life than break the precepts. One should endure a single lifetime of diligent effort to secure the sublime fruit of eternal kalpas.

Moreover, even if one has broken the precepts, one is not entirely without hope. As the Buddha says in the same sūtra:

> If there are sentient beings who, for my sake, leave the household life, shave their heads, and don the kāṣāya robe, even if they do not keep the precepts, they have already been stamped with the seal of nirvāṇa. If anyone, whether lay or monastic, torments such a person by illegal means, reviling, insulting, striking, binding, cutting, or seizing their robe and bowl and various requisites, that person destroys the true reward-body of the buddhas of the three times, gouges out the eyes of all devas and humans, and acts to conceal the true Dharma and extinguish the seed of the Three Treasures, thereby causing devas and humans to lose their benefit and fall into hell.

All beings, from the *nāgas* to the *kaṭapūtanas*, humans and non-humans, all join their palms and say:

> We vow that toward all śrāvaka disciples of the Buddha, even those who do not keep the precepts, even those who have shaved their heads and wear but a scrap of kāṣāya, we shall regard them as our teachers, protect and nurture them, and supply all they need so they lack for nothing. If any other *nāga*, *deva*, or *kaṭapūtana* should torment them, or even look at them with malicious intent, we shall cause those beings to have their marks diminished and disfigured, and shall no longer permit them to dwell, eat, or associate with us.

And all the bodhisattva-mahāsattvas in that assembly said:

> Good sons, when I practiced the bodhisattva path in the past, I made such offerings to the buddhas of former times. By these good roots, may I attain the cause of the three kinds of awakening. Out of compassion for sentient beings, I divide the fruit of these rewards into three parts. One part I retain for myself. The second part I give to those śrāvakas after my passing who dwell in meditation, liberation, and *samādhi* and who are firmly established in their practice, so they lack for nothing. The third part I give to those śrāvakas who break the precepts but still read and recite the sūtras, and to those in the age of the true Dharma and the semblance Dharma who shave their heads and wear the kāṣāya, so they lack for nothing.

The Buddha also said:

> Maitreya, I now further entrust to your hands the śrāvakas whose three karmas are in accord, both monks and nuns, laymen and laywomen. Do not let them lack for anything or end their lives in solitude. And those in the age of the true and semblance Dharma who break the precepts and wear the kāṣāya, I also entrust to your hands. Do not let them lack for any requisites or end their lives in hardship. Nor let any *caṇḍāla* king torment them and cause them physical or mental suffering.

If this is so for those who merely break the precepts, how much more for one who arouses the great aspiration and sincerely recollects the Buddha?

**Question:** If even precept-breakers are protected by the *nāgas* and *devas*, how do we account for the *Brahmajāla Sūtra* saying that five thousand demons brush away the footprints of precept-breaking monks, and the *Mahāparinirvāṇa Sūtra* saying that kings, ministers, and precept-keeping monks should severely discipline, expel, reproach, and censure precept-breakers?

**Answer:** If one disciplines them in accordance with proper principle, this accords with the Buddha's teaching. If one torments them against proper principle, this violates the sacred intent. Therefore there is no contradiction.

As the *Candragarbha Sūtra* records the Buddha saying:

> If kings and ministers see monastics committing great transgressions, such as killing, stealing, sexual misconduct, or lying, and other unwholesome acts, they should expel them from the country, the cities, and the villages in accordance with the Dharma, and not permit them to remain in the monasteries, nor share in the community's rites or material support. They should not be beaten. If they are beaten, this is not in accord with principle. Nor should they be verbally abused. Under no circumstances should physical punishment be imposed upon them. If one willfully violates the Dharma and punishes them, that person will fall back from liberation and will certainly end up in the Avīci hell. How much worse then to beat and punish those monastics who have left home for the Buddha's sake and do keep the precepts.

This matter is not yet fully resolved. The *Brahmajāla Sūtra* unconditionally says their footprints are brushed away. The *Candragarbha Sūtra* unconditionally says they should be provided for. How can these be reconciled?

**Answer:** To understand the intent regarding merits and demerits, one must ascertain the person's actual conduct. One cannot rigidly determine what any particular person will do. Whether restrictive or permissive, each teaching produces great benefit. Or perhaps, just as people's inclinations differ, so too do the wishes of the non-human beings differ. Practitioners should make their own determination.

**Question:** Since this topic has come up, let us ask further: what merit and demerit accrue from making offerings to or tormenting precept-breaking monastics?

**Answer:** A verse in the *Daśacakrakṣitigarbha Sūtra* says:

> To harbor ill will toward one clothed in the robe of liberation,
> the banner of the Ganges-sands of buddhas:
> one will certainly fall into the interminable hell.
> How much worse to shed the blood of one who has left the household life.

If the transgression is this great for tormenting a precept-breaker, how much greater the transgression for tormenting one who keeps the precepts!

**Question:** If there is such great transgression, should one not discipline them?

**Answer:** If one has the authority and does not discipline them, one also incurs transgression. This is a great enemy of the Buddha-Dharma. The third fascicle of the *Mahāparinirvāṇa Sūtra* says:

> If a precept-keeping monk sees one who is destroying the Dharma, he should drive them out, reproach them, and take action against them. If a good monk sees a Dharma-destroyer and lets it pass without reproaching, driving out, or taking action, know that this person is an enemy of the Buddha-Dharma. If one is able to drive them out, reproach, and take action, this person is my disciple, a true student.

And also:

> If there is a monk who, though keeping the precepts, for the sake of profit and sustenance associates with precept-breakers, sitting and walking together with them, or who dwells in a secluded place, with dull faculties, dim and sluggish, with few desires and begging for food, and who on the days of reciting the precepts and of the *pravāraṇā* ceremony teaches his own disciples to confess purely, yet when he sees other monks who are not his disciples committing many transgressions, he cannot instruct them to confess purely, but nonetheless joins with them in the recitation and the ceremony: this is called a foolish monk.

Practitioners should pursue a middle course, gaining the true intent.

---

### Topic Ten: Persons and Teachings That Aid the Path

This is briefly divided into three:

**First, one needs a clear-sighted teacher** who is well versed in both inner and outer discipline and can remove obstructions. One should attend upon such a teacher with reverence. As the *Mahāprajñāpāramitā-śāstra* says:

> Just as rain falls but does not stay on the mountaintop, inevitably flowing down to the low places: if a person is proud and self-exalting, the water of the Dharma will not enter. If one reverently serves a good teacher, merit will flow toward that person.

**Second, one needs companions in practice**, like fellow travelers through perilous terrain, encouraging one another even at the moment of death. As the *Lotus Sūtra* says:

> A good friend is a great cause and condition.

And when Ānanda said, "A good friend is half the cause and condition of the path," the Buddha replied: "Not so. A good friend is the entirety of the cause and condition."

**Third, one should constantly receive, uphold, read, and study the teachings that correspond to buddha-recollection.** As a verse in the *Pratyutpanna Samādhi Sūtra* says:

> This *samādhi* sūtra is the true word of the Buddha.
> If one hears that it exists in a distant place,
> for the sake of the way of the Dharma, one should go and receive it.
> Single-mindedly recite it, never neglecting or abandoning it.
> Even if one seeks it and cannot hear it,
> the merit and blessing of that effort is inexhaustible.

Among the sūtras and treatises that offer evidence regarding the Western Land, these texts comprise the essential ones. However, for the detailed exposition of the contemplative practices of the Western Land, Amitābha's original vow, and the fine details of the Land of Utmost Bliss, none surpasses the *Sūtra on the Contemplation of the Buddha of Immeasurable Life*. For a comprehensive verse summary, none surpasses the *Upadeśa on the Sūtra of Immeasurable Life: Verses of Aspiration for Birth* [by Vasubandhu].

**Question:** The practitioner should study those texts himself. Why have you now gone to the trouble of composing this work?

**Answer:** Did I not say at the outset: for one such as myself, it is difficult to consult the vast literature. Therefore I have simply excerpted the essentials.

**Question:** The *Mahāsaṃnipāta Sūtra* says: "Those who excerpt sūtras, drop words and characters, or damage others' Dharma, or hide others' sūtras, or through excessive excerpting lose the context, or disorder the sequence: this is a cause of being born blind." Is this not self-harming?

**Answer:** Treatise masters and dharma masters of India and China, when quoting from sūtras and treatises, have often abridged and paraphrased. Therefore we know that distorting the intent of the sūtras is a cause of blindness, but abbreviating the wording is not.

Or, among the ten practices regarding the Dharma, the first, "copying," may create a fault through dropping words. But excerpting for the purpose of understanding is not a fault. Moreover, what I have excerpted here mostly consists of direct quotations. Where it is the interpretations of various masters, that is noted. And where the full text could not be reproduced, I have added notes such as "and so on," "abridged," or "gist." This is so that students may easily check the original texts.

**Question:** The direct quotations are indeed worthy of trust. But you have frequently interjected your own words. Does this not invite others' criticism?

**Answer:** Though they are not the sūtras' own words, they do not depart from the principle. If there are still errors, let the reader not cling to them. Let those who see it accept or reject as they see fit, bringing it into accord with the correct principle. If some should devote themselves entirely to criticism, I would not dare refuse. As a verse of the *Avataṃsaka* says:

> If there are those who see a bodhisattva
> practicing all manner of deeds,
> and give rise to thoughts both good and ill:
> the bodhisattva gathers them all in.

They draw in such beings. And if those beings should attain the path, may they in turn draw me in, from now until awakening, each being teacher and student to the other in turn.

**Question:** Since the topic has arisen, let us ask further: through many days of putting brush to paper, laboring in body and mind, your effort is not without purpose. What do you aspire to?

**Answer:**

> Relying on all these merits,
> at the moment of my life's end,
> may I behold Amitābha Buddha's
> body of boundless merit.
>
> May I and all who share this faith,
> having beheld that buddha,
> attain the eye free of defilement
> and realize unsurpassed awakening.

---

[Colophon]

*The Collection on the Essentials of Birth* (*Ōjōyōshū*), fascicles complete.

In the second year of Eikan (984), *kinoe-saru* (甲申 *jiǎshēn*, the cyclical year), in the winter, the eleventh month, this text was compiled at the Śūraṅgama Hall (*Shūryōgon-in*) of the Enryaku-ji monastery on Mount Hiei (Tendai-san). In the summer of the following year, the fourth month, the work was completed.

A certain monk dreamt that Vaiśravaṇa, accompanied by a child bearing a rain-charm, came and declared: "The *Collection on the Essentials of Birth* compiled by Genshin consists entirely of passages from the sūtras and treatises. All who see it or hear it, however briefly, may realize unsurpassed awakening. You should add a single verse and disseminate it widely." On a later day, the dream was recounted. Therefore the following verse was composed:

> Already relying on holy scripture and right principle,
> I encourage all beings to seek birth in the Land of Utmost Bliss.
> May even those who hear of it at third or fourth remove
> together swiftly realize unsurpassed awakening.

---

[Letter from Genshin to Song China]

The Buddha's son Genshin. Having temporarily left the home mountain, I traveled as an ascetic (*dhūtaguṇa*) practitioner through the provinces and sacred peaks and caverns of the western sea road. By chance, on the very day a distant traveler touched shore, we met face to face, unplanned. This must be a karmic bond from a former life. Yet our spoken languages did not yet communicate, and each of us was pressed to return. Therefore I now seal a letter and commit my thoughts to writing.

I have heard, from the side, that in your land the Three Treasures flourish greatly, and I rejoice in this. In our country, the teaching that flowed eastward shines once again with the Buddha's sun at its zenith. Those who engrave in their hearts the Land of Utmost Bliss and take refuge in the *Lotus Sūtra* are in full vigor. This Buddha's son is one of those who hold the Land of Utmost Bliss in mind. Because this aspiration runs deep from long habit, I composed the *Collection on the Essentials of Birth* in three fascicles, devoted to contemplation and mindfulness.

Under this single heaven and within this single Dharma, all belong to the fourfold assembly. Who is near and who is far? Therefore I presume to entrust this text to a returning ship. In my own land I am already embarrassed by its crudeness; how much more so in another country? Yet from the beginning I formed a single vow: whether there be those who slander or those who praise, both alike shall form with me a bond leading to birth in the Land of Utmost Bliss.

I also send along the *Six-Aspect Hymn* (*Rokusōsan*) by my late master, the former Chief Abbot Jie (Ryōgen), together with the *Record of Japanese Births in the Pure Land* (*Nihon ōjōden*), and, composed by the former Secretary Tameyuki, the *Rhapsody on the Lotus Sūtra* (*Hokke-kyō no fu*). These too I present, wishing to make known that this aspiration exists in a distant land.

Alas, one lifetime passes so swiftly, and the shores on both sides are grey with mist. How shall we meet again? I weep tears of blood. Words fail me.

With respectful regards.

---

[Reply from Zhou Wende of Song China]

A reply from the disciple Zhou Wende of Taizhou, Great Song.

Respectfully addressed: as the second month grows warmer and the spring breeze disperses the haze, I trust that your Dharma-position is unshaken and your body at ease. Anxiously, anxiously. With reverence, with reverence.

When Wende first came to your court, I first went to bow toward your meditation chamber. In the early winter past, I was glad to send a letter through a messenger, with full details. The letter was entrusted to the Prefect of the Capital and the man of talent from Teshima, in a single sealed envelope, which should have arrived by now. Have you had occasion to read it? My feelings of longing do not cease, morning or evening. In the midst of agitated thoughts, I take this opportunity to send a further message.

Your great master's selected *Collection on the Essentials of Birth* in three fascicles: I bore it reverently to the Guoqing Temple on Mount Tiantai and formally deposited it there. The monk in charge accepted it, and I have his receipt. Thereupon, both clerics and laity rejoiced. Men and women, over five hundred, became disciples bound by this connection. Each gave rise to a sincere heart and contributed pure offerings to the Guoqing Temple. They promptly constructed a corridor of fifty bays, painted the pillars and walls, and adorned the interior and exterior, for the purpose of worship, reverence, and joyful praise.

The Buddha's sun shines again! The Dharma-lamp burns brightly! The great foundation for the flourishing of the Buddha-Dharma and the karmic bond for birth in the Land of Utmost Bliss: this is precisely it.

Now Wende, having met these declining times, is spared the hardship of food and clothing. Relying on the imperial grace, I have not been cut off by edicts. My daily food and steamer: I would pile up dust, but how can I avoid the bewilderment of famine? I humbly beg the great master to look upon this with compassion.

Your disciple, overcome with fervent emotion, respectfully presents this letter in lieu of a personal visit.

With respectful regards.

The eleventh day of the second month. Submitted by the disciple Zhou Wende of the Great Song.

---

[Later Colophons]

The *Collection on the Essentials of Birth*: the heart-essence of the entire canon, the eyes and feet for the nine grades of birth. Though its circulation has been wide, printed editions have been few. Therefore characters are carved into woodblocks and verses arranged as strung flowers. The aspiration is mutual; the strength, not yet sufficient. We pray to the Three Treasures and encourage all people. Through their assistance, may this merit be accomplished. Whether kept within the private chamber or disseminated in the world: if there are any who wish to borrow it, do not begrudge it.

On the nineteenth day of the sixth month of the third year of Nin'an (1168), the carving was completed.

Carving completed on the eighth day of the fourth month of the fourth year of Shōgen (1210). The patron, the Great Dharma Master Jitsugen. He exerted his modest strength from his own heart and cast his treasure toward the realm of the Buddha. The work was completed in one hundred printing blocks. However, these blocks are from an earlier time when a holy person had encouraged carving from contributions gathered in the ten directions. Unexpectedly, a fire destroyed them. Therefore he had them recarved by his own effort, entirely for the attainment of buddhahood and the liberation of the four benefactors, the seven generations, and all beings throughout the dharma-realm with whom we share no particular karmic bond.

This *Collection on the Essentials*: its source is at Yokawa on Mount Hiei, and it has spread to the four seas. But with additions and deletions to the text, which version is correct and which in error? Where both text and meaning are excellent, there is no basis for choosing one over the other. After broadly consulting various texts and comparing with old editions: there are two recensions, the one sent to China and the one retained in Japan. The present text is the version sent to China. The version at the Gion Monastery... therefore we know: the version sent to China is the revised recension. Now, using the version sent to China, the blocks are carved and printed. By this merit, may both self and others be benefited. May I and all sentient beings together gather in the Land of Bliss.

Carving commenced in the fourth month of the fifth year of Kenchō (1253), the year *mizunoto-ushi*. Completed in the ninth month. Patron: Dōmyō.

On the tenth day of the fourth month of the second year of Enkyū (1070), at the Nanzen-bō of the Byōdō-in, many manuscripts were gathered and collated. Among them, the best manuscript, with the Hino reading, was followed. The assembly: the Chamberlain of the Empress's Household. The transcription overseer: the Ajari of Kabao, who completed the copying on the eleventh day of the twelfth month of the first year of Jōan (1171). The manuscript of the Śramaṇa Kōe.

Transmitted and received. Transmitted and received by Eikō.

The above *Collection on the Essentials of Birth*, upper, middle, and lower fascicles, have been obtained.

In the middle of the eleventh month of the third year of Keian (1650). Transmitted and received. Recorded by the elder monk Sonjun.
